TRANSLATOR'S PREFACE

Alright, some context on Dostoevsky might help you get into his work.

Dostoevsky was a doctor's son. His folks were hard-working, deeply religious, but broke. They shared two rooms with five kids. Nights were spent reading serious books to them.

Despite being sickly, he ranked third in his graduating class at the Petersburg school of Engineering. That's where he began his first piece, "Poor Folk."

Nekrassov, a poet, published it, and bam — the shy kid turned into a celebrity overnight. But that didn't last. In 1849, he got arrested.

He wasn't a revolutionist by nature or belief, but he was in a group reading Fourier and Proudhon. They nailed him for chatting against censorship, reading Byelinsky's letter to Gogol, and knowing about plans for a printing press. Under Nicholas I (who Maurice Baring called "stern and just"), that was enough to get him a death sentence. After eight months, they dragged him and twenty-one others to Semyonovsky Square for execution. In a letter to his brother Mihail, Dostoevsky recounted: “They fired words over our heads, put on the white shirts for the condemned. We were tied in threes to stakes. I figured I had minutes left. Thought of you and kissed Plestcheiev and Dourov goodbye. Suddenly, they called it off, untied us, and said the Tsar spared us.” The sentence changed to hard labor.

One guy, Grigoryev, lost his mind when untied and never got it back.

This ordeal marked Dostoevsky deeply. His faith made him accept suffering and see it as a personal blessing, but he often returned to the theme in his writing, detailing the condemned man's torment and criticizing the cruelty. Then came four years in Siberia with criminals, where he started "Dead House," and later served in a disciplinary battalion.

He had shown signs of a nervous issue before his arrest, which turned into severe epilepsy, hitting him three or four times a year, more during stress. In 1859, he got back to Russia. Began a journal, "Vremya," shut down by Censorship due to a misunderstanding. In 1864, he lost his first wife and brother Mihail. Despite his own poverty, he took on his brother's debts. He started another journal, "The Epoch," but it got banned too. He was drowning in debt, supporting his brother's family, writing fast with little editing. His later years softened by his second wife's care.

In June 1880, he gave a famous speech at Pushkin’s monument unveiling in Moscow, greeted with love and honor.

a few months later dostoevsky died, followed by a huge crowd, like a king's funeral. still probably the most read writer in russia.

a russian critic said about him: "he was one of us, a man of our blood and bone, but he suffered and saw so much more. his insight feels like wisdom... the wisdom of the heart we want to learn from to live better. all his other gifts were natural, but this one he earned, and it made him great."

CRIME AND PUNISHMENT

PART I

CHAPTER I

it was a crazy hot evening in early july when a young man stepped out of his garret in S. Place, moving slowly toward K. bridge like he wasn't sure about it.

he managed to dodge his landlady on the stairs. his garret was up in a five-story building, more like a cupboard than a room. she lived below, supplying garret, dinners, and attendance. every time he left, he had to pass her open kitchen door, feeling sick and scared, scowling and ashamed. he was deep in debt to her and dreaded seeing her.

not because he was a coward; far from it. but lately, he'd been wound up, irritable, almost hypochondriac. he'd isolated himself so much he feared meeting anyone, not just her. poverty crushed him, but he stopped caring about his problems. wasn't scared of any landlady. but to get stopped on the stairs, listen to her gossip, demands for payment, threats, complaints, and have to make excuses, lie—he'd rather sneak down the stairs like a cat.

but this evening, stepping into the street, his fear hit him hard.

“i want to try something like that and am scared of these little things,” he thought, smiling oddly. “hmm... everything’s in a man’s hands and he lets it slip from cowardice, that’s just true. what are men most afraid of? taking a new step, saying something new... but i talk too much. i talk because i do nothing. maybe i do nothing because i talk. learned to chatter this last month, thinking in my den... like jack the giant-killer. why am i going there now? am i really capable of that? is it serious? not serious. just fantasy to entertain myself; a plaything. maybe it’s just a plaything.”

the street was a mess. heat, dust everywhere, that usual stink you can't escape in summer. it all hit hard on the nerves, especially with all those pot-houses and drunks out even on a workday. he was all disgusted, face showing it for a second. tall, good-looking guy but lost in thought, more like his mind just shut down. wandered, mumbling to himself, ideas tangled. hadn't eaten in two days.

dressed like a wreck, even for a shabby place like that. near the Hay Market, nobody cared about rags. but he was so bitter and full of contempt, he didn’t care either, except when he saw someone he knew. hated that. then a drunk guy on a wagon shouted something about his hat. he stopped, grabbed it. old thing from Zimmerman’s, falling apart. fear hit him, not shame.

"knew it," he muttered. "that hat’s a problem. stands out too much. should’ve been a cap, not this joke. people remember the wrong details. trifles mess up plans. gotta be invisible in this."

he only had a short walk ahead. he knew the steps too—exactly seven hundred thirty from the gate. counted them once when he was lost in his head. back then, those dreams seemed pointless, just teasing himself with wild ideas. now, a month later, they felt different. even though he mocked himself for being powerless, he started seeing that "hideous" dream as something to try. he didn't even realize it yet. he was off for a "rehearsal," and each step made him more excited.

heart sinking and hands shaky, he approached a big house by the canal. working folks lived there—tailors, locksmiths, cooks, some Germans, a mix. always people coming and going through two gates and courtyards. no doorkeepers spotted him, so he slipped through the right door, up the back staircase. dark and narrow, but he knew it well. liked it too. in the dark, no prying eyes.

“if im this scared now, what if i actually did it?” he thought, reaching the fourth floor. porters blocked him, moving stuff out. a German clerk lived there, now moving out, leaving just the old woman on this floor. "that’s good," he thought, ringing her bell. faint tinkle, almost tinny. he forgot that sound, but now it sparked something. he jumped, nerves shot. door cracked open, old woman peeked through, just her eyes visible. saw people around, opened up more. he stepped into the dark entry, partitioned from the tiny kitchen. she stood, silent, sizing him up. small, withered, sharp eyes and nose. oily hair, no kerchief. thin neck with a flannel rag, old fur cape hanging despite the heat. she coughed and groaned plenty. he must've looked odd, mistrust flashed in her eyes.

“raskolnikov, student, i came here a month ago,” he rushed out, half-bowing, trying to be polite.

"i remember you coming here," the old woman said, watching him closely.

"yeah, im back for the same reason," Raskolnikov replied, a bit thrown by her suspicion. "maybe she's always like this, and i just missed it last time," he thought, feeling uneasy.

she hesitated, then moved aside and pointed to the room's door, letting him go first: "step in."

the room was lit up by the setting sun, with yellow wallpaper, geraniums, and muslin curtains. "sun'll shine like this then too," he thought, quickly taking in the room's layout. nothing special: old yellow wood furniture, a sofa, an oval table, a dressing table between the windows, chairs along the walls, some cheap prints of German girls with birds. a light burned in the corner before a small ikon. everything was spotless, shining.

"lizaveta's work," he thought. not a speck of dust anywhere.

"it's in spiteful old widows' homes you find this kind of cleanliness," he mused, glancing at a cotton curtain over the door to another small room, where the old woman's bed and drawers stood. he'd never seen inside. these two rooms made up the flat.

"what do you want?" she asked sharply, standing in front of him to look him in the eye.

"ive brought something to pawn," and he pulled out an old flat silver watch with a globe engraved on it; the chain was steel.

"but your last pledge's time is up. month ended two days ago."

"ill pay the interest for another month; just wait."

"that's for me to decide, to wait or sell it."

"how much for the watch, Alyona Ivanovna?"

"you bring such trifles, it's barely worth it. gave you two roubles for your ring last time, could buy it new for a rouble and a half."

"give me four roubles, i'll redeem it, it was my father's. getting money soon."

"a rouble and a half, interest upfront, if you want."

"a rouble and a half!" he shouted.

"your choice," she said, handing back the watch. he took it, angry enough to leave, but stopped, remembering he had nowhere else to go and another reason for being there.

"hand it over," he said roughly.

she fumbled for her keys and disappeared behind the curtain. left alone, he listened carefully, hearing her unlock the chest of drawers.

gotta be the top drawer, he thought. carries the keys in her right pocket. all bunched up on a steel ring... and there's this one key, way bigger than the others, deep notches; can't be for the chest of drawers... so some other chest or strong-box... that's good to know. strong-boxes have keys like that... but man, it's all just degrading.

the old woman came back.

“here, sir: ten copecks a rouble a month, so i take fifteen copecks from a rouble and a half for the month ahead. but for those two roubles i lent you, you owe me twenty copecks in advance. makes thirty-five copecks total. so here's a rouble and fifteen copecks for the watch.”

“what! just a rouble and fifteen copecks now!”

“just so.”

the young man took the money, no fight. looked at her, lingered like he had something else to say or do, but didn't know what.

“might bring you something else soon, alyona ivanovna--a silver cigarette-box, once i get it back from a friend...” he trailed off, uneasy.

“we'll talk about it then, sir.”

“good-bye--you always home alone, your sister not with you?” he asked, trying to sound casual as he left.

“what business is she of yours, my good sir?”

“oh, nothing, just asked. you're too quick.... good-day, alyona ivanovna.”

raskolnikov left, all mixed up. confusion grew. stopped on the stairs a couple times, hit by thoughts. out on the street, he blurted, “oh, god, how loathsome! and can i, can i possibly... no, it's nonsense, rubbish!” he said firmly. “how could such a thing enter my head? what filthy stuff my heart can hold. yes, filthy, disgusting, loathsome!--and for a month i've been....” but no words matched his turmoil. the feeling, pressing on him since heading to the old woman, now clear and sharp, left him stuck. he stumbled along the pavement like he'd been drinking, bumping into folks, only snapped out of it in the next street. he found himself near a tavern, steps leading down. two drunken guys staggered out, swearing and leaning on each other. without thinking, raskolnikov went in. never been in a tavern before, but felt dizzy, craved a cold beer. blamed weakness on lack of food. sat at a grubby little table in a dark corner; ordered beer, gulped the first glass. felt better; thoughts clearer.

“all nonsense," he said, hopeful, "nothing to worry about! just physical derangement. a glass of beer, dry bread—and suddenly the brain's sharper, mind's clear, will's firm! phew, how petty it all is!”

he's looking around all cheerful like he just dropped a heavy load. but even then, he kinda knew it wasn't gonna last. tavern's almost empty now. the two drunks he met outside left with a group and a girl with a concertina. room's quiet now.

just a couple of folks left. an artisan dude, a little drunk but not overboard, sipping beer. his buddy, big guy with a grey beard, is out cold on the bench, mumbling some lines in his sleep:

“His wife a year he fondly loved
    His wife a... a year he... fondly loved.”

then waking up with:

“Walking along the crowded row
    He met the one he used to know.”

nobody really paying him any mind. his friend looks kinda annoyed. there's another guy, might be a retired clerk, sipping his drink, looking agitated too.

raskolnikov, not one for crowds usually, suddenly wanted to be around people. something's shifting inside him, and he's craving company. he's been in a dark place for a month and just wants a break, even in this dive. the tavern's filthy, but he's okay with staying.

the owner pops in now and then, red-topped boots first. wearing a greasy coat and waistcoat, no cravat, face shiny like an old lock. a boy at the counter, maybe 14, and another younger one helping. counter's got sliced cucumber, dried black bread, and chopped fish, all smelling rank. air's thick with booze fumes, enough to get you tipsy just standing there.

sometimes you meet someone and you just know they're gonna be interesting before they even say anything. thats what happened with Raskolnikov and this guy who looked like he'd been a clerk before. Raskolnikov kept thinking about it, sorta felt like a gut feeling. this clerk dude kept staring at him, like he wanted to chat, ignoring everyone else in the room who he seemed kinda bored with, like they were beneath him. 

the guy was over fifty, balding, kinda solid build, with a face that showed he drank too much, skin kinda yellow-green, eyes red but sharp. something off about him, like he was smart but a bit mad too. his coat was a mess, only one button left and he had it done up like he was holding onto some dignity. no beard, no mustache, but his chin was a mess of grey stubble. had this official vibe, but couldnt sit still, kept messing with his hair and putting his head in his hands. 

then he just looks at Raskolnikov and says, loud enough for everyone to hear, “mind if i talk to you, sir? you seem educated, not a drinker. i respect that. im Marmeladov, a titular counsellor. you ever been in the service?”

Raskolnikov says, “no, im studying,” a bit thrown off by the guy's fancy talk. he wanted company a second ago, but now he feels his usual itch to keep strangers at a distance.

“a student!” the clerk shouts. “thought so! ive got experience, real experience,” tapping his forehead like it proves something. “you've studied somewhere, right?... but hang on.” he gets up, wobbles, grabs his drink, and sits next to Raskolnikov, kinda sideways. guys drunk but talks like hes not, just losing his way sometimes. he latched onto Raskolnikov like he hadn't talked to anyone in ages.

"hey sir," he started, kind of serious, "being poor ain't a crime, true enough. but drinking? not exactly a badge of honor either. now beggary, well, that's a different beast. you can be broke and still keep your dignity, but begging? nah, that's a whole other story. folks don't just kick you out; they sweep you away like trash. deservedly so, cause if i'm begging, i'm already throwing away my pride. hence the bar! a month ago, Lebeziatnikov smacked my wife around, and she's not like me, you know? got a question for you: ever spent a night on a hay barge on the Neva?"

"no, can't say i have," Raskolnikov replied. "what's your point?"

"well, i just crawled off one, fifth night running." he poured another drink, knocked it back, paused. bits of hay were stuck to him, all over. looked like he hadn't changed or washed in days. hands filthy, fat, red, nails black.

his talk stirred up some interest, even if folks were half-bored. kids at the counter giggled. the innkeeper came down, maybe just to watch the "funny guy," sat nearby, yawning like he owned the place. Marmeladov was a regular here, probably picked up his fancy talk from chatting up strangers. some drunks need that. kept on a tight leash at home, so they let loose in a bar, try to justify themselves, maybe even earn a bit of respect.

"funny guy!" the innkeeper said. "why ain't you working, doing your job, if you've got one?"

"why ain't i working, sir," Marmeladov kept on, like Raskolnikov had asked. "why ain't i at it? doesn't it kill me to be useless? a month back, when Lebeziatnikov hit my wife, and i was drunk, didn't i feel it? ever begged for a loan without hope, young man?"

"yeah, but what's hopelessly?"

"hopelessly, when you know it's a no-go from the start. you know this guy, this upstanding citizen, won't lend you a dime. why would he? he knows i won't pay back. from pity? but Lebeziatnikov says compassion’s out, science says so, like in England with their economics. why give me anything? and still, i go to him and..."

"why bother?" Raskolnikov cut in.

look, when you got nowhere else to go, you just gotta go somewhere. everybody needs a place to be, even when there's nowhere left. my daughter got tangled up with a yellow ticket, so i had to move. she's got a yellow passport now. doesn't matter, doesn't matter! yeah, they laugh, but everyone knows, and secrets don't stay secrets. i take it all in, not with spite, but with acceptance. that's how it is. 

excuse me, can you, no... will you look at me and say i'm not a pig?

no answer, huh? alright then, maybe i'm a pig, but she's a lady. i might look like a beast, but katerina ivanovna, my wife, she's educated, a noble's daughter. sure, i'm a scoundrel, but she's got a noble heart. if only she felt for me, you know? a man needs a place where people care. but katerina, she's grand but not fair. pulls my hair out of pity—yes, pity. believe it or not, she does. but oh, just once... never mind, it's useless. no point talking.

i've sold her stockings for drink, not just shoes, stockings! even her mohair shawl, her own gift. we live cold, she coughs blood. three kids, and katerina works day and night, scrubbing, cleaning. she's got a weak chest, prone to illness. i feel it, and the more i drink, the more i feel it. that's why i drink, to feel more. i drink to double the suffering. 

then he just drops his head on the table, like he's got nothing left.

young man, he said, looking up, you seem troubled. saw it when you walked in. that's why i spoke. i don't want to be a joke here. they know my story already. but i'm trying to find someone who'd get it. my wife went to a top-notch school for noble girls. danced for the governor, got a medal and a certificate. sold the medal ages back. certificate's still in her trunk. showed it to our landlady once. even though they're always at each other's throats, she wanted to share her past glories. can't blame her. all she's got now is memories. everything else is dust. she's proud, scrubs floors, eats black bread. won't take disrespect. she didn't let mr. lebeziatnikov's rudeness slide, took a beating for it, but it was more the hurt feelings than the bruises that put her in bed. she was a widow when i met her. three kids, each tinier than the last. married her first husband, an infantry officer, for love. ran away from home for him. loved him dearly. but he got caught up in cards, died leaving trouble behind. used to hit her. she hit back though. got proof of that. yet she still talks about him with tears. throws him up to me sometimes. glad she thinks she was happy once, even if it's just in her mind. when he died, left her in the middle of nowhere. in poverty i can't even put into words. all her people abandoned her. she was proud, too proud... and i, a widower then, with a daughter, offered to marry her. couldn't stand her suffering. shows how desperate she was, a woman of her background marrying me. she did though. cried through the ceremony. had nowhere else to go. you can't imagine having nowhere to turn, can you? no. a whole year, i was good. didn't touch this (taps the jug). but i couldn't make her happy. lost my job too, not my fault. office changes. then i did hit the bottle. been a year and a half since we ended up here in this big city full of monuments. got a job, lost it. my fault this time, couldn't stay off the drink. living now in a shared room with amalia fyodorovna lippevechsel. how we survive, no clue. lots of folks there. chaos. my girl from my first wife, she's grown. her step-mother's been tough on her. katerina ivanovna's got a good heart but she's fiery and quick to anger. no point in going over that. sonia, she's had no schooling. tried geography and history with her four years back, but i wasn't much help. no books, or the ones we had, well, they're gone. stopped at cyrus of persia. she's read some romance novels, and recently, a book called lewes’ physiology. got it from mr. lebeziatnikov. that's her education. now, let me ask you, can a decent poor girl make a living? not even fifteen farthings a day without a special skill, and even then, it's non-stop work. ivan ivanitch klopstock, the civil counselor, you know him? never paid her for the shirts she made. turned her away, insulted her. collars weren't right, he said. meanwhile, the little ones are starving. katerina ivanovna pacing, cheeks flushed from her illness. 'you're here eating and drinking,' she says, 'and you do nothing.' as if there's anything to eat or drink when the kids haven't had a crumb for days. i was drunk, lying there. heard sonia's soft voice. 'am i really to do that?' she asked. darya frantsovna, a known troublemaker, tried to get at her. 'why not?' katerina ivanovna sneered. don't blame her. she wasn't herself. sickness and kids crying drove her. she lashes out when it gets too much. at six, sonia got up. wrapped her kerchief, left. came back at nine, laid thirty roubles on the table without a word. didn't even look at her. picked up our big green shawl, covered herself, laid down facing the wall. her little frame shivering. i just lay there, drunk. then i saw katerina ivanovna kneel by sonia's bed, kissed her feet all evening, wouldn't leave her side. finally, they fell asleep holding each other. and me... i stayed drunk.

Marmeladov paused like his voice just quit on him. Then he quickly poured another drink, downed it, and cleared his throat.

“Since then, sir,” he continued after a short break, “since then, due to some bad luck and some meddling folks — mainly Darya Frantsovna claiming she was disrespected — my daughter Sofya Semyonovna had to take a yellow ticket. Because of that, she can't live with us anymore. Our landlady, Amalia Fyodorovna, wouldn't have it (even though she used to side with Darya Frantsovna), and Mr. Lebeziatnikov too... hm... The fight with Katerina Ivanovna was all about Sonia. First, he tried to get close to Sonia, then suddenly acted all high and mighty: ‘how,’ he said, ‘can an educated man like me live with a girl like that?’ And Katerina Ivanovna wouldn’t let that slide. She stood up for her... and that’s how it went down. Now Sonia visits us mostly at night; she comforts Katerina Ivanovna and gives her whatever she can... She’s got a room with the Kapernaumovs, the tailors; they’re all cleft palates, Kapernaumov and his whole family. And the wife too. They all live in one room, but Sonia’s got her own little space partitioned off... Hm... yes, very poor folks, all cleft palates... yes. Then I got up one morning, put on my rags, looked up to heaven, and headed to his excellency Ivan Afanasyvitch. You know him? No? Well, then you don’t know a real man of God. He’s like wax... wax in the Lord’s hands; melts like wax... His eyes went dim hearing my story. ‘Marmeladov, you’ve let me down once before... I’ll give you another chance, on my own responsibility,’ he said, ‘remember,’ he said, ‘and now you can go.’ I thought of kissing the dust at his feet—only in thought, though, because he wouldn’t allow it, being a modern political man with enlightened ideas. I went home and when I announced I was back in service with a salary, man, it was something...”

Marmeladov stopped, all wound up. Just then, a bunch of drunk folks came in from outside, with a concertina and a seven-year-old singing “The Hamlet.” The room filled with noise. The tavern-keeper and the boys got busy with the newcomers. Marmeladov ignored them and kept on. He looked pretty weak now, but the more he drank, the more he talked. Getting that job seemed to light him up, and it showed on his face. Raskolnikov listened closely.

that was five weeks back. yeah... soon as katerina ivanovna and sonia got word, man, it was like stepping into heaven. before, it was all lies and yelling. now they’re tiptoeing around, quieting the kids. "semyon zaharovitch worked hard, let him rest, shh!" they made me coffee before work, real cream too. how they scraped together money for a decent outfit—eleven roubles, fifty copecks—I cant figure. boots, shirt-fronts, all fancy for eleven roubles. first morning back from the office, katerina cooked soup and salt meat with horseradish—never had that before. she didn’t have nice dresses, but she fixed herself up like she was going out. hair done, clean collar, cuffs—looked younger, better. sonia helped with money, said she shouldn’t visit too often. maybe after dark, she said. you hear? after lunch, i nap, and katerina, who fought with landlady amalia a week ago, invites her for coffee. they chat for hours. “semyon zaharovitch is back in service, getting a salary,” she says. made up a story about me meeting with his excellency, him leading me in by hand. katerina believes it herself. when i brought my first pay—twenty-three roubles forty copecks—she called me her poppet, pinched my cheek. 

marmeladov tried to smile but his chin trembled. he held it together. the tavern, his sorry look, nights on the hay barge, the booze, and still such love for his wife and kids—left raskolnikov confused. he listened, feeling sick and regretting coming here.

"honored sir," marmeladov started, "maybe this seems funny to you like it does to others, but it's not to me. i feel it all... that day i dreamed of fixing everything, dressing the kids, saving my daughter... lots of plans. but then, just five days ago, i stole the key from katerina ivanovna, took what was left of my earnings. now look at me, it's been five days gone, lost my job, my uniform's in a tavern on the egyptian bridge. traded it for these clothes... it's the end."

he hit his head with his fist, teeth clenched, eyes shut, leaning on the table. then his face changed, he looked at raskolnikov, laughed, and said, "this morning i saw sonia, asked her for a drink! he-he-he!"

"she gave it to you?" someone new shouted, laughing.

"this quart was bought with her money," marmeladov said to raskolnikov. "thirty copecks, her last. she said nothing, just looked at me... up there, they grieve but don’t blame, and it hurts more. thirty copecks! maybe she needs them now, eh? you know, keeping up appearances costs. shoes, pomatum, all that smartness. and here i took thirty copecks for a drink! already drunk it! who'll pity a man like me? are you sorry for me or not? he-he-he!"

he tried to fill his glass, but it was empty.

"why pity you?" the tavern-keeper shouted.

laughter and curses followed from those watching the ex-clerk.

"to be pitied! why?" marmeladov shouted, arm outstretched, as if waiting for that question.

"Why pity me, huh? No pity needed! I should be nailed up, not pitied! Crucify me, judge, but spare your pity! I'll walk myself to the cross. I didn't want a good time, just tears and struggle... You think I enjoyed this pint? I wanted the trouble at the bottom of it, and I found it. But He, the one who understands everyone, He's the judge. He'll ask, ‘Where's the daughter who gave herself for her family, for her sick step-mother and her siblings? Where’s the daughter who cared for her drunk father despite his mess?’ And He'll say, ‘Come to me! I forgave you once... your sins are forgiven because you loved much...’ He'll forgive my Sonia, I know it... felt it in my heart just now! He'll forgive all, good and bad, wise and meek... When He's done, He'll call us. ‘You too, come forth,’ He'll say, ‘Come forth, you drunkards, you weak ones, you children of shame!’ And we'll come, no shame, stand before Him. And He'll say, ‘You’re swine, made like the Beast; but come, too!’ The wise will ask, ‘Why take these in?’ And He'll say, ‘Because none of them thought they were worthy.’ He'll reach out, and we'll fall before Him... we'll weep... we'll understand all! Everyone will understand, even Katerina Ivanovna... she will... Lord, Thy kingdom come!” He collapsed on the bench, exhausted, lost in thought. His words hit home; silence followed, then laughter and curses filled the air again.

"That's his idea!"

"Talked himself silly!"

"What a clerk!"

And on it went.

"Let's go, sir," Marmeladov said suddenly, looking at Raskolnikov—“come with me... Kozel’s house, facing the yard. I'm heading to Katerina Ivanovna—about time."

Raskolnikov had wanted to go, planning to help him. Marmeladov, steadier in speech than on his feet, leaned heavily on him. They had a couple hundred paces to go. The closer they got, the more dismayed and confused Marmeladov became.

"it's not katerina ivanovna i’m worried about now," he muttered, all restless. "and not her yanking my hair either. hair's whatever! let her yank it, that ain't what scares me... it's her eyes... yeah, those eyes... and the red on her cheeks too, that's unsettling... and her breathing... you ever notice how folks breathe when they’re riled up with that illness? i'm spooked by the kids crying too... if sonia hasn't fed them... no clue what's up! no clue! but getting hit? nah, that doesn't bother me... listen, those hits don't hurt; they’re almost comforting. actually need it... it's better this way. let her hit me, it eases her mind... yeah, it's better... there's the place. kozels house, the cabinet-maker... german guy, doing alright. lead on!"

they went in through the yard, climbed up to the fourth floor. stairwell got darker with each step. it was close to eleven. summer in petersburg doesn't really get dark, but up those stairs, it was pitch.

at the top, a grubby little door stood open. a shabby room, maybe ten paces across, lit by just a candle stub; you could see it all from the doorway. a mess, bits of cloth everywhere, mostly kids' clothes. a ragged sheet hung in one corner, probably hiding a bed. room had just two chairs and a torn-up sofa covered in worn-out american leather, and an old kitchen table, bare wood, no paint or cover. on the table's edge, a tallow candle burned in an iron holder. seemed like the family had their own room, not a shared space, but it was more like a hallway. the door to other rooms, or really little closets, in amalia lippevechsel's flat was half open. inside, shouting, noise, laughter. sounded like they were playing cards, maybe drinking tea. rough words flying out now and then.

Raskolnikov spotted Katerina Ivanovna right away. Tall, slim, kind of elegant, but way too thin. Her dark brown hair was striking, but her cheeks had that unhealthy flush. She was pacing in her cramped room, hands pressed to her chest, lips all dry, breathing in these short, nervous bursts. Her eyes were fever-bright, staring hard at nothing. The dim candlelight flickered on her face, making her look even more worn. She seemed around thirty, an odd match for Marmeladov. She didn’t hear them come in, lost in her own world. The room was stuffy, no window open, the door left ajar letting in the stairwell stench. Tobacco smoke drifted from inside, she kept coughing but didn’t shut the door. A six-year-old girl slept on the floor, head on the sofa. A year older boy stood crying, trembling in the corner, probably just got hit. Next to him, a nine-year-old girl, tall, thin, in a ragged chemise with an old pelisse barely covering her. Her stick-thin arm wrapped around her brother’s neck, whispering to calm him down, all while her big dark eyes watched her mom with worry. Marmeladov didn’t step in, just knelt at the doorway, pushing Raskolnikov ahead. The woman, noticing a stranger, paused, seemingly wondering why he was there. She figured he was just passing through to the next room. Ignoring him, she headed to shut the outer door and screamed on seeing her husband kneeling there.

“Ah!” she yelled, frantic. “He’s back! The criminal! Monster!... Where’s the money? What’s in your pockets, show me! And your clothes are different! Where are your clothes? Where’s the money! Speak!”

She started searching him. Marmeladov obediently raised his arms to make it easier. Not a coin found.

“Where’s the money?” she cried. “God, did he drink it all? There were twelve silver roubles left!” In rage, she grabbed his hair, dragging him inside. Marmeladov helped by crawling on his knees.

“And this comforts me! Doesn’t hurt, but is a con-so-la-tion, ho-nou-red sir,” he yelled, shaking as she yanked him, banging his head on the floor. The sleeping child woke, crying. The boy in the corner, panicked, started shaking and screaming, rushing to his sister, almost seizing. The eldest girl trembled like a leaf.

“He’s drunk it! Drank it all,” the woman screamed, desperate. “His clothes are gone! And they’re starving, starving!” She waved toward the kids. “Oh, cursed life! And you, aren’t you ashamed?” she snapped at Raskolnikov. “From the tavern! Drinking with him? You drank with him too! Get out!”

young guy's bolting out, not a word. door swings open, folks poking in—laughing, smoking, caps on. inside, some in robes half-open, cards in hand. Marmeladov's yelling, they think it's a riot. they start coming in, then Amalia Lippevechsel storms through, trying to boss everyone around, cursing at the poor woman to clear out next day. as Raskolnikov leaves, he slips some coins on the window, second-guessing himself on the stairs. "dumb move," he thinks, "I need that too." but can't take it back now, walks on. "Sonia needs pomatum," he smirks, "smart ideas cost." wonders if Sonia's broke today too, chasing dreams...then they're all broke but for me. cheers to Sonia! they've got a goldmine there, milking it dry. they've cried over it, got used to it. people get used to anything, the bastards.

he stops, thinks, "what if I'm wrong? maybe people ain't scoundrels, maybe it's all just fear made up, no real barriers. maybe this is how it should be."

next day, wakes late, rough sleep. not rested, just cranky, hates the room. it's a cramped little box, paper peeling, low ceiling—duck or you'll hit your head. furniture's old and shaky, table's dusty with old papers. big old sofa takes up half the space, used to be chintz-covered, now in rags. it's his bed. he crashes on it as is, no sheets, just his old coat, pillow, and a pile of linen for a bolster. little table in front of the sofa.

raskolnikov's world was a mess and somehow that chaos was comforting. he’d shut himself off, like a turtle in its shell. even seeing the servant girl bugged him. he was all wrapped up in one thought, like some folks get. his landlady stopped sending him meals two weeks ago, and he hadn’t even thought to argue it, even though he skipped dinner. nastasya, the cook and only servant, didn’t mind his mood. she barely cleaned his room, maybe once a week. she woke him that day.

“wake up, why are you still asleep?” she called. “it’s past nine. i brought you some tea. you must be starving.”

his eyes opened, recognized nastasya.

“from the landlady?” he asked slowly, looking sick as he sat up.

“from the landlady, ha!”

she put down her cracked teapot with weak tea and a couple of yellow sugar lumps.

“nastasya, take this,” he said, searching his pocket (he’d slept in his clothes) and pulled out some coppers. “get me a loaf and the cheapest sausage from the pork-butcher.”

“i’ll get the loaf now, but how about some leftover cabbage soup instead of sausage? it’s real good, saved it for you yesterday, but you came in late.”

when she brought the soup and he started on it, nastasya sat beside him on the sofa, chatting away. she was a talkative country woman.

“praskovya pavlovna’s thinking of calling the police on you,” she said.

he frowned.

“the police? what’s her game?”

“you don’t pay her and won’t leave the room. that’s her game.”

“damn it, that’s the last thing i need,” he muttered, grinding his teeth. “no, not now. she’s a fool,” he added louder. “i’ll go talk to her today.”

“fool she is, like me. but if you’re so smart, why lie here doing nothing? you used to teach kids, right? what happened?”

“i am doing...” raskolnikov started, sullen and slow.

“what’re you doing?”

“work...”

“what kind?”

“thinking,” he said seriously after a pause.

nastasya burst into laughter. when she laughed, she did it silently, shaking till she felt sick.

“made much money thinking?” she finally got out.

“can’t teach without boots. and i’m sick of it.”

“don’t fight your bread and butter.”

“lesson money’s a pittance. what's a few coppers?” he said, like he was talking to himself.

“trying to get rich quick?”

he looked at her, odd.

“yeah, i want a fortune,” he said firmly after a pause.

“don’t rush, you’re scaring me! should i get the loaf or not?”

“up to you.”

“oh, i forgot! a letter came for you yesterday when you were out.”

“a letter? for me! from who?”

“no idea. i gave three copecks to the postman for it. you pay me back?”

“then bring it to me, please, bring it,” raskolnikov shouted, all excited—“good god!”

a minute later, the letter showed up. from his mom, from R----. he went pale holding it. been ages since his last letter, but something else hit him hard.

“Nastasya, leave me alone, please. here’s your three copecks, just go!”

the letter shook in his hand; he didnt want to open it with her there; wanted to be by _himself_ with it. when Nastasya left, he kissed it quick; stared at the address, the familiar small sloping handwriting from his mom who taught him reading and writing. he hesitated; almost seemed scared. finally opened it; thick heavy letter, over two ounces, two big sheets full of tiny writing.

hey Rodya,

been a couple months since i last wrote. that's been eating at me, kept me up some nights. but i know you'll understand why i had to keep quiet. you know how much we depend on you, Dounia and i. you're our everything. it hit me hard when i heard you left the university cuz you couldn't afford it anymore, lost your lessons and other work. wish i could've helped with my pension, but those 120 roubles a year don't stretch far. the 15 roubles i sent was borrowed from Vassily Ivanovitch, a merchant here who's a good friend of your dad's. i had to pay that back first before sending you more, but finally, things are looking up.

first off, Dounia's been with me for six weeks now, and she's staying. her troubles with the Svidrigaïlovs are over, but i'll give you the whole rundown so you know what went down. you asked about it two months ago, but i couldn't tell you then. if i had, you'd have dropped everything to help, and you know Dounia wouldn't have let that happen. she took that governess job to send you 60 roubles last year. we said it was from her savings, but that wasn't true.

Svidrigaïlov was awful to her, and though his wife Marfa was kind, there was no escaping without causing a fuss. then Marfa misjudged a scene in the garden, blamed Dounia, and things got ugly. Dounia came home in a rainstorm, her things thrown into a cart. for a whole month after, gossip was everywhere, and we couldn't even show our faces at church. Marfa spread lies, and folks wouldn't even nod to us in the street.

but then Svidrigaïlov showed Marfa a letter from Dounia proving her innocence. Dounia had written it to reject his advances. Marfa realized her mistake, apologized everywhere, and cleared Dounia's name. Dounia's now getting respect again, and she's got a suitor – Pyotr Petrovitch Luzhin. he's a counsellor, related to Marfa, and made his intentions clear quickly. we didn't wait for your input, knowing how you'd judge from a distance.

Luzhin's 45, a practical man with his quirks. Dounia sees this as a sensible match, even if there's no great love. she's smart, patient, and will make a good home. he's a bit full of himself, but she's ready to handle it. she prayed long and hard before deciding, knowing it's the right step for her future.

take care,  
mom

pyotr petrovitch is heading to petersburg to set up a legal bureau. he’s been handling civil and commercial cases for years. just won a big one, and now he's got another before the senate. he might be a huge help for you, Rodya. dounia and i think this could kickstart your career. she’s really banking on it. we've hinted at it to pyotr, but he’s cautious. said he’d rather hire family than a stranger, but worried about your school schedule. dounia is dreaming big, seeing you as his partner someday. i’m on board with her plans. pyotr hasn’t promised anything yet, but dounia’s confident. we haven’t told him we’re hoping he'll help with your tuition. want you to meet him on equal footing. he’s practical, might not like hearing long-term dreams right off. he’ll decide more when he meets you.

i’ve been thinking i might live on my own after the wedding. pyotr will probably invite me to stay, but i’d rather be independent. don’t want to be a burden or clash with him. i’ll be nearby, so that’s good. best news: dounia and i are heading to petersburg soon, maybe a week. depends on pyotr. wedding might be before or after the fast. can’t wait to see you again after almost three years. dounia's excited too. she joked she’d marry pyotr just to see you. she’s not writing right now but sends love.

i’ll try to send some money soon, maybe 25 or 30 roubles. with dounia marrying pyotr, my credit’s better. afanasy ivanovitch might lend on my pension. we’ve planned the trip costs, and pyotr’s covering some. we’ll travel third class to save. might send more if possible. written a lot already, but so much happening. embrace you till we meet. love dounia like she loves you. she’s our angel and you’re our hope. are you still praying? i worry you’ve strayed from faith. remember your childhood prayers. goodbye till we meet. many kisses.

"yours till death,

PULCHERIA RASKOLNIKOV."

right from the start, tears just ran down his face reading that letter. but by the end, he was pale, twisted, with this bitter, angry grin. he dropped his head onto his worn-out pillow and just thought. heart racing, mind a mess. felt trapped in that small, yellow room. needed out. grabbed his hat, left. didn’t care who he ran into; fear wasn’t even there. headed towards Vassilyevsky Ostrov, just moving, like he had somewhere to be, but really, he was talking to himself out loud, not watching his path. folks thought he was drunk.

CHAPTER IV

his mom’s letter was pure agony. but about the main thing, he had zero doubt, even while reading. decision made, no turning back: "no way this marriage happens while im alive, and Luzhin can go to hell!" "it’s clear as day," he muttered, grinning like he’d won. "no, mom, no, Dounia, you’re not fooling me! then they apologize for not asking me first, making decisions without me! right. they think it’s done and can’t be undone; well, we’ll see about that! what an excuse: ‘Pyotr Petrovitch is so busy his wedding’s on fast-forward.’ no, Dounia, i see it all, know what you mean to tell me; i know what you were thinking pacing all night, praying to the Holy Mother of Kazan in mom’s room. bitter road to Golgotha... Hm... so it’s settled; you’re marrying a ‘sensible’ businessman, Avdotya Romanovna, one with a fortune (already made it, which is more solid), a guy holding two government posts, with ideas of our ‘rising generation,’ as mom writes, and who _seems_ kind, as Dounia notes. that _seems_ tops everything! and for that _seems_, Dounia’s marrying him! just great!

"... but why’d mom write about ‘our rising generation’? just to describe or to make me like Luzhin? oh, their tricks! i wonder—how honest were they with each other that day and since then? did they talk it out, or just knew, no need to say it? probably both. mom’s letter shows it: she found him a bit rude, and in her simple way, told Dounia. she’d be annoyed and ‘answered angrily.’ understandable! who wouldn’t be mad when it’s obvious, no need for dumb questions. why write, ‘love Dounia, Rodya, and she loves you more than herself’? is she guilty about sacrificing her for me? ‘you’re our one comfort, everything to us.’ oh, mom!"

his bitterness just burned hotter, and if Luzhin showed up right then, he might’ve done something terrible.

yeah, that’s true. it’s like they say, “takes time and care to know a man,” but no mistake with mr. luzhin. he’s a “businessman and seems kind.” sure, sending bags and the big box. kind move, right? but then his bride and her mom are riding in a peasant cart with sacking for cover. been there. it’s only 90 versts, and then they can go third class for a thousand. makes sense, spend what you’ve got. but luzhin? your bride, man. and her mom’s got to pawn her pension for this trip. business, sure. equal shares, right? but the luggage costs less than their fares, maybe even free. why don’t they see that? or maybe they don’t want to. and they’re happy about it. this is just the start. the real issue’s the tone, not the stinginess. that’s what marriage will be like. and the mom, spending all she’s got. what’s left when she gets to petersburg? maybe three silver roubles if she’s lucky. she can’t live with dounia after the wedding. luzhin’s probably hinted at that. she’ll deny it, but who’s she counting on then? her pension after paying ivanovitch’s debt? she makes shawls and cuffs, but that’s just twenty roubles a year extra. betting on luzhin’s generosity; “he’ll offer it, he’ll insist.” don’t hold your breath. these types, every goose is a swan to them till the end. they won’t see the truth till it’s in their face. they know, but they avoid it till they have no choice. does luzhin have any honors? bet he’s got an order of anna for show at dinners and weddings. enough about him, forget it.

man, i get the mother part, she's always been like that, bless her heart. but Dounia? i knew you when you were almost twenty, understood you back then. mom writes, “Dounia can handle a lot.” yeah, i knew that two and a half years ago. been thinking on it since. if she could deal with Svidrigaïlov, she can handle just about anything. and now, they think she can manage with Luzhin, who thinks wives should be grateful for being lifted from poverty. he even said it right off the bat. maybe it wasn’t a slip, maybe he meant it clear. but Dounia, she gets him, but living with him? she’d live on scraps before selling her soul. she wouldn’t trade her freedom for all the comforts Luzhin offers. she wasn’t like that then and she’s not now. 

living as a governess for two hundred roubles is rough. but better that than chaining herself to a man she can’t respect. even if Luzhin was pure gold, she wouldn’t agree. so why is she? what’s the reason? she wouldn’t do it for herself, but for someone she loves, she might. for her brother, her mother, she’d sacrifice. she thinks it's her duty for a good cause. it’s clear Rodya’s at the center of this. keep him in school, secure his future, maybe make him rich and respected. but what about mom? it’s all about Rodya, the firstborn. for him, who wouldn’t sacrifice? 

but Dounia, Sonia’s life isn’t any worse than life with Luzhin. mom says there’s no love there. what if there’s no respect either? what then? keeping up appearances, that’s just like living Sonia’s life, maybe worse. it’s for luxuries, not survival. can you handle it if you regret it later? the hidden misery, the tears. how will mom feel then? and me? i won’t accept your sacrifice, Dounia, not while i’m around. won’t happen.

he stopped cold in his tracks.

"it wont happen? but how're you stopping it? you'll forbid it? and who gave you that right? what're you offering them for that right? you'll dedicate your life after you've graduated and got a job? sure, we’ve heard that before, just words. but now? something's gotta happen now, got it? what're you doing now? you're living off them. they're borrowing on their tiny pension. borrowing from the Svidrigaïlovs. how're you gonna save them from Svidrigaïlovs, from Vahrushin, oh future millionaire who's supposed to set their lives straight? in ten years? by then, mom'll be blind from knitting, maybe from crying too. she'll be worn to nothing from fasting. and my sister? imagine what could happen to her in ten years. can you even picture it?"

he kept torturing himself, stressing over these questions, almost finding a strange satisfaction in it. but these weren't new questions, they were old wounds. they'd been nagging at him for a long time, building up until now they screamed for an answer. his mom's letter hit him like a thunderclap. he couldn't just sit around worrying, he had to act, and fast. he needed a decision, or else...

"or just give up!" he shouted in frustration—"accept everything as it is, stop fighting, and quit on life, love, everything!"

"do you understand, sir, what it means to have nowhere to turn?" Marmeladov's words echoed in his mind. "everyone needs somewhere to go...."

he jolted, a thought from yesterday crept back in. but it didn't surprise him; he knew it would circle back. it wasn't just yesterday's thought. the difference? a month ago, yesterday even, it felt like a dream. now... now it was real, threatening, unfamiliar. and he knew it. his head was pounding, vision going dark.

he was hustling down K---- Boulevard, looking for a spot to sit. Spotted one about a hundred paces ahead. But on the way, something else caught his eye. A woman, maybe 20 paces in front, moving in a strange way. First, he didn’t give her much thought, but there was something odd about her. Young, no hat or parasol, arms waving like she didn’t care. Her dress, silky but off-kilter, not buttoned right, a big tear hanging loose. She was stumbling, too, like she’d had too much to drink.

when he reached the seat, she was already there, collapsed in the corner. Head back, eyes closed, out cold. Drunk as a skunk. She was young, maybe 16 or 15, pretty face but flushed and swollen, didn’t seem aware she was even on the street. 

he didn’t sit. Just stood there, unsure what to do. Boulevard was empty, hot as hell. Except across the way, a guy was lurking, watching. Looked like he had his own plans for her. Fashionably dressed, about 30, red lips, mustache — a real dandy. Raskolnikov was pissed, wanted to hit him. Left the girl for a second to confront him.

“Hey! Svidrigaïlov! What do you want?” he yelled, fists ready.

“What’s your problem?” the guy shot back, acting all high and mighty.

“Get lost, that’s what.”

“Who do you think you are?”

he raised his cane. Raskolnikov charged, fists flying, not thinking. But then someone grabbed him, a cop stepped in between.

"alright, guys, knock it off. no fighting here. what do you need? who are you?" he asked Raskolnikov, eyeing his ragged clothes.

Raskolnikov gave him a hard look. the man had a no-nonsense, soldier vibe with his grey moustaches and whiskers.

"you’re exactly who i need," Raskolnikov said, grabbing his arm. "i’m a student, Raskolnikov... might as well know that," he added, talking to the guy. "come on, got something to show you."

he led the cop to the bench.

"see this? she’s hammered, just came down the boulevard. no idea who she is, but she don’t look like she's in the game. probably got drugged and dumped... first-time, get it? dressed by someone else, look at her clothes. not her own doing, and definitely not by someone who knows what they’re doing. now that guy over there, the one i almost fought, he’s lurking. first time i seen him too, but he’s definitely after her, trying to take advantage while she’s out of it. he’s pretending to light a cigarette, waiting for us to leave. we gotta keep her away from him. how do we get her home?"

the cop caught on quick. the other guy was easy to read. he turned to the girl. the cop leaned in closer, his face showing he really cared.

"what a shame," he said, shaking his head. "poor thing, just a kid! she’s been tricked, clear as day. hey, miss," he said to her, "where do you stay?" she opened her tired eyes, looked at him blankly, and waved him off.

"here," Raskolnikov said, fishing out twenty copecks. "grab a cab, get her home. just need her address!"

"missy, missy!" the cop said, taking the cash. "i’ll get a cab, take you home myself. where should i take you? where’s home?"

"go away! they won’t leave me alone," she mumbled, waving again.

"oh, this is bad! shameful, missy, really shameful!" he said, shaking his head, feeling for her.

"it’s tricky," the cop said to Raskolnikov, sizing him up. Raskolnikov must have seemed odd too, dressed in rags but handing him money.

"where’d you find her?" he asked.

"she was right in front of me, stumbling here on the boulevard. just made it to the bench and collapsed."

"man, the world's messed up these days. innocent kid like that, already drunk! she’s been tricked, no doubt. look at her clothes... torn up. society’s gone downhill. and she probably comes from a decent family, even if they’re broke now. lots like her these days. she seems like she had a good background," he said, leaning over her again.

maybe he had daughters himself, "looking like ladies, refined," but struggling to keep up appearances...

the main thing, Raskolnikov kept on, is keeping her away from that creep. why should he get near her? it's so obvious what he's after. the guy's not even leaving.

Raskolnikov said this out loud, pointing right at him. the guy heard, looked like he'd get mad again, but just gave a nasty look and walked off a few paces, then stopped again.

"we can keep her safe," the constable said, thinking, "if she'd just say where to go. missy, hey, missy!" he leaned down over her again.

she suddenly opened her eyes, looked like she realized something, got up, and headed back the way she came. "horrible people, won't leave me alone!" she said, waving her hand. she walked quick, still staggering. the dandy followed, taking a different path, but watching her.

"don’t worry, i won’t let him get her," the policeman said firmly, and went after them.

“ah, the things you see nowadays!” he muttered, sighing.

then something hit Raskolnikov; everything flipped in his mind.

“hey, wait!” he shouted at the policeman.

the cop turned around.

“leave them be! what's it to you? let her go! let him have his fun.” he pointed at the dandy, “what’s it to you?”

the policeman looked shocked, staring at him. Raskolnikov laughed.

“well!” the policeman said, with a contemptuous gesture, and went after the girl and the dandy, probably thinking Raskolnikov was crazy.

“he took my twenty copecks,” Raskolnikov mumbled angrily when alone. “fine, let him get more from the other guy to keep the girl, and end it. why did i try to help? is it even my place? let them tear each other apart—what’s it to me? how did i dare give him twenty copecks? were they even mine?”

even saying those words, he felt miserable. he sat on the empty bench. his thoughts wandered... couldn’t focus. just wanted to forget everything, start fresh...

“poor girl,” he said, looking at the spot where she’d sat—“she’ll come around, cry, then her mom will find out... give her a beating, throw her out maybe... and if not, the Darya Frantsovnas will hear, and she’ll sneak around more. then the hospital (that’s the fate of those girls with strict moms who go wrong on the sly), and again the hospital... drink... taverns... more hospital, in a few years—a wreck, life done by eighteen or nineteen.... haven’t i seen it before? and how’d they end up like that? all the same way. ugh. but does it matter? they say it’s necessary. a certain percentage, they say, must go that way... to hell, i guess, so the rest stay clean. a percentage! what a word; sounds so scientific, comforting... say ‘percentage,’ nothing to worry about. if we had another word... maybe we’d feel worse.... but what if Dounia were part of that percentage? or another one if not that?”

where am i headed? just left after reading that letter. oh yeah, Vassilyevsky Ostrov, to see Razumihin. that's it. but why? what made me think of him now? weird.

razumihin, from university. not that i had many friends there. kept to myself mostly. didn't join in much. worked hard, sure, but didn't make me popular. had this sort of pride, like i was holding something back. maybe i thought i was above it all, or maybe they thought i did.

with Razumihin, things were different. he was easy to talk to. just that kind of guy. good-natured, simple on the outside but more to him if you looked closer. people liked him. smart guy, though a bit of a joker. tall, thin, always looked like he just rolled out of bed. strong too. once knocked a cop flat with one punch. drank like a fish but could quit just as easy. nothing seemed to bother him. lived rough but handled it.

hadn't seen him in months. he didn't even know where i lived now. last time we crossed paths, i avoided him. didn't want to deal with it.

been thinking of asking him for work, maybe tutoring gigs. but really, what's the point? even if he helps, gets me some jobs, a few coins. what's that gonna change? feels pointless going to him...

the thought of heading to Razumihin’s got me uneasy, like there’s something more to it. is he really gonna solve anything?

he rubbed his forehead, thinking hard. out of nowhere, a wild thought hit him. "hm... to Razumihin’s," he said, like it was decided. "i’ll go to Razumihin’s, but not now. after It, when it’s all over and new things start..."

suddenly he realized. "after It," he shouted, jumping up, "but is It really gonna happen?" he left his seat, nearly running. he wanted to head home, but the thought of that cramped little place made him sick. it’s where all this had brewed for months, so he wandered off.

his shivers turned into a feverish chill. looking around, he tried distracting himself. but he kept zoning out. when he snapped back, he forgot what he was thinking or where he was headed. he wandered across Vassilyevsky Ostrov, hit the Lesser Neva, crossed the bridge, then towards the islands. the green and fresh air felt good on his eyes, a break from the dusty town and looming buildings. no taverns, no stink. but soon, the peace turned into an itch. he’d stop and stare at painted villas, women on verandas, kids in gardens. flowers caught his eye the longest. fancy carriages, riders passed by, and he’d forget them as soon as they were gone. once he stopped, counted his money: thirty copecks. "twenty to the cop, three to Nastasya for the letter, must’ve given forty-seven or fifty to the Marmeladovs yesterday," he thought, but soon forgot why he counted. remembered when he passed a tavern, feeling hungry. went in, had a vodka, a pie. finished the pie outside. hadn’t touched vodka in a while, felt it quick. legs heavy, drowsy hit hard. headed home, but at Petrovsky Ostrov, he was done. veered off, lay in the grass, and knocked out.

dreams in a messed-up head can feel too real, like living art. bizarre images but so detailed and consistent that even an artist couldn’t dream them up awake. these sick dreams stick, leaving a mark on a frazzled mind.

Raskolnikov had this wild dream. He was back as a kid, maybe seven, in his hometown. Walking with his dad on a holiday evening. The sky was all grey and heavy, just like he remembered. The town was flat, nothing around, no trees, except way off, a dark copse on the horizon. There was this big tavern just past the last garden, always kind of freaked him out as a kid. Noise, fights, drunk folks hanging around. He'd grip his dad's hand tight, scared out of his mind. The road near it turned dusty and black, winding to the graveyard. In the middle was a stone church with a green dome. They'd go there a couple of times a year for mass, remembering his grandma he never knew. They'd bring a rice pudding with raisins in a cross pattern. He loved that place. Old priest, old ikons. His grandma's grave had a stone, and next to it, his baby brother's who died too young. Didn't remember him, but he'd cross himself and kiss the grave every visit. Now in his dream, he's going past the tavern, holding his dad's hand, scared of the place. But there's some festival going on, people everywhere, singing, drunk. Near the tavern, a strange cart catches his eye. Usually big horses pull those, but here, a thin little nag was hitched up. He'd seen those poor things struggling with heavy loads before, and it always broke his heart. Suddenly, a loud ruckus from the tavern, drunken peasants spilling out, one with a red carrot face yelling, “Get in, get in! I’ll take you all, get in!”

then it all went off like a firecracker in the crowd. 

“all of us? with that sorry beast?”

“mikolkas lost it — that cart cant take a nag like her.”

“more like twenty years old, easy!”

“hop in! ill carry you all!” mikolka yelled, hopping in first, grabbing reins, standing tall. “matveys got the bay,” he called out, “and this thing breaks my heart. id kill her. shes just eating her head off. get in! ill make her gallop! she will!” he got the whip ready, itching to lash that poor mare.

“come on! get in!” the crowd was in stitches. “hear that? she'll gallop!”

“gallop? she hasnt in a decade!”

“more like a trot!”

“forget it, mates. grab a whip. be ready!”

“all right! let her have it!”

they climbed into mikolkas cart, cracking jokes. six men, and room for more. they dragged in a plump, rosy woman in red cotton, beaded headdress, leather shoes, cracking nuts and laughing. the whole crowd was laughing too. couldnt help it, watching that pitiful nag try to drag all of them at a gallop! two young guys in the cart readied their whips to help mikolka. the cry went up, “now!” and the mare pulled with all she had but couldnt even move, legs shaking, gasping under the rain of whips. laughter doubled, but mikolka lost it, thrashing her like he thought she could actually gallop.

“let me in too!” yelled a young man, eager to join.

“get in, all of you!” cried mikolka, “she'll pull you all. ill beat her to death!” and he whipped her in a fury.

“father, father,” a young voice cried, “what are they doing? theyre beating the poor horse!”

“come on, lets go” his father said. “theyre drunk, joking around; dont watch!” trying to pull him away, but the kid broke free, horror-struck, ran to the horse. she was struggling, gasping, trying, almost falling.

“beat her to death,” mikolka yelled. “thats it. ill do it!”

“what are you doing? you a christian, demon?” an old man shouted.

“ever seen this? a poor nag pulling that load,” another said.

“youll kill her,” a third yelled.

“dont interfere! shes mine, my choice. get in, more of you! all of you! she'll gallop!...”

and then laughter roared, drowning it all: the mare, roused by the blows, started weakly kicking. even the old man smiled. that sad little thing trying to kick!

two lads grabbed whips, ran to beat her ribs. one on each side.

“hit her face, in the eyes, the eyes,” mikolka shouted.

“sing, mates!” someone in the cart yelled, and they all burst into a wild song, tambourine rattling, whistling. the woman kept cracking nuts, laughing.

he ran alongside the mare, then ahead of her, saw her whipped right in the eyes. he was crying hard, couldn't breathe, tears everywhere. someone whipped him across the face, but he didn't even feel it. screaming, he ran to a grey-haired old man shaking his head. a woman tried to pull him away, but he broke free and ran back. the mare was almost done, but still kicking.

“i’ll teach you to kick,” shouted Mikolka. he dropped the whip, grabbed a thick shaft from the cart, swung it over the mare.

“he’ll kill her!” someone yelled. 

“it's my property,” Mikolka yelled and slammed the shaft down. a heavy thud sounded.

“keep at it!” the crowd shouted. 

Mikolka swung again and again on the mare's spine. she staggered but tried to move the cart. six whips hit her from everywhere, and the shaft came down a third, fourth time. Mikolka couldn't kill her with one blow.

“tough one,” someone in the crowd said.

“she'll fall soon,” said another.

“get an axe!” yelled a third.

“stand back!” Mikolka screamed, grabbing an iron crowbar. he swung it with all his might. the mare staggered, tried to pull, but the bar hit again, and she fell like a log.

“finish her!” Mikolka yelled, leaping out of the cart. young men grabbed whatever they could—whips, sticks, poles—and ran at the dying mare. Mikolka swung the crowbar wildly. the mare stretched her head, breathed, and died.

“you butchered her,” someone shouted.

“why wouldn't she gallop?”

“my property!” Mikolka yelled, eyes bloodshot, bar in hand. he stood there like he wanted more to beat.

“you're no Christian,” voices shouted.

the boy, out of his mind, pushed through to the dead mare, hugged her bleeding head, kissed her eyes and lips. then he charged at Mikolka. just then, his father grabbed him, pulled him away.

“let's go home,” his father said.

“father! why did they... kill... the poor horse!” he sobbed, voice breaking, words like shrieks.

“they're drunk... brutal... it's not our business,” his father said. the boy hugged him, but he was choking. tried to breathe, to cry out—then woke up.

he gasped for air, hair soaked, stood up in terror.

thank god, just a dream. sits down, tries to breathe. "but why that dream? fever? terrible."

he's a mess. everything's dark and tangled up inside. drops his head to his hands.

"good god," he mutters, "could i really... use an axe? strike her... blood everywhere... hide with the axe? my god, no."

he's shaking hard.

"why am i doing this?" he wonders, sitting up, confused. "i know i can't do it. tried yesterday. couldn't even handle it then. why keep torturing myself? felt sick just thinking about it."

"no, can't. even if the logic's solid, can't do it. couldn't. couldn't. so why still...?"

stands up, looks around like he's surprised where he ended up. heads to the bridge. pale, eyes burning, every bit of him worn out, but breathing easier. feels lighter, like he's dropped a heavy weight. "lord, show me my path. i give up that cursed dream."

crosses the bridge, looks at the neva, sun setting red. weak but no fatigue. like a boil in his heart finally burst. freedom. no more spell, no more obsession.

later, thinking back on it all, he can't shake this one thing. a twist of fate, maybe. why, when exhausted, did he take the long way home through the hay market? didn't need to go there. no reason at all. but right then, right there, he met someone. the meeting that changed everything. felt like it was waiting for him.

around nine, he passed through the Hay Market. stalls closing, people packing up. rag pickers and costermongers crowding the taverns. Raskolnikov liked this area. his worn clothes didn’t draw looks here. a huckster and his wife had two tables, selling odds and ends. they were chatting with Lizaveta, the pawnbroker’s sister. he knew her a bit. she was timid, worked to the bone by her sister. caught sight of her, felt a strange shock. nothing unusual, but it hit him hard.

the huckster said, “come by tomorrow around seven. they’ll be here.”

Lizaveta hesitated, “tomorrow?”

“you’re too scared of Alyona,” the wife said. “she’s not even your real sister.”

“come without asking her. worth your while,” the husband advised. “you’ll see.”

“about seven tomorrow. they’ll be here. decide yourself.”

“and we’ll have tea,” the wife added.

“all right, i’ll come,” Lizaveta agreed, moving away.

Raskolnikov passed by, trying to catch every word. a chill hit him—learned Lizaveta would be gone at seven, leaving the old woman alone.

just steps to his place. went in like a man condemned. couldn’t think, but felt everything was decided. couldn’t have hoped for a better chance. no need for risky inquiries, it was all laid out.

later raskolnikov figured out why the huckster and his wife called lizaveta over. nothing special. some family hit hard times, selling off their things, clothes mostly. stuff wouldn't sell for much at market so they needed a dealer. lizaveta's thing. she was honest, priced fair, stuck to it. didn't talk much, submissive, timid, you know the type.

but raskolnikov, he'd been getting superstitious. that stuff clung to him, made him see odd patterns and coincidences everywhere. like last winter, this student pokorev, before leaving for harkov, tossed him the old pawnbroker alyona ivanovna's address. said in case he needed to pawn something. raskolnikov didn't bother then, had lessons, scraped by. then six weeks back, remembered it. had his dad's old silver watch and a little gold ring from his sister. decided on the ring. met the old woman, felt an immediate disgust, no real reason why. pawned it for two roubles, hit a dingy tavern on his way back, ordered tea, and got lost in his head. some weird idea pecking at him like a chick in an egg.

next table over, a student and a young officer, never seen them before. they'd played billiards, now drinking tea. student starts talking about alyona ivanovna. gave the officer her address. just came from her, now hearing her name again. odd. student went on about her, said she's rich, loans big, but a real harpy. spiteful, unpredictable, late with interest, pledge gone. gives a quarter of item value, charges high interest. mentioned lizaveta too, how she was treated like a child despite being six feet tall. "a phenomenon," he laughed.

they got talking about Lizaveta. the student was all into it, laughing a lot, and the officer was really interested, even asked if Lizaveta could do some mending for him. Raskolnikov caught every word, learned all about her. she's younger than the old lady, her half-sister, different moms. she's thirty-five, working all the time for her sister, cooking, washing, sewing, charwoman stuff, and hands over everything she earns. can't take a job without her sister's say-so. the old lady's will leaves nothing to Lizaveta but some furniture; all the cash goes to some monastery for prayers. Lizaveta's lower in rank, unmarried, and looks rough—tall, awkward feet, old goatskin shoes, clean though. the student found it funny that she was always pregnant.

“but you say she's ugly?” the officer asked.

“yeah, she's dark-skinned, looks like a soldier in a dress, but not really ugly. her face and eyes are so kind. a lot of folks like her. she's gentle, ready to help, always smiling sweetly.”

“sounds like you find her attractive,” the officer laughed.

“just her oddness. look, i'd kill that old woman, take her money, and not feel a thing,” the student said, heated. the officer laughed again; Raskolnikov got a chill. strange stuff.

“listen, a serious question,” the student said. “i'm kidding, but hear me out: on one side, a useless old woman, spiteful, doing harm, gonna die soon anyway. you get me?”

“yeah, i get you,” the officer replied, watching him closely.

“on the other side, young lives wasted, needing help. her money could save thousands, put families back on track, keep them from ruin. kill her, use her money for humanity, for good. wouldn’t one crime be offset by thousands of good deeds? one life for many - simple math. her life’s less than a bug's cause she's harming others. bit Lizaveta's finger, almost lost it.”

“she doesn’t deserve to live,” the officer said, “but that’s nature.”

"man, we gotta shape nature or we're stuck in a sea of bias. without that, no great men. folks talk about duty and conscience—fine, but what's that mean? hold up, got a question for you. listen!"

"nah, you listen. i've got one for you."

"yeah?"

"you're going on and on, but tell me, would you off the old lady yourself?"

"no way! just debating the fairness of it... not my business..."

"if you won't do it yourself, there's no justice in it... let's play again."

Raskolnikov was all worked up. just typical young guy talk he'd heard before, different spins, different topics. but why hear this now, right when he's brewing the same thoughts? why right after leaving the old lady's with his own idea? that timing always felt odd, like it was meant to be, giving him some nudge...

*****

back from the Market, he crashed on the sofa, sat an hour unmoving. it got dark; no candle, didn't even think to light one. can't recall what he thought about. then the fever hit again, and he was glad to just lie down. sleep hit hard, like a weight.

he slept deep, no dreams. Nastasya came in at ten, struggled to wake him. brought tea and bread. same old second brew, her teapot.

"man, you sleep like a rock!" she said annoyed. "always sleeping."

he dragged himself up. head pounding, paced the room, then back on the sofa.

"sleeping again?" Nastasya asked. "you sick or something?"

he said nothing.

"want some tea?"

"later," he muttered, eyes shut, facing the wall.

Nastasya watched him.

"maybe he's really sick," she said, then left. came back at two with soup. he hadn't moved. tea untouched. she was ticked, tried to wake him.

"why you just lying there?" she snapped, disgusted.

he got up, sat again, stayed silent, eyes on the floor.

"sick or not?" she asked. no answer. "get some fresh air," she said. "gonna eat or not?"

"later," he said weakly. "you can go."

she lingered, looked at him with some pity, then left.

minutes later, he stared at the tea and soup. then slowly, he grabbed the bread, the spoon, and started eating.

he ate a little, just a few spoonfuls, not really hungry. headache was easing up. after that, he stretched out on the sofa again but no sleep this time. just lay there, face buried in the pillow. started daydreaming, strange stuff. kept seeing himself in africa, egypt maybe, at an oasis. caravan resting, camels chilling, palms all around. everyone eating, and he’s drinking cool water from a spring nearby. that water was amazing, like blue and cold, running over shiny stones and sand that looked like gold. then a clock chimed. startled him awake, looked out the window, realized how late it was. jumped up like he got yanked off the sofa. tiptoed to the door, listened on the staircase. heart hammering. everything quiet, like everyone else was asleep. weird how he’d slept so deep, done nothing, prepped nothing. maybe it was already six. drowsiness turned into a wild rush. few things to prep. focused hard on remembering everything, heart pounding so hard it was hard to breathe. first job: make a noose and sew it into his coat. quick work. dug under the pillow, found an old shirt. tore a strip, about two inches wide, sixteen inches long. folded it, took off his summer coat, and sewed the ends of the rag inside, under the left armhole. hands shaking, but got it done right, nothing showed outside. needle and thread were ready on the table. the noose was smart, his own idea, for the axe. couldn’t just carry an axe in the street. with the noose, the axe head sat under his arm. hand in the pocket could hold the handle steady. the coat was loose enough to hide it all. thought of this noose two weeks ago.

he reached into that gap between the sofa and the floor, fished out a piece of wood he’d stashed there. this wasn’t just any wood — it was planed smooth, about the size of a cigarette case. found it in some workshop courtyard. later added a thin piece of iron, picked up from the street. wrapped them tight with thread, then in white paper, tied up tight. all to keep the old woman busy untying it, give him a moment. the iron added weight, make it seem real. kept it under the sofa till now. 

heard noise in the yard. 

“it’s been six for a while.”

“for a while! damn it!”

grabbed his hat, headed down the steps quiet like a cat. had to get the axe from the kitchen. he’d decided long ago it had to be an axe. had a pocket knife, but didn’t trust it or his strength. funny thing, the more final his plans got, the more absurd they seemed. even with all the struggle, he never fully believed he’d go through with it.

if ever all the details had been settled, no doubts left, he’d have scrapped it as impossible. but there were always loose ends. getting the axe was easy. nastasya was often out, door left ajar. landlady always scolded her for it. when the time came, he’d just slip into the kitchen, grab it, put it back later. but what if nastasya returned and noticed it missing? suspicion could brew quick.

all those little things? he hadn't even started on them. no time for that. he focused on the big picture, putting off the details till he could actually believe in it all. but believing seemed way out of reach. he couldn't picture himself just stopping his thoughts, getting up, and going there. his last attempt, visiting the place, was more like a test run, not the real deal. like saying, "let's just go try it instead of dreaming." but, he chickened out, ran off cursing himself.

on the moral side, though, he'd sharpened his analysis to a razor's edge, finding no logical objections in himself. yet, in the end, he doubted himself and desperately hunted for arguments, like something was dragging him toward it.

he'd been caught up on one question for a long time: why are most crimes so sloppily hidden and easy to catch? he figured it wasn't about the impossibility of hiding them but more about the criminal. almost every criminal messes up, losing will and logic at the worst time. it was like a disease, hitting just before and during the crime, then fading. he wasn't sure if the disease caused the crime or if crime just brought on this sickness.

when he figured all this out, he decided he wouldn't have that kind of breakdown. his reason and will would stay intact since his plan wasn't “a crime...” let's skip how he reached that conclusion. we've gone too far ahead already. just know that the practical issues were secondary to him. he thought if he could keep his will and reason, he'd handle those issues. but he never started preparing. his last decisions were the ones he trusted least, and when the time came, everything happened in a completely different way, like an accident.

one tiny thing threw my plan off before i even left the stairs. got to the landlady’s kitchen, door open like always, and peeked in. wanted to see if the landlady was around or if the coast was clear. but surprise, surprise, Nastasya was there, busy with laundry. she stopped, stared at me the whole time i walked past. tried to act like i didn’t see her, but man, i didn’t have the axe. totally wrecked my plan.

“why’d i think she wouldn’t be home just then?” i thought as i hit the gateway. “why was i so sure?”

felt like a joke, angry at myself... this stupid rage bubbling up. stood there in the gateway, stuck. going out for show? no way. going back to my room? even worse. “blown the chance for good!” i mumbled, hanging around by the porter’s dark little room. then, something shiny under the bench caught my eye. two steps in, i called softly for the porter. “not here, must be nearby, door’s open.” grabbed the axe, made it fast, shoved hands in pockets, slipped out. nobody saw a thing. “when logic bails, devil pitches in,” i thought, grinning weirdly. gave me a big lift.

walked on calm, no rush, didn’t want to draw eyes. avoided looking at faces, kept low-key. then i remembered my hat. “damn, had cash two days ago and didn’t buy a cap.” curse came up from my soul.
