spotted a clock in a shop window, saw it was ten past seven. had to hustle, but had to loop around to come at the house from the other side...

thought id be scared, but nah, wasnt. mind drifted to random stuff. walking by the Yusupov garden, thinking about big fountains and how they'd cool the city down. then i figured expanding the summer garden to the field of Mars, maybe even hooking it up to the Mihailovsky Palace, would be awesome for the town. wondered why people cram into the dirtiest parts of the city without gardens or fountains. remembered my own times at the Hay Market. snapped back to reality thinking, “what nonsense! just zone out.”

"maybe folks headed to the gallows hang onto any thought," crossed my mind quick, but i ditched it. getting close now—there's the house, the gate. clock struck half-past seven. “no way, must be fast.”

at the gate, luck was on my side. a big hay wagon rolled in just as i got there, covering me. slipped through quick, heard some yelling, but nobody saw me. windows open all around but i kept my head down. stairs to the old woman's were right there. headed up, heart pounding, hand on the axe, going slow.

stairs were empty, all doors shut. painters on the first floor didn’t notice me. paused, then kept going. “better if they weren’t here, but... two floors up.”

fourth floor now, door right in front of me. apartment across was empty, card ripped off the door. thought about turning back for a split second, but just listened at her door. silence. listened on the stairs, nothing. checked my axe, “am i pale? look nervous? she’s sharp... maybe wait till my heart calms?”

heart raced more. couldnt wait. reached for the bell, rang it. half a minute, rang again, louder.

no answer. keeping on ringing was pointless. the old woman was home, no doubt, just suspicious. he knew her habits... so he pressed his ear to the door again. maybe his senses were sharp, or the sound was just clear. either way, he heard a faint touch on the lock, a skirt moving softly. someone was right there, listening just like him. he shifted a bit, muttered something so it didn’t look like he was hiding, then rang a third time, calm and patient. thinking about it later, that moment stayed sharp in his mind; couldn’t figure out how he managed such a move, mind felt hazy, body almost disconnected... then he heard the latch.

CHAPTER VII

door cracked open, same as before, two sharp eyes peering out. then raskolnikov almost messed up. afraid she'd freak out being alone with him, not counting on his face easing her mind, he grabbed the door, pulling it to keep her from shutting it. she didn’t pull back, but didn’t let go either, almost dragging her out onto the stairs. with her blocking the way, he pushed forward. she stepped back, scared, tried to talk, but nothing came out, just staring.

“good evening, alyona ivanovna,” he started, trying to keep it light, but his voice was shaky. “i’ve come... brought something... let’s step in... to the light....”

he moved into the room without waiting for an invite. she followed, finding her voice.

“good heavens! who is it? what do you want?”

“why, alyona ivanovna, you know me... raskolnikov... brought the pledge i promised...” he handed it over.

she glanced at it, then right at him, eyes cold and distrustful. a minute passed; he thought she might be sneering, like she'd figured it all out. panic crept in, so strong he thought he might bolt if she kept staring.

“why look at me like you don't know me?” he snapped back, with a touch of spite. “take it or i’ll go, i’m in a hurry.”

he hadn’t planned to say that, it just slipped out. her confidence seemed to return with his firm tone.

“but why, sir, so sudden.... what is it?” she asked, eyeing the pledge.

“the silver cigarette case; mentioned it before.”

she reached out for it.

“but how pale you are... hands trembling too? been bathing?”

“fever,” he cut in. “hard not to get pale... when you’ve got nothing to eat,” he added, words barely forming.

strength was slipping again. but his answer rang true enough; she took the pledge.

"what's this?" she asked again, eyeing Raskolnikov and the pledge in her hand.

"thing... cigarette case... silver... take a look."

"doesn't seem like silver... how's it wrapped?"

she fiddled with the string, turned to the window for light. windows were shut, heat was stifling. left him standing for a moment. he unbuttoned his coat, got the axe from the noose, kept it hidden. hands numb, felt like wood. worried it'd slip. dizziness hit him.

"why'd he tie it like this?" she muttered, moving closer.

no time left. he pulled the axe, swung it down on her head, almost like he wasn't doing it himself. strength came back after.

she was bareheaded. thin greasy hair, tied in a rat's tail with a broken comb. short, so the blow hit the top of her skull. she cried out weakly, collapsed, hands to her head, still holding the pledge. hit her again and again in the same spot. blood poured like a tipped glass. stepped back, let her fall, checked her face. she was dead. eyes bulging, face twisted.

he put the axe down, right there next to the body. reached into her pocket, the same one she used to grab the key last time. tried not to get blood on him. hands shaking, but his mind was clear. keys were in the same bunch, steel ring and all. headed straight for the bedroom. tiny room, lots of holy images. big bed, silk quilt. chest of drawers there too. started fitting keys, and the sound of them made him shudder. thought about quitting, but it was too late. smiled at the thought and then worried she might still be alive. ran back, axe up, but didn’t swing. she was clearly dead, skull broken. saw a string on her neck, tried to yank it, but it was soaked and tough. wanted to cut it with the axe but didn’t dare. after a struggle, got it off. it was a purse. crosses and an image hung with it. stuffed the purse in his pocket, left the crosses on her, and rushed back to the bedroom, axe in hand.

he grabbed the keys in a rush, fumbling them one by one. none fit. not just shaky hands, more like ignoring the obvious. the big key, deep notches, stood out. remembered it wasn't for the drawers. last visit, it clicked — strong box key. left the drawers, headed for the bed. old women stash things under beds, right? bingo. a long, red leather box with steel nails. key fit perfect. inside, a red brocade coat, hareskin lining. a silk dress, a shawl. thought he was losing it, wiping blood on the brocade — red hides red, right? snapped back. "what am i doing?"

then a gold watch tumbled out. turned everything over fast. gold bits everywhere — bracelets, chains, pins. pledges, maybe. all wrapped tight, some in cases, some in newspaper with tape. stuffed pockets fast, no time to sort.

then footsteps. froze. must be nothing. then a faint moan. silence again. crouched by the box, barely breathing. then sprang up, grabbed the axe, bolted from the room.

lizaveta stood there in shock, clutching a big bundle. she looked at her murdered sister, pale, like she couldn't even scream. when he ran out of the bedroom, she started shaking, like a leaf in the wind. her mouth opened, hand raised, but still no sound. she backed up into the corner, eyes locked on him, but silent, like she just couldn't scream. he charged at her with the axe; her mouth twitched like a scared baby's, about to cry. she was too scared, too crushed to even defend herself, though she needed to. just raised her empty left hand, not to her face, just out in front. the axe came down, splitting her skull in one blow. she fell hard. raskolnikov lost it, grabbed her bundle, dropped it, and bolted into the entryway.

fear took over, especially after this second unexpected murder. he wanted to run far, fast. if he could see clearly, if he grasped how deep he was, how messed up and pointless it all was, if he saw the obstacles and maybe more crimes he'd have to face to get out and home, he might've just given up, turned himself in—not from fear, but from horror and disgust at what he'd done. that disgust just kept growing. he wouldn't go back in that room now for anything.

kinda like being in a fog. brain started drifting, grabbing onto small things that didnt matter. peeked into the kitchen, saw a bucket half-full of water on a bench. figured id clean up. hands were sticky with blood. dropped the axe in the water, grabbed some soap from a broken saucer on the window, and started washing up. got my hands clean, took the axe out, scrubbed the blade, and spent a good three minutes getting the blood off the wood with soap. wiped it all down with some linen hanging to dry. checked the axe at the window — no trace left, but the wood was still damp. hung it under my coat. dim light in the kitchen, tried to check my overcoat, trousers, boots. boots had stains. wetted a rag, rubbed them clean. knew i mightve missed something obvious. stood there, lost in thought. dark ideas creeping in — felt like maybe id gone mad, couldnt think straight, couldnt protect myself. maybe should be doing something else entirely. muttered, “gotta get out, get out,” and rushed to the entry.

then terror hit me. couldnt believe it: the door, the outer door from the stairs, was open, six inches wide. no lock, no bolt, all this time. the old woman hadnt shut it. but id seen Lizaveta after! how had i not realized she mustve come in somehow? not through a wall!

dashed to the door, fastened the latch. “no, wrong move! gotta go, gotta go...” unlatched it, opened the door, listened on the staircase.

listened for ages. far away, maybe at the gateway, two voices shouting, quarreling. “whats going on?” waited it out. silence. theyd split up. was about to go, but a door below opened, someone started downstairs, humming. “why all this noise?” thought. closed the door, waited again. silence, nothing moving. took a step towards the stairs, heard fresh footsteps.

steps sounded distant, way down the stairs, but he knew right away someone was headed up to the old woman on the fourth floor. why? maybe the way they walked — heavy, even, not in a rush. now past the first floor, getting clearer. heard the breathing too. third floor now. coming here. he felt frozen, like in a dream where you cant move, even when danger's close.

but as the visitor reached the fourth floor, he snapped out of it. slipped back into the flat, shut the door, hooked it quietly. instinct kicked in. then crouched, holding his breath at the door. the visitor was right there too, just like he'd stood with the old woman, door between them, listening.

the visitor panted. "big guy," thought Raskolnikov, gripping the axe. like a dream. the visitor grabbed the bell, rang it loud.

tin bell rang, something moved inside. listened hard. bell rang again, then a hard yank on the door handle. he watched the hook shake, felt terror it might break. wanted to hold it, but that'd give him away. dizziness hit. "I'll fall," he thought, but the visitor spoke, snapping him back.

"what's up? asleep or dead? damn them!" he yelled in a thick voice. "hey, Alyona Ivanovna, old witch! Lizaveta Ivanovna, hey, open up! damn them! asleep or what?"

angry, he yanked the bell hard. must be someone with pull, close to them.

then light steps on the stairs. someone else coming. Raskolnikov hadn't noticed at first.

"nobody home?" the new one called out, cheerful voice, talking to the first guy still on the bell. "evening, Koch."

"young voice," thought Raskolnikov.

"who knows? almost broke the lock," Koch replied. "how'd you know me?"

"beat you at billiards at Gambrinus’ couple days back."

"oh!"

"they're not home? strange. but it's stupid. where'd she go? got business."

"yeah, me too."

"guess we leave. damn, wanted money!" young guy said.

"give up, but she set this time. out of my way. where'd she go? never leaves, old hag; bad legs. now she's out for a walk!"

"ask the porter?"

"what?"

"where she went, when she'll be back."

"damn it all, man... stuck again. maybe we knock? but she never leaves."

he yanks the handle again.

"damn it. nothing else for it, gotta go!"

"wait!" the young guy says. "see how the door shakes when you pull?"

"yeah?"

"means it ain't locked, just hooked. hear it clank?"

"so?"

"look, if it was locked, they’d use a key from outside. hook means someone’s home. hear it? they’re in there, just not opening up!"

"well, then they must be," Koch says, surprised. "what's going on?" and starts shaking the door.

"hold up!" says the young guy. "don't yank it! something's wrong... we’ve rung and knocked, still nothing. maybe they're out cold or..."

"what?"

"let's get the porter to wake 'em."

"sure."

they start heading down.

"wait, you stay here, i'll fetch the porter."

"why?"

"better that way."

"fine."

"i’m studying law! it’s obvious there’s an issue!" he shouts, running downstairs.

Koch stays. touches the bell again, hears a tinkle, then fiddles with the handle, testing it’s just hooked. bends to peek in the keyhole, but the key’s blocking it.

raskolnikov clutches the axe. he’s in a fog, ready to fight if they come in. thoughts of ending it all, shouting through the door, or mocking them cross his mind. "just hurry up," he thinks.

"what's he doing?" Koch wonders. time ticks. no one comes. restless, he gives up and clomps down the stairs too, disappearing.

"what now?"

raskolnikov unhooks the door, slips out silently. three flights down, he hears shouting. no hiding place. turns back.

"hey! catch him!"

someone bursts out below, shouting, tumbling down the stairs.

"mitka! mitka! mitka! damn him!"

the shout becomes a scream, echoing in the yard, then silence. but then, voices, loud and fast, coming up. three, four men, the young guy among them. "hey!"

he was desperate. headed straight for them, thinking, “whatever happens!” if they stopped him—game over; if they let him pass—also game over; they'd remember him. closing in, just a flight away—then a lucky break! to his right, an open flat. second floor, painters just bailed. shouting. floor fresh painted, bucket, broken pot, brushes. he ducked in, hid behind the wall, just in time; they hit the landing, then up to the fourth floor, loud talking. waited, tiptoed out, bolted down the stairs.

stairs empty, gateway clear. slipped through, left on the street.

he knew, he knew they were at the flat now, shocked it was unlocked. door had been fastened, now they're looking at the bodies. any second they'd figure it out, know the killer was just there, hid, got away. they'd guess he was in that empty flat as they went up. but he couldn't speed up much, hundred yards to the next turn. duck into a gateway, hide somewhere random? nah, no good. ditch the axe? cab? no, no.

finally hit the turn. barely standing, halfway to safety. safer here with the crowd, lost in it like sand. but he was wrecked, barely moving. sweating buckets, neck drenched. “wow, he's been at it!” someone shouted when he hit the canal bank.

barely aware of himself now, getting worse. remembered when he hit the canal, fewer people, too visible, thought about turning back. almost collapsing, took a long detour to come home from a different way.

he wasn't all there when he got through the door. was halfway up the stairs before he remembered the axe. had a big problem to solve—putting it back without anyone noticing. should've just ditched it in someone's yard maybe, but luck was on his side. the porter’s room was shut but not locked. porter might've been home, but no thinking—just walked in. if the porter had asked, might’ve just handed him the axe. but luck again—porter was out. slipped the axe back under the bench, covered it with wood. nobody saw him, doors all closed. when he hit his room, dropped on the sofa, not asleep, just blank. if someone had barged in, he'd have freaked. mind buzzing, couldn't grab a single thought.

laid there forever. sometimes snapped awake, realized it was late, but stayed put. finally, noticed it getting light. still foggy. street noise—drunks out of the taverns. that snapped him up—past two? bolted upright, remembered everything at once. thought he was losing it. chills from a fever, shaking all over. opened the door, house quiet. in disbelief, looked around, wondering how he got in without locking up, still clothed, hat on the floor.

“what if anyone came in? they'd think i was drunk...”

headed to the window, checked himself in the light. no visible marks. freezing, stripped down, checked every piece three times. nothing except some dried blood on his trousers. used a knife, cut it off. nothing more.

he suddenly realized the purse and stuff from the old woman’s box were still in his pockets. hadn’t thought about pulling them out or hiding them. wasn’t even on his mind when checking his clothes. panic hit. he yanked everything out, flipped the pocket inside out, and dumped it in the corner. the wallpaper hung loose there, torn at the bottom. he stuffed it all in that gap. “all hidden, purse too!” he thought, but then dread hit: “what’s wrong with me? is that hiding?”

never planned for trinkets. just money. no hiding spot ready.

“why am i relieved?” he thought, “this isn’t hiding. losing my mind.”

collapsed on the sofa, shivering. grabbed his old winter coat from the chair, wrapped it around, and drifted into a fevered haze. blacked out.

not even five minutes and he jolted up, frantic. “how’d i sleep with nothing done? oh, the loop on the armhole! forgot about that evidence!”

tore the loop off, cut it up, and stuffed the pieces under the pillow.

“torn linen won’t look suspicious,” he reassured himself, scanning the room, trying to ensure he missed nothing. realizing his mind was slipping was torture.

“it’s starting already. punishment’s here.”

the rags from his trousers lay on the floor in plain sight.

“what’s wrong with me!” he shouted.

a thought hit him—maybe his clothes were bloodstained, but he couldn’t see it. reason failing, vision blurred. remembered blood on the purse. “then the pocket too!”

turned the pocket inside out—stains on the lining.

“so i’m not completely gone. guessed right,” he thought, relieved. “just fever’s weakness,” and tore the lining from his trousers. sunlight hit his boot; saw traces on the sock. yanked off the boots. “sock soaked with blood.” stepped in that pool. “what do i do with this sock, the rags, the pocket?”

he gathered up all the stuff in his hands, standing there in the middle of the room, thinking hard.

"in the stove? they'd check there first. burn them? with what? no matches. better to ditch it all somewhere. yeah, just throw it away," he thought, sitting back down on the sofa, "and do it now, right now, no waiting..."

but instead, his head hit the pillow. that icy shiver hit him again, and he pulled his coat over himself.

hours passed with him thinking, "get up, go now, toss it all, get it out of sight, done with it, right now!" but every time he tried to get up, he couldn't.

then, a loud banging on the door woke him.

"open up! are you dead in there? sleeping all day!" nastasya shouted, pounding on the door. "like a dog he sleeps! open up, it's past ten."

"maybe he's not home," said another voice, a man's voice.

"ha! that's the porter... what does he want?"

he jumped up, heart pounding painfully.

"then who bolted the door?" nastasya replied. "he's locking himself in! like he's got something worth stealing! open up, wake up!"

"what do they want? why the porter? everything's out. fight or open? whatever happens..."

he half-rose, reached forward, and unlocked the door.

the room was so tiny he could reach the latch from the bed. there they were, the porter and nastasya.

nastasya gave him a strange look. he glared defiantly at the porter, who silently handed him a grey paper sealed with bottle-wax.

"a notice from the office," the porter said, handing him the paper.

"which office?"

"a summons to the police office. you know."

"the police?... why?..."

"how should i know? you're called, so you go."

the man looked him over, glanced around, and left.

"he's sick," nastasya said, staring. the porter turned briefly. "he's had a fever since yesterday," she noted.

raskolnikov just held the paper, not opening it. "don't get up," nastasya added, seeing him lowering his feet from the sofa. "you're sick, no rush. what's that in your hand?"

he looked; he was holding shreds from his trousers, a sock, and pocket rags. he’d slept with them clutched in his hand. thinking back, he remembered grabbing them in a feverish daze and falling asleep again.

"look at him, collecting rags and sleeping with them, like they're treasure..."

nastasya giggled, a bit hysterical.

he shoved everything under his coat, staring at her. even in his fog, he knew they wouldn't act like this if he was getting arrested. "but... the police?"

"tea? want some? i'll bring it."

"no... i'm going; i'll go right now," he mumbled, trying to stand.

"you can't even get downstairs!"

"yes, i'll go."

"as you like."

she and the porter left.

he rushed to the light, checking the sock and rags.

stains everywhere, but they blend in with the dirt and wear. nastasya wouldn’t notice from a distance, thank god. broke the seal—it's a police summons for today at 9:30.

"why now? i never deal with the police." panic hit. "just let it be over."

almost prayed but laughed instead—at himself. dressed quickly. "if i’m done for, so be it. do i put the sock on? it'll just get dirtier, hide the marks."

sock on, sock off. no other options, so on it went, and i laughed again.

"all perspective," i thought, while shivering. got it on, but laughter turned to despair.

"too much..." legs weak. "it's a trap," i muttered, heading down, head spinning. "might say something stupid..."

realized everything's left in the wall. "they'll search while i’m gone," but waved it off. "just want it over."

heat outside unbearable; no rain for days. dust, stink, drunks, and peddlers. sun in the eyes, head spinning like always with a fever in daylight.

turned toward that street, heart racing, avoided looking at the house.

"if they ask, maybe i’ll spill," nearing the station.

quarter mile to the new fourth-floor office. been to the old one ages ago. saw a peasant with a book, must be the place. took the stairs, no questions.

"i’ll just confess everything," i thought, reaching the fourth floor.

stairs were steep and narrow, covered in filthy water. kitchen doors from the flats opened right onto the stairs, left open most of the day, so it stank in there and the heat was unbearable. porters with books under their arms, cops, all kinds of folks, men and women, packed the staircase. office door was wide open too, peasants waiting inside. same heat inside, with a nasty mix of fresh paint and stale oil smells from the newly done-up rooms.

after a bit, he decided to push forward into the next room. all the rooms were small, low ceilings. impatience just pulling him along. nobody cared or noticed him. in the second room, clerks were writing, dressed no better than him, looking odd. he approached one.

“what is it?”

showed the notice he got.

“student?” the clerk asked, glancing at it.

“yes, was a student.”

clerk looked, uninterested. unkempt guy, eyes fixed on some idea.

“wont get anything from him, he's got no interest,” Raskolnikov thought.

“go to the head clerk,” the clerk said, pointing to the far room.

he moved to that room, the fourth; crowded space, folks dressed better than outside. two ladies there. one, in mourning, at the table writing what the chief clerk dictated. the other, stout and flashy, standing aside, waiting. Raskolnikov handed the notice to the head clerk. clerk glanced, said: “wait a minute,” kept attending to the mourning lady.

he relaxed a bit. “cant be that!” started regaining confidence, pushing himself to stay calm.

“some silly mistake, i might give it away! pity there's no air here,” he added, “its suffocating... makes your head spin... mind too...”

inside, it was chaos. scared of losing control; tried focusing on something random, but failed. yet the head clerk fascinated him; kept hoping to read something in his face.

head clerk was young, maybe 22, dark, lively face, seemed older. dressed sharp, hair parted and pomaded, rings on his fingers, gold chain on his vest. spoke a bit of French to a foreigner, got it mostly right.

“Luise Ivanovna, have a seat,” he said, casually to the flashy woman, still standing unsure, though a chair was right there.

“Ich danke,” she said, and with a soft rustle of silk, sat down. her blue dress with white lace billowed like a balloon, filling the room. scented, clearly uneasy about taking up space and the strong perfume, her smile was bold yet uneasy.

the lady finally wrapped it up and stood. suddenly, this officer strolls in, swinging his shoulders like he owns the place. he chucks his cap on the table, plops into an easy chair. the little lady jumps up like she's electrified, curtsying like mad, but he doesn’t even glance at her. she stays standing. the guy’s got this horizontal red moustache, tiny features, but mostly just oozes rudeness. he gives Raskolnikov the stink eye for his shabby clothes, like his attitude doesn't match his outfit. Raskolnikov stares back too long, the officer gets ticked.

“what do you want?” he barks, like it's a shock Raskolnikov wasn’t flattened by his glare.

“i got a notice…” Raskolnikov stammers.

“for money recovery from _the student_,” the head clerk jumps in, barely looking up. “here!” he tosses a document at Raskolnikov. “read that!”

“money? what money?” Raskolnikov thinks. “but... it’s not _that_.” relief washes over him, a weight gone.

“and what time were you supposed to be here, sir?” the officer shouts, more annoyed. “you were told nine, now it’s noon!”

“just got the notice 15 minutes ago,” Raskolnikov shoots back, angry too, and kinda liking it. “and i’m here sick with fever.”

“stop shouting!”

“not shouting, talking calmly. you’re shouting. i’m a student, i don’t take shouting.”

the officer’s fuming, almost speechless. he jumps up. “silent! this is a government office. don’t be cheeky!”

“you’re in one too,” Raskolnikov yells. “smoking and shouting, disrespecting us all.”

satisfaction in his words. the head clerk smirks, the officer’s thrown off.

“not your business!” he finally yells. “make the declaration. Alexandr Grigorievitch, show him. complaint against you! unpaid debts! fine bird!”

Raskolnikov’s done listening, grabs the paper, looking for answers. reads it twice, still lost.

“what’s this?” he asks the head clerk.

“recovery of money on an IOU, a writ. you pay it, plus costs, or declare when you can pay, promise not to leave town, or hide property. creditor can sell your stuff, take action.”

“but i’m not in debt!”

“not our problem. here’s an IOU, 115 roubles, legally attested, due, from you to widow Zarnitsyn, passed to Mr. Tchebarov. summoned, here you are.”

“but she’s my landlady!”

“and if she is?”

head clerk gave him this look, like, "how you holding up now?" but he was past caring about IOUs or writs. nothing to stress over now. he answered, asked questions, but all on autopilot. the relief, that sense of dodging a bullet, flooded him. pure gut-level joy, not a thought for what came next.

then, boom, office storm. assistant super, still raw from Raskolnikov’s sass, turned on this lady who'd been grinning at him. “you disgrace!” he roared. “what's with last night at your place? you want a correction house visit? been warned ten times!” and on he went.

Raskolnikov dropped his paper, stared at the lady catching the heat. then he got it, and suddenly it was all pretty funny. couldn’t help but want to laugh.

“ilya petrovitch!” head clerk tried to step in, but knew better — only force stopped him when he was like this.

and the lady, she flinched first but then got smoother the longer he yelled. smiling sweetly, curtsying, just waiting to jump in with her own say.

there wasn't any noise or fighting in my house, Mr. Captain," she rattled off quick like a machine, speaking Russian with a heavy German twist. "no scandal either. he came in drunk, that's the truth, Mr. Captain, and i'm not at fault... mine's an honest place, Mr. Captain, and i hate scandal myself. but he was tipsy, asked for three more bottles, then started playing the piano with his foot. not right for an honest house, broke it too, and i told him off. then he picked up a bottle, started swinging at everyone. called the porter, Karl, but he hit Karl in the eye, hit Henriette too, slapped me five times. ungentlemanly in an honest house, Mr. Captain, so i screamed. he opened the window over the canal, stood there squealing like a pig; disgraceful. Karl tried pulling him from the window by his coat, tore it. then he demanded fifteen roubles for damages, i paid him five, Mr. Captain. ungentlemanly visitor, caused all the scandal. 'i'll write to the papers,' he said."

"so he's an author?"

"yes, Mr. Captain, and what an ungentlemanly visitor..."

"enough! i told you already..."

"Ilya Petrovitch!" the head clerk chimed in.

the assistant shot a quick look at him; the head clerk gave a slight shake of his head.

"so listen, Luise Ivanovna, and this is the last time," the assistant said. "if there's another scandal in your place, i'll put you in the lock-up. you hear me? so an author took five roubles for his coat in an 'honest house'? some authors we have!"

he tossed a scornful look at Raskolnikov. "there was trouble in a restaurant too. author ate, wouldn't pay; 'i’ll write a satire,' he says. another one on a steamer last week, disgraced a councilor's family. one got kicked out of a confectioner's shop. authors, literary types, students, town-criers... pfoo! i'll drop by your place myself one day. be careful!"

Luise Ivanovna curtsied all around, edged to the door, but bumped into a good-looking officer with fair whiskers. this was Nikodim Fomitch, the district superintendent. she curtsied nearly to the floor, fluttered out.

"another storm, a hurricane!" said Nikodim Fomitch to Ilya Petrovitch, friendly-like. "you're wound up again! i heard it on the stairs!"

"Alright, what now!" Ilya Petrovitch said, not caring much, as he swaggered over to another table with some papers. "Look at this: a so-called author or student, doesn't pay up, has an IOU, won't leave his room, and there's constant griping about him. Oh, and he's upset about me smoking near him! Acts like a jerk and just look at him. Quite the gentleman, huh?"

"Poverty's no crime, but you get riled up quick, can't take a slight. Maybe you took offense and overdid it," Nikodim Fomitch said to Raskolnikov. "But you're wrong there; he's a decent guy, just explosive! He heats up, goes off, and then it's done! Heart of gold, though! They called him the Explosive Lieutenant in the regiment..."

"And what a regiment it was," Ilya Petrovitch added, still sulky but enjoying the banter.

Raskolnikov suddenly wanted to say something nice. "Excuse me, Captain," he said to Nikodim Fomitch, "can you see my side? I'll apologize if I was rude. I'm a poor student, sick and worn down by poverty. Not studying because I can't afford it, but I'll get money... My mom and sister are in the province of X. They'll send money, and I'll pay. My landlady's good-hearted but fed up with me not paying for four months, she doesn't even send dinner. I don't get this IOU. She's asking me to pay her with it. How am I to do that? Judge for yourselves!"

"But that's not our problem," the head clerk said.

"Yes, yes, I agree. But let me explain..." Raskolnikov pressed on, still talking to Nikodim Fomitch, trying to reach Ilya Petrovitch too, though Ilya was busy with papers, acting like he didn't care. "Let me explain, I've been living there nearly three years. At first... at first, honestly, I promised to marry her daughter. It was verbal, freely given... she was a girl... I liked her, but wasn't in love... just young stuff... my landlady gave me credit back then, and I was careless..."

"No need for personal stories, sir, we're short on time," Ilya Petrovitch interrupted sharply, sounding pleased, but Raskolnikov cut him off, finding it suddenly hard to speak.

hold up, let me explain what went down... if i can. though, yeah, probably no need. last year, the girl died of typhus. i stayed at the place like before. when my landlady shifted to her new spot, she told me, all friendly-like, that she trusted me, but maybe i could give her an IOU for 115 roubles, the whole debt. she swore she wouldn’t use it till i could pay. but now, without my lessons and nothing to eat, she’s suing me. what do you say to that?

“we're not interested in that drama,” Ilya Petrovitch cut in, all rude. “just write down you’ll pay, we don’t need your love life and tragedies.”

“harsh, man,” grumbled Nikodim Fomitch, sitting down and writing too, looking a bit guilty.

“write,” the head clerk ordered Raskolnikov.

“write what?” he barked back.

“i’ll dictate.”

Raskolnikov noticed the head clerk was more dismissive after his rant, but weirdly, he didn’t care anymore. if he thought about it, he'd be shocked he even spoke like that. where’d those feelings come from? if the room was full of loved ones, he’d still have nothing to say, feeling empty inside. a heavy sense of loneliness hit him. it wasn’t about the embarrassment in front of Ilya Petrovitch or the clerk’s smugness. he was beyond caring about his own pettiness or debts. even hearing a death sentence wouldn’t have moved him. something new and strange was happening. he felt he’d never appeal to these people again, not even if they were family. the sensation was raw, more painful than any thought.

the head clerk started dictating the usual—he couldn’t pay, would pay later, wouldn’t leave town or sell stuff.

“you can barely hold the pen,” the head clerk noted, curious. “you sick?”

“yeah, dizzy. keep going.”

“that’s it. sign.”

the head clerk took the paper and moved on to others.

raskolnikov handed back the pen but didn't move. sat there with his head in his hands like he was trying to hold his brain together. it felt like a nail was getting driven right into his skull. out of nowhere, he thought about just standing up, going straight to nikodim fomitch, confessing everything from yesterday, and dragging him to see the stuff hidden in the corner. the urge was so strong he actually stood up to do it. then a thought hit him—"should i pause?" but then, "nah, just dump this weight now." but he froze. nikodim fomitch was deep in conversation with ilya petrovitch. he caught parts of it:

"they'll both be let go. the whole story doesn't add up. why call the porter if they did it themselves? too clever by half. plus, pestryakov was seen at the gate with three pals. he asked directions in front of them. now, why do that if he was up to no good? as for koch, he was at the silversmith’s for half an hour before going up. left him at quarter to eight. now think..."

“how do you explain this? they knocked, the door was locked; three minutes later, unfastened.”

“that’s just it; murderer was inside, bolted in. they’d have caught him if koch hadn’t gone for the porter. he must’ve slipped past. koch keeps saying, ‘if i'd been there, he'd have axed me.’ gonna have a thanksgiving service—ha, ha!”

“no one saw him?”

“house is a regular noah’s ark,” said the head clerk.

“clear, quite clear," nikodim said.

“it’s not clear,” ilya insisted.

raskolnikov picked up his hat, headed for the door, but didn’t make it...

when he came to, he was in a chair, someone on each side. one held a yellowish glass of something. nikodim fomitch was right in front of him.

“what's wrong? you sick?” nikodim asked sharply.

“he could barely hold his pen,” the head clerk said, getting back to work.

“been sick long?” ilya petrovitch called over, papers in hand.

“since yesterday,” raskolnikov mumbled.

“you went out yesterday?”

“yes.”

“while sick?”

“yes.”

“what time?”

“around seven.”

“where?”

“just along the street.”

“short and clear.”

raskolnikov was pale, answering curtly, eyes burning as he looked at ilya petrovitch.

“he can barely stand. and you...” nikodim started.

“doesn’t matter,” ilya said oddly.

nikodim wanted to argue but stopped when the head clerk gave him a look. silence fell. it was strange.

“very well, then,” ilya concluded, “you’re free to go.”

raskolnikov stepped out, hearing chatter behind him. nikodim fomitch's voice cut through the noise. once outside, he felt steady again.

“they're gonna search the place,” he thought, rushing home. “they're onto me.”

fear gripped him again.

CHAPTER II

“what if they've already searched? what if they're in my room?”

but when he got there, it was untouched. no one had been in, not even nastasya. but damn, how could he leave those things in the wall?

he hurried to the corner, reached behind the paper, and stuffed his pockets with the stash. eight items: a couple of boxes with earrings or something, four leather cases, a chain wrapped in newspaper, and another thing that looked like a medal. he crammed them into his coat and pants, trying to hide them. grabbed the purse too. left his door open and walked out fast, his mind sharp despite feeling wrecked. he had to ditch the evidence before they came for him. had to think straight while he still could.

he'd known for a while: “toss them in the canal, let the water hide them, and it's done.” that's what he'd decided during his fevered nights. but doing it was harder than he'd thought. he paced the canal bank for over half an hour, eyeing the steps to the water. but there were always people around—rafts, women washing, boats tied up. too visible. what if the boxes floated instead of sank? he felt eyes on him everywhere. “is it real or just my imagination?” he wondered.

finally, it hit him—go to the neva. fewer people, less attention, and further away. he kicked himself for wasting time on a bad plan, just because he'd thought of it while sick. he'd been so absent-minded lately, and he knew it. he needed to move fast.

headed towards the Neva down V---- Prospect, but another thought hit me. "Why the Neva? Maybe better to head out to the Islands, stash everything somewhere quiet, mark it." Felt like I couldn't think straight, but sounded good. Didn't get there, though. Saw a passage by the square, between two blank walls. Left side, a wooden fence ran deep into the yard, turned left. Deserted spot, junk everywhere. At the end, a corner of a dingy shed peeked out. Looked like a workshop, coal dust covered everything. Thought, "This'll do."

No one around, slipped in. Near the gate, a sink for workers or cabbies. Chalked above, "Standing here strictly forbidden." Good sign, nothing weird about being here. "Dump it all, walk away!"

Checked the yard, hand in pocket, saw a big uncut stone by the wall. Maybe 60 pounds. Street on the other side, could hear people but couldn't see me unless they walked in. Had to hurry.

Grabbed the stone, flipped it with all I had. Small hollow underneath, emptied my pocket. Purse on top, hollow still had room. Flipped the stone back, stood a bit higher, but kicked the dirt around, pressed it down. Invisible.

Walked out, into the square. Joy hit me hard, like at the police office. "Tracks covered! Who'd look under that stone? It's been there ages, will stay ages more. No one's finding me." Laughed. Remembered that thin, nervous laugh, kept going till I hit K---- Boulevard. Laughter died. New thoughts creeping in. Couldn't stand passing by where I sat after meeting that girl, thinking of that cop I gave twenty copecks. "Damn him!"

Walked on, angry, distracted. Everything circling one spot, felt like I was finally facing it after two months.

damn it all, he thought suddenly, fury boiling over. it's begun, then it's begun. screw the new life! stupid... and what lies today! fawning over Ilya Petrovitch, pathetic. but that's nonsense! who cares about them or that fawning! it's not even about that!

he stopped; a new simple question hit him hard.

if this was deliberate not stupid, if i had a clear goal, why didn't i check the purse? i don't even know what i got for all this agony, this dirty business. and i almost tossed the purse with everything inside... how's that?

yeah, that's how it was. he knew it before, not new even when he decided overnight without thinking, like it was meant to be, no other way. he'd known it, understood it; all settled yesterday pulling jewel-cases out...

it's cause i'm sick, he decided grimly. worrying myself sick, don't know what i'm doing... yesterday, day before, all this time worrying... i'll get well, i won't worry... but what if i don't? sick of it all!

he walked on, restless. craved distraction but had no idea what to do. a new feeling took over—hatred for everything around him. everyone seemed loathsome—faces, movements, gestures. if anyone spoke to him, he felt he'd spit or bite...

suddenly stopped by Little Neva near the bridge to Vassilyevsky Ostrov. why, he lives here, he thought, not like i came here on purpose! same thing again... interesting though; did i come on purpose or just wander here? doesn't matter, said i'd see him today; so i will. can't go further anyway.

he went up to Razumihin’s fifth-floor room.

Razumihin was home, writing, opened the door himself. hadn't seen each other in four months. Razumihin in a ragged dressing-gown, slippers, unkempt, unshaven, unwashed. surprised face.

"is it you?" he said, eyeing him up and down, then whistled. "as hard up as that! brother, you’ve outdone me!" looking at Raskolnikov’s rags. "sit down, you’re tired, i bet."

Raskolnikov sank onto the worse-for-wear sofa. Razumihin saw he was ill.

"you’re seriously ill, you know that?" started checking his pulse. Raskolnikov pulled away.

"never mind," he said, "i came for this: no lessons... wanted... but don’t really want lessons..."

"but you’re delirious, you know!" Razumihin said, watching him closely.

"no, im not."

raskolnikov got off the sofa. climbing up to razumihin’s, he hadn’t thought it’d turn into this face-to-face. now it hit him—last thing he wanted was to be around anyone. felt his anger coil up inside. almost choked on it crossing razumihin’s threshold.

“good-bye,” he snapped, heading for the door.

“hold on, you oddball.”

“dont want to,” he muttered, pulling his hand back.

“then why come in the first place? are you nuts? this is... pretty insulting! i wont let you go like this.”

“fine, i came cause i dont know anyone else who could help... start off... cause youre nicer than most—smarter, i mean, and you can judge... and now i realize i want nothing. you hear? nothing at all... no help... no sympathy. im alone... leave me alone.”

“hang on, you nut! you’re acting crazy. whatever, suit yourself. i got no lessons, and i dont care about that, but there’s this bookseller, heruvimov—he’s worth more than any lesson. he’s into publishing, putting out natural science manuals—big circulation! titles alone are worth it! you always said i was a fool, but man, bigger fools exist! now he’s playing the advanced card, but he doesn’t know squat, i just play along. here’s two signatures of a german text—total nonsense; debates if women are human, and of course says they are. heruvimov’s pushing this as a ‘woman question’ piece; i’m translating it; he’ll stretch it out to six signatures, we’ll slap a fancy title on it and sell it for half a rouble. it’ll fly! he pays six roubles per signature, it’s like fifteen roubles for the lot, and i’ve got six upfront. after this, we’re starting a whale translation, then some dry scandals from part two of _les confessions_; someone convinced heruvimov that rousseau’s like radishchev. i don’t set him straight, forget it! hey, want the second signature of ‘_is woman a human being?_’? if you do, grab the german, pens, paper—all sorted, and take three roubles; cause ive had six upfront, you get three for your part. finish the signature, another three for you. and dont think im doing you a favor; honestly, as soon as you walked in, i knew you’d help me; im bad at spelling, and german’s a mess for me, mostly wing it. could be better, maybe worse. interested?”

raskolnikov took the german sheets, the three roubles, and left without a word. razumihin watched, baffled. but in the next street, raskolnikov turned back, climbed razumihin’s stairs again, laid the german article and the three roubles on the table, and left again, still silent.

"are you losing it?" Razumihin snapped, finally fed up. "what's this nonsense? you're making me crazy... why'd you even come to see me?"

"don't want... translation," Raskolnikov mumbled from the stairs.

"then what the hell do you want?" Razumihin yelled after him. Raskolnikov just kept going down in silence.

"hey! where are you staying?"

no answer.

"fine, forget it then!"

Raskolnikov was already in the street. On the Nikolaevsky Bridge, something snapped him back. A coachman, after yelling at him a few times, smacked him hard with a whip because he almost got trampled by the horses. That hit pissed him off. He ran to the railing, teeth clenched, grinding. Laughter followed.

"serves him right!"

"probably a pickpocket."

"acting drunk, trying to get run over; then you're stuck dealing with him."

"a scam, that's what it is."

but while he stared after the carriage, still mad and confused, rubbing his back, someone shoved money in his hand. An old woman with a girl, probably her daughter, wearing a hat and holding a green parasol.

"take it, my good man, in Christ's name."

he took it and they moved on. It was twenty copecks. From his look, they might've thought he was a beggar, and he probably got the twenty copecks because they felt bad after seeing him get hit.

he closed his hand on the twenty copecks, walked a bit, then turned to face the Neva, looking towards the palace. sky was clear, water almost bright blue, a rare sight for the Neva. the cathedral dome glittered in the sunlight, every detail sharp in the pure air. pain from the lash faded, and Raskolnikov forgot it; now some uneasy but undefined idea took over his mind. he stood still, staring intently into the distance; this spot, he knew it well. back at university, he'd stop here on his way home, caught by the scene and the strange feeling it stirred in him. always left him cold, this magnificent view, blank and lifeless. puzzled by his own dark impression, he kept putting off figuring it out. those old doubts popped up now, not by chance, he thought. weird and grotesque, stopping here like before, thinking he'd care about the same stuff as back then, amused him but also hit him hard. deep down, everything seemed distant now—old past, old thoughts, everything. felt like he was flying up, everything slipping away. suddenly noticed the money in his hand, opened it, stared at the coin, and tossed it into the water; then, he turned and went home. felt like he'd cut himself off from everything right then.

evening was setting in by the time he got back, must've been wandering for six hours. didn't recall how or where he went. undressed, shaking like an overworked horse, he lay on the sofa, pulled his greatcoat over him, and drifted into oblivion...

dusk when a scream jolted him awake. man, what a scream! those sounds—howling, wailing, grinding, tears, blows, curses—nothing like he'd ever heard.

couldn't wrap his head around it—such madness. shot up in bed, panic like a punch to the gut. noise was insane, shouting and curses, just louder and louder. then he hears her. his landlady. she's screaming, all jumbled up, like words are tripping over each other. begging maybe, cause she’s getting beat down on the stairs. whoever's doing it sounds like they’re spitting venom, voice gone ugly with rage. then it hits him—it’s Ilya Petrovitch’s voice. dude’s lost it, kicking her, slamming her head on the steps. can hear it clear as day. people are piling out from everywhere, yelling and banging doors. “what the hell?” he's thinking, “am i losing it?” but it’s too real. they’ll be after him next, must be about... yesterday... oh god. wants to lock the door, but his arm’s dead. terror’s got him frozen. noise fades after ten minutes. landlady’s still moaning, Ilya’s cursing but quieter. then silence. “did he leave? oh man!” landlady’s door slams. crowd breaks up, murmurs and shouts all over. “but why? why here?” 

drops onto the sofa, mind’s a storm, can't sleep. half an hour passes, terror like he’s never known. light floods in. Nastasya's there, candle and soup. sets it down, checks him out. 

“you’ve eaten nothing. you’re burning up.”

“Nastasya... what happened with the landlady?”

she studies him, eyes sharp.

“who beat her?”

“just now... Ilya Petrovitch... why was he here?”

she keeps staring, like she’s digging into him. 

“Nastasya, say something,” he pleads, voice barely there.

“it’s the blood,” she says, like talking to herself.

“blood? what blood?” he whispers, face draining.

no answer, just her eyes on him.

“no beating,” she finally says, voice solid.

can barely breathe, “i heard it... wasn’t asleep... everyone came out.”

“no one’s been here. it’s the blood, playing tricks. when it clogs up, you start imagining... want some soup?”

he didnt answer. nastasya was still watching him.

"give me something to drink... nastasya."

she came back with a white jug of water. remembered taking a sip and spilling some on his neck. then just blank.

CHAPTER III

he wasnt fully out, but feverish, sometimes talking nonsense, sometimes half-aware. he remembered bits later. people seemed to be around him, arguing about taking him somewhere. then he'd be alone, they'd peek in, laugh, mock. nastasya was there a lot. another person too, familiar but he couldnt place him, which drove him nuts. felt like forever, maybe a month, maybe just a day. something important slipped his mind and it bothered him. he'd get mad, scared, try to get up and leave, but they'd hold him down and he'd fade out again. finally, he came back to himself.

it was around ten in the morning. the sun lit up the room, hit the right wall near the door. nastasya was there with a stranger, looking at him closely. young guy with a beard, messenger type. landlady peeked in. raskolnikov sat up.

"who's this, nastasya?" he asked, pointing.

"he's himself again!" she said.

"he is himself," echoed the stranger.

landlady closed the door, avoiding talk. she was shy, always dodging conversations. woman of forty, not bad-looking, kind of plump, with black eyes, friendly because she was lazy and shy.

"who... are you?" raskolnikov asked the guy. but then the door swung open and in came razumihin, bending a bit because he was so tall.

"what a place!" he said. "always knocking my head. you call this a room? you're conscious, brother? heard from pashenka."

"he just came to," nastasya said.

"just came to," the guy echoed again, smiling.

"and who are you?" razumihin asked the guy. "vrazumihin here, at your service; not razumihin, like they say, but vrazumihin, student and gentleman; and he's my friend. and you?"

"messenger from our office, from merchant shelopaev, here on business."

"sit down." razumihin settled across the table. "good you're back with us, brother," he said to raskolnikov. "you barely ate or drank the last four days. we had to spoon-feed you tea. brought zossimov around twice. remember zossimov? checked you out, said it's not serious. just nerves, bad diet. thinks you need beer and radish. it'll pass, he says. zossimov's solid, making a name. now," turning to the messenger, "what's up? they've sent twice from the office; different guy last time. who was it?"

"day before yesterday, sir. alexey semyonovitch from our office."

"smarter than you, wouldn't you say?"

"yes, sir, he's more senior."

"right, go on."

"your mom sent a remittance through afanasy ivanovitch vahrushin. thirty-five roubles if you're coherent enough. heard of him?"

"yeah, vahrushin," raskolnikov mumbled.

"he knows vahrushin," razumihin cheered. "he's coherent! and you're sharp too. nice to hear wisdom."

"that's him, vahrushin. mom's request, same as before. sent instructions for thirty-five roubles hoping for better times."

"‘hoping for better’ is the best line. so, is he conscious?"

"just needs to sign."

"he can scribble. got the book?"

"right here."

"hand it over. rodya, sit up. i'll hold you. just scribble 'raskolnikov.' money's sweeter than treacle right now."

"don't want it," raskolnikov said, pushing the pen away.

"not want it?"

"won't sign."

"how'll you get by without signing?"

"don't want... the money."

"come on, brother, that's nonsense. he's just wandering again. we'll guide his hand. here."

"i can come back."

"no need. you're sharp. rodya, don't keep him waiting," and razumihin steadied raskolnikov's hand.

"stop, i'll do it myself," raskolnikov said, taking the pen and signing.

the messenger handed over the money and left.

"bravo! hungry, brother?"

"yeah," raskolnikov replied.

"soup around?"

"yesterday's," nastasya said.

"potatoes and rice?"

"yep."

"know it well. bring soup, and tea."

"on it."

Raskolnikov sat there, eyes wide, scared but not making a sound. He thought, "I'm not losing it. This is real."

Nastasya came back, soup in hand, said tea's coming soon. Brought all the fixings—salt, pepper, mustard. Table looked good, even had a clean cloth.

"You think Praskovya could send up some beer, Nastasya? We could use it."

"You're something else," she muttered, heading out to get it done.

Raskolnikov kept staring, tense. Razumihin plopped next to him on the sofa, like a bear, wrapped his arm around Raskolnikov's head, and fed him soup, blowing on it to cool it down even though it was just warm. Raskolnikov gulped it down hungrily, spoon after spoon. Razumihin paused after a bit, saying they should check with Zossimov before giving more.

Nastasya came back with the beer.

"And tea?"

"Yeah."

"Go on, Nastasya, bring the tea. No need for a doctor's note on that. But here's the beer!" He settled back, dove into the food like he hadn't eaten in days.

"Rodya, I eat like this every day now," he said, mouth full. "Thanks to Pashenka, your landlady. She takes care of me. I don't ask, but I don't say no either. And here's Nastasya with the tea. She's quick. Nastasya, want some beer?"

"Stop your nonsense!"

"Tea then?"

"Maybe."

"I'll pour it. Sit."

He poured two cups, left his food, and sat back on the sofa. He held Raskolnikov, fed him tea by the spoon, blowing on each spoon like it was the cure. Raskolnikov didn't fight it, though he was strong enough to sit up and handle a cup himself. He figured he'd play weak, see what's happening. But the fuss wore on him. After a dozen spoons of tea, he leaned back, pushed the spoon away, noticed the clean, soft pillows under his head.

"We need raspberry jam today, make him raspberry tea," said Razumihin, going back to his food.

"Where's she getting raspberries from?" Nastasya asked, balancing a saucer, sipping tea through sugar.

she’ll catch up on it at the shop. while you were out of touch, all sorts of things went down. you disappeared without a trace, and i was pissed enough to hunt you down. i started the search that day, running around asking about you. this place of yours slipped my mind since i never knew it, and your old spot, well, i just remembered it was around Five Corners, Harlamov’s, or so i thought. turns out it wasn’t Harlamov’s, but Buch’s. messed up the names. anyway, i lost it and hit the address bureau the next day. crazy how fast they found you. two minutes. your name’s listed there.

“my name!”

“yeah, and a General Kobelev, they couldn’t find him while i was there. long story. but once i found this place, i got the lowdown on all your business—everything. Nastasya knows. i met Nikodim Fomitch, Ilya Petrovitch, the house-porter, Mr. Zametov, Alexandr Grigorievitch, the head clerk at the police office, and Pashenka too; Nastasya knows…”

“he’s got her wrapped around his finger,” Nastasya muttered, grinning.

“why aren’t you putting sugar in your tea, Nastasya Petrovna?”

“you’re a riot!” Nastasya burst out, laughing. “i’m not Nikiforovna, it’s Petrovna,” she added, still chuckling.

“noted. well, brother, to sum it up, i was ready to raise hell here to clear out bad vibes, but Pashenka turned the tables. didn’t expect her to be so... interesting. what do you think?”

Raskolnikov just watched him, eyes wide, not saying a word.

“she’s got everything going for her,” Razumihin continued, unfazed by the silence.

“sneaky guy!” Nastasya shrieked, loving every second.

“shame, brother, you didn’t handle things right from the start. you should’ve approached her differently. she’s quite a mysterious character. but we’ll talk about her quirks later. how’d you let it get so bad she stopped sending meals? and that I O U? signing that was nuts. and the marriage promise when her daughter, Natalya Yegorovna, was alive? i’m aware of it all. but i see that’s touchy, so i’m a fool. sorry. but speaking of foolishness, Praskovya Pavlovna’s not as dumb as she seems, right?”

“no,” Raskolnikov mumbled, glancing away, thinking it’d be best to keep the chatter going.

"she's not, huh?" Razumihin jumped at that response. “But she ain't too sharp either, right? She's real unpredictable. Sometimes I just don't get her. Says she's thirty-six but looks more like forty. Guess she's got the right to say whatever. I swear, it's like there's this weird symbolic thing between us, some kind of algebra. Makes no sense. Anyway, since you’re not a student anymore and lost all your stuff, she got scared. With the young lady gone, she saw no reason to keep you around and figured she'd cut ties. Planned it a while, but didn’t want to lose the IOU since you promised her your mom would cover it."

“i was low to say that... my mom’s nearly broke... lied just to keep a roof over my head and some food," Raskolnikov said clear as day.

“yeah, seemed smart then. But then Tchebarov showed up, that businessman. Pashenka's too shy to act alone, but this guy isn't. He asked about the IOU. Said you’ve got a mom who’d scrape together her last roubles for you, a sister who’d sacrifice for you. That’s his angle. Why’re you surprised? I know all about your stuff now, buddy. You were open with Pashenka when you were gonna be family, and I’m saying this as a friend. But here's the thing: honest guys are open; business guys just listen and eat you alive. So she handed the IOU to Tchebarov, and he demanded payment. When I heard, I wanted to blow up at him, but things were good with Pashenka then, and I promised you’d pay. I vouched for you. Got Tchebarov, tossed him ten roubles, took back the IOU, and here it is. She trusts you now. Here, see it’s torn.”

Razumihin put the note on the table. Raskolnikov just looked at him and turned to face the wall, saying nothing. Even Razumihin felt a bit guilty.

“i see, man,” he said a bit later, “i’ve been a fool again. Thought my talk would cheer you up, but guess I just annoyed you.”

“was it you I didn’t recognize when I was out of it?” Raskolnikov asked, still not turning.

“yeah, you got mad about it, especially when I brought Zametov.”

“Zametov? The head clerk? Why’d you do that?” Raskolnikov quickly turned back to Razumihin.

what's up with you? why're you upset? he wanted to meet you 'cause i told him a lot about you... how else could i know all this? he's a good guy, really, in his own way. we're friends now—see each other almost daily. i just moved here. been to see Luise Ivanovna with him a couple times... you remember her?

did i say anything while i was out of it?

you sure did, you were out of your mind.

what'd i go on about?

same stuff people usually do... well, gotta get to work. he got up and grabbed his cap.

what'd i say?

wow, you keep asking! worried you spilled a secret? don't sweat it; nothing about a countess. but you did go on about a bulldog, earrings, chains, Krestovsky Island, some porter, and Nikodim Fomitch and Ilya Petrovitch, the assistant superintendent. and you were real hung up on your sock. kept asking for it. Zametov searched your room for that sock and finally gave it to you. you wouldn't let it go for a day, clutching it like treasure. probably still under your quilt. then you wanted fringe for your trousers. we couldn't figure that one out. anyway, back to it. here's thirty-five roubles; i'll take ten and report back in a couple hours. i'll let Zossimov know too, though he should've been here by now, it's nearly noon. Nastasya, check in on him while i'm out, see if he needs anything. i'll handle Pashenka myself. later!

"calls her Pashenka! he's got layers," Nastasya said as he left. she listened at the door but couldn't resist running after him. she really wanted to hear what he'd say to the landlady. clearly fascinated by Razumihin.

as soon as she left, the sick man threw off the covers and jumped out of bed like a madman. he'd been waiting, burning with impatience for them to leave so he could get to work. but what work? it slipped away from him just to spite him.

"God, just tell me one thing: do they know or not? what if they know and are just pretending, mocking me while i'm stuck here, and then they'll come in and tell me it's been found out and they've just... what do i do now? i've forgotten, like on purpose; remembered a minute ago."

he's standing there, lost in a fog, trying to piece it all together. checks the door, listens—nope, that's not it. then it hits him, dashes to the corner, pokes around the hole in the wall. nothing. heads to the stove, digs through the ashes and finds his raggedy pants in there. untouched. remembers the sock Razumihin mentioned. there it is, filthy under the quilt, untouched too.

“damn, Zametov. police office. why did they call me in? where's the notice? mixing it up... that was before. i checked the sock then too. now i've been sick. but why did Zametov show up? what's Razumihin's deal?” he mumbles, dropping onto the sofa. “what's real? gotta get out. gotta escape. but where? clothes are gone. boots missing. they hid them! ah, there's my coat and some money on the table, thank God! and the I O U... i’ll grab that and find a new place. they won’t find me... but Razumihin might. better disappear... America maybe. take the I O U... could be useful there. what else? they think i'm too sick to move! saw it in their eyes. if only i can slip past them! what if they've got cops downstairs? what's this, tea? and beer, half a bottle, cold.”

he grabs the beer, downs it, like putting out a fire inside. but then, it hits him, lightheaded and a shiver runs down his spine. lies down, pulls the quilt tight. thoughts scatter, a light sleepiness creeps in. he nestles into the pillow, wraps up in the quilt, sighs, and drifts into a deep sleep.

wakes up to someone coming in. opens his eyes, sees Razumihin at the door, unsure. raskolnikov sits up, trying to remember.

“oh, you’re awake! here i am! Nastasya, bring the parcel!” Razumihin yells down the stairs. “i’ll give you the rundown soon.”

“what time?” raskolnikov asks, looking around, uneasy.

“yeah, you slept well, brother, it’s almost evening, nearly six. you’ve been out over six hours.”

“really? that long?”

why not, huh? it'll do you some good. what's the rush? got a date or something? plenty of time. been waiting on you for three hours. checked in twice, found you asleep. called on Zossimov, no luck there. but he'll show. got my own stuff too, moving day with my uncle. he's living with me now. anyway, to business. Nastasya, give me the parcel. let's open it. how you feeling, brother?

"im fine. not sick. you been here long, Razumihin?"

"told you, three hours."

"no, before."

"what do you mean?"

"how long have you been visiting?"

"told you this morning. don't you remember?"

Raskolnikov thinking hard. morning feels fuzzy. can't remember, looks at Razumihin for help.

"ah," Razumihin says, "he's forgotten. thought you were off this morning. better now after sleep. looking good. alright, to business. look here."

he starts untying the bundle, clearly interested.

"this is important, brother. we gotta make a man of you. start at the top. see this cap?" pulls out a decent but cheap cap. "try it on."

"later," Raskolnikov waves it off.

"come on, Rodya, don't fight it. later's too late; won't sleep if it's wrong size. perfect fit!" he says, putting it on. "right size! a proper cap's key to dressing well. my friend Tolstyakov always takes off his hat in public. people think he's polite, but he's just hiding a mess. look, Nastasya, two hats: this old thing," he picks up Raskolnikov’s beat-up hat, "or this gem! guess the price, Rodya, what did i pay, Nastasya?"

"twenty copecks, no more," Nastasya replies.

"twenty copecks, silly!" he snapped. "today you'd go for eighty copecks! worn though. and it's a deal—wear it out, they give you a new one next year. swear it. now, let's move to the us of a, like school days. these trousers, man, i’m proud of 'em," he showed off some light summer pants, gray wool. "no holes, no stains, still pretty sharp, just a bit worn; waistcoat to match. worn makes 'em better, softer, smoother... see, Rodya, i figure if you stick to seasons, you save cash; skip asparagus in january, keep your coins. same with clothes. it's summer, so summer gear. autumn? time for warmer stuff. these'll be toast by then—not from high living, just falling apart. guess the price! two roubles twenty-five copecks! remember, wear 'em out, free replacement! only at Fedyaev's; buy once, never go back by choice. now, the boots. what do you think? bit worn but good for a couple months, foreign make, foreign leather; English Embassy guy sold 'em last week—worn six days, needed cash. price? rouble and a half. deal?" 

"but maybe they won’t fit," Nastasya chimed in.

"not fit? check it!" he pulled out Raskolnikov's old, busted boot, caked in dried mud. "didn't show up empty-handed—they used this beast's size. we all pitched in. your linen, landlady sorted that. here, three shirts, hemp but trendy front... so, eighty copecks cap, two roubles twenty-five suit—three roubles five copecks total—a rouble and a half for boots, they're solid—that's four roubles fifty-five copecks; five roubles for underclothes—whole lot—nine roubles fifty-five copecks total. forty-five copecks change in coppers. you taking it? Rodya, you're fully kitted out, overcoat's still got style, from Sharmer’s! socks and extras? your call; twenty-five roubles left. about Pashenka and rent, don't sweat it. she trusts you'll pay. now, let me swap your linen, shed this sickness with your shirt."

"leave me alone! i don't want to!" Raskolnikov waved him off, disgusted at Razumihin's playful shopping talk.

"come on, brother, don’t waste my efforts," Razumihin insisted. "Nastasya, pitch in—there we go," and despite Raskolnikov’s protests, swapped his linen. he sank back, silent for a moment.

"getting rid of them won't be quick," he thought. "what money paid for all this?" he finally asked, staring at the wall.

"money? yours, messenger brought it from Vahrushin, your mom sent it. forgot that?"

"i remember," Raskolnikov muttered after a long, grim pause. Razumihin watched him, frowning, uneasy.

the door opened, and a tall, stout man, familiar to Raskolnikov, entered.

zossimov's this big guy, tall and round, face like a blank slate, no beard, straight pale hair. always wears specs and a gold ring that looks too tight on his finger. he's 27. got on this light grey loose coat, summer pants, everything loose and trendy, spotless linen, heavy watch-chain. moves slow, acts all casual, but you can tell he thinks he's pretty important. folks find him boring, but say he's sharp at work.

"been by twice today, man. see, he's awake," razumihin says.

"yeah, i see. how you feeling now?" zossimov asks raskolnikov, sitting at the sofa's foot, getting comfy.

"he's still down," razumihin adds. "we changed his clothes, nearly cried."

"makes sense; could've waited if he wasn't up for it... pulse is good. headache?"

"im fine, perfectly fine!" raskolnikov snaps, sitting up then flopping back to the pillow, turning away. zossimov watches him close.

"good enough... he's on track," zossimov says, lazy-like. "eaten anything?"

they fill him in, ask what's okay to eat.

"he can have soup, tea... skip mushrooms, cucumbers, no meat either... but you know that. no more meds. i'll check him tomorrow, maybe today... we'll see."

"tomorrow, i'll take him out," razumihin says. "yusupov garden, then palais de cristal."

"better let him be tomorrow, but maybe a bit... we'll see."

"ugh, got a house-warming tonight. it's close. think he could come? he can lie on the sofa. you're coming?" razumihin asks zossimov. "don’t forget, you promised."

"yeah, just later. what's the plan?"

"nothing big--tea, vodka, herrings. maybe a pie... just friends."

"who's coming?"

"all locals, mostly new friends, except my uncle, who’s new too—just got to petersburg yesterday. we meet every five years."

"what's he do?"

"been a district postmaster forever; small pension. he's 65--not much to say... but I like him. porfiry petrovitch, investigation dept. head... you know him."

"relation too?"

"distant. why the frown? still mad from before, won’t come?"

"don't care about him."

"good. students, a teacher, a clerk, musician, officer, and zametov."

"what do you or he"—zossimov nods at raskolnikov—"have with zametov?"

"oh, you and your principles! you're all gears and springs; won't change course. if he's a good guy, that's my principle. zametov's a decent guy."

"even if he takes bribes."

"so what? don't care if he takes bribes," razumihin says, annoyed. "not praising him for bribes, just saying he's nice in his way! look at folks all ways—how many good ones left? i probably ain't worth a baked onion myself... maybe with you in."

"that's low; i'd give two onions for you."

"i wouldn’t give more than one for you. cut the jokes. zametov's just a kid. gotta draw him in, not push him away. you dont fix a guy by pushing, especially a kid. need extra care with kids. you progressive types miss this. hurt yourself tearing others down... but, we got something in common."

"what's that?"

"house-painter story... we’re bailing him out. nothing to fear now. it’s obvious. just added pressure."

"a painter?"

"did i miss telling you? only shared the start... about the old pawnbroker murder. painter’s caught up in it..."

"heard about that. caught my interest... for a reason... saw it in the papers too..."

"lizaveta was killed too," nastasya suddenly tells raskolnikov. she’s been by the door, listening.

"lizaveta," raskolnikov mutters, barely audible.

"lizaveta, the clothes seller. didn’t you know her? she’d come by. fixed a shirt for you once."

raskolnikov turns to the wall, eyes fix on a clumsy flower in the dirty wallpaper, counting petals, lines. feels numb, like limbs cut off. doesn’t move, just stares.

"and the painter?" zossimov cuts nastasya off, annoyed. she sighs, quiets.

"they accused him," razumihin says, heated.

"evidence?"

"evidence? nothing solid. that’s what we’re proving. like they picked on koch and pestryakov first. stupid process, makes you sick, but not our problem. pestryakov might come tonight... by the way, rodya, you know this already; happened before you got sick, before you fainted at the police station."

zossimov eyes raskolnikov. doesn’t budge.

"razumihin, you’re a busybody," zossimov notes.

"maybe, but we’ll clear him," razumihin insists, fist hitting the table. "the worst isn’t the lies—lies lead to truth—but their worship of lies... porfiry’s respectable, but... what threw them? door locked, then open with porter—so koch and pestryakov did it? that’s their logic!"

"don’t stress. they just held them, had to... met koch, bought from the old woman?"

"yeah, swindler. buys bad debts. makes a living on it. but enough! what angers me is the old, rotten routine... this case could change methods. psychological clues alone can lead to the real guy. ‘we have facts,’ they say. facts aren't everything—half the work’s in how you read them!"

"can you read them?"

"can’t keep quiet with a gut feeling, a real sense you could help if... eh! know the case details?"

"waiting to hear about the painter."

so here's the deal. on the third day after the murder, when they were grilling Koch and Pestryakov, a peasant named Dushkin, who runs a dram-shop across from the crime scene, shows up at the police station. he's got a jeweller's case with gold earrings and spins this long tale. says a house-painter, Nikolay, came by his shop the other day at eight, tried to pawn the earrings for two roubles, and claimed he found them in the street. Dushkin gave him a rouble, figuring Nikolay would just drink it away, so better the earrings stay with him. he's lying though, because Dushkin's a known fence. didn't cheat Nikolay out of a 30-rouble trinket to hand it to the cops. he was scared.

anyway, Dushkin knows Nikolay from way back—they're both from Ryazan. Nikolay's not a drunk, but he does drink. he was working on painting at the house where the murder happened, with a guy named Dmitri from the same village. after getting the rouble, Nikolay had a couple of drinks and left. next day, Dushkin hears about the murder of Alyona Ivanovna and her sister on that same staircase where Nikolay was working. got suspicious about those earrings since the murdered woman loaned out on pledges. started snooping around without letting on.

asked if Nikolay was around. Dmitri said Nikolay went on a bender, came home drunk at dawn, stayed ten minutes, and took off again. Dmitri's finishing the job alone. their work's on the second floor, same as the murder. Dushkin kept his mouth shut, but he dug around for info and then went home just as suspicious.

morning of the third day, Nikolay shows up at the shop again. not sober, but not wasted. he’s coherent. Dushkin asks about Dmitri—nothing. asks about the earrings—Nikolay sticks to his story about finding them on the street, but he’s acting strange, won’t meet his eye. told Nikolay about the murder, and he turned white as a ghost, eyes bugging out. Nikolay grabbed his hat, tried to leave. Dushkin offered him a drink to stall him, but Nikolay bolted out the door. hasn’t seen him since. Dushkin's convinced now—figures Nikolay's the one behind it.

"yeah, i figured," said Zossimov.

"now listen up. so they searched everywhere for Nikolay, right? they grabbed Dushkin, tore apart his place, nabbed Dmitri; even shook down those guys from Kolomensky. then just the other day, they caught Nikolay at some tavern, end of town. he tried to swap his silver cross for a drink. a lady saw him in the barn trying to hang himself. she yelled, folks came running. 'guess you're caught,' they said. 'take me to the cop, i'll spill it all,' he told them. they dragged him here. they asked him the usual: age, 'twenty-two', stuff like that. when he was at Dmitri’s, did he see anyone on the stairs? 'maybe, but i didnt notice.' 'hear anything?' 'nothing strange.' 'did you hear about the murders?' 'first i heard was from Afanasy Pavlovitch, just the other day.' 'where'd you find the earrings?' 'on the street.' 'why skip work with Dmitri?' 'was drinking.' 'where at?' 'some place.' 'why bolt from Dushkin's?' 'scared they'd pin it on me.' 'why scared if innocent?' man, they actually asked him that. word for word. can you believe it?"

"well, that’s what they’ve got."

"im not on about the evidence. it's about how they think about themselves. anyway, they pressed him hard, and he caved: 'didnt find it on the street, found it in the flat with Dmitri.' 'how?' 'we were painting all day. Dmitri painted my face, ran off. i chased him. at the stairs, ran into the porter and some guys. dont remember how many. everyone swore at us. some guy with a lady swore too. Dmitri and i were scuffling, but just playing. he ran out, i went back to clean up, expecting him to come back. then i stepped on this box in the hallway. wrapped in paper. opened it, found the earrings...'"

"Behind the door? Behind the door?" Raskolnikov burst out, eyes wide, staring at Razumihin. He sat up slow, leaning on his hand.

"Yeah... what's wrong?" Razumihin asked, standing up too.

"Nothing," Raskolnikov mumbled, turning to the wall. Silence.

"He must've been dreaming," Razumihin finally said, glancing at Zossimov. Zossimov shook his head slightly.

"Well, go on," said Zossimov. "What's next?"

"What's next? Soon as he saw the earrings, ditched Dmitri, grabbed his cap, ran to Dushkin, got a rouble. Lied, said he found them on the street, then went drinking. Keeps saying he knows nothing about the murder: 'I didn't know till yesterday.' 'Why didn't you come sooner?' 'I was scared.' 'Why'd you try to hang yourself?' 'Anxiety.' 'What anxiety?' 'Scared I'd get blamed.' That's the story. Now what do they figure from that?"

"No guessing. There's a clue, a fact. You wouldn't let your painter go free?"

"Now they just think he's the killer. No doubt."

"That's nonsense. You're worked up. But the earrings? If they showed up with Nikolay on the same day, same hour, they got there somehow. That's big in a case like this."

"How'd they get there?" Razumihin shouted. "How can you, a doctor, who studies people, not see the man's character in all this? Don't you get that his answers are the truth? He found the box, picked it up."

"The truth! But he admitted he lied at first."

"Listen up. The porter, Koch, Pestryakov, another porter, the first porter's wife, a woman in the porter's lodge, Kryukov and a lady, eight or ten witnesses, all say Nikolay had Dmitri on the ground, beating him, Dmitri pulling his hair, also hitting. They were kids, blocking the way, getting cursed at, laughing, fighting, and then ran into the street. Note this: the bodies upstairs were warm. If they or Nikolay alone killed them, broke boxes, or robbed, let me ask: do their giggles and childish scuffle fit with axes, blood, and robbery? They'd just killed them, left the flat open knowing folks would check, threw away the loot, and acted like kids in the street. And a dozen people swear to this!"

"Sure it's strange! Impossible, almost, but..."

no, brother, no _buts_. those earrings found in his hands on the exact day and time of the murder, sure, they look bad. but he's got a story for it, makes sense too. we gotta look at what proves him innocent too, especially those undeniable facts. but you think our legal system's gonna take a psychological impossibility as proof? nah, they won't. they found the jewel-case, and the guy tried to hang himself. they think that screams guilt. that's the real kicker, you see?

"oh, you're worked up! hang on. i forgot to ask; what's the proof the box belonged to the old lady?"

"it's been proven," Razumihin said, not too happy, frowning. "Koch recognized the case, gave the owner's name, and it checked out."

"that's bad news. now, another thing. anyone spot Nikolay when Koch and Pestryakov went upstairs? any evidence there?"

"no one saw him," Razumihin grumbled. "that's the problem. even Koch and Pestryakov didn't notice anyone. they said the flat was open, must've been work going on, but they weren't paying attention."

"hm!... so the defense's only real point is they were beating each other and laughing. that's a strong guess, but... how do you explain the facts yourself?"

"how do i explain them? it's clear enough. the jewel-case leads the way. the real murderer dropped those earrings. he was upstairs, locked in when Koch and Pestryakov knocked.
