Koch left the door alone, so the murderer slipped out, ran down, had no other escape. he hid when Nikolay and Dmitri ran out. waited till the coast was clear, then walked downstairs when no one was watching. maybe someone saw him, but didn't notice. lots of folks coming and going. he must've dropped the earrings from his pocket without realizing. jewel-case proves he hid there... that's my take."

"too clever! no, man, that's too clever. too perfect."

"but, why, why?"

"why, cause everything fits too well... it's like a drama."

"a-ach!" Razumihin was saying, and then the door swung open. a stranger walked in.

so this guy shows up, not young, looking stiff and like he's got a sour taste in his mouth. stops right at the door, like he's walked into a zoo. checks out the place like it's a dump, eyeing Raskolnikov, who's sprawled out on a dirty couch. then he gives Razumihin the once-over too, who's just sitting there, staring back at him.

silence hangs for a bit, then the guy gets the hint he's not gonna scare anyone into anything here. he softens up, but still talks like he's asking a kid to repeat back a lesson, pointing at Zossimov:

“rodion romanovitch raskolnikov, a student, or formerly a student?”

zossimov's about to answer, but Razumihin cuts in.

“there he is on the sofa! what do you want?”

that knocks the wind outta the guy. he almost turns to Razumihin, then stops himself, looks back to Zossimov.

“this is raskolnikov,” zossimov mumbles, nodding over. then, like he's got all the time in the world, checks his watch before putting it away.

raskolnikov's just lying there, pale as a ghost, looking like he's been put through the wringer. but this new guy's got his attention now, first curious, then suspicious. when zossimov confirms it's him, raskolnikov sits up, weak but defiant:

“yeah, i am raskolnikov! what do you want?”

the visitor sizes him up, then says with weight:

“pyotr petrovitch luzhin. i figure you might know the name?”

but raskolnikov just stares blankly, like he's never heard it before.

“no news reached you?” luzhin asks, thrown off.

raskolnikov just falls back, staring at the ceiling, leaving luzhin looking a bit lost. zossimov and razumihin keep watching him, and he starts fidgeting.

“i thought, calculated even, a letter sent over ten days ago...”

razumihin butts in again: “why're you standing there? if you've got something, sit down. nastasya, make room. here's a chair, squeeze in!”

he moves his chair, makes a little space and waits for luzhin to awkwardly maneuver in, finally sitting with a suspicious look at razumihin.

“Dont worry,” the guy said. “Rodya's been sick for five days, out of it for three, but he's getting better and wants to eat. This is his doctor, just checked him. I'm a buddy of Rodya's, used to be a student like him, now I'm helping him out; ignore us, do your thing.”

“Thanks. But will I bother him by being here and talking?” Pyotr Petrovitch asked Zossimov.

“N-no,” Zossimov mumbled, yawning again. “You might even entertain him.”

“He's been awake a while, since morning,” Razumihin added, friendly enough to make Pyotr Petrovitch relax a bit, maybe because the scruffy guy called himself a student.

“Your mom,” Luzhin started.

“Hm!” Razumihin cleared his throat, making Luzhin pause.

“It's fine, keep going.”

Luzhin shrugged.

“Your mom started a letter to you while I was near her. I waited a few days before visiting you, to make sure you got the news; but now, to my surprise…”

“I know, I know!” Raskolnikov interrupted, annoyed. “So you're the fiancé? Got it, enough!”

Pyotr Petrovitch was clearly offended but stayed quiet, trying hard to figure out what was happening. Silence hung for a moment.

Meanwhile, Raskolnikov, who had turned to face him, suddenly eyed him again with curiosity, like he hadn't really looked at him before or noticed something new. He propped himself up to get a better look. There was definitely something about Pyotr Petrovitch that matched the "fiancé" label thrown at him. First off, he’d clearly used his time in the city to spruce up for his bride-to-be—innocent enough. Even if he was a bit too proud of his makeover, you could forgive it, given his role. His clothes were brand new, maybe too new and a tad too perfect. Even his smart new hat seemed overdone, treated with too much care. The fancy lavender gloves, real Louvain, were more for show than wear. Light, youthful colors dominated his outfit—a nice summer jacket, light trousers, matching waistcoat, fresh linen, and a cravat with pink stripes. All of it suited him, making his face look younger than forty-five. His dark sideburns framed his face well, and even his slightly gray hair, styled by a barber, didn’t make him look silly, like some grooms. If there was something off-putting about his good looks, it came from something else. After giving Luzhin a bold once-over, Raskolnikov grinned maliciously, settled back on his pillow, and stared at the ceiling again.

But Luzhin steeled himself, deciding to ignore their strangeness.

"man, sorry you’re in this spot," he started, trying to break the silence. "if i’d known you were sick, id've come sooner. but you know how it is with work. got a big legal thing in the Senate too, plus other stuff you can guess. your mom and sister should be here soon."

Raskolnikov shifted, maybe ready to talk; looked a bit excited. Pyotr Petrovitch paused, waited, then kept going:

"...any minute now. found them a place to stay when they get here."

"where?" Raskolnikov asked weakly.

"close by, at Bakaleyev’s."

"that’s on Voskresensky," Razumihin chimed in. "two floors, rented by some guy Yushin; been there."

"yeah, rooms..."

"awful place—dirty, smells bad, shady folks. stuff’s happened there. but it’s cheap..."

"couldn’t find that out, being new to Petersburg," Pyotr Petrovitch said, a bit miffed. "but those rooms are clean, and it's just temporary. got our permanent place, fixing it up. staying with Andrey Semyonovitch Lebeziatnikov meantime, at Madame Lippevechsel’s; he told me about Bakaleyev’s too..."

"Lebeziatnikov?" Raskolnikov said slowly, like he was trying to remember.

"yeah, Andrey Semyonovitch Lebeziatnikov, a Ministry clerk. you know him?"

"yes... no," Raskolnikov replied.

"sorry, thought you might. used to be his guardian. good guy, progressive. like talking to young people, you learn new things." Luzhin looked hopeful at them.

"how so?" Razumihin asked.

"serious stuff," Pyotr Petrovitch replied, happy to explain. "been ten years since i was here. heard about changes and ideas in the provinces, but to see it clear, you gotta be in Petersburg. younger folks teach you a lot. and i’m impressed..."

"at what?"

"it’s broad. maybe i’m wrong, but i see clearer views, more criticism, more practical thinking..."

"that’s true," Zossimov added.

"nonsense! no practicality," Razumihin cut in. "hard to find, doesn’t just happen. for 200 years, we’ve been away from practical life. ideas are brewing," he said to Pyotr Petrovitch, "and there’s a want for good, though immature, and honesty’s there, despite many crooks. but practicality? it’s lacking."

"i disagree," Pyotr Petrovitch replied, enjoying it. "sure, people mess up, but you gotta forgive; those mistakes show passion and tough surroundings. little’s done, but it’s a short time; not talking means. personally, some things are done. new ideas, valuable works instead of old romantic authors. literature’s growing up, bad prejudices getting mocked... we’ve cut off from the past, which is great..."

"he’s memorized that to show off!" Raskolnikov burst out.

"what?" Pyotr Petrovitch asked, missing it; got no answer.

"all true," Zossimov quickly added.

"Right?" Pyotr Petrovitch keeps going, throwing a look at Zossimov. Turns to Razumihin, a bit smug, like he's about to call him "young man." "Gotta admit, there's progress, or as they say in science and economics, advancement..."

"Just talk."

"No, listen," Pyotr pushes on, maybe too fast. "Before, you hear ‘love thy neighbor,’ and what happens? You rip your coat in two, both end up cold. Russian saying: ‘Chase two rabbits, catch none.’ Science now says, put yourself first. Handle your business, keep your coat intact. More whole coats, better society. Get rich for myself, but indirectly for all. Not out of generosity, just progress. Simple idea, but stuck because of idealism. Shouldn't take much to see it..."

"Look, I'm no genius," Razumihin cuts in, sharp. "Let's drop it. Started with a point, but three years of this talk, same old stuff, makes me cringe. Sure, show off your smarts, that's fine. Just wanted to see what kind of guy you are. So many abusing the cause, dragging it down. That's it."

"Sir," Luzhin says, offended, all dignified. "Do you suggest I'm..."

"Oh no... wouldn't think of it," Razumihin wraps it up, shifts back to Zossimov.

Pyotr Petrovitch wisely decides to leave soon. "Hope we get to know each other better, Raskolnikov, once you're better..."

Raskolnikov doesn’t even look. Pyotr Petrovitch starts standing up.

"Probably one of her clients killed her," Zossimov says sure as anything.

"No doubt," Razumihin replies. "Porfiry's checking those who left stuff with her."

"Checking how?" Raskolnikov asks.

"Names from Koch, some on the pledges, others came forward."

"Had to be a skilled crook. Bold move, cool execution!"

"That's exactly what it wasn't!" Razumihin cuts in. "You're all missing the mark. I stand by it—he's not clever, not experienced. Probably his first crime! Thinking it was premeditated with a slick criminal doesn't add up. Picture him as a rookie—it was dumb luck that got him through. Maybe he didn't even see obstacles coming! How'd he pull it off? He grabbed jewels worth peanuts, stuffed 'em in his pockets, rummaged through the old lady's junk—and they found fifteen hundred roubles and notes in a top drawer! He didn't know how to steal; just knew murder. First crime, I'm telling you, his first. He panicked. Got away by chance, not skill!"

"You're talking about that pawnbroker murder, right?" Pyotr Petrovitch asks Zossimov. He's standing there, hat and gloves in hand, and wants to drop a few more smart lines before heading out. Trying to leave a good impression, vanity all over it.

"Yeah, heard of it?"

"Sure, being nearby."

"You know the details?"

"Can't say that; but I'm intrigued by the broader issue—the whole case. Besides, crime's spiking in lower classes these last five years, robbery and arson everywhere, and the bizarre bit? It's climbing in higher classes too. Students robbing mail, folks forging banknotes; in Moscow, a gang forging lottery tickets, with a history lecturer leading it. Our secretary abroad got murdered for some shady gain... And if this pawnbroker was killed by someone upper-class—peasants don't pawn gold—what's with this moral collapse in the 'civilized' part of society?"

"Economic changes," Zossimov chimes in.

"Explain it?" Razumihin jumps in. "Maybe it's our chronic impracticality."

"What do you mean?"

"What'd your Moscow lecturer say when asked why he forged notes? 'Everyone's getting rich somehow, so I want in quick.' Not the exact words, but he wanted money without the work! We're used to shortcuts, everything done for us. Then the big moment hit,[*] and people showed their true colors."

[*] The emancipation of the serfs in 1861.
     --TRANSLATOR’S NOTE.

"But principles? Morality?"

"Why worry?" Raskolnikov cuts in suddenly. "Fits your theory!"

"My theory?"

"Think it through—your theory allows people to be killed..."

"Seriously!" Luzhin bursts out.

"No, that's wrong," Zossimov adds.

Raskolnikov lays there, pale, lip twitching, struggling to breathe.

"There's limits," Luzhin argues, all superior. "Economic ideas aren't a murder pass, just suppose..."

"Is it true," Raskolnikov interrupts again, voice shaking with anger and the thrill of mocking him, "you told your fiancée... right after she accepted... that what you liked most... was her being broke... so it's easier to control her, and remind her you're her benefactor?"

"damn it," Luzhin snapped, face red, "twisting my words like that! let me tell ya, whatever you've heard, it's bogus. and i think i know who’s behind it... your mom... she's got some wild ideas... never thought she'd twist things like this..." 

"listen," Raskolnikov said, sitting up with eyes like daggers, "listen."

"what?" Luzhin stopped, waiting, looking all puffed up.

"you ever talk about my mom again... you'll be flying down those stairs!"

"what's wrong with you?" Razumihin shouted.

"so that's it?" Luzhin went pale, biting his lip. "from the start you hated me, but i stayed to see more. i can forgive a sick man, but you... never again..."

"im not sick," Raskolnikov yelled.

"worse then..."

"go to hell!"

Luzhin was already squeezing out the door, ignoring everyone, not even nodding at Zossimov who’d been signaling him to back off. He ducked through the door, hat held high so it wouldn’t get crushed, spine all bent like he'd been slapped.

"what'd you do that for?" Razumihin said, shaking his head.

"leave me alone—all of you!" Raskolnikov shouted. "quit bugging me! im not scared of you! not scared of anyone! get out! i want to be alone!"

"let's go," Zossimov said, nodding to Razumihin.

"but we can't leave him like this!"

"let's go," Zossimov insisted, heading out. Razumihin hesitated, then followed.

"might be worse to stay," Zossimov said on the stairs. "don't rile him up."

"what's wrong with him?"

"needs a shock to shake him up. he's got something weighing heavy on him... some idea stuck in his head."

"maybe it's that guy, Pyotr Petrovitch. he's gonna marry his sister, and Raskolnikov got a letter about it before he fell sick."

"yeah, that jerk might’ve worsened things. noticed he doesn’t care about much except the murder?"

"yeah," Razumihin agreed. "he’s spooked by it. it hit him hard that day at the station; he fainted."

"tell me more tonight, and i'll share something. he intrigues me. I'll check on him later... he's not inflamed though."

"thanks! ill hang with Pashenka and keep an eye through Nastasya..."

left alone, Raskolnikov glared at Nastasya, frustrated.

"tea?" she asked.

"later! im tired! leave me."

he turned to the wall; Nastasya left.

soon as she left, he got up, latched the door, untied the parcel Razumihin brought, and started dressing. calm hit him, like right then. no more delirium or panic. his moves were sharp, like he had a plan. "today, today," he kept saying. he knew he was weak but his focus was like a power boost. figured he could make it, not collapse in the street. dressed up in new clothes, pocketed the money on the table, 25 roubles. took all the change from the 10 Razumihin used on the clothes. unlatched the door, slipped out, down the stairs, peeked in the kitchen. Nastasya was busy with the samovar, didn't hear a thing. who'd think he'd leave anyway? next thing, he's outside.

almost eight, sun setting. still stifling, but he drank in the dusty air. head spinning, eyes fierce, face pale and yellow. no clue where he was headed, just one thing in mind: "end this today, can't live like this." no plan, didn't wanna think how. just knew everything had to change, "one way or another," he kept telling himself, dead set on it.

he walked towards the Hay Market, like always. dark-haired guy with a barrel organ was out front a shop, playing a sappy tune. girl, maybe fifteen, singing with him. she was in a worn-out crinoline and a hat with a bright feather. Raskolnikov joined the crowd, gave the girl a five-copeck coin. she stopped, shouted to the organ grinder, and they moved to the next shop.

"you into street music?" Raskolnikov asked some middle-aged guy next to him. guy looked startled.

"love it with a street organ," Raskolnikov said, voice not matching the mood, "especially on cold, dark, wet autumn nights, with sickly faces around and wet snow falling." 

"don't know... excuse me..." the guy muttered, freaked out, and crossed the street.

Raskolnikov kept walking, got to the Hay Market corner where the huckster and Lizaveta talked before; but they were gone. he stopped, looked around, and asked a young guy in a red shirt by the corn shop, "isn't there a guy with a booth here with his wife?"

all kinds of folks set up booths here, the young man said, giving Raskolnikov a bit of a side-eye.

"what’s his name?"

"his baptized name."

"you from Zaraïsky too? which part?"

the young man glanced back at him.

"not a province, sir, just a district. pardon me, sir!"

"is that a tavern up there?"

"yeah, it's got food, billiards, and even some princesses... la-la!"

Raskolnikov moved through the square. Peasants packed the area. He pushed into the crowd, scanning faces. Felt a weird pull to chat, but they ignored him, yelling in groups. He stood for a bit, then turned right towards V.

lately, he'd been drawn to this street leading to Sadovy, especially when feeling low, to feel even lower.

he walked, mind blank. big building on the corner full of bars and eateries. women rushing in and out, no hats, just home clothes. gathered here and there, chatting outside the party joints. inside, you could hear singing, guitar, laughter. women crowded the doors, sitting and standing. a drunk soldier wandered nearby, cursing, lost. two beggars were at it, and one guy lay passed out in the street. Raskolnikov joined the women, listening to their rough voices. no hats, cotton dresses, rough shoes. some old, some young, most with dark eyes.

the noise from below pulled him in... someone inside danced wildly, tapping heels to guitar and a high voice singing a catchy tune. he leaned down, peeking in, trying to catch the song.

“oh, my handsome soldier, don't beat me for nothing,” sang the thin voice. Raskolnikov wanted to know what he was singing, like it mattered.

“should i go in?” he thought. “they’re drunk and laughing. should i join?”

“coming in?” one woman asked, her voice still sweet, younger and not too bad looking.

“she's pretty,” he said, standing up straight.

she smiled at the compliment.

“you're good-looking too,” she replied.

“isn’t he skinny though!” another woman chimed in with a deep voice. “just out of the hospital?”

“all generals’ daughters, but all got snub noses,” joked a drunk peasant in a loose coat. “look how happy they are.”

“get along!”

“i’m going, sweetheart!”

and he rushed into the saloon. Raskolnikov moved on.

“hey, sir,” the girl called after him.

“what?”

she paused.

“i’d love an hour with you, kind sir, but i’m shy. got six copecks for a drink, handsome?”

Raskolnikov handed her the first thing he found—fifteen copecks.

“ah, such a kind gentleman!”

“what’s your name?”

“ask for Duclida.”

"too much," the woman said, shaking her head at Duclida. "dont know how you can ask like that. id drop from shame..."

Raskolnikov watched her, curious. A woman marked by life, bruises everywhere, swollen lip, spoke her mind low and serious. "where is it," he thought, "where'd i read about someone facing death thinking if he had to live on a rock, just enough to stand, with ocean and storm forever, better that than dying right away? just live, whatever it takes. man is awful... and the one who calls him awful is too," he added.

he moved on. "palais de cristal! Razumihin was on about that. what was i after? right, the papers... Zossimov mentioned it. got the papers?" he asked in a clean but empty restaurant. a few folks sipping tea, four men deeper in with champagne. thought Zametov might be there but couldnt be sure. "so what if it is?" he thought.

"vodka?" the waiter asked.

"tea and papers, last five days, and you'll get something."

"yes, sir, today's. no vodka?"

old papers and tea arrived. Raskolnikov dug in.

"oh, hell... accidents, drunken shopkeeper burns up, fires in Peski... fire in Petersburg... and another... and another... there it is!" he found it, reading though the lines danced. hands shook from nerves flipping pages. suddenly someone sat next to him. looked up, it was Zametov, with his rings and chain, slick hair, flashy vest, worn coat. seemed in a good mood, champagne from before making him merry.

"you here?" Zametov said, like they'd known each other forever. "Razumihin told me you were out cold yesterday. strange! i went to see you, you know."

Raskolnikov knew he'd come. put the papers down, faced Zametov, a smile with a touch of impatience.

"heard you did," he said. "you hunted for my sock... and Razumihin's quite taken with you? says you went to Luise Ivanovna's with him--the woman you tried to help, winked at the Explosive Lieutenant but he didnt get it. remember? how could he miss it--was obvious, right?"

"what a hot head!"

"the explosive one?"

"no, Razumihin."

"must be nice, Zametov; free pass to the best spots. who's been filling you with champagne?"

"just having a drink together... and you say pouring it into me!"

"Fee for everything, huh?" Raskolnikov chuckled, giving Zametov a friendly slap on the shoulder. "Not angry, just messing around, like your guy said when he was wrestling Dmitri over the old lady..."

"How do you know that?"

"Maybe I know more than you think."

"You’re acting strange... still look unwell. Shouldn’t have come out."

"I seem strange to you?"

"Yes. Reading the papers?"

"Yeah."

"Lots about the fires."

"Not reading that." He gave Zametov a sly grin. "Curious what I'm reading?"

"Not at all. Just asking. Why keep going on...?"

"You’re educated, right?"

"Sixth class at the gymnasium," Zametov said, trying to sound important.

"Sixth class! Fancy with your style and rings. Gentleman of fortune, eh? Cute!" Raskolnikov laughed in his face. Zametov looked more confused than offended.

"Strange, you are!" Zametov said seriously. "Think you're still not right."

"Me, delirious? You’re talking nonsense! Strange, you say?"

"Yes, curious."

"Wanna know what I was reading? See all these papers I got them to bring? Suspicious, huh?"

"Well, what?"

"Got your attention?"

"What do you mean?"

"I’ll explain later, but now... I declare... nah, I confess... no, that’s off. I was reading, searching..." He squinted, paused. "Looking for news on the old pawnbroker murder," he whispered, getting real close. Zametov just stared back. Silence dragged a whole minute.

"What if you were reading it?" Zametov finally snapped. "None of my business! So what?"

"Same old lady," Raskolnikov whispered again, ignoring Zametov. "Remember the police office chat when I fainted? Get it now?"

"What do you mean? Understand what?" Zametov asked, almost scared.

Raskolnikov’s serious look cracked, and he burst into laughter, recalling standing with an axe, door latch shaking, wanting to shout and laugh at the guys outside.

"You’re mad, or..." Zametov started, then stopped, shocked by his own thought.

"Or what? What? Tell me!"

"Nothing," Zametov said, angry. "Just nonsense!"

They both went quiet. Raskolnikov leaned on the table, lost in thought, completely forgetting Zametov. Silence stretched on.

"Why dont you drink your tea? It's getting cold," Zametov said.

"What! Tea? Oh, yeah..." Raskolnikov took a sip, ate some bread, then looked at Zametov and seemed to snap back to reality. His face slipped back into that mocking look. He kept drinking his tea.

“Lot of these crimes lately,” Zametov said. “Just read in the _Moscow News_ about a whole gang of counterfeiters caught in Moscow. They were practically a society. Forging tickets!”

“Oh, but that was a while ago! I read about it a month back,” Raskolnikov replied calmly. “So you think they’re criminals?” he added with a smile.

“Of course they’re criminals.”

“They? They’re kids, fools, not real criminals! Imagine, fifty people meeting up for this—what a joke! Three’s a crowd. They gotta trust each other more than they trust themselves! One slips up after a drink and it all falls apart. Fools! They hired unreliable people to swap the notes—bad move trusting a random guy! Even if they made a million each, they'd be stuck with each other forever. Might as well hang themselves now! And they couldn’t even swap the notes right; the guy changing the notes pocketed five thousand rubles, hands shaking. Counted four thousand, didn’t even bother with the fifth. Just wanted the cash and to bolt. Raised suspicion. The whole thing blew up cause of one idiot! Can you believe it?”

“That his hands trembled?” Zametov noted, “yeah, that’s believable. I can see that happening. Sometimes you just cant handle it.”

“Cant handle what?”

“Could you handle it? I couldn’t. For a hundred rubles, face that kind of stress? Walk into a bank with fake notes where they’re trained to spot them? No, I couldn’t do it. Could you?”

Raskolnikov felt the urge to stick his tongue out. Shivers ran down his spine.

“I’d do it differently,” Raskolnikov started. “Here’s how I’d switch the notes: Count the first thousand three, four times, double-check every note. Move to the second thousand; halfway through, hold a fifty-rouble note to the light, turn it, check again—make sure it's legit. ‘I’m worried,’ I’d say, ‘a relative lost twenty-five rubles on a fake note,’ then I'd tell the whole story. Halfway through the third, ‘Wait,’ I’d say, ‘I think I messed up on the seventh hundred in that second thousand, not sure.’ Drop the third thousand, go back, and keep doing that. At the end, pick one from the fifth and second thousand, check them in the light, and ask, ‘Please swap these,’ and make the clerk so flustered he won’t know what to do. Once done and out, I'd come back, ‘No, excuse me,’ and ask for some clarification. That’s my way.”

"Man, you say some wild things," Zametov laughed. "But talk's cheap. When it comes to action, you'd probably mess up. Even the best lose their nerve sometimes. Look at that old lady who got whacked in our district. Guy was desperate, went for it in broad daylight, barely got away. Hands were shaking, couldn't even finish the job."

Raskolnikov looked ticked off.

"Yeah? If it's so clear, why haven't you caught him?" he shot back.

"We will."

"You? Think you can? It's tough. You just watch who's suddenly got cash. Anyone could fool you with that."

"True, they do. Pull off a smart murder, then go blow it at the bar. Not all killers are clever like you. You wouldn't be that dumb, right?"

Raskolnikov frowned, stared hard at Zametov.

"You really wanna know what I'd do?" he asked, a bit annoyed.

"Yeah, I do," Zametov said, serious now.

"Alright. I'd take the loot, find some quiet spot with fences, like a garden. Pick a big rock, lift it, stash the goods underneath. Roll it back, make it look untouched, press it down, walk away. Leave it for years. Let them search."

"You're nuts," Zametov whispered, backing off. Raskolnikov's face was pale, lips twitching. He leaned in, lips moving without sound, fighting himself.

"What if I killed the old woman and Lizaveta?" he blurted out, realizing what he'd said.

Zametov stared, pale as a sheet, forced a smile.

"Is it true?" he asked, voice shaky. Raskolnikov glared.

"Admit it, you believed it."

"No, less now than ever," Zametov said quickly.

"Got you. You believed it before, didn't you?"

"No way," Zametov stammered, clearly flustered. "Were you just trying to scare me?"

you dont believe it? what was the chat when i left the station? and why'd that explosive lieutenant grill me after i fainted? hey, waiter," he shouted, grabbing his cap, "how much?"

"thirty copecks," the waiter replied, rushing over.

"and twenty more for vodka. see this? lots of money!" he showed his shaking hand to zametov, full of notes. "red and blue, twenty-five roubles. where'd it all come from? and these new clothes? you know i was broke. bet you even grilled my landlady... well, enough of this! till next time!"

he left, trembling with a wild, hysterical mix of joy and gloom. he felt twisted, like after a fit. any shock or irritation spiked his energy, but he'd crash just as quick.

zametov stayed behind, lost in thought. raskolnikov had unknowingly flipped a switch in his brain. "ilya petrovitch is a blockhead," he concluded.

as raskolnikov stepped out of the restaurant, he bumped into razumihin. they almost collided, staring each other down. razumihin was shocked, then anger flared in his eyes.

"so here you are!" he yelled. "you left your bed! i've been tearing the place apart looking for you! and here you are. rodya! what's going on? tell me the truth!"

"means im sick of you all and want to be alone," raskolnikov said calmly.

"alone? when you can barely walk, looking like a ghost! idiot!... what were you doing at the palais de cristal? spill it!"

"let me go!" raskolnikov tried to push past. razumihin grabbed his shoulder, not having it.

"let you go? you tell me that? i'll carry you home and lock you up!"

"listen, razumihin," raskolnikov spoke quietly, "can't you see i don't want your help? you have this need to help someone who... curses it, finds it a burden! why'd you find me when i got sick? maybe i wanted to die. didn't i say you're torturing me, making me sick of you! you want to torture! all this is slowing my recovery, always irritating me. zossimov left to avoid it. you do the same! what right do you have to keep me by force? can't you see i'm in my right mind? how can i make you stop with your kindness? maybe i'm ungrateful, maybe i'm mean, but let me be, for god's sake, let me be! let me be, let me be!"

he started calm, ready to spit venom, but ended up panting, furious like with luzhin.

razumihin paused, thought, then let go.

"well, go to hell then," he said, like he was thinking it through. "stay," he shouted, just as Raskolnikov was about to leave. "listen. you're all a bunch of babbling idiots! got a problem? you sit on it like a hen on an egg. and you copy even that! no independent life in you! made of ointment and lymph instead of blood. i don't believe in any of you! always trying to be unlike human beings. stop!" he yelled, seeing Raskolnikov move again. "hear me out! got a house-warming tonight, bet they're there already. left my uncle to host. if you weren’t a fool, a plain fool, you'd join us. you'd have a chair, tea, company. or the sofa. Zossimov will be there. will you come?"

"no."

"rubbish!" Razumihin shouted, losing patience. "how do you know? can't even answer for yourself! fought with folks, went back to them after. you get ashamed and return. so remember, Potchinkov’s place, third floor."

"Mr. Razumihin, you'd let anyone beat you out of kindness."

"beat me? i'd twist his nose off! Potchinkov’s, 47, Babushkin’s flat..."

"i won't come, Razumihin." Raskolnikov turned away.

"bet you will," Razumihin shouted. "i won't know you if you don’t! stay, is Zametov in there?"

"yes."

"see him?"

"yes."

"talked?"

"yes."

"about what? fine, don't tell me. Potchinkov’s, 47, Babushkin’s, remember!"

Raskolnikov walked off, turned the corner. Razumihin watched him go, waved, went inside, stopped short of the stairs.

"confound it," he muttered. "talked sense but... i'm a fool! madmen talk sense too! just what Zossimov feared." He tapped his forehead. "what if... how'd i let him go alone? he might drown himself... what a blunder! can't have that." He ran to catch Raskolnikov, but he was gone. Cursed, went back quick to the Palais de Cristal to find Zametov.

raskolnikov trudged to X---- Bridge, barely holding himself together. leaned on the rail, elbows down, eyes lost in the sunset. he felt drained after leaving razumihin, just wanted to crash right there. stared at the fading light, buildings going dark, one window like it was on fire in the sunset, water dark, caught his eye. red circles in his vision, everything danced — houses, people, carriages. then a shock, something off. noticed a tall woman next to him, face yellow and wasted, eyes red. she didn't see him, didn't see anything. suddenly, she climbed the rail and threw herself into the canal. water swallowed her, then she bobbed up, floating, skirt puffed up.

“a woman drowning!” voices yelled. people rushed, packed the bridge. “mercy! it's our afrosinya!” someone cried. “save her!” they yelled for a boat, but a cop was faster. he jumped in, grabbed her, pulled her out with help. laid her on the pavement, she coughed, sneezed, confused, just wiped at her dress.

“she's out of her mind, drunk,” same woman's voice. “tried to hang herself before, we stopped her. left her for a second, and now this! we live close, second house from the end.” crowd thinned. police around her, someone mentioned the station. raskolnikov watched, felt nothing. disgusted. “water... it's not enough,” he muttered. “nothing comes of it.” thought about the police office, why zametov wasn't there. turned from the rail, looked around.

“fine then!” he said, firm. left the bridge, headed for the station. heart empty. didn't want to think, even lost the drive to end it all. just apathy now.

"might be a way out," he thought, dragging his feet along the canal bank. “i wanna end it... but is it really a way out? who cares! there's that small space. ha! but what an end! do i tell them or not? ah, damn! im so tired! if i could just find a place to sit or lie down! what bothers me most is how stupid it all feels. but i dont care! what dumb ideas pop into your head."

to get to the police office, he needed to go straight and take the second left. just a few steps really. but at the first turn, he paused, thought for a bit, then veered off down a side street, maybe to stall a moment. walking with his eyes on the ground, suddenly someone seemed to whisper in his ear; he looked up and found himself right at the gate of the house. he hadn't been near it since that night. some strong pull drew him in. he went through the gate, into the first entrance on the right, and started climbing the familiar staircase to the fourth floor. the staircase was narrow, steep, and dark. he stopped on each landing, looking around curiously; the window frame had been taken out on the first landing. "wasn't like that before," he thought. here was the flat on the second floor where nikolay and dmitri had worked. "it's shut now and the door's newly painted. to let, huh." then the third floor and the fourth. "here!" he was surprised to see the flat door wide open. voices inside; he hadn't expected that. after hesitating, he climbed the last steps and went in. it was being worked on; there were workmen. it amazed him; he kinda thought everything would be how he left it, maybe even the bodies on the floor. now, just bare walls, no furniture; strange. he walked to the window and sat on the sill. two workmen, both young, one much younger. they were papering the walls with fresh white paper covered in lilac flowers, replacing the old dirty yellow one. for some reason, the new paper annoyed him. he looked at it with dislike, feeling sorry for the change. the workmen, staying late, hurriedly rolled up their paper, ready to leave. they ignored raskolnikov; they were talking. raskolnikov folded his arms and listened.

“she comes to me in the morning,” the older one said to the younger, “all dressed up. ‘why so fancy?’ i ask. ‘im ready to do anything for you, tit vassilitch!’ she’s all dressed like a fashion book!”

“what’s a fashion book?” the younger asked, clearly seeing the other as an expert.

“it's a bunch of colored pictures. they arrive for the tailors every saturday, from abroad, showing folks how to dress, men and women. the pictures usually show the gentlemen in fur coats and the ladies in frills you can’t even imagine.”

“you can find everything in petersburg,” the younger said excitedly, “except father and mother, everything!”

“except them, there's everything here, my boy,” the elder stated firmly.

raskolnikov got up, wandered into the other room. no furniture, just memories. looked tiny now. paper on the walls still had marks from where the icons stood. he stared, then moved to the window. the older worker eyed him.

"what you want?" the worker asked.

raskolnikov didn’t answer, just went and pulled the bell. same cracked sound. rang it again, and again. each ring took him back, chills running through him, but he liked it.

"well, what do you want? who are you?" the worker called, stepping out.

raskolnikov walked back in. "want to rent a flat," he said. "just looking around."

"not the time for that, especially without the porter."

"floors clean now, gonna paint them? no blood left?"

"blood? what blood?"

"the old woman and her sister, they got killed here. this place was a pool."

"but who are you?" the worker asked, nervous.

"who am i?"

"yeah."

"want to find out? come to the station, i'll tell you."

the workers stared at him, baffled.

"time to lock up, alyoshka. we're late," said the older worker.

"sure, let's go," raskolnikov replied, casually heading downstairs. "hey, porter," he called in the gate.

a crowd stood by, staring. porters, a peasant woman, a man in a long coat. raskolnikov approached.

"what do you want?" asked a porter.

"been to the police office?"

"just came from there. what do you need?"

"is it open?"

"yeah, open."

"assistant there?"

"was for a bit. what do you want?"

raskolnikov didn't answer, just stood there, lost in thought.

"he's checked out the flat," the older worker explained.

"which flat?"

"where we're working. he says, 'why wash the blood?' like, 'there's been a murder,' and 'i'm here to rent it.' started ringing the bell, almost busted it. said, 'come to the station, i'll spill it all.' wouldn't leave."

the porter frowned, staring at raskolnikov.

"who are you?" he shouted.

"rodion romanovitch raskolnikov, ex-student. live in shil’s house, not far, flat 14. ask the porter, he knows me." raskolnikov replied, lazy-like, gazing into the dark street.

"why see the flat?"

"just looking."

"take him to the station," the man in the coat said abruptly.

raskolnikov turned, same lazy tone, "let's go."

"yeah, take him," the man urged. "what's he up to, eh?"

"not drunk, but something's off," muttered the worker.

"what do you want?" the porter yelled, getting mad. "why hang around?"

"you scared of the station?" raskolnikov taunted.

"scared? why you hanging around?"

"he's a rogue!" the peasant woman shouted.

"why talk to him?" the other porter yelled, a big guy with keys clinking. "move on! he's trouble, for sure. move on!"

Raskolnikov got tossed out into the street. He stumbled a bit, but caught himself, gave the onlookers a silent glance, and walked off.

“Strange man!” said the workman.

“Lots of odd folks around these days,” replied the woman.

“Should've taken him to the police,” added the man in the long coat.

“Best to steer clear,” the big porter decided. “A real rogue. Get involved, and you'll never shake him off... we know the type.”

Raskolnikov stood at the crossroads, thinking, “Should I go or not?” He looked around, hoping for some kind of sign. Nothing. Just silence and the cold stones underfoot. Suddenly, he saw a crowd down the street, some noise and shouts. A carriage was there. A light flickered. “What’s going on?” Raskolnikov turned right, heading toward the commotion. He felt like he was grabbing at everything, a cold smile on his face. He’d already decided to head to the police station, knowing it’d soon all be over.

A fancy carriage sat in the street with a pair of spirited gray horses. Empty. Coachman off the box, holding the reins. Crowd gathered, cops in front. One had a lantern, shining it on something by the wheels. Lots of noise. The coachman kept repeating, “What a misfortune! Good Lord, what a misfortune!”

Raskolnikov pushed through and finally saw the cause of the fuss. On the ground, a man run over, unconscious, blood everywhere. Clothes rough, but not a worker’s. Head and face a mess, clearly hurt bad.

“Merciful heaven!” the coachman wailed. “What else could I do? I wasn’t speeding, shouted warnings. Going slow like everyone else. Drunk man can’t walk straight. Saw him stagger, almost fall. Shouted again and again, held the horses back, but he fell right under. Either deliberate or too drunk. Horses young, spooked easy, screamed... made it worse. That’s how it happened!”

“That’s right,” a voice in the crowd confirmed.

“He shouted, three times,” said another.

“Three times, we all heard it,” shouted a third.

The coachman, not too worried. Carriage belonged to someone rich, important. The police didn’t want to mess up his plans. Just needed to get the injured man to the station and hospital. No one knew his name.

Raskolnikov squeezed in closer, bent over the man. The lantern lit up his face. Recognition hit him.

"i know him! i know him!" he shouted, pushing forward. "that's Marmeladov, retired clerk. lives nearby in Kozel's house... get a doctor quick! i'll cover it, see?" he flashed some cash to the cop. guy was all worked up.

police felt relieved knowing who he was. Raskolnikov gave his info, begging like he was his own dad to take Marmeladov to his place.

"just three houses away," he insisted, "Kozel's house, rich German. he was probably drunk. i know him, he's a drinker. got a family, wife, kids, one daughter... hospital's far, there's a doctor here. i'll pay, really! at home they'll help right away. he won't make it to the hospital." slipped something to the cop, no fuss. help was closer. folks pitched in to carry him.

Kozel's place was just thirty yards off. Raskolnikov trailed, holding Marmeladov's head, guiding.

"this way! head first upstairs. turn around! i'll pay, make it worth it," he mumbled.

Katerina Ivanovna was pacing as usual, arms crossed, talking to herself, coughing. lately, she talked more to Polenka, her eldest at ten. Polenka didn't get all of it but knew her mom needed her, watching with big eyes, trying to understand. this time, Polenka was helping her sick little brother to bed, waiting to wash his shirt. he sat still, serious, legs straight—like good kids do when getting ready for bed. a younger girl, in rags, waited her turn by the screen. door open, letting smoke out from other rooms, helping Katerina's coughing. she looked thinner that week, face more flushed.

you wouldn’t believe it, Polenka. man, life was good with papa. he was almost a governor! people always said, “Ivan Mihailovitch, you’re our governor!” but this drunkard dragged us down. when i was at the last ball, Princess Bezzemelny—who blessed your dad and me—saw me and said, “isn’t that the pretty girl from the shawl dance?” (you gotta fix that tear, Polenka, or it’ll just get worse.)

Prince Schegolskoy, a kammerjunker, wanted to propose after dancing the mazurka with me. but i told him my heart was taken—by your dad. papa was furious. is the water ready? hand me the shirt and socks, Lida. we’ll make do without your chemise tonight. i’ll wash them all together. where’s that vagabond? his shirt’s a rag now. (cough, cough) what’s this commotion?

“where do we put him?” the cop asked when they dragged in Marmeladov, unconscious and bloody.

“on the sofa,” Raskolnikov said, pointing.

“drunk and run over!” someone yelled.

Katerina Ivanovna went pale, gasping. the kids were scared stiff. Lida screamed and grabbed Polenka.

Raskolnikov rushed to Katerina Ivanovna. “calm down, he’ll be okay! just got hit crossing the road. i’ll pay!”

“he’s done it now!” she cried, rushing to her husband. she didn’t faint, just got to work, putting a pillow under his head, checking him over. she stayed strong, biting her lips to stop from screaming.

Raskolnikov got someone to fetch a doctor who’s nearby. “i’ve sent for a doctor,” he told her. “don’t worry, i’ll pay. got any water? a towel? he’s hurt but not dead. we’ll see what the doctor says.”

katerina ivanovna rushed to the window. there was this old chair in the corner, and on it, she had this big earthenware basin ready for the night. wash night, twice a week, sometimes more. family's down to nothing clean to wear. she couldn't stand the dirt, you know. so she'd work herself to the bone when everyone else slept, getting it all dry for morning. raskolnikov asked her for the water, she tried but nearly dropped it. he found a towel, wetted it, started cleaning marmeladov's bloody face.

she stood there, breathing hard, clutching her chest. she needed help too. raskolnikov started thinking maybe this was a bad call, bringing him here. policeman looked unsure too.

“polenka,” katerina ivanovna called, “get to sonia, quick. if she's not home, tell her her dad’s run over, she needs to come right away. now go, put on the shawl.”

“run fast!” the little boy on the chair yelled, then went quiet again, eyes wide, feet all spread out.

the room was packed now, you couldn't drop a pin. most of madame lippevechsel’s tenants squeezed in from the back rooms, first crowding the doorway, then spilling in. katerina ivanovna got furious.

“let him die in peace!” she yelled at them. “here for a show? with cigarettes! (cough, cough, cough!) might as well wear your hats... one’s got his hat on! out! respect the dead!”

her cough cut her off, but folks listened. they seemed to respect her. they shuffled back to the doorway, caught in that weird mix of horror and curiosity people get around accidents, even when they care.

outside, voices talked about the hospital, saying they shouldn't cause trouble here.

“no business to die here!” katerina shouted and headed for the door, ready to lay into them, but bumped into madame lippevechsel coming in, all in a flap about the chaos. she's a quarrelsome german.

“oh, my god!” she cried, “your husband, trampling horses! take him to the hospital! i’m the landlady!”

“amalia ludwigovna, mind your words,” katerina ivanovna said, all proud (she always put the landlady in her place, even now couldn't resist). “amalia ludwigovna...”

“i’ve told you before,” the landlady snapped, “you dare not call me amalia ludwigovna; i am amalia ivanovna.”

you’re not Amalia Ivanovna, you’re Amalia Ludwigovna. im not one of those suck-ups like Lebeziatnikov laughing behind the door. ill always call you Amalia Ludwigovna. no idea why you hate it. see Semyon Zaharovitch? he’s dying. shut that door. let him die in peace, or the Governor-General hears about this. the prince knew me as a girl and remembers Semyon. lots of friends and protectors, Semyon had. he left them because of his pride. now this generous young man (points to Raskolnikov) is helping us. he’s wealthy, connected, known Semyon since he was a kid. you can count on that, Amalia Ludwigovna...

Katerina Ivanovna spat all that out quick till a cough stopped her. Semyon groaned awake. eyes open, but he didn’t know Raskolnikov bending over him. deep painful breaths, blood at his mouth, sweat on his forehead. he looked around confused. Katerina’s face was sad but firm, tears trickling.

“My God! His chest is crushed! He’s bleeding,” she cried. “we need to get his clothes off. turn a little, Semyon, if you can.”

Marmeladov knew her. “a priest,” he rasped.

Katerina went to the window, leaned on the frame, “Oh, cursed life!”

“a priest,” he said again.

“They’re fetching him,” Katerina shouted. he quieted down, looking for her. she came back to his side. he seemed a bit easier, but not for long.

his eyes found little Lida, his favorite, shaking in the corner, staring at him.

“A-ah,” he sighed toward her. wanted to say something.

“What now?” Katerina snapped.

“Barefoot, barefoot!” he muttered, pointing at the child’s bare feet.

“Be silent,” Katerina said sharply, “you know why she’s barefoot.”

“Thank God, the doctor,” Raskolnikov said, relieved.

the doctor came in, a precise old German, looked around wary; checked the sick man, took his pulse, felt his head. with Katerina's help, unbuttoned the blood-stained shirt, bared the injured chest. gashed, crushed, ribs broken. left side, over the heart, a nasty yellow-black bruise. horse’s hoof did that. doctor frowned. policeman said he was caught in the wheel, spun for thirty yards.

“amazing he’s conscious,” the doctor whispered to Raskolnikov.

“what do you think?” Raskolnikov asked.

“he’ll die soon.”

“really no hope?”

“none. last gasp... head’s badly injured too... hm... i could bleed him if you want, but... pointless. he’ll die in five, ten minutes.”

“better bleed him then.”

“if you like... but it’s useless.”

more footsteps. crowd moved aside. priest, small grey old guy, showed up with the sacrament. cop fetched him after the accident. doctor stepped aside, exchanged a glance. Raskolnikov asked him to stay. doctor shrugged, stayed.

everyone stepped back. confession finished quick. dying man probably barely understood, just mumbled. Katerina Ivanovna grabbed little Lida, lifted the boy, knelt by the stove with the kids. girl was shaking; boy kneeled, crossed himself, forehead to the floor. seemed to enjoy it. Katerina bit her lips, fought tears, kept praying, fixed the boy’s shirt, covered the girl’s shoulders with a kerchief without stopping. door to inner rooms opened again. crowd from the flats packed the passage, but stayed at the threshold. single candle lit it all.

Polenka pushed through the door crowd, breathless from running, took off her kerchief, looked for mom, said, “She’s coming, met her outside.” mom made her kneel too.

quietly, a young girl slipped through the crowd, strange sight in a room full of need, rags, death, despair. she was in rags too, cheap stuff, flashy but cheap, obvious in purpose. Sonia stopped at the door, bewildered, forgot her gaudy dress, long train, silly crinoline, light shoes, useless parasol, silly hat with a bright feather. under the tilted hat, a pale, scared face, lips parted, eyes wide. Sonia was small, thin, eighteen, fair hair, pretty with striking blue eyes. she stared at the bed, the priest, catching her breath. whispers from the crowd reached her, she looked down, stepped into the room, stayed near the door.

service ended. Katerina Ivanovna went back to her husband. priest stepped back, tried to offer some comfort to Katerina Ivanovna.

“What am I to do with these?” she snapped, motioning to the little ones.

“God is merciful; look to the Most High for help,” priest started.

“Ach! He is merciful, but not to us.”

“That's a sin, madam,” priest said, shaking his head.

“And isn’t that a sin?” Katerina Ivanovna shot back, pointing to the dying man.

“Maybe those who caused the accident will compensate for his earnings.”

“You don’t get it!” Katerina Ivanovna waved angrily. “Why would they pay me? He was drunk, threw himself under the horses! What earnings? Brought us nothing but misery. Drank everything away, the drunkard! Robbed us for drink, wasted their lives and mine for drink! Thank God he’s dying! One less to feed!”

“You must forgive in the hour of death, that’s a sin, madam, such feelings are a great sin.”

katerina ivanovna was working hard with the dying man, getting him water, wiping blood and sweat off his face, fixing his pillow. she only turned to the priest every so often. then she snapped at him.

“ah, father! just words, all words! forgive? if the accident hadn’t happened, he’d have stumbled in today drunk, shirt filthy, passed out like a log. i’d be up all night, washing those rags and the kids', drying them by the window, then darning as soon as it’s light. that’s my night... forgiveness doesn’t change that. i’ve forgiven plenty already!”

a harsh cough stopped her. she pressed a handkerchief to her lips, showed it to the priest, while holding her chest. blood on the cloth. priest just bowed his head.

marmeladov was dying; eyes locked on katerina ivanovna. he struggled to speak, trying to ask her forgiveness, but she cut him off:

“be quiet! i know what you want!” and he fell silent, but his eyes drifted to the door and saw sonia.

he hadn’t noticed her before, standing in the shadows.

“who’s there? who’s that?” he gasped, staring in horror toward his daughter, trying to sit up.

“lie down! lie do-own!” katerina ivanovna shouted.

with a burst of strength, he propped himself on his elbow, looking wildly at his daughter, not recognizing her at first. then he did, seeing her in her humble state, dressed up, waiting to say goodbye. his face twisted in pain.

“sonia! daughter! forgive!” he cried, reaching for her, but fell off the sofa, face down. they rushed, got him back on the sofa, but he was dying. sonia ran to him, held him, and he died there.

“he’s got what he wanted,” katerina ivanovna said, looking at his body. “what now? how do i bury him? what do i feed them tomorrow?”

raskolnikov approached her.

“katerina ivanovna,” he started, “last week, your husband told me everything... he spoke of you with such respect. from that night, knowing his love for you all, we became friends... let me help now... here’s twenty roubles. if it helps, i’ll come again, tomorrow maybe... goodbye!”

he left quickly, pushing through the crowd. bumped into nikodim fomitch, who’d come to handle the accident. they hadn’t seen each other since the police station, but nikodim recognized him right away.

“ah, is that you?” he asked.

"He's dead," Raskolnikov said. "Doctor and priest did their thing. Don't stress her out, she's already sick. Try to lift her spirits... you're a good guy," he added, smiling right at him.

"But you're covered in blood," Nikodim Fomitch pointed out, spotting fresh stains in the light.

"Yeah... blood everywhere," Raskolnikov replied, in a strange way; then smiled, nodded, and headed downstairs.

He walked slow, deliberate, burning up but didn't feel it, caught up in this crazy rush of life and strength, like getting pardoned before the drop. Halfway down, the priest passed him; Raskolnikov let him go, just nodding. Almost at the bottom, he heard quick steps. Someone caught up—Polenka. She was calling after him, "Wait! wait!"

He turned. She stopped just above him. Light from the yard lit up her thin, pretty face, smiling bright. She had a message, thrilled to share it.

"What's your name?... where do you live?" she asked, out of breath.

He put his hands on her shoulders, looking at her like he couldn't help it. So much joy, no idea why.

"Who sent you?"

"Sister Sonia," she said, smiling bigger.

"Knew it was Sonia."

"Mama sent me too... when Sonia did, Mama said, 'Run fast, Polenka.'"

"You love Sonia?"

"More than anyone," Polenka said seriously, her smile less playful.

"And will you love me?"

Instead of words, she came closer, lips ready to kiss. Her thin arms hugged him tight, head on his shoulder, and she cried softly against him.

"I'm sorry for father," she said after a bit, wiping her tears. "Nothing but bad luck now," she added, trying to sound grown-up.

"Did he love you?"

"He loved Lida most," she went on serious, like an adult, "since she's little and sick. He brought her gifts. But he taught us to read, taught me grammar and scripture," she added, proud. "Mother never said anything, but we knew she liked it, and father knew too. Mother wants to teach me French now."

"Know your prayers?"

"Of course! Knew them ages ago. I say them to myself now, being big, but Kolya and Lida say them with mother. First 'Ave Maria,' then 'Lord, forgive and bless sister Sonia,' then 'Lord, forgive and bless our second father.' Our elder father's gone, but we pray for him too."

"Polenka, I'm Rodion. Pray for me too, 'And Thy servant Rodion,' that's all."

"I'll pray for you forever," she promised, smiling again, rushing to hug him tight once more.

raskolnikov gave her his name and address, promised he'd be there the next day. kid left all smiles. past ten when he hit the street again. five minutes later, he's on the bridge where the woman jumped.

“enough,” he said, solid and sure. “no more made-up fears. life's real! haven't i just lived it? my life ain't over with that old woman! heaven for her, but leave me be now! time for reason, strength... let's see what we're made of!” he said, almost daring the darkness. “and i was ready to live in a tiny space!

“i'm weak now, but... my sickness is done. knew it'd pass when i stepped out. potchinkov's place is close. gotta see razumihin even if it wasn't. let him win his bet! give him that satisfaction—whatever! strength, that's what you need, can't get anything without it—and you earn it by being strong—that's what they don't get,” he said, full of pride, shuffling away from the bridge. pride kept growing; every step, he felt more alive. what sparked this change in him? he didn't know; like grasping a straw, he felt life was still there, not gone with the old woman. maybe he rushed his thoughts, but didn't care.

“but i did ask her to remember ‘thy servant rodion’ in prayers,” he thought. “well, that was... just in case,” and chuckled at his own childishness. he was in high spirits.

found razumihin easy; new guy was known at potchinkov’s. porter showed him up. halfway there, he heard the noise and chatter of a big crowd. door wide open; voices and debate spilled out. razumihin's room was big; fifteen folks crammed in. raskolnikov paused in the entry, where two servants handled two samovars, bottles, plates, pies, and snacks from the kitchen. he sent for razumihin. razumihin came out thrilled. first look, it was clear he'd been drinking, and though he never got fully drunk, he was definitely tipsy this time.

“listen,” raskolnikov said quickly, “just came to tell you, you won your bet and that nobody knows what might happen to him. can't come in; i'm so weak i might collapse. good evening and goodbye! see me tomorrow.”

“know what? i'll take you home. if you say you're weak, you must...”

“and your guests? who's the curly-headed one who peeked out?”

“him? who knows! some friend of uncle's, i think, maybe uninvited... i'll leave uncle with them, he's great, shame i can't introduce you now. but forget them! need some air, you came just in time—two more minutes, i'd have lost it! they're talking such nonsense... you wouldn't believe it! though why not? don't we talk rubbish too? let them... that's how you learn what not to say! wait, i'll get zossimov.”

zossimov jumped at raskolnikov like he was starving, really focused on him. then he kinda lit up.

"you need to hit the bed now," he said, checking him out as best he could. "and take this for tonight. will you? i prepped it earlier... just a powder."

"two, if you want," raskolnikov goes. he took the powder right away.

"it's good you're taking him home," zossimov told razumihin. "we'll see how he is tomorrow. today, not bad—big change since earlier. live and learn..."

"know what zossimov whispered when we left?" razumihin blurts out once they're outside. "i won't spill everything, bro, but they're idiots. zossimov wants me to chat you up on the way and find out what you're thinking, then report back to him. he's got this wild idea you're... nuts or nearly there. can you believe it? first, you've got triple his smarts. second, if you're not nuts, who cares about his crazy idea? third, that surgeon's gone loopy over mental stuff, and he's basing his conclusion on your chat today with zametov."

"zametov spilled it all?"

"yeah, and good thing. now it makes sense, and zametov gets it too. see, the thing is, rody... the point is... i'm kinda tipsy now... but that's... whatever... the point is this idea... you get me? was brewing in their heads... you follow? nobody dared say it because it's nuts, especially since they nabbed that painter, that bubble's popped. but why are they such fools? i gave zametov a piece of my mind—just between us, don't let on you know; he's touchy. it was at luise ivanovna’s. but today, it's all clear. ilya petrovitch is behind it! he used your fainting at the station, but he's embarrassed about it now; i know that..."

raskolnikov listened hard. razumihin was loose-lipped from the drink.

"i fainted 'cause it was stuffy and the paint smell," raskolnikov said.

"no need to explain! it wasn't just the paint: fever's been brewing for a month; zossimov backs that up! but how down that kid is now, you wouldn't believe! 'i'm not worth his little finger,' he says. yours, he means. he's got good moments, brother. but the lesson, the lesson you gave him at palais de cristal, that was gold! scared him stiff, you did, he nearly had fits! almost had him believing all that crazy stuff again, then you just—stuck your tongue out at him: 'what do you make of that?' it was brilliant! he's wrecked, totally crushed now! masterful, really, what they had coming! wish i'd seen it! he was dying to see you again. porfiry too wants to meet you..."

"ah!... him too... but why'd they label me mad?"

"oh, not mad. i must've said too much, brother... what got him was it was the only thing that caught your interest; now it's clear why. knowing everything... and how it ticked you off and tied into your sickness... i'm tipsy, brother, but damn, he's got his own theory... i'm telling you, he's obsessed with mental stuff. but just ignore him..."

they both went quiet for a bit.

razumihin, i was just at a deathbed. a clerk died... gave them all my money. then someone kissed me, and if i had killed someone, they’d still... no, it’s nonsense. i’m weak. need support. stairs are right there...

“what's wrong?” asked razumihin, worried.

“bit dizzy, but it’s not that. just sad, like real sad... like a woman. hey, look!”

“what?”

“don’t you see it? light in my room, right there, through the crack...”

at the last flight, near the landlady’s door, they both saw the light in raskolnikov’s room.

“weird. maybe nastasya,” said razumihin.

“she’s never in my room now. should be asleep. but... doesn’t matter. goodbye!”

“what are you saying? i’m coming with you!”

“i know, but let’s shake hands here. goodbye!”

“what’s up with you, rodya?”

“nothing... let’s go... you’ll see.”

as they climbed, razumihin thought maybe zossimov was right. “ah, my chatter upset him!” he muttered.

at the door, they heard voices inside.

“what’s up?” shouted razumihin. raskolnikov opened the door wide and froze.

his mom and sister on the sofa, waiting over an hour. why hadn’t he thought of them, even after knowing they were on their way? they’d been grilling nastasya for answers, worried over his “running away” today, sick and delirious. “what’s happened to him?” both had cried, both panicked for that hour and a half.

raskolnikov’s entrance brought joy. they rushed him, but he stood stunned. couldn’t even lift his arms. they hugged, kissed him. he took a step, then fainted.

panic, cries, moans... razumihin rushed in, grabbed him, put him on the sofa.

“it’s nothing, nothing!” he reassured them—“just a faint, no big deal! the doctor said he’s better! water! see, he’s coming round, he’s fine!”

and pulling dounia close, he showed her, “look, he’s fine.” the mother and sister looked at razumihin with gratitude. they’d heard from nastasya all he’d done for rodya during the illness. pulcheria called him “very competent,” talking with dounia that night.

raskolnikov got up, sank onto the sofa. waved razumihin off to stop the babble. grabbed his mom's and sister’s hands, looked at them in silence. his mom freaked out a bit — saw something wild and deep in his eyes. she started crying. 

avdotya's face went pale, hand shook in his. 

"go home... with him," raskolnikov croaked, nodding at razumihin. "bye till tomorrow; tomorrow everything... when'd you get here?"

"tonight, rody," pulcheria said, "train was late. but, rody, i can't leave you. i'll stay right here..."

"don’t torture me!" he snapped.

"i'll stay," razumihin said, "won’t leave him. visitors can wait. my uncle's handling it."

"thank you!" pulcheria began, but raskolnikov cut her off.

"i can’t have it! enough, go away... i can’t stand it!"

"come, mama, let's step out," dounia whispered, "we're stressing him out."

"can’t i see him after three years?" pulcheria cried.

"hold on," he stopped them, "you keep breaking my thoughts... seen luzhin?"

"no, rody, but he knows we’re here. heard he visited you today," she said, cautious.

"yeah... he did... dounia, i told luzhin i'd throw him out, told him off..."

"rody, you can’t mean that..." pulcheria started, but stopped, looking at dounia.

dounia watched him closely, waiting. they’d heard bits from nastasya, unsure what really happened.

"dounia," raskolnikov struggled, "i don’t want that marriage. refuse luzhin tomorrow, cut him off."

"good heavens!" pulcheria gasped.

"brother, think!" dounia started, then softened. "you're not well to talk now, maybe; you're tired."

"you think i’m delirious? no... you're marrying luzhin for me. but i won’t take that. write him off by morning... let me see it, end it!"

"i can’t!" dounia snapped, "what right have you..."

"dounia, you're too quick, calm down, tomorrow..." their mom jumped in, worried. "let’s go!"

"he's raving," razumihin slurred, "or he wouldn’t dare! tomorrow it's over... today he did send luzhin off. that's true. luzhin got mad... made speeches, left defeated..."

"so it’s true?" pulcheria cried.

"goodbye till tomorrow, brother," dounia said kindly, "let’s go, mother... bye, rody."

"hear me, sister," he repeated, struggling, "i’m not mad; this marriage's a disgrace. let me be the bad one, but not you... it’s me or luzhin! go now..."

"you’re mad! tyrant!" razumihin shouted; but raskolnikov didn’t, maybe couldn’t, reply. he turned to the wall, worn out. dounia glanced at razumihin; her eyes fierce. he jolted at her look.

pulcheria stood, overwhelmed.

"im not going," she whispered to Razumihin, sounding defeated. "im staying nearby... take Dounia home."

"youll mess everything up," Razumihin shot back, whispering too, losing his cool. "come out to the stairs. Nastasya, get a light! look," he kept whispering on the stairs, "he nearly beat the doctor and me earlier! you get it? even the doctor backed off to not rile him up. i stayed on watch, but he got dressed and slipped out. he'll do it again if you push him now, and he'll hurt himself..."

"what do you mean?"

"and Avdotya Romanovna cant stay there without you. think about where youre lodging! that fool Pyotr Petrovitch couldnt find worse... but ive had a bit to drink, so excuse my language..."

"im going to talk to the landlady here," Pulcheria Alexandrovna insisted, "ill beg for a spot for Dounia and me tonight. i cant leave him like this, i just cant!"

this all happened right outside the landlady's door. Nastasya held a light from a step down. Razumihin was buzzing with energy. half an hour ago, bringing Raskolnikov home, he'd talked too much, knew it, but his head was clear despite all the drinks. now he was almost ecstatic, everything he'd drunk hitting him harder. he stood gripping both women's hands, convincing them with blunt honesty, squeezing like a vise to make his point. he stared at Avdotya Romanovna, ignoring manners. they tried pulling their hands free, but he'd just pull them back. if they'd told him to dive headfirst off the stairs, he'd have done it without thinking. Pulcheria Alexandrovna found him odd and his grip too tight, but with her worry for Rodya, she saw him as a godsend, ignoring his eccentricities. Avdotya Romanovna, though anxious but brave, couldn't look at his blazing eyes without some alarm. only Nastasya's words about her brother's strange friend kept her from fleeing and taking her mother with her. she knew running might not be an option now. ten minutes later, she was calmer; Razumihin's nature showed quickly, no matter his mood, so people soon knew what kind of guy he was.

you cant go to the landlady, thats nonsense! if you stay, even as his mother, you'll push him to a frenzy. listen, here's what i'll do: Nastasya will stay with him now, and i'll take you both home. petersburg's not safe alone. then i'll be back in 15 mins with news. i'll grab Zossimov, the doc who's watching him—he's sober, trust me. you’ll get two reports in an hour—one from the doctor. if things go sideways, i'll bring you back. all good, you sleep. i'll crash here in the hallway, quiet. Zossimov can stay at the landlady's, closer that way. doctor beats mother, right? so come on! landlady's not an option for you, trust me. she's... complicated. jealous type. but let's move. do you trust me?

“let's go, mother,” Avdotya Romanovna said, “he’ll do what he promised. he saved Rodya, and if the doc stays here, what’s better?”

"you get me, because you're an angel!" Razumihin said with excitement, “let’s go! Nastasya! quick, sit with him, i'll be back in 15.”

though Pulcheria Alexandrovna wasn’t fully convinced, she didn’t argue. Razumihin helped them down the stairs. she wondered, was he reliable? he seemed a bit off...

“oh, you think i’m off!” Razumihin interrupted her thoughts, walking fast enough they struggled to keep up. “nonsense! i'm drunk on seeing you, not wine. i’ll dump water on my head once you're home. if you knew how much i care for you both! don’t laugh, don’t get mad. i’m his friend, so yours too... last year i felt this moment coming, or maybe not. feels like you dropped from the sky. i won’t sleep all night... Zossimov worried he might snap... that’s why no stress.”

“what?” the mother shouted.

“did the doctor really say that?” Avdotya Romanovna asked, worried.

yeah, not like that at all. he gave him some powder—saw it myself—then you showed up. would've been better tomorrow. good thing we left. Zossimov will fill you in later. he's not drunk, and i won't be either. damn argument got me tight. swore off arguing, but they always talk trash. almost threw punches. left my uncle to handle it. believe this—complete absence of individualism, and they love it. wanna be as un-like themselves as possible. they think that's progress. if only their nonsense was original...

"listen!" Pulcheria Alexandrovna cut in, but just fired Razumihin up more.

"you think i'm mad at them for talking nonsense? not at all!" Razumihin yelled. "talking nonsense is our privilege. through error, you find truth! i'm human because i mess up! you won't find truth without a hundred mistakes. fine and all, but we can't even make mistakes on our own. talk your own nonsense, and i'll respect it. better to be wrong your way than right someone else's. in one case, you're a human; in the other, a bird. truth won't slip away, but life can get cramped. look at us now, science, thought, invention—still in school. living on borrowed ideas, it's habit! am i right?" Razumihin grabbed their hands.

"oh, mercy, i don't know," Pulcheria Alexandrovna cried.

"yes, yes... though i don't fully agree," Avdotya Romanovna added, then yelped—hand squeezed too hard.

"yes, you say yes... well then you..." he burst out, "you're a fount of goodness, sense... perfection. give me your hand... yours too! gotta kiss them, right here, on my knees..." and down he went on the deserted pavement.

"stop, please, what are you doing?" Pulcheria Alexandrovna was distressed.

"get up, get up!" Dounia laughed, though worried too.

"not till i kiss your hands! there! done! up we go! i'm a fool, unworthy, drunk... ashamed... not worthy to love, but honoring you is a duty. and i've done it... and here's your place. Rodya was right kicking Pyotr Petrovitch out... how dare he put you here! scandalous! know what kinda folks rent here? and you're his betrothed? yes? then i'll say it, your fiancé's a scoundrel."

"excuse me, Mr. Razumihin, you're forgetting..." Pulcheria Alexandrovna started.

"yeah, you're right, i lost myself there, sorry," Razumihin said quick. "but you can't be mad at me for how i spoke. i'm real about it, not just... well, anyway, i won’t say why. but we all saw it today, that man ain't one of us. not cause his hair was done up or cause he was quick to show off, but cause he's a spy, a speculator, a cheap clown. obvious. you think he's smart? nah, he's a fool. and is he a match for you? no way! see, ladies?" he paused on the stairs, "even if my friends are drunk, they're honest. we might talk nonsense, but we'll find the truth. we're on the right track, but Pyotr Petrovitch... he's not. i called them all names, but i respect them... not Zametov, but i like him cause he's a pup, and Zossimov cause he knows his stuff. enough said, let's move on. i know this hall, been here before, scandal at Number 3... where are you? number eight? lock up for the night. don't let anyone in. i'll be back with news in fifteen, and in half an hour, Zossimov. see you! gotta run."

"good heavens, Dounia, what's happening?" Pulcheria Alexandrovna asked her daughter, anxious.

"don't worry, mother," Dounia said, taking off her hat and cape. "God sent him to help, even if he's from a drinking party. we can count on him, i promise. he's done so much for Rodya..."

"ah, Dounia, who knows if he'll come! how could i leave Rodya?... and i imagined our meeting so different! he seemed so sullen, like he didn't want to see us..."

tears filled her eyes.

"no, it’s not that, mother. you didn’t see, you were crying. he’s unhinged by illness—that’s why."

"ah, that illness! what's gonna happen? and how he talked to you, Dounia!" the mother said, looking at her daughter, half-consoled by Dounia defending her brother. "i’m sure he’ll rethink tomorrow," she added, probing.

"and i’m sure he’ll say the same tomorrow... about that," Avdotya Romanovna said finally. Pulcheria Alexandrovna didn’t want to push further. Dounia kissed her mother. she embraced her warmly, then sat down to wait for Razumihin, watching her daughter pace the room, lost in thought. it was Avdotya Romanovna’s habit to walk when thinking, and her mother never interrupted these moments.

razumihin was kinda funny, all smitten with avdotya romanovna right off the bat. but hey, even sober folks would've got it if they saw her then. she had this way about her—tall, strong, carried herself with grace and confidence. looked like her brother but way prettier. dark brown hair, nearly black eyes with this proud light in them, but also kind. pale but healthy, face fresh and full of energy. small mouth, full lower lip gave her this unique, almost haughty look. not always smiling, but when she did, man, it fit her just right. razumihin, big-hearted and a bit tipsy, didn’t stand a chance. he saw her all lit up with love for her brother. then he saw her lip tremble when her brother was a jerk. that was it for him.

and he wasn't lying when he joked about praskovya pavlovna, the landlady, being jealous of both pulcheria alexandrovna and avdotya. pulcheria, at forty-three, still had that beauty you get from staying true and warm-hearted. grey hair, some wrinkles, but still handsome. like dounia, twenty years older, minus the lip. emotional but not over the top, easygoing till she hit her wall of principles. wouldn’t budge past that.

twenty minutes after razumihin left, he knocked again. "cant come in, no time," he said quick when they opened up. "he’s sleeping deep, thank god. nastasya's with him, told her to stay put. off to get zossimov. he'll fill you in, then you should get some rest. youre wiped."

and off he went down the corridor.

"what a capable and devoted young man!" pulcheria alexandrovna said, real pleased.

"he seems like a great guy!" avdotya replied warmly, pacing the room again.

about an hour later, footsteps in the hall and another knock. both women waited, counting on Razumihin’s promise. he came through, dragging Zossimov along. Zossimov ditched his drinking buddies for this but wasn’t too thrilled, skeptical about Razumihin’s state. but when he saw they were serious about needing his opinion, his ego perked up. he spent ten minutes there, comforting Pulcheria Alexandrovna with that serious young doctor vibe, treating the visit like a big consultation. he avoided chit-chat and personal stuff, barely acknowledging Avdotya Romanovna's beauty, focusing solely on Pulcheria Alexandrovna. he left feeling pretty good about himself. 

he told them the patient was doing okay, issue stemming from poor conditions and some moral stress—“a mix of material and moral stuff, worries, fears, ideas...” when Pulcheria Alexandrovna nervously asked about insanity, he assured her with a smile it was exaggerated, mentioning a fixed idea or slight monomania. he was studying this field and thought family presence would help, “if no new shocks happen.” he left with a polite bow, receiving thanks and Avdotya Romanovna’s handshake, quite pleased with the whole deal.

“we’ll talk tomorrow; get to bed now!” Razumihin said, trailing Zossimov. “i’ll report first thing.”

“Avdotya Romanovna’s a looker,” Zossimov remarked, almost drooling as they hit the street.

“a looker? you said looker?” Razumihin shouted, grabbing Zossimov by the throat. “if you ever dare... understand?” he yelled, shoving him against the wall.

“let go, you drunk,” Zossimov said, struggling, then laughed after being released. Razumihin stood there, brooding.

“yeah, i’m an ass,” he said, dark as a thunderhead, “but so are you.”

“no, not like that. no foolishness here.”

they walked quietly till near Raskolnikov’s place, Razumihin breaking the silence, clearly worried.

listen, you’re a good guy, but honestly, you’re kind of a mess. loose fish, yeah, and you let yourself go. you’re soft and lazy, can’t say no to anything, and that’s a problem. got no clue how you’re still a solid doc. you sleep on feathers and still get up for patients? another few years, you won’t. but whatever, that’s not the point. you’re crashing at the landlady’s flat tonight. took some convincing, but it’s happening. get to know her better. not what you think, man, she’s just... melting. help me out, she’s quite the lady. i’ll owe you big time.

zossimov cracked up hard. 

“so you're into her. what am i supposed to do?”

“just hang with her, talk nonsense, cure her of something. she’s got a piano, i strum a bit. russian song got her hooked. now you’re the performer. won’t regret it.”

“did you promise her anything? marriage?”

“nah, nothing like that. she’s not like that. tchebarov tried.”

“then let her go.”

“can’t do that, man. there’s something there.”

“why’d you get her interested?”

“didn’t mean to, maybe i got caught myself. she doesn’t care who’s there, long as someone is. can’t explain it. teach her calculus if you want, she’ll just sigh away. talked about the Prussian House of Lords for days, she just sighed. no love talk, she’s too shy. but make it like you can’t leave. it’s cozy, you can do whatever. maybe sneak a kiss, if you’re smart.”

“but why would i want any of this?”

man, i cant make you understand! yall are made for each other, trust me. ive thought of you often... you’ll get there eventually! does it matter if its sooner or later? its like finding where you belong, a place to rest, like you're dead but still alive. all the comforts at once. anyway, im rambling. i'll check on him tonight, but dont stress. maybe peek in once, but if you see anything off, delirium or fever, wake me up. but honestly, it should be fine.

CHAPTER II

razumihin woke up at eight, feeling troubled. lots of unexpected problems on his mind. never thought he'd wake up like this. remembered every detail from yesterday, a new feeling hit him, something he never felt before. but he knew it was just a dream, unattainable, even felt ashamed thinking about it. moved on to practical worries from that cursed yesterday.
