worst part? how he acted "base and mean," not just drunk, but using a girl's situation to trash her fiancé in stupid jealousy, knowing nothing about them. who was he to judge? could avdotya romanovna really be marrying a bad guy for money? no way. there must be more to him. and the lodgings? how did he know what they were like? furnishing a flat... what a mess! blaming it on being drunk was pathetic. the truth came out, all his jealousy and envy. did he, razumihin, even deserve to dream about her? he was just a loud, drunk fool last night. imagining anything else was absurd. he cringed remembering he joked about the landlady being jealous of avdotya romanovna... unbearable. slammed his fist on the stove, hurt his hand, sent a brick flying.

“of course,” he muttered, feeling ashamed, “these wrongs cant be undone... no point thinking about it. gotta go to them in silence, do my duty... silent... no asking forgiveness, no words... all is lost now!”

and here he was, fussing over his clothes like never before. didn’t have another suit, but even if he did, might not have worn it. “i’d make a point of not wearing it.” couldn't be a slob when folks needed his help and asked to see him. so he brushed up. linen was always clean—proud of that.

washed up real good that morning—thanks to some soap from nastasya. scrubbed his hair, neck, and hands. shaving? nah. praskovya pavlovna had razors from her late husband, but no way. let the stubble stay. “what if they think i shaved on purpose to...?” they'd definitely think that. “not happening!”

he was rough around the edges, like a barkeep, but even if he had a bit of a gentleman in him, who cares? everyone should be a gentleman, and then some. but he’d done things... not exactly dishonest, yet... and those thoughts. to be compared to avdotya romanovna? damn it! fine, he’d just be coarse and not give a damn. could be worse if he tried.

caught in his own head when zossimov showed up. spent the night at praskovya pavlovna’s. wanted to check on the patient before heading out. razumihin said raskolnikov was out cold. zossimov said not to wake him, would be back by eleven.

“if he’s home,” he added. “damn it, if you can't control your patients, how do you treat them? you know if he’s going to them, or they’re coming here?”

“they’re coming, i think,” razumihin said, catching the drift. “family talk, i guess. i’ll head out. you’re the doc, you got the right to be here.”

“but i’m not a confessor; i’ll pop in and leave. got other things to handle.”

“one thing’s bugging me,” razumihin jumped in, frowning. “on the way home, i babbled a bunch of drunk nonsense... all kinds of stuff... told him you were worried he might go insane.”

“told the ladies that too.”

“i know it was dumb! you can smack me for it! did you really think that?”

that's wild, honestly. how could i take it seriously? you yourself called him a monomaniac when you brought me to him. and yesterday, we just went and stirred the pot with that story about the painter. he might've been on edge already. if i'd known what went down at the police station—someone accused him like that—i wouldn't have let that conversation happen. these monomaniacs, they blow things up and believe their own fantasies. zametov's story kind of cleared things up for me. reminds me of this hypochondriac case, guy was 40, cut a kid's throat cause he couldn't stand the jokes at dinner. and here we got his ragged situation, a rude cop, the fever, and this suspicion—all hitting a guy who's already dealing with hypochondria and vanity. could've been what set him off. anyway, zametov's a nice guy but talks too much, shouldn't have spilled everything last night.

"who'd he tell?"

"you, me, porfiry."

"so what?"

"hey, can you talk to his mom and sister? they need to handle him carefully today."

"they'll be fine," razumihin grumbled.

"why's he so against luzhin? guy's got money, she doesn't seem to mind him, and they're broke, right?"

"what's it to you?" razumihin snapped. "how should i know if they're broke? ask them yourself."

"man, you're a real piece of work sometimes. still hungover from last night, huh? later. tell praskovya pavlovna thanks for the stay. she locked her door, didn't answer when i said morning through it. up at seven, got the samovar in. never got to see her."

razumihin hit bakaleyev's at nine sharp. both women were waiting, edgy. they'd been up early. he walked in, all dark clouds, bowed awkwardly, mad at himself for it. didn't expect pulcheria alexandrovna to rush him, grab his hands, almost kiss them. shot a nervous look at avdotya romanovna, expecting sneers, but she was all gratitude and respect. threw him off more than if she'd yelled. lucky there was stuff to talk about, he jumped on it quick.

pulcheria alexandrovna was glad to hear rodya was fine, said they needed to talk about something important. asked about breakfast, invited him to join—they'd waited for him. avdotya romanovna rang for tea. a dirty waiter brought it, messy service, embarrassed the ladies. razumihin started criticizing the place, remembered luzhin, got embarrassed, but pulcheria alexandrovna's questions poured in, saved him.

he talked for like 45 minutes, kept getting cut off by their questions, but he managed to lay out the big stuff from the last year of Raskolnikov's life, ending with details on his illness. left out some bits though, like the police station scene. they were all ears but when he wrapped up, they were like, “that’s it?”

“tell me, tell me! what do you think...? excuse me, your name?” Pulcheria Alexandrovna jumped in.

“Dmitri Prokofitch.”

“i really want to know, Dmitri Prokofitch... what’s his take on things now? what’s he into? is he always this touchy? what are his hopes, his dreams? what's influencing him?”

“mom, how can he answer all that?” Dounia cut in.

“didn’t expect him to be like this, Dmitri Prokofitch!”

“understandable,” Razumihin replied. “i’ve known Rodion for a year and a half; he’s moody, proud, suspicious, but he’s got a good heart. hates showing feelings, but sometimes he’s just cold. says he’s busy but does nothing. doesn’t mock things, just doesn’t bother. thinks highly of himself, maybe he’s right. your visit might help.”

“i hope so,” Pulcheria Alexandrovna said, worried.

Razumihin finally looked at Avdotya Romanovna. she listened, then paced with folded arms, firing off questions without stopping. he noticed their poverty. if she dressed like a queen, he wouldn’t be scared, but seeing their struggles made him nervous.

“you’ve told us a lot about my brother... and you’ve been fair. i thought you'd be too devoted to him,” Avdotya Romanovna smiled. “you’re right, he needs a woman’s care.”

“i didn’t say that, but...”

“what?”

“he loves no one, maybe never will,” Razumihin said flatly.

“you think he can’t love?”

"you know, Avdotya Romanovna, you're a lot like your brother," he just blurted it out, caught off guard by his own words. remembered what he'd said about her brother, face went red like a crab. she couldn't help but laugh at his reaction.

"maybe we're wrong about Rodya," Pulcheria Alexandrovna said, a bit annoyed. "i'm not talking about our current mess, Dounia. Pyotr Petrovitch's letter and what we've guessed could be off, but Dmitri Prokofitch, you wouldn't believe how unpredictable he is. never knew what he'd do even at fifteen. he might do something now no one would expect... like that time a year and a half ago, when he almost married that girl — the landlady's daughter, remember?"

"did you hear about that?" Avdotya asked.

"do you really think," Pulcheria Alexandrovna continued, "that my tears, my pleas, my illness, the thought of me dying from grief, our poverty would've stopped him? no, he'd have ignored all that. and it's not like he doesn't love us!"

"he's never mentioned it to me," Razumihin replied carefully. "but i did hear something from Praskovya Pavlovna, though she's not a gossip. what i heard was odd."

"what did you hear?" both ladies asked.

"nothing much. just that the marriage, which didn't happen 'cause the girl died, wasn't to Praskovya Pavlovna's liking. heard the girl wasn't pretty, actually ugly... and sickly... and strange. but she must've had good qualities. otherwise, it's hard to explain. she had no money, and he wasn't after that. judging these things is always tricky."

"i'm sure she was a good girl," Avdotya said simply.

"God forgive me, but i was relieved at her death. though i can't tell who would've made who more miserable," Pulcheria Alexandrovna admitted. then she started asking about what happened with Luzhin the day before, hesitating and looking at Dounia, which seemed to annoy her. this seemed to worry her more than anything else. Razumihin went over it again, adding his thoughts: he blamed Raskolnikov for insulting Pyotr Petrovitch on purpose, not blaming his illness.

"he planned it before he got sick," he added.

"i think so too," Pulcheria Alexandrovna said, looking down. she was surprised Razumihin talked so carefully about Pyotr Petrovitch. Avdotya noticed it too.

"so that's how you see Pyotr Petrovitch?" Pulcheria Alexandrovna couldn't help but ask.

"i can't have any other view of your daughter's future husband," Razumihin said, firmly. "not just out of politeness, but because Avdotya Romanovna chose him herself. i was rude last night 'cause i was drunk and... lost it completely. i feel ashamed this morning."

he stopped talking, red-faced. Avdotya Romanovna blushed too but didn't say anything. without her daughter's backing, Pulcheria Alexandrovna seemed lost. eventually, she admitted, looking at her daughter, that one thing was really worrying her.

“listen, Dmitri Prokofitch,” she started. “can i be straight with you?”

“sure, mother,” said Dounia, firm.

“here's the deal,” she rushed, like spilling it lifted a load. “early this morning, got a note from Pyotr Petrovitch. he was supposed to meet us at the station, right? instead, he sent a servant with our lodging address and said he'd come this morning. but then this note came. you should read it; there's something in there really bothering me... you'll see what i mean, and... i need your honest take, Dmitri Prokofitch! you know Rodya better than anyone, and no one gives advice like you. Dounia made up her mind right away, but i'm still unsure what to do and... i've been waiting to hear from you.”

Razumihin opened the note from the night before and read:

“Dear Madam, Pulcheria Alexandrovna, unforeseen issues kept me from meeting you at the station; I sent someone capable in my place. I can't see you tomorrow morning due to urgent Senate business and to give you space with your son and Avdotya with her brother. I will visit tomorrow evening at eight sharp. I must ask that Rodion Romanovitch not be present at our meeting, as he insulted me during my visit yesterday. I also need a detailed explanation from you on a certain matter. If I encounter Rodion Romanovitch despite my request, I’ll have to leave immediately, and that’ll be on you. I understand he recovered quickly after my visit and might come to you. I saw him at the home of a drunken man, now deceased, who received twenty-five roubles from your son for the funeral, which puzzled me knowing how hard you worked to gather that amount. My respects to your esteemed daughter, Avdotya Romanovna. Sincerely,

“P. LUZHIN.”

“what do i do now, Dmitri Prokofitch?” Pulcheria Alexandrovna nearly cried. “how can i tell Rodya not to come? he was so firm about refusing Pyotr Petrovitch yesterday, and now we're told not to see Rodya! he’ll come just because of that, and... what then?”

“follow Avdotya Romanovna’s decision,” Razumihin said calmly.

oh man, she says... who knows what. no clear reason. just that Rodya has to be here at eight. they need to meet. i didn't even want to show him the letter, trying to keep him from going with your help... he's so touchy. and i don't get the bit about the drunk who died and the daughter, or how he gave her all that money...

"money you sacrificed for, mother," Avdotya Romanovna chimed in.

"he wasn't himself yesterday," Razumihin thought, "you should've seen him at the restaurant. some sense in it though... muttered something on the way home about a dead guy and a girl, but i didn't catch it... last night, i..."

"best if we go ourselves, mother. we'll figure it out then. it's late already—past ten," she checked a fancy gold watch on a chain, clashing with her outfit. "fiancé's gift," Razumihin guessed.

"we need to go, Dounia," her mother urged, flustered. "he'll think we're still upset from yesterday, being late. heavens!"

as she spoke, she hurried into her hat and mantle. Dounia followed, her gloves worn, with holes, yet they carried a dignity that comes with knowing how to wear poverty. Razumihin felt proud to escort her. "like a queen mending stockings in prison," he thought.

"God!" Pulcheria Alexandrovna burst out, "never thought i'd fear seeing my Rodya! i'm scared, Dmitri Prokofitch," she glanced at him nervously.

"don't worry, mother," Dounia reassured, kissing her. "have faith in him."

"oh, i do," she sighed, "but i haven't slept."

they stepped outside.

"Dounia, i dreamed of Marfa Petrovna this morning... she was in white... took my hand, shook her head, sternly, like she blamed me... is it a sign? you don't know, Dmitri Prokofitch, Marfa Petrovna's dead!"

"no, didn't know. who's Marfa Petrovna?"

"she died suddenly; and can you believe..."

"later, mom," Dounia interjected. "he doesn't know who she is."

"ah, you don't? thought you knew us well. forgive me, Dmitri Prokofitch, my mind's a mess lately. see you as providence, like family... don't be mad. oh, what's with your hand? hurt it?"

"yeah, bruised it," Razumihin mumbled, happy.

"i speak too much from the heart, Dounia scolds me... but man, what a tiny place he lives in! i wonder if he's up? does the landlady think this is a room? you said he hides his feelings, might i bother him with my... emotions? what should i do, Dmitri Prokofitch? i'm all over the place."

dont push him on anything if you see him frown. dont ask about his health; hates that.

“Dmitri, being a mom is tough! Look at these stairs... terrible!” 

“Mom, you look pale, don’t stress, love,” Dounia said, then with a glare: “he should be happy to see you, you’re just overthinking it.”

“Hold on, I’ll check if he’s awake.”

They followed Razumihin up, and when they hit the landlady’s door on the fourth floor, they caught it cracked open and two sharp eyes peeking out. As their eyes met, the door slammed shut so hard Pulcheria Alexandrovna nearly screamed.

CHAPTER III

“He’s good, really good!” Zossimov cheered as they came in.

He’d arrived ten minutes earlier, sitting on the sofa. Raskolnikov was opposite, tidy and dressed, unlike before. The room was packed now, but Nastasya squeezed in to listen.

Raskolnikov was better than yesterday, but still pale, tired, dark. Like a guy who’s been through the wringer. Brows tight, lips set, eyes feverish. Talked little, like it was a chore, moved uneasy.

Looked like he needed a sling or bandage to complete the picture. When his mom and sister came in, his face lit up briefly, but it showed more pain than before. That light faded, pain stayed, and Zossimov, young doctor keen on his work, saw no joy at family, just grit to endure more hours of this. Every word seemed to hit a nerve. Still, he was impressed by Raskolnikov’s control. Yesterday, a word would set him off.

“Yes, I see I’m almost well now,” Raskolnikov said, kissing his mom and sister, making Pulcheria Alexandrovna glow. “And I don’t say this like I did yesterday,” he told Razumihin, gripping his hand friendly.

“Yes, he’s surprising today,” Zossimov started, glad the ladies were there, as he couldn’t keep Raskolnikov talking for long. “In a few days, if he keeps this up, he’ll be back to how he was, month or two back... maybe even three. This has been building, right? Admit maybe it’s partly your fault?” he said with a careful smile, still cautious.

“Very possible,” Raskolnikov replied, cold.

zossimov jumped in, "look, your recovery's on you. now that we can chat, avoid the stuff making you sick. know yourself, right? probably started when you left school. keep busy, set goals. that'll help."

"yeah, yeah, you're right... i'll get back to school, then it's all good..."

zossimov, trying to impress the ladies, saw Raskolnikov's smirk. that threw him for a sec. Pulcheria thanked zossimov for stopping by last night.

"what? he saw you last night?" Raskolnikov asked, surprised. "you haven't slept after your trip?"

"oh, Rodya, just till two. Dounia and i never sleep before two at home."

"dont know how to thank him either," Raskolnikov frowned, looking down. "forget payment—sorry for bringing it up (he turned to zossimov)—i dont get why you'd give me special attention! it’s heavy on me not understanding. just being honest."

"don't be upset," zossimov chuckled. "think of it as you're my first patient—we newbies love our first ones, like kids. and my patient list isn't long."

"not talking about him," Raskolnikov pointed at Razumihin, "though all he's gotten from me is trouble."

"what rubbish! you're sentimental today, huh?" Razumihin shouted.

if he saw deeper, he'd have noticed no sentimentality, something else entirely. Avdotya Romanovna noticed, watching her brother closely.

"as for you, mom, i barely dare to speak," he continued, like reciting lines. "only now i get how worried you must've been yesterday, waiting for me."

he reached out to his sister, smiling silently. a genuine flash of feeling. Dounia felt it, squeezed his hand, happy. first words to her since yesterday's fight. mom's face lit up with joy at this silent truce. "yes, that's why i love him," Razumihin muttered, shifting in his chair. "he's got these moments."

"and he does it so well," mom thought. "such generous impulses, ended the fight with a hand and a look... what fine eyes, and such a fine face!... even better looking than Dounia... but, oh, that suit—he's dressed so poorly!... Vasya, the messenger boy, dresses better! i could hug him... cry over him—but i'm scared... oh, why am i scared?..."

oh, Rodya, you wouldn't believe how bad yesterday got for Dounia and me. now things are settled and we're happy again, i can tell you. we rushed over right from the train to see you and bam—Nastasya tells us you were burning up with fever, ran off from the doc in a fit, and folks were out looking for you in the streets. it was a panic. couldn't shake the thought of Lieutenant Potanchikov—you don't remember him—running out like that and ending up in a well. we freaked out, almost went to Pyotr Petrovitch for help... because we were alone, just us, she said, then stopped, remembering maybe we shouldn't bring him up, though we're happy again now.

“yeah, yeah, sure... that’s annoying,” Raskolnikov mumbled, not really there, and Dounia just stared at him all confused.

“what was i gonna say?” he tried to recall. “oh, right; mom, Dounia, don’t think i didn’t plan to visit today—wasn’t waiting for you to come first.”

“what are you saying, Rodya?” Pulcheria Alexandrovna was surprised.

“is he just going through the motions?” Dounia thought. “is he trying to patch things up like it’s some ritual?”

“i just woke up, meant to come to you, but got held up—my clothes, you see; forgot to ask Nastasya to wash out the blood yesterday... just got dressed.”

“blood? what blood?” Pulcheria Alexandrovna asked, worried.

“oh, nothing—don’t worry. wandered off yesterday, a bit out of it, bumped into a guy who’d been run over... a clerk...”

“delirious? but you remember everything!” Razumihin cut in.

“that’s true,” Raskolnikov replied carefully. “i remember every detail, but why i did it, went there, said that... can’t say.”

“common thing,” Zossimov added, “actions done smartly, but the why's all mixed up, like dreaming.”

“maybe it’s good he thinks i’m nearly mad,” Raskolnikov thought.

“even healthy folks act like this,” Dounia said, eyeing Zossimov.

“there’s some truth,” Zossimov replied. “we all act mad sometimes, difference is the truly mad are just a bit more so. a normal man? doesn't really exist. among thousands, maybe you find one.”

at “madman,” everyone frowned. Raskolnikov, not really listening, was deep in thought, a strange smile on his pale face.

“anyway, what about the guy who got run over?” Razumihin asked eagerly.

"what?" raskolnikov snapped out of it. "oh... got blood on me moving him to his place. mom, i messed up yesterday. lost it. gave all the money you sent to his wife for the funeral. she's a widow now, sick, poor... three kids, starving... nothing at home... a daughter too... maybe you'd have done the same seeing them. but i shouldn't have, knowing you needed it too. helping others needs the right, or just let them be." he laughed, "right, dounia?"

"no, it's not," dounia shot back, firm.

"bah! you got ideals too," he muttered, looking at her almost angry, smiling sarcastically. "should've thought of that... praiseworthy for you... but if you draw a line and stick to it, you'll be unhappy... cross it, maybe worse.... but that's nonsense," he added, annoyed. "just meant to say sorry, mom," he ended, short and quick.

“that’s enough, rodya, everything you do is good,” his mom said, happy.

“don’t count on it,” he replied, twisting a smile.

silence. awkwardness in the talk, in the quiet, in the peace, in the forgiveness, and everyone felt it.

“it's like they're scared of me,” raskolnikov thought, glancing at his mom and sister. pulcheria alexandrovna was getting more nervous the longer the silence stretched.

“yet when they're not here, i think i love them so much,” flashed through his mind.

“you know, rodya, marfa petrovna died,” pulcheria alexandrovna suddenly blurted out.

“what marfa petrovna?”

“oh, mercy—marfa petrovna svidrigailov. wrote you a bunch about her.”

“a-a-h! yeah, i remember... so she's dead! oh, really?” he perked up, like waking up. “what did she die of?”

“just imagine, out of the blue,” pulcheria alexandrovna replied quickly, encouraged by his interest. “the day i sent you that letter! can you believe it, that awful man seems to have caused her death. they say he beat her bad.”

“were they really on such bad terms?” he asked his sister.

“not at all. quite the opposite. with her, he was always patient, too much even. but suddenly, he lost it.”

“then he couldn't have been so bad if he held back for seven years? you defending him, dounia?”

“no, no, he's awful! can't think of anything worse!” dounia replied, almost shuddering, frowning, deep in thought.

“that happened in the morning,” pulcheria alexandrovna continued quickly. “and right after, she ordered the horses to go to town after dinner. always did that. had a good meal, i hear....”

“after the beating?”

“that was her... routine; and right after dinner, so she wouldn't be late, she went to the bathhouse.... she was doing some bath treatment. cold spring there, bathed daily, and as soon as she got in, stroke!”

“i should think so,” zossimov said.

“and did he beat her bad?”

“what does that matter!” dounia interjected.

“h’m! don’t know why you’re telling us this, mom,” raskolnikov said irritably, almost against his will.

“ah, my dear, i don't know what else to talk about,” pulcheria alexandrovna let out.

"Why, you all scared of me?" he asked with a tight smile.

"That's true," Dounia said, staring hard at her brother. "Mom was crossing herself all the way up the stairs."

His face twisted up like he was in pain.

"Dounia, what're you saying? Don’t be mad, Rodya.... Why’d you say that, Dounia?" Pulcheria Alexandrovna started, overwhelmed. "On the train, I was dreaming about meeting, talking about everything.... I was so happy, didn’t notice the trip! But what am I saying? I'm happy now.... Just seeing you, Rodya...."

"Hush, mom," he muttered, not looking at her, but squeezing her hand. "We'll have time to talk everything out!"

As he said it, confusion hit him hard. That cold feeling again. He'd lied—he'd never talk freely again. The thought tore him up, almost made him forget where he was. He got up, headed for the door.

"What’re you doing?" Razumihin shouted, grabbing his arm.

He sat back down, looking around in silence. They all stared at him, confused.

"But why so quiet?" he shouted suddenly. "Say something! No point sitting like this. Come on, talk.... We meet and sit silent.... Anything!"

"Thank God; I was scared yesterday was starting again," Pulcheria Alexandrovna said, crossing herself.

"What’s up, Rodya?" Avdotya Romanovna asked, suspicious.

"Oh, nothing! Just remembered something," he said, suddenly laughing.

"Well, if you remembered, that’s fine!... I was starting to think..." Zossimov said, getting up. "Time for me to head out. I'll come back... if I can..." He nodded and left.

"Great guy!" Pulcheria Alexandrovna said.

"Yeah, great, smart, educated," Raskolnikov suddenly burst out, talking fast, lively. "I can't recall where I met him before I got sick.... I must’ve met him somewhere----... And he's good too," nodding at Razumihin. "You like him, Dounia?" he asked and for some reason, laughed.

"Very much," Dounia replied.

"Man, you pig!" Razumihin protested, blushing and standing up. Pulcheria Alexandrovna smiled faintly, but Raskolnikov laughed out loud.

"Where you going?"

"I gotta go."

"You don't need to. Stay. Zossimov's gone, so you gotta. Don’t leave. What time is it? Noon? Nice watch, Dounia. Why so quiet again? I'm doing all the talking."

"It was a gift from Marfa Petrovna," Dounia said.

"And pricey too!" Pulcheria Alexandrovna added.

"Wow! Big one! Not much like a lady’s."

"I like it," Dounia said.

"So it’s not from her fiancé," Razumihin thought, feeling oddly happy.

"Thought it was Luzhin’s gift," Raskolnikov said.

"No, he hasn’t given Dounia any gifts yet."

"Ah! Remember, mom, I was in love and wanted to marry?" he asked suddenly, eyeing his mom who looked thrown by the sudden topic change.

"Oh, yes, dear."

Pulcheria Alexandrovna exchanged glances with Dounia and Razumihin.

hmm, yeah. what do i even say? don’t remember much. she was always sick, like, real frail, you know? always wanted to help the poor, talked about nunnery dreams, once she just broke down crying about it. yeah, i remember. she wasn’t much to look at, honestly. not sure why i was drawn to her—maybe ‘cause she was so ill. if she’d been lame or hunchbacked, i might’ve liked her even more, heh. yeah, spring delirium or something.

having them here, talking like this after three years, it’s almost too much. but something urgent’s got to get sorted today—decided that when i woke up. glad for the distraction.

within limits, yeah. i saw what pyotr petrovitch was after from the start. maybe he’s full of himself, but i hope he respects me too... why’re you laughing?

and why’re you blushing? you’re lying, sister. just stubbornness, trying to prove me wrong... you can’t respect luzhin. i’ve met him, talked to him. you’re selling out for money, acting low, and it’s good you can still blush about it.

he muttered it to himself but out loud, staring at his sister. finally, he opened the letter, still looking puzzled. read it twice, slowly. pulcheria alexandrovna was anxious; they all expected something big.

“what surprises me,” he started, handing the letter back to his mom, not talking to anyone, “is he’s a lawyer, talks fancy, but writes like this.”

they all startled, expecting something else.

“they all write like that,” razumihin cut in.

“you read it?”

“yeah.”

“legal? yeah, it’s legalese—business talk—not too uneducated, not quite educated—just business!”

if he’s proud, then he’s got his reasons. i get it. you’re upset, sis, thinking i’m nitpicking the letter just to bug you. not true. there’s one line that stands out: “blame yourselves.” he says he’ll leave if i’m there. that’s like saying he’ll drop you both if you don’t toe the line. you were called to petersburg, and now he’s pulling this? how do you feel about that? if luzhin wrote it, does it sting like if it came from razumihin or zossimov?

it’s legalese, probably harsher than he meant. but let’s clear one thing up. there’s a jab at me. i gave money to a widow for a funeral, not on some flimsy excuse, and not to her daughter, like he wrote, but to the widow herself. he’s quick to stir trouble and pit us against each other. he’s smart, but that’s not enough to act right. i’m telling you this because i care.

dounia stayed quiet, firm on her choice, waiting for the evening.

door creaked open, and in walked this young girl, looking all kinds of timid. everyone's heads turned, full of surprise and curiosity. raskolnikov didn't recognize her at first. it was sofya semyonovna marmeladov. he'd seen her just yesterday, but it was a whole different scene then. now she's here, modest, poor clothes, almost like a kid, but with this refined way about her. her face was open but scared. plain dress, old hat, still carrying a parasol. room full of folks caught her off guard, more shy than embarrassed, like she might just bolt. her lips and chin started trembling, but she held it together, looking down.

raskolnikov watched her closely. thin face, sharp nose and chin, blue eyes that lit up with kindness. not pretty, but something about her drew you in. she was eighteen but looked like a kid. her gestures had this childishness that was almost absurd.

pulcheria alexandrovna tried to greet sonia, but it didn’t happen, and she left in a rush.

but avdotya romanovna waited, giving sonia a polite bow. sonia, flustered, curtsied quickly. she looked uncomfortable, like avdotya romanovna’s politeness was too much.

he's around fifty, tall, broad shoulders making him seem a bit stooped. dressed sharp, carrying a fancy cane, spotless gloves. broad, pleasant face with high cheekbones. hair still full, just a hint of gray, beard lighter. blue eyes, cold and thoughtful, crimson lips. looks younger than his age.
