zametov said, kinda dry, “you talk well, even clever, but man, you’re cranky.”

porfiry chimed in, “nikodim told me he ran into you late last night at some guy’s place who got run over.”

razumihin jumped in, “and weren’t you just losing it then? gave your last penny to the widow for a funeral. if you wanted to help, why not just fifteen or twenty? keep a few for yourself, but no, you threw away all twenty-five!”

“maybe i found a treasure and kept it hush-hush?” he laughed, but his lips were shaking. “sorry for wasting your time with this nonsense,” he looked at porfiry.

“not at all! you’re quite fascinating. glad you spoke up.”

razumihin shouted, “how about some tea? mouth’s dry.”

“good idea! maybe we’ll all join. need something before tea?”

“get outta here!”

porfiry left to get tea.

raskolnikov’s mind was spinning. he was fuming inside.

“they’re not even pretending. how’d you know me to talk to nikodim? they’re trailing me like dogs. just spit in my face then.” his rage was barely contained. “come on, hit me outright, don’t toy like this. it’s rude, porfiry, but i won’t take it! i’ll spill everything, show you my disdain.” he was barely breathing. “and what if it’s all in my head? what if i’m just overreacting? maybe it’s all normal talk, but something’s off... why’d he say ‘with her’ so bluntly? zametov said i spoke artfully... why the tone? razumihin’s here, blind as ever. fever’s back! did porfiry just wink? nonsense! why would he? are they messing with me or do they know? zametov’s changed his tune. i expected it! he’s comfortable here, i’m the stranger. they’re plotting for sure! were they talking about the flat? they should hurry! when i mentioned the flat, he didn’t bite... maybe that was smart. delirious, ha! knows about last night! didn’t know mom’s arrival! pencil mark! you won’t catch me! no facts, just guesses! show me facts! the flat’s not real, just a fever dream. i know what to say... do they know about the flat? can’t leave without knowing. why am i here? but being mad might be smart! idiot, why am i so touchy? maybe playing sick’s the move... he’s testing me. gonna try to trap me. why’d i come?”

all this shot through his mind in a flash.

porfiry came back, suddenly all cheerful.

"man, your party yesterday messed with my head," he started, laughing with Razumihin. "was it good? i left at the best part. who came out on top?"

"nobody really. they got into endless debates, drifted off into space."

"imagine, Rodya, what we hit yesterday—whether crime's even a thing. talked till our heads hurt."

"that's a regular question," Raskolnikov said, casual.

"wasn't exactly like that," Porfiry chimed in.

"not quite," Razumihin jumped in, getting heated. "listen, Rodion, i want your take. i was arguing hard and needed backup. started with socialist ideas. you know it—crime's just a protest against society's mess, nothing more."

"you're wrong there," Porfiry cut in, grinning, making Razumihin more fired up.

"nothing else counts," Razumihin argued.

"not wrong. their pamphlets say it all. 'environment's influence,' their favorite. fix society, crime stops. everyone righteous instantly. human nature? not considered. they ignore it. think some perfect plan will make society just instantly, like magic. history? they hate it, call it stupid. life's messy process? they don't want it. no room for a soul. soul wants life, doesn't follow mechanical rules. but they want something lifeless, obedient. it's like building walls, planning rooms in a phalanstery. but human nature isn't ready. wants life, not a graveyard. can't skip nature with logic. logic's got three options, life's got millions. just make it about comfort. easy solution, don't think. life's secret in two pages!"

"now he's drumming! hold him!" Porfiry laughed. "imagine," he turned to Raskolnikov, "six people like that last night, one room, punch first! brother, you're wrong, environment affects crime a lot."

"i know, but tell me: a 40-year-old harms a 10-year-old; was that environment?"

"well, it could be," Porfiry said, serious. "that crime can be influenced by environment."

razumihin was all worked up. "oh, if you’re into it," he shouted. "i’ll show you that your white eyelashes can totally be linked to the Church of Ivan the Great’s height. and i'll do it clear, precise, step-by-step, even with a Liberal twist. i’m on it! wanna bet?"

"done! let's hear it!"

"he's always messing around, damn him," razuminin yelled, jumping up, arms flailing. "why even talk to you? he does it on purpose; you don’t know him, rodion! took their side yesterday just to make fools of them. and the stuff he said! they loved it! he can keep this up for weeks. last year he convinced us he was joining a monastery: stuck to it for two months. recently, he claimed he was getting married, had everything ready. ordered new clothes. we all congratulated him. no bride, nothing, all nonsense!"

"ah, you’re wrong! i got the clothes first. it was the new clothes that made me think of fooling you."

"are you really that good at pretending?" raskolnikov asked, barely interested.

"you wouldn’t think it, huh? just wait, i’ll fool you too. ha-ha-ha! no, here’s the truth. all this crime talk reminds me of an article of yours that caught my interest. ‘on crime’... or something like that, i read it two months back in the _periodical review_."

"my article? in the _periodical review_?" raskolnikov was shocked. "i wrote something on a book six months ago after leaving university, but sent it to the _weekly review_."

"but it came out in the _periodical_."

"and the _weekly review_ shut down, so it wasn’t printed then."

"that’s right; when it closed, the _weekly review_ merged with the _periodical_, so your article showed up two months ago. didn’t you know?"

raskolnikov hadn’t known.

"why, you could get some cash for that article! you’re strange! you live so isolated you miss things that matter to you. it’s true, trust me."

"bravo, rodya! i didn’t know either!" razuminin yelled. "i’ll run today to the reading-room and ask for the issue. two months ago? what date? doesn’t matter, i’ll find it. can’t believe you didn’t tell us!"

"how’d you find out it was mine? it’s just signed with an initial."

"i found out by chance, recently. through the editor; i know him... very interesting."

"i think i analyzed the criminal’s psychology before and after the crime."

"yes, and you said committing a crime always comes with illness. very original, but... it wasn’t that part that got me, but an idea at the end that you barely touched on. you suggested there are certain people who... not just can, but have a right to break the law and commit crimes, and the law doesn’t apply to them."

raskolnikov grinned at the twist razuminin put on his idea.

"what? what do you mean? a right to crime? but not because of their environment?" razuminin asked, a bit worried.

"no, not because of that," porfiry said. "his article splits people into 'ordinary' and 'extraordinary.' ordinary folks gotta follow the rules. extraordinary ones, well, they can break 'em just because they're special. that's your idea, right?"

"what? that can't be right?" razumihin said, all confused.

raskolnikov just smiled. he got the game and knew their angle. he was ready to take it on.

nah, you got it almost right, really close. but i’m not saying extraordinary people gotta break morals all the time. i just mean an "extraordinary" person might feel they’ve got an inner right to step over some barriers if it's crucial for their idea to come through. like, if Kepler or Newton had needed to sacrifice a few for their discoveries to benefit humanity as a whole, they’d have that duty, but that doesn’t mean they can just murder or steal at will.

look at the big leaders like Napoleon or Mahomet — they broke old laws to make new ones, sometimes with bloodshed. but you see, most of these big names in history ended up with blood on their hands for their cause. so yeah, great people or even those a bit out of the ordinary, might have to be rule-breakers to push beyond the norm, and it’s kinda in their nature not to stay in the rut.

this idea ain’t new — it’s been around forever. dividing people into ordinary and extraordinary is a bit arbitrary, sure, but i think there’s truth in saying humanity’s split into those who reproduce and those who bring something new. the ordinary folks, they’re conservative, they follow rules. it’s their role, and there’s nothing wrong with that. but the extraordinary ones, they break laws, they destroy for better things. if they gotta step over bodies for their ideas, they find their own justification. but don't worry, most won’t accept this right, they’ll punish them, maybe hang them. yet, eventually, they get worshipped by the next generation. first type's all about now, the second's about the future. both have their place. everyone’s got a right, and we keep on with the eternal struggle till things change.

"so you believe in the New Jerusalem?"

"i do," Raskolnikov said, eyes fixed on one spot on the carpet.

"and... you believe in God? just curious."

"i do," Raskolnikov said, looking up at Porfiry.

"and Lazarus rising from the dead?"

"i... i do. why ask?"

"you take it literally?"

"literally."

"really... just curious. but back to the point; not all are executed. some..."

"live to see their goals? sure, some do, and then..."

"they start executing others?"

"if needed; mostly they do. that's sharp."

"thanks. but how do you tell extraordinary from ordinary? birth marks? should they wear something? a uniform maybe? if someone from one group thinks they're from the other and starts 'removing obstacles'..."

"oh, that happens! even sharper than before."

"thanks."

"no need; but note, mistakes happen only with ordinary folks. they fancy themselves as ‘destroyers’ and join movements sincerely. the really new folks get missed or seen as backward. no big danger though; they don’t go far. maybe a thrashing now and then to remind them of their place, but they usually self-correct. they take on acts of penance on their own... no need to worry. it's natural law."

"well, you've eased my mind on that. but here's another worry. how many have the right to kill? these extraordinary folks? i respect them, sure, but it's scary if there are lots, right?"

"nah, don't sweat it," Raskolnikov said, still in that same tone. "Folks with new ideas, ones who can actually say something _new_, are rare. Really rare. There's a pattern, a natural law to it, even if we don't know it yet. Most people, they're just raw material. Maybe one out of a thousand has that spark of independence. Maybe one in ten thousand. A true genius, that's one in a million. There's a law at work here, not just random chance."

"Are you two joking?" Razumihin finally broke in. "Rodya, are you serious?"

Raskolnikov just looked at him with a pale, somber face and stayed quiet. Porfiry's sarcasm seemed odd to Razumihin in that moment.

"Well, if you're serious... Sure, it's not new. We've read this stuff a million times. But what's shocking is you justifying bloodshed _in the name of conscience_. That's the scary part of your article."

"You're right, it's more terrifying," Porfiry said.

"Must be a mistake. I'll read it. I can't believe you mean that! I'll check it out."

"It's not spelled out in the article, just hinted," Raskolnikov replied.

"Yeah, yeah." Porfiry was restless. "Your take on crime is clear now, but... sorry for bothering you, but what if someone thinks they're a new Lycurgus or Mahomet and starts removing obstacles for some great cause... and needs money... you see?"

Zametov laughed in the corner. Raskolnikov didn't look up.

"I admit," he continued calmly, "those cases will happen. The vain and foolish fall into that trap; especially the young."

"Yeah, you see. So?"

"So what?" Raskolnikov smiled. "Not my problem. Society's got prisons, banishment, investigators. No need to worry. Just catch the thief."

"And if we do?"

"Then he gets his due."

"You're logical, sure. But what about his conscience?"

"Why worry about that?"

"Just out of humanity."

"If he's got a conscience, he'll suffer for his mistake. That's his punishment—along with prison."

"But the real geniuses," Razumihin asked, frowning, "the ones with the right to kill? Shouldn't they suffer for the blood they've shed?"

"why even use 'ought'? not about permission. if he's sorry, he's gonna suffer. big brains and big hearts always end up in pain. great folks carry great sadness," he said, eyes distant.

he looked up, gave a small smile, grabbed his cap. seemed quieter now, felt a shift. everybody stood.

"you can be mad at me," Porfiry Petrovitch continued, "but here's a quick question. just a thought, don't want to forget it."

"alright, what's your thought?" Raskolnikov was pale, serious.

"well... it's tricky to say... it's a playful idea... when you wrote your article, didn't you feel a bit like a special man, saying something new? that's right, isn't it?"

"probably," Raskolnikov replied, sneering.

Razumihin shifted.

"and if so, could you, in tough times, to help humanity, break barriers? rob, murder?"

he winked again, silent laugh.

"if i did, i wouldn't tell you," Raskolnikov shot back, defiant.

"no, just curious from a literary angle..."

"ugh, how blatant," Raskolnikov thought, disgusted.

"allow me to say," he replied coolly, "i'm no Mahomet or Napoleon, so i can't tell you how i'd act."

"oh come on, aren't we all Napoleons now in Russia?" Porfiry said, way too chummy.

something odd in his voice.

"maybe one of these future Napoleons killed Alyona Ivanovna last week?" Zametov piped up.

Raskolnikov stayed silent, staring at Porfiry. Razumihin was darkly scowling. he'd noticed something. angry glances around. a grim pause. Raskolnikov turned to leave.

"leaving already?" Porfiry offered his hand, overly polite. "glad to meet. don't worry about your request, write like i said, or better, come see me in a day or two... tomorrow at eleven. we'll sort it, chat. as one of the last there, maybe you can share something," he added, beaming.

"you want to formally question me?" Raskolnikov asked sharply.

"oh, no need now. you misunderstand. i talk to everyone who had pledges... got info from some, you're the last... oh," he suddenly remembered, "right, Nikolay... i know he's innocent, but what can we do? had to question Dmitri too... here's the thing, when you went up the stairs, it was past seven, correct?"

"yes," Raskolnikov answered, feeling uneasy, knowing he shouldn't have said it.

"when you went upstairs between seven and eight, did you see a flat open on the second floor? remember if there were workmen, painters?"

"painters? no, didn’t see them," Raskolnikov says slow, digging through his memory, nerves on edge, trying to spot the trap. "no painters, no open flat... but on the fourth floor," he gets it now, "someone was moving out across from Alyona Ivanovna’s. porters moving a sofa, squeezed me against the wall. but painters? no, don’t recall any."

"what are you saying?" Razumihin jumps in, realizing. "painters were there the day of the murder, you were there three days before!"

"ah, i've mixed it up!" Porfiry smacks his forehead. "this stuff’s messing with my head!" he tells Raskolnikov, half-apologetic. "would help if someone saw them between seven and eight... but i got it wrong."

"you’ve got to be more careful," Razumihin says, not amused.

they leave with Porfiry being overly polite. once outside, they walk in silence. Raskolnikov takes a deep breath.

***

"don’t buy it, can’t buy it!" Razumihin argues, trying to counter Raskolnikov’s points.

they’re near Bakaleyev’s place now, where Pulcheria Alexandrovna and Dounia wait. Razumihin stops, heated, talking openly about it for the first time.

"don’t believe it, then!" Raskolnikov shoots back, smiling cold. "you missed it all, i caught every word."

"you’re suspicious, that’s why you weighed it... yeah, Porfiry’s tone was odd, and Zametov’s too! but why?"

"he’s changed since last night."

"no, if they believed it, they’d hide it, catch you later... but they were bold, careless."

"if they had real facts, they’d hide their cards, hope for more (a search would’ve happened). but no facts, just a floating idea. they use boldness to throw me off. maybe Porfiry’s annoyed, no facts, blurts it out—or has a plan... smart guy. maybe he pretends to know to scare me. their psychology, man. but explaining it sucks. stop!"

damn, it's just insulting, that's what it is. ok, we've put it all out there now, and that's good. but listen, i saw it coming ages ago. just a hint, a whisper of it, but why even that? who do they think they are? if you only knew how angry i've been. just because some poor student, losing his mind from being broke and sick, in the middle of a fever, gets hassled by cops and hit with a surprise debt and crap weather, all while starving, he passes out. and they base everything on that? damn them! i know it's hard, but if i were you, Rodya, i'd just laugh at them or better, spit in their faces. get mad in every direction and shut them down. damn them! don't let it get to you.

"he's got a point," Raskolnikov thinks.

"damn them? but the cross-examination's tomorrow," he says bitterly. "do i really have to explain myself to them? i'm already annoyed i talked to Zametov yesterday..."

"hell, i'll just go to Porfiry myself. i'll get the truth out of him. and about Zametov..."

"finally, he gets it," Raskolnikov thinks.

"wait!" Razumihin grabs him again. "hold up! you're wrong. i've thought about it. you're wrong! how was that a trap? you said the question about the workmen was a trap. but if you did it, would you say you saw them painting? no, you'd see nothing, even if you did see it."

"if i did it, i'd definitely say i saw the workmen and the flat," Raskolnikov replies, not wanting to say it.

"but why talk against yourself?"

"because only fools or beginners deny everything in an interrogation. anyone with a bit of sense will admit the unavoidable facts but twist them, give them a new spin. Porfiry expected me to say i saw them to make it seem real, then explain it away."

"but he'd catch you right there, saying the workmen couldn't have been there two days before, meaning you were there on the murder day at eight. a detail would trip you up."

"exactly, that's what he was banking on, that i'd not think it through, rush to answer, and forget the workmen weren't there two days before."

"how could you forget?"

"easily done. clever people get caught on simple things. the smarter you are, the simpler the trap you fall into. Porfiry's not as dumb as you think."

"then he's a real piece of work if that's true!"

couldn't help but laugh. then it hit him how weird his own honesty was, jumping to explain himself when he'd been all moody just before, like he had to. "starting to enjoy some of this," he thought, but then got real uneasy, like a bad idea snuck up on him. it only got worse. when they hit Bakaleyev’s entrance, he blurted, "go in alone! i'll be back soon."

"we're right here, where you going?" 

"doesn't matter... back in half an hour. just tell them."

"i'll come with you." 

"you're just trying to mess with me!" he snapped, eyes full of irritation and despair. Razumihin stood there, hands dropped, staring after him as Raskolnikov stormed off. clenching his fists, Razumihin swore he'd squeeze Porfiry like a lemon that day, then climbed up to calm Pulcheria Alexandrovna, worried by their delay.

home, Raskolnikov was drenched in sweat, breathing hard. he rushed up, locked his door, and scrambled to that corner with the hole under the paper. he fumbled around, checking every crack. nothing. relieved, he thought back to Bakaleyev’s, worried something might've fallen out, like a chain or paper, something with that old woman's writing that could nail him.

stood there, lost in thought, a weird, embarrassed smile on his face. grabbed his cap and slipped out quietly, thoughts a mess, drifting through the gateway.

“here he is,” someone shouted.

looked up. porter pointed him out to a short guy, like an artisan, but dressed long coat, waistcoat, almost woman-like. greasy cap, face wrinkled, over fifty, eyes hidden in fat, watching grimly.

“what’s up?” Raskolnikov asked the porter.

the man checked him out, took his time, then turned and walked off without a word.

“what’s up?” Raskolnikov asked again.

“guy was asking if a student lived here, mentioned you. saw you coming, pointed you out, he left. odd.”

the porter seemed puzzled too but didn't dwell, went back inside.

Raskolnikov ran after the guy, spotted him across the street, steady walk, eyes down, deep in thought. caught up, walked beside him, looked at his face. the man noticed, glanced quickly, eyes down again; they walked side by side, silent.

“you were asking for me... at the porter?” Raskolnikov finally said, but real quiet.

The guy didn’t answer; didn’t even look at him. Silence again.

“why do you... come ask for me... and say nothing.... What’s this about?”

Raskolnikov’s voice got shaky, words just wouldn’t come out right.

The guy looked up, gave Raskolnikov a dark, mean look.

“Murderer!” he said suddenly, quiet but clear.

Raskolnikov kept walking next to him. Legs went weak, cold shiver hit his spine, heart stopped a beat then pounded hard like it was let loose. They walked side by side, not a word, for about a hundred steps.

The guy still didn’t look at him.

“what do you mean... who’s.... who’s a murderer?” Raskolnikov mumbled, barely a whisper.

“_you_ are a murderer,” the guy said clearer, like he enjoyed it, glaring right at Raskolnikov’s pale, shocked face.

They reached a crossroads. The guy turned left without a glance back. Raskolnikov just stood there, watching. He saw him turn around about fifty steps away, staring back. Couldn’t see perfectly, but he imagined that same cold, hateful smile.

Raskolnikov dragged himself back to his tiny room, every step shaky. Took off his cap, put it on the table, stood there ten minutes. Then collapsed onto the sofa with a weak groan. Lay there for half an hour.

No real thoughts. Just fragments, random images floating in his head—faces from childhood, a church belfry, a billiard table with officers playing, cigar smoke from some underground shop, a tavern’s back staircase wet and messy, Sunday bells in the distance.... Images spun around like a storm. Some he liked, tried to grab, but they slipped away. Felt a weight inside him, but not crushing, sometimes even nice.... Still shivering slightly, almost pleasant.

He heard Razumihin’s hurried steps, shut his eyes, pretended to sleep. Razumihin opened the door, paused, then tiptoed in. Raskolnikov heard Nastasya whisper:

“Don’t wake him! Let him rest. He can eat later.”

“right,” Razumihin replied. They left quietly, shut the door. Another half-hour. Raskolnikov opened his eyes, rolled onto his back, hands behind his head.

who's that guy? where'd he spring from? what did he see? looks like he's seen everything. where was he anyway? and how'd he see it? just popped out of nowhere now? is that even possible? hm... raskolnikov shivered, cold creeping in. and that jewel case nikolay found behind the door—can you believe it? a clue? miss a tiny line and you build a pyramid of evidence! even a fly saw it. is that possible? he felt weak, disgusted by it. "should've known," he thought with a bitter smile. "how did i, knowing myself, think i could pick up an axe and shed blood? i knew... ah, but i did know!" he whispered, sinking into despair.

"no, men like that aren't made like this. the real masters, they can storm toulon, massacre in paris, forget armies in egypt, lose half a million in moscow, and laugh it off in vilna. they get altars after death. everything's allowed for them. they're not flesh—they're bronze!"

a random thought almost made him laugh. napoleon, pyramids, waterloo, and some skinny old pawnbroker with a red trunk under her bed—a real mess for porfiry petrovitch to chew on! how can they swallow that? it's all too clumsy. "napoleon hiding under an old woman's bed! ugh, disgusting!"

sometimes it feels like im spinning out. like im wired and disconnected all at once. the old woman? she dont matter, really. maybe she was a mistake, but she aint the point. she was just a fever i got caught in... i rushed to cross a line, but didnt do it, just hesitated. i thought i was doing something big, but maybe i wasnt even up for it... principle? why did that idiot Razumihin rag on socialists? theyre just business folk pushing "happiness for all." but i only get one life, and i aint waiting for "happiness for all." gotta live now or whats the point. cant watch mom starve while i hang onto my coin, waiting on some big picture. i add my own piece to the puzzle and call it good. but here i am, some aesthetic parasite, laughing like a fool. sure, im a louse, because i can think it, because i wrapped it all up in some grand idea. i picked her, the most pointless, took just what i needed. oh, she wanted the rest to go to a monastery, ha! and what makes me the worst louse is knowing id think this way after the deed, feeling it coming. the horror, the smallness! i get the "prophet" with his sword, bossing the world with no explanation. obey, dont wish... but i cant forgive the old woman.

sweat's dripping, mouth's dry, eyes stuck on the ceiling.

mom, sis—i loved them! why hate them now? its physical, cant stand them near... i kissed mom once, remember that. held her, thought what if she knew... should i tell her? maybe shes like me, i wonder, fighting through this fog. i hate the old woman still, id do it again if she stood here now! Lizaveta? why dont i feel the same about her, like she wasnt even there? Sonia, Lizaveta! soft, gentle... why dont they cry? they just give it all up... their eyes, so tender... Sonia, dear Sonia!

he blacked out, couldn't figure out how he ended up on the street. evening, that kind of heavy air. folks everywhere, heading home from work or just out for a stroll. dust and stale water smells hanging around. raskolnikov knew he had a job to do, but it slipped his mind. then he spots a guy across the street waving him over. crosses to him, guy turns and walks off like he hadn't done a thing. "did he wave?" raskolnikov wonders, trying to catch up. gets close and realizes he knows the guy, gets spooked. follows him at a distance. the guy heads into a big building. raskolnikov follows, sees him turn in the courtyard. goes after him, up the stairs. place feels familiar. moonlight through the windows, eerie. reaches the second floor, recognizes it now. "painters were here... why didn't i see that?" steps go silent. "he's hiding." keeps going, footsteps echoing, it's dark. finds an open flat. inside, everything's still there, bathed in moonlight. chairs, mirror, yellow sofa. moon's playing tricks, making it all feel strange. stands there, heart racing, silence pressing down. then a crack, like wood snapping. a fly buzzes against the window. sees a cloak in the corner. "that wasn't there before..." moves it, finds the old woman sitting, can't see her face. bends down, the old woman's laughing, shaking in silence. hears whispers and laughter from the bedroom. panic hits, starts swinging the axe, but the laughter just gets louder. tries to run, but the halls are packed with silent faces, all watching. heart's pounding, can't move his legs. wants to scream, wakes up.

he took a deep breath, but the dream wouldn't quit. door flies open and there's a man he's never seen just staring.

raskolnikov barely opens his eyes, then shuts them again. lies still on his back.

"is this still a dream?" he thinks. peeks again. the guy hasn't moved, still staring.

stranger steps in quiet, shuts the door, heads to the table. pauses, eyes locked on raskolnikov, then sits by the sofa. hat on the floor, hands on cane, chin on hands. looks ready to wait him out. raskolnikov sneaks peeks — older guy, a bit stout, beard almost white.

ten minutes drag. light fades, room's silent. only a fly buzzing at the window. can't stand it. raskolnikov sits up.

"alright, what do you want?"

"knew you weren't sleeping," stranger laughs calm. "arkady ivanovitch svidrigaïlov, at your service..."

PART IV

CHAPTER I

"still dreaming?" raskolnikov wonders.

he studies the visitor, suspicious.

"svidrigaïlov? nonsense. can't be," he blurts, confused.

visitor doesn't flinch.

"came for two reasons. first, wanted to meet you—heard a lot, all good. second, need your help with your sister, avdotya romanovna. she won't see me, but with you on board..."

"you're wrong," raskolnikov cuts in.

"they just got here yesterday, right?"

no answer from raskolnikov.

"yesterday, yeah. i showed up the day before. rodion romanovitch, no need to justify, but tell me what's so criminal about my actions, speaking plainly?"

raskolnikov keeps silent.

"in my house, they say i harassed a girl and made 'infamous proposals'—right? (figured you'd go there.) but what if i'm just human, capable of love? then it's all natural. am i a monster or a victim? maybe i was ready to elope for love, thinking of happiness. reason's a slave to passion, maybe i hurt myself most!"

"missing the point," raskolnikov interrupts, disgusted. "right or wrong, we don't like you. want nothing to do with you. there's the door. out!"

svidrigaïlov laughs suddenly.

"but you're... no fooling you," he says, laughing openly. "thought i'd sway you, but you nailed it from the start!"

"and you're still trying to sway me!"

"What's the big deal?" Svidrigaïlov laughed. "It's what the French call _bonne guerre_, a harmless trick. But you broke my flow. Anyway, nothing would've been awkward if not for the garden incident. Marfa Petrovna..."

"Heard you got rid of her too?" Raskolnikov cut in.

"Oh, that? You'd hear it anyway. But honestly, I'm not worried. Everything was above board—medical report said apoplexy from a bath after a big meal and wine. Couldn't say otherwise. But on the train, I thought, did I somehow cause it with irritation or something? Decided, nah, no way."

Raskolnikov chuckled. "Why even think about it?"

"Why laugh? I hit her twice with a switch—no marks. Don't think I'm a cynic; I know it was wrong. Still, Marfa might've been pleased by my 'passion.' She'd exhausted the story of your sister, stayed home for three days, bored everyone with that letter. You heard about that? Then, boom, a little drama with the switch. She wanted the carriage ready straight away. Some women, despite the fuss, secretly like a little insult. People generally do, actually. Ever notice that?"

Raskolnikov almost left but stayed a bit out of curiosity.

"Like a fight?" he asked casually.

"Not really," Svidrigaïlov said. "Marfa and I rarely fought. We got along well. Used the whip twice in seven years (not counting a third odd time). First was two months after marriage, last was what we're talking about. Think I'm some monster? Remember a nobleman shamed for thrashing a German woman on a train a few years back? Papers went wild. I didn't sympathize much. But some folks can be provoking, even progressives might snap. Nobody saw it like that, but that's the real humane view."

Svidrigaïlov laughed out of nowhere again. Raskolnikov pegged him as a guy with a mission who keeps it under wraps.

"Been chatting with anyone lately?" Raskolnikov asked.

"Hardly. Wondering how I fit in everywhere?"

"No, more how you fit in too well."

"Cause I'm not ruffled by your bluntness? Why get ruffled? You ask, I answer," Svidrigaïlov said, sounding oddly plain. "Not much catches my interest these days, especially now with nothing to do... Think what you want, like I'm cozying up for a reason, especially since I said I need to see your sister. Truth is, I'm bored. Last three days have been dull, so I'm glad to see you... Don't get mad, Rodion Romanovitch. But you seem off yourself. Something's not right with you, not just now, but in general... Okay, okay, no need to glare. I'm not as rough as you think."

Raskolnikov gave him a dark look.

"Maybe not rough at all," he said. "You seem well-mannered, or at least can act like it."

"Don’t care much about opinions," Svidrigaïlov replied, a bit haughty. "Why not be crass when it’s handy... especially if it suits you," he laughed again.

"Thought you had a lot of friends here. Why bother with me unless you've got a reason?"

"True, I know people here," Svidrigaïlov agreed, dodging the main question. "Seen a few, been around three days, bumped into them. Normal stuff. I look the part and I’m not broke; serfs being freed didn’t hit me; got forests and meadows. Revenue's steady... But I’m not visiting them, tired of them long ago. Been here three days, no calls... What a town! How's it even here? Full of officials and students. Missed a lot eight years back when I was running wild... Anatomy’s my only hope now, by God!"

"Anatomy?"

"As for clubs, Dussauts, parades, or whatever—let them run without me," he continued, ignoring the question. "Who wants to be a card-sharper anyway?"

"You were a card-sharper then?"

how could i not be? we were a tight group, high society types, eight years back. poets, men with money. funny thing in russian circles, the best manners come from those who've been knocked around a bit, you notice that? anyway, i got stuck in the countryside, ended up in debt jail thanks to some shady greek from nezhin. then marfa petrovna showed up, paid him off with thirty thousand silver (i owed seventy), and we tied the knot. she was five years older but quite fond of me. seven years in the country, never left. she always had that IOU hanging over me, thirty thousand roubles. if i ever acted up, she'd use it. she would’ve done it too, nothing stops women like that.

“if not for that, would you have left her?”

hard to say. it wasn’t really the IOU keeping me. didn’t wanna go anywhere. marfa petrovna even suggested i travel abroad, but been there, hated it. bay of naples, the sunrise, all that beauty just made me sad. better at home, where you can blame others. maybe i'd go to the north pole, since i hate drinking but it's all that's left. heard berg's doing a balloon ride next sunday from yusupov garden, taking passengers for a fee. true?

“would you go up?”

“me? no, no,” svidrigaïlov muttered, lost in thought.

“what's he mean? is he serious?” raskolnikov wondered.

“nah, wasn't the document holding me back,” svidrigaïlov continued, deep in thought. “i chose to stay. nearly a year ago, marfa petrovna returned the IOU on my name-day, gave me a chunk of money too. she was wealthy, you know. ‘i trust you, arkady ivanovitch’ she said. you think she didn’t mean it? but i ran the estate well, got books, marfa petrovna liked it at first, then worried i'd overdo it.”

“you missing marfa petrovna much?”

“maybe. maybe i am. do you believe in ghosts?”

“ghosts? what kind?”

“ordinary ones.”

“do you?”

“maybe not, _pour vous plaire_.... can’t say no.”

“you see them?”

svidrigaïlov gave a strange smile. “marfa petrovna visits me,” he said, twisting his mouth.

“how does she ‘visit’ you?”

“three times. first was the funeral day, an hour after she was buried. second was two days ago, dawn at the malaya vishera station, third was two hours ago in my room. just me.”

“you awake?”

“wide awake. every time. she comes, talks for a minute, leaves through the door. can almost hear her.”

“why did i feel something like that was happening to you?” raskolnikov said suddenly, surprised at his own words. he was really stirred up.

"what! you thought that?" svidrigaïlov asked, surprised. "really? didnt i say we had something in common?"

"you never said that!" raskolnikov snapped.

"didnt i?"

"no!"

"thought i did. when i saw you acting asleep, i thought 'here's the guy.'"

"what do you mean by 'the guy'? what are you talking about?" raskolnikov demanded.

"what do i mean? not really sure..." svidrigaïlov muttered, confused himself.

they both went quiet, staring each other down.

"that's nonsense!" raskolnikov shouted, irritated. "what does she say when she visits?"

"she? silly stuff, really. first time she came, i was worn out. funeral, lunch, finally alone. lit a cigar to think, she shows up. ‘you forgot the dining-room clock, arkady ivanovitch,’ she said. seven years winding that clock, every week. next day, off to the station, tired, half asleep, coffee in hand, there she is again. ‘shall i tell your fortune?’ she loved that. ran off, bell rang. today, felt heavy after a bad meal, smoking, she comes in again, new green dress. ‘how do you like my dress?’ she asked. i teased her, ‘i want to get married, marfa petrovna.’ she said, ‘you’re a joke, arkady ivanovitch.’ then she left. nonsense, right?"

"are you lying?" raskolnikov asked.

"rarely lie," svidrigaïlov said, ignoring the rudeness.

"seen ghosts before?"

"yeah, once. six years ago. my serf, filka, after his burial. called for my pipe, he came in like always. thought it was revenge. 'how dare you,' i said. he left, never came back. didnt tell marfa petrovna. wanted a service for him, but too ashamed."

"go see a doctor."

"i know im not well, without you telling me, but i dont know what's wrong; im five times as strong as you. i didnt ask if you believe ghosts are seen; i asked if you believe they exist."

"no, i wont believe it!" raskolnikov snapped, angry.

"what do people say?" svidrigaïlov muttered, talking to himself, looking aside. "they say, 'youre ill, so what you see is fantasy.' but thats not logical. ghosts appear to the sick, but that just means they cant appear to the healthy, not that they dont exist."

"nothing like that," raskolnikov insisted, irritated.

"no? dont think so?" svidrigaïlov continued, watching him. "how about this: ghosts are fragments of other worlds, their start. a healthy man wont see them, because he's grounded in this life. but when one is ill, the body's order breaks, and another world's possible. the sicker one gets, the closer the contact, till death takes you straight there. i thought of that ages ago. if you believe in a future life, you could believe that too."

"i dont believe in a future life," said raskolnikov.

svidrigaïlov sat, lost in thought.

"and what if there are only spiders there?" he said suddenly.

"he is mad," thought raskolnikov.

"we imagine eternity as vast, beyond conception. but why vast? what if it's just a cramped, grimy room, spiders in every corner? i sometimes imagine that."

"cant you imagine anything better than that?" raskolnikov cried, anguished.

"better? how do we know? maybe thats just, and id have made it that way," svidrigaïlov said with a vague smile.

this answer chilled raskolnikov. svidrigaïlov looked up and laughed.

"just think," he said, "half an hour ago, we were strangers, enemies; we have unresolved issues, yet here we are in the abstract! wasnt i right? birds of a feather?"

"let me," raskolnikov said, irritated, "ask why you visited... and im in a hurry. i need to go."

"of course. your sister, avdotya romanovna, is marrying mr. luzhin, pyotr petrovitch?"

"can you not talk about my sister or mention her name? i dont understand how you dare say her name if youre really svidrigaïlov."

"but i came to talk about her; how can i not mention her?"

"fine, speak, but hurry up."

you've probably got your own take on Luzhin already. he's tied to my family through my wife, but let's be real — he's not a fit for Avdotya Romanovna. she's throwing herself into this for family, kinda recklessly. figured you'd want the engagement off without losing any perks. now that i know you, i'm sure of it.

"that's naive... or impudent," raskolnikov says.

"think i'm out for myself? don’t worry, if i was, i wouldn't be this straightforward. not dumb. i’ll admit something: defending my feelings for Avdotya, i said i was a victim. truth is, i don't feel a thing now, which surprises me, because i did feel something..."

"out of idleness and depravity," raskolnikov interrupts.

"yeah, i'm idle and depraved, but your sister's got qualities that even caught my attention. but it's pointless now."

"you've known long?"

"started seeing it earlier, but got sure right when i hit petersburg. still thought in moscow i was coming to win her over and beat Luzhin."

"get to the point. i'm in a rush."

"gladly. with this trip coming up, i need to sort a few things. left my kids with an aunt, they're set. not much of a dad anyway. haven’t taken anything but what marfa gave me last year. more than enough. here's the deal. before i leave, i want Luzhin out of the picture. not that i hate him, but he messed things up with marfa. want Avdotya to know, through you, that Luzhin's trouble. then, apologize, gift her ten thousand roubles to help break it off, which i think she's already open to."

"you're mad!" raskolnikov says, more shocked than angry. "how dare you say that!"

knew you'd get mad, but first off, this ten thousand roubles isn’t doing me any good. if Avdotya Romanovna doesn’t take it, I’ll just blow it on something dumb. no catch here. i really messed up with your sister and want to make amends. not payback, just a gesture that i’m not all bad. if there was any selfishness in this, i wouldn’t be making it so public. i even offered her more a few weeks back. and hey, i'm probably getting married soon, so don’t worry. marrying Luzhin is still taking money. think on it, Rodion Romanovitch.

Svidrigaïlov was cool as a cucumber saying all this.

“stop,” Raskolnikov said. “this is out of line.”

“not at all. why can’t a guy make things right without jumping through hoops? if i died and left it in my will, she’d take it, right?”

“she might not.”

“nah, she would. but fine, if you say no, i’ll drop it, though ten thousand is nice to have. just pass it on to Avdotya Romanovna.”

“i won’t.”

“then i’ll have to tell her myself.”

“and if i do tell her, will you back off?”

“not sure. i’d like to see her.”

“don’t count on it.”

“that’s too bad. you don’t know me yet. we might even be friends.”

“friends, really?”

“why not?” Svidrigaïlov said with a smile. he stood, grabbed his hat. “didn’t mean to bother you, just curious after seeing you this morning.”

“where?”

“saw you by chance... thought you seemed familiar. don’t worry, i’m not nosy. i’ve rubbed elbows with all sorts, never bored the big shots, and i’ve got a few tales to tell.”

“okay. heading off soon?”

“heading where?”

“that ‘journey’ you mentioned.”

“oh, the journey. well, that’s a big topic... maybe marriage instead. they’re setting me up.”

“here?”

“yeah.”

“how’d you manage that?”

"i really want to see Avdotya Romanovna just once. gotta ask for it. anyway, goodbye for now. oh, wait. almost forgot. tell your sister, Rodion Romanovitch, Marfa Petrovna left her three thousand roubles in her will. that's definite. she set it up a week before she died, and i was there. Avdotya should get the money in two or three weeks."

"are you serious?"

"yeah, tell her. well, see you around. im staying close by."

as he left, Svidrigaïlov bumped into Razumihin at the door.

CHAPTER II

almost eight now. the two guys rushed to Bakaleyev’s to beat Luzhin there.

"who was that?" Razumihin asked once outside.

"that was Svidrigaïlov, the landlord where my sister got insulted while she was the governess. he kept pestering her, and his wife, Marfa Petrovna, kicked her out. Marfa later asked for Dounia's forgiveness and just died suddenly. we talked about her this morning. i don’t know why, but that guy scares me. he showed up right after his wife's funeral. he's up to something... we need to protect Dounia... that's why i needed to talk to you, got it?"

"protect her! what's he gonna do to Avdotya Romanovna? thanks, Rodya, for telling me... we will, we'll protect her. where does he live?"

"i don’t know."

"why didn’t you ask? too bad! i’ll figure it out."

"did you see him?" Raskolnikov asked after a pause.

"yeah, i spotted him, got a good look."

"you really saw him? clearly?" Raskolnikov pressed.

"yeah, i’d recognize him anywhere; i’m good with faces."

silence again.

"Hm!... alright," Raskolnikov muttered. "you know, i thought... maybe it was a hallucination."

"what do you mean? i don’t follow."

"well, everyone says," Raskolnikov smiled crookedly, "that im mad. thought maybe i really am, and it's all just a ghost."

"what do you mean?"

"who knows? maybe im mad, and everything that's happened is just in my head."

"Rodya, you’re stressed again!... but what did he say, why’d he come?"

Raskolnikov stayed quiet. Razumihin thought a bit.

"let me tell you what happened," he started. "i came to you, you were sleeping. we had dinner, then i went to Porfiry’s. Zametov was still there. tried to talk but it didn't work. they don’t get it and don’t care. i pulled Porfiry aside, tried again, still no use. we both looked away. finally, i shook my fist at him, said id bash him. he just stared, i cursed, left. stupid. didn’t say a word to Zametov. thought i messed up, but downstairs i had a great idea: why worry? unless you're in danger, why care? don't give a damn. we'll laugh later, and if i were you, id mess with them even more. they'll be so embarrassed! forget them! let's laugh now!"

"yeah, for sure," Raskolnikov replied, but he was already thinking about tomorrow. Funny, never crossed his mind what Razumihin would think once he knew. He glanced at him, not really caring about Razumihin’s story about Porfiry, too much happened since then.

In the hallway, they bumped into Luzhin. He showed up right on time at eight, looking for the number, so all three walked in together, not a word or look between them. The young guys went in first, and Pyotr Petrovitch hung back a bit, taking off his coat. Pulcheria Alexandrovna greeted him at the door, while Dounia was saying hi to her brother. Pyotr Petrovitch came in, all polite and dignified, gave the ladies a nod. He seemed a bit off, like he was still trying to get his bearings. Pulcheria Alexandrovna, also a bit flustered, hurried to get them seated at the table with the samovar. Dounia and Luzhin sat opposite each other, Razumihin and Raskolnikov across from Pulcheria, with Razumihin next to Luzhin and Raskolnikov by his sister.

Silence hung for a moment. Pyotr Petrovitch took out a fancy, scented handkerchief, blew his nose like a man who felt snubbed but was determined to get answers. Thought about leaving his coat on and walking out to make a point, but he needed answers. If they ignored his request, something was up, and he wanted to know now. Punishment could wait.

“Hope your trip was good,” he asked Pulcheria Alexandrovna, all formal.

“Oh, very, Pyotr Petrovitch.”

“Glad to hear. And Avdotya Romanovna, not too tired?”

“I’m young and strong, I don’t tire easily, but it was hard on mother,” Dounia replied.

“Can’t be helped! Our railways are endless. ‘Mother Russia’ is huge. I wanted to meet you yesterday, but couldn’t. Hope it wasn’t too rough?”

“Oh, no, Pyotr Petrovitch, it was disheartening,” Pulcheria Alexandrovna quickly said, “and if Dmitri Prokofitch hadn’t been sent by God Himself, we’d be lost. Here he is! Dmitri Prokofitch Razumihin.”

“Met yesterday,” Pyotr Petrovitch muttered with a sidelong glare at Razumihin, then went silent.

Pyotr Petrovitch was one of those overly polite types who fall apart when things don't go their way, turning into lifeless sacks of flour rather than the lively gentlemen they try to be. Silence again; Raskolnikov said nothing, Avdotya Romanovna hesitated to start talking. Razumihin had no words, so Pulcheria Alexandrovna grew anxious.

“Marfa Petrovna passed, did you hear?” she finally said, bringing up her main topic.

“Yeah, heard right away. Came to let you know Arkady Ivanovitch Svidrigaïlov rushed off to Petersburg after the funeral. Got good sources for that.”

“To Petersburg? Here?” Dounia asked, worried, looking at her mom.

“Yep, and probably not without a plan, considering how fast he left and everything leading up to it.”

"man, won’t he just leave Dounia alone?" Pulcheria Alexandrovna burst out.

"i think neither you nor Avdotya Romanovna need to worry. unless you plan on reaching out to him. I'm keeping tabs on where he's staying."

"Oh, Pyotr Petrovitch, you scared me so bad," Pulcheria Alexandrovna continued. "i only saw him twice, but he seemed awful, just awful! i’m sure he's why Marfa Petrovna died."

"can't be sure about that. i've got solid info. maybe he sped things up with his behavior, but as for his overall character, i agree with you. i don't know his financial state or what Marfa Petrovna left him, but if he’s got money, he'll fall back into his old habits here in Petersburg. he’s a nasty piece of work. i think Marfa Petrovna, who fell for him and cleared his debts years ago, helped him out in another big way. she hushed up a criminal charge that could’ve sent him to Siberia. that’s the kind of guy he is, if you want to know."

"good heavens!" Pulcheria Alexandrovna gasped. Raskolnikov listened closely.

"are you sure about this evidence?" Dounia asked sharply.

"i’m just saying what Marfa Petrovna told me in secret. legally, the case wasn’t clear. there’s this woman, Resslich, who lent money and did odd jobs. Svidrigaïlov was close to her. she had a niece, a deaf and dumb girl, who was treated horribly. one day she was found hanging. they said it was suicide. but later, rumors spread that Svidrigaïlov was involved in something awful with her. it wasn’t proven — the info came from another unreliable woman. thanks to Marfa Petrovna’s money, it never reached the police. but the story’s telling. you must have heard about Philip, the servant who died six years ago."

"i heard he hanged himself."

"yeah, but it was Svidrigaïlov’s abuse that pushed him there."

"i don’t know about that," Dounia replied flatly. "i heard Philip was a hypochondriac, a sort of house philosopher, and that he hanged himself partly because Svidrigaïlov mocked him, not because of beatings. when i was there, he treated the servants well, and they liked him, even if they blamed him for Philip’s death."

"so, Avdotya Romanovna, you suddenly wanna defend him, huh?" Luzhin said, twisting his lips into a weird smile. "no doubt he's slick, especially with women. Marfa Petrovna, who passed in such a strange way, is proof of that. i'm just trying to help you and your mom with some advice, since he'll likely be up to his old tricks. i'm convinced he'll land back in debtor's prison. Marfa Petrovna wasn't planning to leave him anything substantial—just a little something, not enough to last a year with his lifestyle."

"please, Pyotr Petrovitch," Dounia said, "stop talking about Mr. Svidrigaïlov. it makes me miserable."

"he just visited me," Raskolnikov broke in for the first time.

everyone turned to him, even Pyotr Petrovitch perked up.

"about an hour and a half ago, he woke me up and introduced himself," Raskolnikov continued. "he seemed pretty cheerful, thinking we might become friends. he really wants to meet you, Dounia, and asked me to be there. he mentioned Marfa Petrovna left you three thousand roubles in her will, and you can get the money soon."

"thank God!" Pulcheria Alexandrovna cried, crossing herself. "pray for her soul, Dounia!"

"it’s true!" Luzhin confirmed.

"tell us more," Dounia urged Raskolnikov.

"he said he's not rich, and the estate's left to his kids with an aunt. he's staying nearby, but i didn’t ask where..."

"but what does he want to propose to Dounia?" Pulcheria Alexandrovna asked in a panic. "did he tell you?"

"yes."

"what was it?"

"i’ll tell you later."

Raskolnikov stopped talking and focused on his tea.

Pyotr Petrovitch checked his watch.

"i have a business meeting, so i’ll leave," he said with a hint of irritation, starting to get up.

"don’t go, Pyotr Petrovitch," Dounia said. "you planned to stay the evening. you wrote that you wanted to clear things up with mother."

"exactly, Avdotya Romanovna," Pyotr Petrovitch replied, sitting back down but still holding his hat. "i did want a serious talk with you and your mother. but since your brother can't speak freely about Svidrigaïlov's proposals in front of me, i also can't discuss certain serious matters openly... in front of others. plus, my main request was ignored..."

Looking aggrieved, Luzhin stayed silent.

"your request that my brother not be here was ignored because of me," Dounia said. "you wrote he insulted you; we need to clear that up, and you should reconcile. if Rodya insulted you, he _should_ and _will_ apologize."

Pyotr Petrovitch took a firmer stance.

"some insults, Avdotya Romanovna, can't be forgotten. there's a line you shouldn't cross; once crossed, there's no going back."

"That's not what I meant, Pyotr," Dounia cut in, sounding a bit impatient. "Our whole future hinges on getting this sorted out fast. Honestly, if you care about me at all, we've got to end this today, no matter how tough. If my brother's at fault, he'll apologize."

"I'm surprised you're framing it like this," Luzhin replied, getting worked up. "I admire you, but that doesn't mean I have to like everyone in your family. Even if I'm asking for your hand, I can't take on duties that don't fit..."

"Come on, Pyotr, don’t get so offended," Dounia jumped in again, trying to calm things down. "Be the sensible guy I thought you were. I've promised a lot—I'm engaged to you. Trust me on this, and I'll judge fairly. My role as judge surprises my brother as much as you. I told him to come today after your letter, but not what I planned. If things aren't fixed, I have to choose—you or him. That's the deal for both sides. I can't make the wrong choice. If it's for you, I'd break with my brother. If it's for him, I'd break with you. I need to know where everyone stands."

"Avdotya Romanovna," Luzhin said, all huffy, "your words matter too much; they're even offensive given our relationship. You're putting me on the same level as some kid and hinting you might break your promise to me. Saying 'you or he' makes me feel like I'm nothing to you... I can't just let that slide given our situation."

"What!" Dounia fired back, cheeks flushed. "I'm balancing your interest with what's been the most important part of my life, and you think I'm not valuing you enough?"

Raskolnikov gave a sarcastic grin, Razumihin squirmed, but Luzhin didn't budge; if anything, he got more stubborn and riled up, almost like he was enjoying the whole thing.

"Your love for a future partner, like your husband, should be greater than for your brother," he said, sounding all serious. "I'm not on the same level here... Even though I said I wouldn't bring this up around your brother, I need to ask your mother something important for my dignity's sake. Your son," he looked at Pulcheria Alexandrovna, "yesterday in front of Mr. Razsudkin (or is that right? sorry, forgot the name)" (nodding to Razumihin), "twisted my words from our private chat over coffee. I said marrying a girl who's been through tough times is better for character than one who's only known luxury. Your son blew it out of proportion and made it sound malicious, maybe based on your letters to him. I'd be glad, Pulcheria Alexandrovna, if you could clear this up for me. Could you tell me exactly how you wrote my words to Rodion?"

"I don't remember," Pulcheria Alexandrovna stammered. "I wrote what I thought I understood. Maybe Rodya exaggerated."

"He couldn't have exaggerated without you pushing it."

"Pyotr Petrovitch," Pulcheria Alexandrovna replied firmly, "the fact that Dounia and I are here shows we didn't take your words badly."

"Good point, mom," Dounia nodded.

"So it's my fault again," Luzhin grumbled.

"Well, Pyotr Petrovitch, you blame Rodion, but you wrote lies about him," Pulcheria Alexandrovna said, gaining confidence.

"I don't remember writing anything false."

"You wrote," Raskolnikov said sharply, not looking at Luzhin, "that I gave money to the daughter of the man killed, not the widow, and you used crude words to stir trouble in my family. That's slander."

"Excuse me, sir," Luzhin said, shaking with anger. "I described your behavior in my letter based on your family's questions. Show me one falsehood, prove you didn't waste money, and that the family isn't worthless."

"Frankly, with all your virtues, you're not worth the little finger of that unfortunate girl you're insulting."

"Would you let her hang around with your mother and sister?"

"I already have, just so you know. She sat with mom and Dounia today."

"Rodya!" Pulcheria Alexandrovna gasped. Dounia turned red, Razumihin frowned. Luzhin smirked sarcastically.

"See, Avdotya Romanovna," he said, "there's no way we can get along. I hope this is settled now. I'll leave so you can enjoy family time and secrets." He stood, grabbing his hat. "But as I go, I ask to avoid such meetings in the future. Especially you, Pulcheria Alexandrovna, since my letter was meant for you alone."

Pulcheria Alexandrovna felt a bit slighted.

you think you run the show here, Pyotr? Dounia explained why we didn’t follow your orders—it was for the best. but you act like you’re issuing commands. are we supposed to jump at every word? we're here because we counted on you, so maybe show some respect.

“that's not how it is, Pulcheria Alexandrovna, especially now with Marfa Petrovna's inheritance. fits your new attitude,” he sneered.

“sounds like you were banking on us being stuck,” Dounia shot back.

“well, i can’t count on that anymore, especially with Arkady Svidrigaïlov's secret offers. seems you’re quite interested,” he added.

“good grief!” Pulcheria Alexandrovna exclaimed.

Razumihin couldn’t sit still anymore.

“you ashamed, sister?” Raskolnikov asked.

“i am, Rodya,” Dounia said. “Pyotr Petrovitch, leave,” she demanded, boiling with anger.

Pyotr Petrovitch wasn’t expecting this. he thought he had control over us. his face went pale.

“if i leave now, Avdotya Romanovna, i’m not coming back. think about that,” he warned.

“insolence!” Dounia shouted, standing. “i don’t want you back.”

“is that it then?” Luzhin, shocked. “but i could protest, you know.”

“how dare you speak to her like that?” Pulcheria Alexandrovna fired back. “what rights have you got? should i hand my Dounia to someone like you? leave us. we were wrong to agree, especially me...”

“you promised me, Pulcheria Alexandrovna,” Luzhin raged. “and i’ve spent money because of it...”

that last bit was so typical of Pyotr Petrovitch, Raskolnikov couldn’t help but laugh through his anger. Pulcheria Alexandrovna was livid.

“expenses? what expenses? the conductor brought our trunk for free. we didn’t bind you; you bound us!”

“enough, mother, please,” Avdotya Romanovna begged. “Pyotr Petrovitch, just go!”

“i’m going, but one more thing,” he said, losing it. “your mother seems to forget i took you back after all the town gossip. i ignored public opinion for you, expecting some gratitude. now i see i was reckless ignoring everyone’s judgment...”

“does he want a beating?” Razumihin jumped up.

“you’re mean and spiteful!” Dounia yelled.

“Not a word. Not a move,” Raskolnikov snapped, holding Razumihin back. He stepped up to Luzhin, calm and clear, “Get out. Now. Not another word or...”

Pyotr Petrovitch stared at him, pale and seething, then turned and left. He was burning with a hatred aimed squarely at Raskolnikov. To him, Raskolnikov was the root of all his troubles. Funny thing was, even as he stormed down the stairs, he still thought he might patch things up with the women.

CHAPTER III

Up until the end, Luzhin never saw it coming. He was sure he could control two poor, defenseless women. His vanity and overconfidence blinded him. Coming from nothing, Pyotr Petrovitch loved to admire himself, convinced of his own brilliance. More than anything, he valued the money he’d earned, using it to put himself on par with those who once looked down on him.

When he threw Dounia’s past in her face, it was sincere outrage at what he saw as her ‘ingratitude.’ He knew the rumors were false, cleared up by Marfa Petrovna and dismissed by the town. But he prided himself on ‘lifting’ Dounia to his level, thinking it noble. He couldn't grasp why others didn’t see it that way. He went to Raskolnikov like a benefactor expecting praise. Now, heading down the stairs, he felt wronged and underappreciated.

dounia was a big deal to him; couldn't imagine not having her around. he'd been dreaming of marriage for years, but just kept saving up. in his mind, she was this perfect girl—poor, young, pretty, from a good family, who'd see him as her hero forever. he imagined all these romantic scenes after work, and now it was almost real. avdotya romanovna, with her beauty and education, seemed like the dream come true. she was everything he wanted. but then it all fell apart like a bad joke. he just made a small mistake, a little joke, and it blew up. he did love her in his own way, had her in his dreams, and now—gone. had to fix it the next day, had to settle everything. that other guy, svidrigaïlov, he was the real worry. so much to handle...

dounia blamed herself, hugged her mom, said she was tempted by his money but didn't know he was so awful. "don't blame me, brother!" she pleaded. her mom muttered, "god delivered us," still a bit shocked. soon they were laughing, but dounia kept remembering. pulcheria was surprised she felt relieved; she thought breaking with luzhin was bad. razumihin was thrilled, like a weight lifted, ready to devote himself. anything seemed possible now, but he was cautious. raskolnikov, though, just sat there, indifferent. he'd pushed to ditch luzhin, but now seemed the least bothered. dounia worried he was still mad, and pulcheria watched him carefully.

“what did svidrigaïlov say?” dounia asked, getting closer.

“yes, yes!” pulcheria alexandrovna chimed in.

raskolnikov looked up.

“he wants to give you ten thousand roubles and see you once, with me there.”

“see her? absolutely not!” pulcheria alexandrovna shouted. “and how dare he offer money!”

raskolnikov recapped his chat with svidrigaïlov, skipping the bits about marfa petrovna’s ghost to keep things simple.

“what did you tell him?” dounia asked.

“first, i said no messages. then he said he'd find a way to meet you without me. claims he’s over you, doesn’t want you marrying luzhin. it was all pretty confused.”

“what do you think of him, rodya? what impression did he give you?”

“honestly, i don’t get him. offers you ten thousand, yet not wealthy. says he’s leaving, but then forgets. talks about marrying some girl. he’s got a motive, probably a bad one. but he’s so clumsy if he’s plotting. i turned down the money for you, no question. he struck me as odd... almost like he’s mad. but maybe it’s an act. marfa petrovna’s death seems to have hit him hard.”

“god rest her soul,” pulcheria alexandrovna said. “i’ll always pray for her. without her three thousand, where’d we be, dounia? this morning, just three roubles left, and we were considering pawning her watch.”

dounia looked deep in thought over svidrigaïlov’s offer, almost shivering.

raskolnikov noticed her fear. “i think i’ll need to see him again,” he told her.

“we’ll keep an eye on him! i’ll track him!” razumihin declared. “rodya’s asked me to. ‘take care of my sister,’ he said. will you let me, avdotya romanovna?”

dounia gave a small smile, reaching out her hand, but worry stayed on her face. pulcheria alexandrovna watched her, still uneasy, but the three thousand roubles seemed to calm her down.

fifteen minutes later, they were deep in conversation. even raskolnikov was listening, though quiet. razumihin was doing the talking.

why leave? you're all here, you need each other. let's make something happen. take me in, i've got a plan. my uncle's got a thousand roubles doing nothing, wants me to take it. add a thousand from your side, and we start something real.

now, hear me out. publishers, they don't know what they're doing. been dreaming of going into publishing. two years in their offices, speak three languages. told Raskolnikov i was weak in German just to split a translation job. that was a lie, he knew it.

why miss the chance when we've got the funds? it'll be work, sure, but profitable. we know what needs translating and publishing. i got experience, know every detail of their business. you don't need to be a saint to make money. got ideas for books that'd pay a hundred roubles just for thinking of them. publishers wouldn't get it, but i do. we start small, grow big. at least we'd earn a living and get our money back.

Dounia's interested. 

“i like it, Dmitri,” she said.

Pulcheria, unsure. "it's new. untried. but we should stay for now." looking to Rodya. 

“what do you think, brother?” Dounia asked.

“he's got a solid idea,” he said. “too early to think about a publishing firm, but we could put out five or six books and they'd do fine. i know one that would sell. and he can handle it, no question. we’ll talk more later.”

“awesome!” Razumihin shouted. “there’s a flat here in this building, same owner. separate, furnished, rent’s reasonable, three rooms. why not start there? i'll pawn your watch tomorrow for cash, sort it all out. you three can live together, Rodya with you. but where’re you going, Rodya?”

“leaving already?” Pulcheria Alexandrovna asked, worried.

“at a time like this?” Razumihin added.

Dounia stared at her brother, confused. He had his cap in hand, ready to leave.

“sounds like you’re saying goodbye for good,” he said strangely. tried to smile, didn’t work. “but maybe it is the last time we see each other...” he slipped. he was thinking it, said it without meaning to.

“what’s wrong?” his mother cried.

“where you going, Rodya?” Dounia asked, puzzled.

“i just have to...” he replied, vague but firm.

“meant to say... coming here... meant to tell you, mom, Dounia, better we part for a while. i’m not well, not at peace. i'll come back... when i can. remember you, love you... leave me alone. decided this before... resolved. whatever happens, ruin or not, want to be alone. forget me, it's better. don’t ask about me. when i can, i’ll come or... send for you. maybe it’ll pass, but now if you love me, let me go... or i'll hate you, i feel it... goodbye!”

“oh my god!” Pulcheria Alexandrovna cried. both mother and sister were scared. Razumihin too.

“Rodya, make peace with us! let’s be like before!” his mother cried.

He slowly turned to the door, left the room. Dounia caught up to him.

“what are you doing to mom?” she whispered, angry.

He looked at her blankly.

“doesn’t matter, i’ll come... i’m coming,” he muttered, like he wasn't sure himself, and left.

“selfish, heartless!” Dounia yelled.

“he’s lost it, not heartless. he’s not right! you’re heartless saying that!” Razumihin whispered to her, holding her hand tight. “i’ll be back,” he shouted to the shocked mother, and ran out.

Raskolnikov waited at the passage’s end.

“knew you’d follow,” he said. “go back to them—stay with them... be with them always.... i... might come... if i can. goodbye.”

and he walked off without a handshake.

“where you going? what’s up? how can you do this?” Razumihin muttered, confused.

Raskolnikov stopped again.

“don’t ask me anything. got nothing to say. don’t visit. i might come here.... leave me, but _stay_ with them. got it?”

dark hallway. they're by the lamp, just staring. Razumihin never forgot that silence. Raskolnikov's eyes were burning, cutting right into him. something weird passed between them, like a silent message. Razumihin turned pale.

“you get it now?” Raskolnikov asked, his face twitching. “go back, go to them,” he said and quickly left.

i won't detail how Razumihin went back to comfort the ladies, insisting Rodya just needed rest. he promised Rodya would visit, that he'd get a doctor. from that night, Razumihin became like family to them.

chapter 4

Raskolnikov headed to Sonia's on the canal. old green three-story place. asked the porter about Kapernaumov, the tailor. found the stairway and went up to the second floor. wandered in the dark, lost. a door opened nearby; he grabbed it.

"who's there?" a woman asked nervously.

"it's me... here to see you," Raskolnikov replied, stepping into the tiny hallway.

a candle on a broken chair lit the space.

"it's you! oh my!" Sonia gasped, frozen.

"your room? this way?" Raskolnikov avoided her gaze, moving in.

Sonia joined him, candle in hand, looking overwhelmed and scared by the surprise visit. her face flushed, tears welled up... she felt a mix of emotions. Raskolnikov turned away, sat at the table, glanced around the room.

big, low-ceilinged room. only one rented by the Kapernaumovs. closed door on the left led to their place. another locked door on the right went to another flat. Sonia's room was odd-shaped, like a barn. three windows facing the canal, walls slanted, weird corners. hardly any furniture: bed in the right corner, chair by the door, plain table with a blue cloth nearby, two rush chairs. small dresser on the opposite wall, looking lost. yellow wallpaper, blackened at the corners, screamed poverty. no curtain on the bed.

sonia stood there, quiet, watching her visitor look around her room like he was searching for something. she started shaking, like he was some judge about to decide her whole life.

“im late, right? it’s eleven?” he asked, still looking down.

“yes,” sonia mumbled, “it is,” like saying it would help her somehow. “my landlady’s clock just struck... i heard it.”

“i’ve come for the last time,” raskolnikov said, sounding heavy, though it was his first visit. “i might not see you again...”

“are you leaving?”

“maybe... tomorrow....”

“you’re not visiting katerina ivanovna tomorrow?” sonia’s voice wavered.

“dont know. i’ll know by morning.... doesnt matter: i’ve got one thing to say....”

he looked up, realizing she was still standing while he sat.

“why are you standing? sit down,” he said, voice shifting to gentle.

she sat. he looked at her, kind of sad.

“you’re so thin! your hand’s like... almost not there, like a ghost's.”

he took her hand. sonia smiled a bit.

“always been like this,” she said.

“even back home?”

“yes.”

“right, of course,” he snapped back, voice and face shifting again.

he glanced around.

“you rent from the kapernaumovs?”

“yes....”

“they live next door?”

“yes.... they have a room like this.”

“all in one room?”

“yes.”

“i’d be scared here at night,” he said, darkly.

“they’re good people, really kind,” sonia replied, still a bit lost, “everything here’s theirs. they’re kind and the kids visit.”

“they all stammer, dont they?”

“yes.... he stammers and is lame. his wife, not stammering, but she struggles to speak. she’s kind. he was a house serf. seven kids... only the oldest stammers, the rest just sick... but not stammering. how do you know?”

“your father told me. everything... how you left at six, back by nine, how katerina ivanovna knelt by your bed.”

sonia was thrown off.

“thought i saw him today,” she whispered.

“who?”

“father. on the street, corner, ten o’clock. looked like him. i wanted to see katerina ivanovna....”

“you were out walking?”

“yes,” sonia whispered, embarrassed, looking down.

“katerina ivanovna used to hit you?”

“oh no, no!” sonia looked shocked.

“you love her?”

“love her? yes!” sonia said, hands clasped, looking distressed. “you dont get it! she’s like a child... mind broken from sorrow. she used to be so smart... so kind! you don’t understand!”

sonia said it like she was desperate, hands wringing in distress. her cheeks flushed, eyes full of pain. she was deeply moved, wanted to defend, to express something. her face showed a deep compassion.

"beat me! how can you? she's so unhappy, man. she's trying to find righteousness everywhere. she thinks the world should be fair and it's driving her mad. like a kid. she's good."

"what about you?"

sonia looked confused.

"they're on you now. they were before too. your dad came to you for drink. how's it going now?"

"i don’t know," sonia said, sounding down.

"are they gonna stay?"

"i don’t know... they owe rent and the landlady wants them out. katerina ivanovna says she won't stay."

"why's she so bold? is she depending on you?"

"no, don’t say that... we're like one. what else could she do?" sonia got upset, like a small angry bird. "her mind's not right, haven’t you seen? one minute she's worried about tomorrow's lunch, then she's crying and hitting her head against the wall. then she's all hope again. she thinks you’ll help her, and we'll start a new life. she believes it so much. can’t even argue against her. today she was cleaning and washing all day. we went to get shoes for polenka and lida, but didn’t have enough money. she picked such nice boots, but then broke down crying in the store... it was hard to watch."

"well, i get why you're living like this," raskolnikov said with a bitter smile.

"don’t you feel sorry for them? you gave your last penny, even when you didn’t know the whole story. if you knew everything... how often i've made her cry! just last week, before he died. i was cruel! been thinking about it all day," sonia said, wringing her hands.

"you were cruel?"

yeah, i went to see them. father wanted me to read to him, said his head hurt. he had this book from andrey semyonovitch, always got these odd ones. i didn’t want to read, was really there to show katerina ivanovna some collars. lizaveta sold me some cheap, nice ones, embroidered. katerina was thrilled, tried them on, checked herself in the mirror. asked me to give them to her. she never asks for anything, always giving away stuff. but these, she really wanted. i felt bad saying no. she was hurt, not by the collars, but by me refusing. wish i could take back those words.

“did you know lizaveta?”

“yeah... did you?” sonia asked, surprised.

katerina’s got consumption, it’s bad. she’ll die soon, raskolnikov said after a pause.

“no, no, no!”

sonia grabbed his hands, begging it not to be so.

“might be better if she did, though.”

“no, it’s not better!” sonia repeated, scared.

“what about the kids? you’ll take them in?”

“i don’t know,” sonia cried, almost breaking down. she’d thought of it before, he just brought it up again.

“what if you get sick and go to the hospital now? what then?” he pressed on.

“how can you say that?” sonia’s face twisted in fear.

“not insured, are you? what happens to them then? she’ll beg, knock her head on walls, the kids will cry. she’ll end up in the hospital, she’ll die, and the kids...”

“oh no... god won’t let it happen!” sonia cried, desperate.

raskolnikov started pacing. sonia stood there, head down, deeply upset.

“can’t you save? for a rainy day?” he asked, stopping in front of her.

“no,” sonia whispered.

“did you try?”

“yes.”

“and it didn’t work. no surprise.”

he paced again. a minute passed.

“you don’t get money daily?”

sonia blushed, more confused.

“no,” she whispered, struggling.

“same will happen with polenka, right?”

“no, no! it can’t!” sonia shouted, desperate, like she’d been stabbed. “god wouldn’t let it be so awful!”

he lets others find their way.

"no, no! god will protect her, god!" she repeated, almost losing it.

"but maybe there's no god at all," raskolnikov shot back, kinda nasty, laughed, and looked at her.

sonia's face shifted; a shiver ran through it. she looked at him with deep reproach, tried to speak but just broke into bitter sobs, hiding her face in her hands.

"you say katerina ivanovna's lost it; your mind's lost too," he said after a pause.

five minutes ticked by. he paced the room, not looking at her. finally, he approached her; his eyes sharp and feverish. hands on her shoulders, he looked straight into her tear-streaked face. his lips twitched. suddenly, he dropped and kissed her foot. sonia backed away like he was crazy. and, yeah, he looked the part.

"what are you doing to me?" she whispered, pale, heart squeezing.

he stood up.

"didn't bow to you, bowed to all human suffering," he said wild-eyed, then turned to the window. "listen," he said a minute later. "i told an arrogant guy he wasn't worth your little finger... said i honored my sister by having her sit with you."

"oh, you said that? with her there?" sonia cried, scared. "sit with me! an honor! why, i'm... dishonorable... why'd you say that?"

"not 'cause of your dishonor, but your great suffering. but you are a big sinner," he added solemnly, "your worst sin’s betraying yourself for nothing. isn't that terrifying? living in this filth you hate, knowing it helps no one? tell me," he pressed, "how can this shame live alongside holy feelings? better to end it all!"

"but what about them?" sonia asked weakly, looking at him in anguish, not shocked by his words.

raskolnikov gave her a strange look. he saw it in her face; she'd thought of ending it before. she didn't even catch the cruelty in his words. it was clear how her shame tormented her. he finally understood what those orphan kids and the half-mad katerina ivanovna meant to her.

ok so he's looking at Sonia, trying to figure out how she's held it together. she's been through hell, but still standing. he's wondering why she hasn't cracked. he figures three paths: madness, the canal, or going numb and losing herself. he hates the last one but can't shake it.

then he asks her about God. she clings to that, says she'd be nothing without it. she gets mad when he pushes. that's her anchor. she's not crazy, just holding on to faith. he sees it, but can't fully get it. it's like she's a puzzle he can't solve.

he spots a worn New Testament. asks her where it's from. Lizaveta brought it. that’s another piece of the puzzle. something about this feels off but also makes sense. he’s stuck on that thought.

everything about sonia felt stranger and more amazing by the second. he picked up the book, went to the candle, and started flipping through.

“where's the story of lazarus?” he asked out of nowhere.

sonia stared at the floor, not answering. she stood sideways to the table.

“find the raising of lazarus for me, sonia.”

she glanced at him.

“youre looking in the wrong place... it's in the fourth gospel,” she whispered, sternly, eyes still down.

“read it to me,” he said, sitting down, elbow on the table, head in hand, looking away, ready to listen.

“three weeks and they'll have me in the madhouse, if not worse,” he muttered.

sonia heard him, unsure, and moved hesitantly to the table. she picked up the book.

“haven't you read it?” she asked, looking at him.

her voice grew sharper.

“long ago... back in school. read!”

“and not in church?”

“i... haven’t been. do you go often?”

“n-no,” she whispered.

he smiled.

“i see... and you won’t go to your father’s funeral tomorrow?”

“yes, i will. i was at church last week too... had a requiem service.”

“for who?”

“lizaveta. she was killed with an axe.”

his nerves stretched thin. head spinning.

“were you friends with lizaveta?”

“yes... she was good... she came... not often... couldn’t... we read and talked. she will see God.”

the last bit sounded strange to him. another twist: secret meetings with lizaveta, both religious maniacs.

“i'll be a religious maniac soon! it’s catching!”

“read!” he snapped, insistent.

sonia hesitated, heart racing. she barely dared to read. he looked at her, almost angry at the “unhappy lunatic.”

sonia opened the book, found the spot. hands shaking, voice faltered. twice she tried, but couldn’t start.

“now a certain man was sick named lazarus of bethany...” she finally began, but her voice broke on the third word. a catch in her breath.
