raskolnikov saw why sonia struggled to read and pushed harder, rough and irritable. he knew how painful it was for her to expose what was deeply her own. he saw these feelings as her secret treasure, guarded for years, maybe since childhood, living with a troubled father, a stepmother crazed by grief, amidst hungry children and harsh words. but he also knew she had a need, however painful, to read to him, to read now, no matter the cost. he read this in her eyes, her intense emotion. she steadied herself, controlled the spasm, and continued with the eleventh chapter of st. john. she went on to the nineteenth verse:

“and many of the jews came to martha and mary to comfort them concerning their brother.

“then martha as soon as she heard that jesus was coming went and met him: but mary sat still in the house.

“then said martha unto jesus, lord, if thou hadst been here, my brother had not died.

“but i know that even now whatsoever thou wilt ask of god, god will give it thee....”

then she stopped, feeling her voice might break again.

"jesus said, thy brother shall rise again."

martha replied, "i know he'll rise at the resurrection, on the last day."

jesus told her, "i am the resurrection and the life: whoever believes in me, even if dead, will live. whoever lives and believes in me shall never die. do you believe this?"

she took a breath and said clearly, "yes, lord: i believe you are the christ, the son of god who should come into the world."

she paused, looked up, but kept reading. raskolnikov sat still, elbows on the table, eyes turned away. she read to the thirty-second verse.

"when mary reached jesus and saw him, she fell at his feet, saying, 'lord, if you had been here, my brother wouldn’t have died.'

"jesus, seeing her weeping, and the others weeping too, groaned in spirit and was troubled,

"and asked, 'where have you laid him?' they said, 'lord, come and see.'

"jesus wept.

"then they said, 'see how he loved him!'

"but some said, 'couldn't this man who opened the eyes of the blind, have kept this man from dying?'"

raskolnikov looked at her, feeling it. she was trembling, feverish. he expected it. she was nearing the miracle's story, feeling triumph. her voice strong, lines blurred, but she knew them by heart. at the last verse, she echoed the doubt, the reproach of the disbelievers who'd soon fall at jesus's feet, believing. she dreamed he, too, would hear, believe. she was trembling with anticipation.

"jesus, groaning, came to the grave. it was a cave, and a stone lay on it.

"jesus said, 'take away the stone.' martha, the dead man's sister, said, 'lord, he stinks by now: he's been dead four days.'"

she hit the word _four_ hard.

"jesus replied, 'did i not tell you that if you believed, you'd see god's glory?'

"they removed the stone. jesus lifted his eyes, saying, 'father, i thank you for hearing me. i know you always hear me; but for the people's sake, i said it, so they may believe you sent me.'

"he then called out, 'lazarus, come forth.'

"and the dead man came out."

she read loudly, trembling with ecstasy, as if witnessing it.

"bound hand and foot with graveclothes; his face wrapped. jesus said, 'loose him, let him go.'

"many who saw what jesus did, believed on him."

she couldn't read more, closed the book, and got up quickly.

that's the story of Lazarus, she said, sharp and sudden, turning away, not daring to meet his eyes. she was still shaking. the candle was almost out, flickering in the shabby candlestick, barely lighting the room where a killer and a prostitute read together. minutes passed.

"i came to talk," Raskolnikov said, frowning, getting up to go to Sonia. she looked at him silently. his face was hard, determined.

"i left my family today," he said. "my mother, my sister. im done with them."

"why?" Sonia asked, shocked. she'd met them recently, and it stuck with her.

"it's just you now," he said. "let's go together... we're both cursed, let's walk this path together!"

Sonia thought he looked mad.

"go where?" she asked, stepping back.

"how should i know? but it's the same road, same end."

she looked at him, lost, seeing only his deep unhappiness.

"no one else will get it, but i do. i need you, that's why im here."

"i dont get it," Sonia whispered.

"you will. haven't you done the same? crossed the line? you've destroyed your life... your own (it's all the same!). you could've lived differently, but you'll end up lost... alone, you'll lose it like me. you're already on the edge. we gotta walk this road together!"

"why? what's the point?" Sonia asked, shaken by his words.

"because you can't stay like this! face it, stop crying like a kid that God won't allow it. what if you're taken to the hospital tomorrow? she's sick, dying, and the kids? think Polenka won't suffer? haven't you seen kids begging, sent by their mothers? i've seen where they live. kids can't stay kids there! by seven, they're thieves. yet, children are Christ's image: 'theirs is the kingdom of Heaven.' he told us to honor them, they're the future...."

"what do we do, what do we do?" Sonia cried, hands wringing.

"break what needs breaking, take the suffering. don't get it? you will... freedom and power, that's the goal! remember that! this might be the last time i talk to you. if i don't come tomorrow, you'll hear about it, remember my words. someday, you'll understand. if i come tomorrow, i'll tell you who killed Lizaveta... goodbye."

Sonia was terrified.

"wait, you know who did it?" she asked, horrified.

i know and will tell you, only you. picked you out. not asking forgiveness, just telling you. chose you long ago to hear this, when your dad talked of you and when Lizaveta was alive, i thought of it. goodbye, don't shake hands. tomorrow.

he left. Sonia stared like he was nuts. but she felt it too, like she was losing it. head spinning.

how does he know who killed Lizaveta? what does that mean? awful! but didn't even think the idea. oh, he must be so unhappy! left his mom and sister... why? what happened? what did he mean? kissed her foot and said... said he can't live without her. oh, heavens!

Sonia spent the night feverish, out of it. jumped up, cried, wrung her hands, then back to fever dreams of Polenka, Katerina Ivanovna and Lizaveta, reading the gospel, and him... pale face, burning eyes... kissing her feet, crying.

next door, the room's been empty a while. sign on the gate, notice in the window over the canal, for rent. Sonia used to it being empty. but Mr. Svidrigailov was there, listening at the door. when Raskolnikov left, he thought a moment, snuck to his room next door, got a chair, put it by Sonia's door. conversation was interesting, so much he wanted to sit next time, maybe tomorrow, instead of standing an hour to listen.

next morning at eleven, Raskolnikov walks into the criminal investigation office. sends his name in to Porfiry Petrovitch. thought they’d be on him right away, but he stands there waiting, at least ten minutes. folks just walking past him. clerks in the next room writing away, no clue who he is. looks around, half expecting some guard or something to keep him from leaving, but nothing. just clerks and people, busy with their stuff. feels like if that guy from yesterday really saw what happened, they wouldn’t have him just standing there. starts thinking maybe that guy didn’t say anything or didn’t see anything at all. maybe it was just his mind playing tricks, his imagination running wild.

realizes he’s trembling, irritated he’s scared of facing Porfiry Petrovitch. hates the guy, scared it’ll show. stops shaking, decides to go in with a cold, silent front. ready to listen more than talk, control those nerves. then he’s called in.

finds Porfiry alone in a not too big, not too small study. got a big writing table, checked sofa, some government-looking furniture. Porfiry closes the door behind him, acts friendly enough but there’s a bit of awkwardness like he’s hiding something.

“ah, here you are in our domain,” Porfiry says, all friendly, hands out but doesn’t quite shake. Raskolnikov sits, watching him close. those little things, like the “in our domain” bit and the French phrase, all typical Porfiry. both watching each other, eyes meeting then darting away.

“brought the paper about the watch,” Raskolnikov says, handing it over. “is it good or need another go?”

"huh? paper? yeah, it's all good," Porfiry said quick, barely looking at it before tossing it on the table. 

A minute later, still talking, he moved it to his bureau.

"you said yesterday you wanted to ask me, officially, about my connection to the murdered woman?" Raskolnikov started again. "why'd I say 'I believe'?" shot through his mind. "why's that make me so uneasy?" and bam, the tension shot up. Just being around Porfiry, his first words, first look, it all felt dangerous. His nerves shot, emotions high. "this ain't good, im gonna spill too much again."

"yeah, yeah, no rush, no rush," Porfiry muttered, zigzagging around like he had no direction, avoiding Raskolnikov's eyes, then staring him down.

His round little body moved like a ball bouncing around.

"we got time. you smoke? got your own? here, have a cigarette!" he offered, "im seeing you here, but my place is over there, government quarters, been living outside while they fix it up. almost done... government quarters are great, huh?"

"yeah, great," Raskolnikov replied, almost mocking.

"great, great," Porfiry echoed, like thinking of something else. "yeah, great," he shouted suddenly, staring hard at Raskolnikov, stopping a couple steps away.

That dumb echo clashed with his serious, deep look.

Raskolnikov's annoyance peaked, pushing him to challenge Porfiry.

"tell me," he asked, almost rude, enjoying it, "isn't it a lawyer thing to start with something trivial, irrelevant, to throw someone off before hitting 'em with a big question? it's a tradition in all the manuals, right?"

"yeah, yeah... you think that's why i brought up government quarters... huh?"

porfiry petrovitch squinted, gave this crafty little wink. his face loosened up, eyes narrowed, and then he just busted out laughing right at raskolnikov. raskolnikov tried to laugh along, but then porfiry's laugh turned into this huge thing, face going red, and raskolnikov just stopped, glaring at him. porfiry didn't seem fazed, kept on laughing like he didn't care. raskolnikov started thinking maybe he'd walked into something without knowing it.

he stood up, grabbed his cap, and got straight to it. "porfiry petrovitch," he snapped, "you wanted me here for some inquiries yesterday." really hit that word. "so, here i am. ask away or let me go. got a funeral to be at, that guy who got run over, you know." he was mad about adding that bit, madder for getting mad about it. "i'm fed up. it's making me sick. just get on with it or let me leave. and do it right, or i'm gone."

porfiry switched it up, stopped laughing. "what do you mean, what should i ask?" he said, bustling around trying to get raskolnikov to sit. "no rush, no rush. glad you're here, just visiting. my laughter, sorry, rodion romanovitch, right? nerves, you know. sometimes i laugh like crazy, afraid of a stroke. sit, please, or i'll think you're mad."

raskolnikov didn't say a word, still frowning. he sat but kept holding his cap.

so here's the thing, Rodion Romanovitch. im just a bachelor, not really anyone important, not used to all these social games. life's pretty much set for me, nothing new coming. you ever notice how when two smart guys who respect each other but aren't close meet up, it's awkward? like, it takes forever to find something to talk about. we're just sitting there, feeling weird. high society folks always have something to chat about. but guys like us, thinkers, we freeze up. why's that? maybe we're too honest or just not interested in small talk, i dont know. what do you think? put your cap down, you're making me nervous thinking you're about to leave...

Raskolnikov put down his cap, stayed quiet, just listening with a serious look as Porfiry rambled on. "is he trying to throw me off with this nonsense?"

can't offer you coffee here, but why not hang out for a bit? these official duties, man... dont mind me pacing, i need the exercise. sitting all day is killing me. always think about joining a gym. even high-ups go there, it's what they say about keeping fit. but these duties, these inquiries... they're sometimes harder on me than on the person i'm questioning. you said it yourself, smart observation. (raskolnikov hadn't said anything like that.) it gets confusing, like playing one note on a drum over and over. reform's coming, we'll get new titles. and our legal tradition? spot on, every prisoner knows the game, irrelevant questions first, then the real hit. you nailed it. thought you meant something else by 'government quarters'? you're a funny guy. anyway, formality, you brought it up. it's often useless. a chat gets more done sometimes. we can always go back to formalities if needed. investigating, it's its own kind of art, really.

Porfiry stopped to catch his breath. he was just filling the air with random thoughts, throwing in some puzzling bits, then back to rambling. almost buzzing around the room, quick little steps, looking down, one hand behind his back, the other waving around like it had a mind of its own. Raskolnikov noticed him pausing near the door a couple of times like he was listening for something.

"what's he waiting for?"

"you're right about that," Porfiry started, looking straight at Raskolnikov, which threw him off. "you're right to laugh at our legal process, ha! some of these psychological tricks are over the top, kind of pointless if you stick too much to the rules. yep, talking forms again. say I suspect someone of a crime in my case... you're studying law, right, Rodion Romanovitch?"

"yeah, I was..."

"well, it's a precedent for you. but hey, not trying to teach you after your crime articles! just stating a fact. if I think someone's guilty, why press them early, even if I have proof? sometimes you gotta arrest right away, but other times, why not let them roam a bit? ha! maybe if I lock him up too soon, I might just give him moral support, ha! you laughing?"

Raskolnikov wasn't laughing. he sat there tight-lipped, eyes locked on Porfiry.

alright, here's how it goes. people are different, especially men — that's just how it is. you talk about 'evidence'. sure, there might be evidence, but you can usually interpret it in a couple of ways. i'm an examining lawyer, and yeah, not the strongest man around. i'd love to lay out a proof as clear as 2+2=4, something solid and undeniable. but if i pin him down too early, even if i'm sure he's the guy, i might miss out on more evidence. how? if i give him a clear position, he'll relax, go into his shell. 

think of Sevastopol after Alma — folks were scared the enemy would attack outright, take it fast. but when they saw a siege coming, they were kinda relieved, knowing it’d drag on. you’re chuckling, don't think i’m serious? you’re not wrong. these are unique situations. but listen, Rodion Romanovitch, the general case, the one all legal stuff is built for, doesn't really exist. every case, every crime, is unique once it happens, often unlike anything before. funny things happen like that.

leave a guy alone, let him know i’m onto him, watching day and night, keep him scared, he'll slip up. might be obvious as 2+2=4. for a simple guy maybe, but for someone smart, it’s a sure thing. it’s crucial to know what makes a guy tick. and nerves—everyone’s got them, all edgy and irritable! they’re a gold-mine for us. i don't worry about him roaming free. let him! i’ve got him, he can’t run. where’d he go? abroad? a Pole might, but not here. into the countryside? not likely—the city guy would rather be locked up than live with peasants. 

it’s not just geography—it’s psychology. he can’t escape me, no matter what. like a moth to a flame, he’ll circle, get tangled in his own head, wear himself out. and eventually, he’ll give me that solid proof i need, if i just wait. he’ll circle closer and closer, and then—bam! right into my grasp. funny, isn't it? you don’t buy it?

Raskolnikov's just sitting there, pale like a ghost, staring at Porfiry like he could see right through him.

"This is more than a game," he figures, feeling a chill. "Yesterday was cat and mouse. Today? There's more to it. Porfiry's too smart to just show off. What's his angle? Nah, he's trying to rattle me. He doesn't have proof. That guy I saw? Never real. He just wants my head spinning before he makes his move. But it's not gonna work. Why drop hints though? Thinks my nerves are shot? Wrong again, pal. Whatever trap you've set, I'm ready for it."

He's gearing up for whatever's coming, even if it's something brutal. Sometimes he wants to just jump Porfiry, take him down. That anger's been simmering from the start. His lips are dry, heart's pounding, but he's holding back. Staying quiet's the game plan. Talk too much, you slip up. Silence might just push Porfiry to spill more than he wants. At least, that's the hope.

nah, i see you’re not buying it, think im just messing around with you, porfiry started up again, getting all animated, chuckling like he’s got a secret, pacing the room like it's got answers. “and yeah, you’re probably right: ive got the kind of look that makes folks laugh more than listen; a clown. but listen, and i say it again, forgive an old man, rodion romanovitch, youre young, just starting out, so you put brains above all, like most young folks do. sharp wit and big ideas catch your eye, a bit like the old austrian war council, if i know my military stuff. they’d beat napoleon on paper, had him caught all neat and tidy, but then general mack just gave up with his whole army, he-he-he! i see you’re grinning at me, a civilian, pulling from military lore! can’t help it, love that military stuff. always reading those histories. missed my calling, should’ve been in the army. not napoleon, but maybe a major, he-he! so here’s the truth, my friend, about this special case: facts and a man’s nature are tricky, and they can fool even the sharpest mind! listen to an old soul—this is serious, rodion romanovitch” (porfiry petrovitch, not even thirty-five, suddenly looking older like he’s carrying years on his back) “plus, im an honest guy... am i? what do you think? maybe i am: telling you all this for free, not expecting a thing, he-he! so, moving on, wit is a beautiful thing, a natural gift, a life saver, and oh the tricks it can play! it can twist up even the cleverest lawyer, especially if he gets swept up in his own imaginings, cause he’s just human! but the criminal’s own nature, that’s the kicker! young folks, riding high on their wit, don’t see that when they think they’ve jumped every hurdle, like you cleverly said yesterday. they’ll lie—the guy in the special case, the unknown, lies like a pro; you’d think he’d win big, reap his cleverness, but right when it’s all crucial, he’ll faint. sure, it could be sickness or a hot room, but still! still, it gives us a clue! he lied brilliantly, but didn’t count on his own nature. that’s what trips him up! next, he’ll use his wit to mock the guy who suspects him, turning pale to fake it, but his paleness looks too real, too spot on, another clue! the questioner might be fooled at first, but he’ll catch on later if he’s got any sense, and that’s the game! he pushes in where he’s not needed, speaks when he should shut up, throws in all sorts of hints, he-he! comes along asking why wasn’t he caught sooner? he-he-he! and this, you know, even the smartest, the psychologist, the writer can fall for it. temperament mirrors everything! look in it and see what’s there! but why’re you so pale, rodion romanovitch? room too stuffy? want me to crack a window?”

"nah, don’t bother," Raskolnikov chuckled, breaking into laughter. "really, don’t."

Porfiry stood there, paused, then joined in the laugh. Raskolnikov jumped up, cutting off his laughter.

"Porfiry Petrovitch," he said, loud and clear, though shaking like a leaf. "I get it now, you think I killed that old woman and Lizaveta. Well, I'm done with this. If you think you can arrest me, then do it. But I won't stand for being mocked and harassed."

His lips quivered, eyes blazing, voice rising.

"i won’t stand for it!" he shouted, slamming his fist on the table. "hear me, Porfiry Petrovitch? i won’t allow it."

"What's happening?" Porfiry Petrovitch said, looking pretty shaken. "Rodion Romanovitch, my friend, what's wrong?"

"i won’t allow it," Raskolnikov yelled again.

"Quiet, man! They'll hear us. What'll we say?" Porfiry whispered, leaning in close.

"i won’t allow it," Raskolnikov repeated, whispering now.

Porfiry ran to crack open a window.

"Air! You need water, my friend. You're not well!" He was about to call out but grabbed a decanter instead. "Drink this," he whispered, rushing over. "It'll help."

Porfiry's concern seemed real, and Raskolnikov stared at him, curious. But he didn’t drink.

"Rodion Romanovitch, you'll lose your mind at this rate, I swear. Drink some."

He made him take the glass. Raskolnikov lifted it, then put it back, disgusted.

"Yeah, you’re having a fit! You’ll make yourself sick again," Porfiry babbled, still uneasy. "Take care of yourself! Dmitri Prokofitch was here yesterday—talked my ear off! Did he come from you? Sit down, please!"

"No, not from me, but I knew he went to see you and why," Raskolnikov snapped.

"You knew?"

"I knew. So what?"

ok Rodion Romanovitch, i know more than you think. i know you went to check out a flat at night, asked about the blood. workmen and the porter were puzzled. i get why you did it, but youre gonna drive yourself nuts. you're mad at the world, at the cops, so you keep pushing for answers, trying to end the nonsense. but doing that? you'll lose it, and drag Razumihin down too. he's too good for this mess. you're sick, and it's rubbing off on him... we'll talk more when you're clearer. sit down, you look terrible.

Raskolnikov sat, no longer shivering, just hot. listened to Porfiry Petrovitch who looked worried but friendly. didn't buy a word, though felt like he wanted to. Porfiry mentioning the flat stunned him. "how does he know about the flat?" he thought. 

“had a case like this in law, a guy confessed to murder, convinced everyone, but he was just messed up in the head. turns out he wasn't guilty. Court of Appeal sorted it. same thing here, Rodion. you keep playing with your nerves, ringing bells, asking about blood. it's an illness. you need a real doctor, not that quack. you're lightheaded, delirious!”

Raskolnikov felt dizzy. “is he lying?” he thought, “no, can't be.” the thought of it made him furious.

“i wasn't delirious. i knew exactly what i was doing,” he shouted, trying to see through Porfiry's angle. “i was in control, you hear?”

“yes, i hear you. you said yesterday you weren't delirious. got it! but listen, if you were mixed up in this, would you insist you weren't delirious? seems like you’d claim the opposite. right?”

Porfiry’s got that sly look again. Raskolnikov pulled back on the sofa, eyeing him, confused.

"About Razumihin—you should’ve said he came on his own. But you don’t hide it. You highlight that he came because you asked."

Raskolnikov didn’t do that. A chill ran through him.

"You keep lying," he said slowly, twisting his lips into a weak smile. "You’re trying to show you know my game, like you know what I’ll say before I say it," he muttered, not choosing his words carefully. "You’re here to scare me... or you’re mocking me..."

He stared hard at Porfiry, hatred flickering in his eyes.

"You lie," he said. "You know the best move for a criminal is to tell the truth as close as possible... to hide as little as possible. I don’t buy it!"

"What a crafty guy you are!" Porfiry chuckled. "Can’t catch you; you’ve got a monomania. You don’t believe me? But you sorta do. I’ll make you believe the whole, 'cause I like you and genuinely want good for you."

Raskolnikov’s lips twitched.

"Yes, I do," Porfiry continued, patting Raskolnikov’s arm. "Take care of your health. Your mom and sister are here now; think of them. Comfort them, instead of scaring them..."

"What’s that to you? How do you know? What’s your business in this? You’re watching me and want me to know it?"

"Good heavens! Learned it from you! You spill everything in your excitement. Razumihin shared a lot yesterday too. Your suspicious mind makes you miss the obvious. Like the bell-ringing. If I suspected you, I’d handle it differently. I’d disarm your suspicion, then hit hard with questions: ‘What were you doing at the victim’s place at ten? Why ring the bell and ask about blood? Why take porters to the police?’ That’s what I’d do if I suspected you. But I didn’t do any of that. So, no suspicion. Yet you can’t see it clearly."

Raskolnikov jolted, and Porfiry noticed.

"Lying all along," Raskolnikov shouted. "I don’t get your angle, but you’re lying. You didn’t talk like this before, and I’m sure of it!"

"im lying?" Porfiry said, acting all surprised but keeping that annoying smirk like he didnt care what Raskolnikov thought. "im lying... but how did i treat you, huh? im the examining lawyer here. i gave you all the outs for your defense; illness, delirium, injury, melancholy, and all that stuff. heh! though, those defenses dont always hold up: illness, delirium, i don’t remember--thats fine, but why in your illness and delirium did you have those specific delusions, huh? maybe there were others? heh!”

Raskolnikov stared him down, full of contempt.

“straight to the point,” he said, loud and commanding, standing up and nudging Porfiry back a bit, “straight to the point, am i free from suspicion or not? tell me, Porfiry Petrovitch, and dont waste time!”

“what a mess this is with you!” Porfiry laughed, still keeping that sly, calm face. “why do you need to know so bad, since theyre not hassling you yet? youre like a kid with matches! why so uneasy? why push yourself on us, eh? heh!”

“i repeat,” Raskolnikov yelled, “that i wont tolerate this!”

“tolerate what? uncertainty?” Porfiry cut in.

“dont mock me! i wont stand it! i cant and i wont, you hear?” he shouted, slamming his fist on the table.

“quiet! quiet! theyll hear us! im serious, watch yourself. im not joking,” Porfiry whispered, but now with a stern face, dropping the act.

just for a second though. Raskolnikov, confused, was in a frenzy but somehow still kept his voice down.

“i wont be tortured,” he whispered, hating that he couldnt resist the command and getting even angrier. “arrest me, search me, but do it by the book and quit playing games! dont you dare!”

“dont worry about procedure,” Porfiry said with that same sly grin, clearly enjoying Raskolnikov’s frustration. “i invited you here as a friend.”

“i dont want your friendship and i spit on it! you hear? im taking my cap and leaving. what will you do if youre gonna arrest me?”

he grabbed his cap and headed for the door.

“and wont you see my little surprise?” Porfiry chuckled, grabbing his arm and stopping him.

he got even more playful, which drove Raskolnikov nuts.

“what surprise?” he asked, alarmed, looking at Porfiry.

“my little surprise, sitting right behind that door, heh!” (he pointed to the locked door.) “i locked him in so he wouldnt escape.”

“what is it? where? what?...”

Raskolnikov went to the door, tried to open it, but it was locked.

“its locked, ive got the key!”

“youre lying,” Raskolnikov roared, “you lie, you damned clown!” and he lunged at Porfiry, who backed away, unfazed.

“i get it all! you’re lying and mocking me to make me slip up…”

“why, you couldn’t betray yourself any more, Rodion Romanovitch. you’re in a rage. dont shout, or ill call the clerks.”

"You're lying! Get the clerks in. You knew I was sick, tried pushing me till I broke and confessed. That was your plan, right? Show your evidence! I see through this. No real proof, just flimsy suspicions like Zametov's! You knew how I'd react, wanted to rile me up and then corner me with priests and deputies... Are they coming? Where are they? Bring them out!"

"Deputies? Man, the stories people make up! That's not even procedure, you don't get it, buddy... And you can't skip procedure," Porfiry mumbled, ear to the door where noise was coming from.

"They're here," Raskolnikov shouted. "You called them! You were expecting them! Fine, bring them all: deputies, witnesses, whoever!... I'm ready!"

Then something unexpected happened, not even Raskolnikov or Porfiry could've seen this coming.

CHAPTER VI

Remembering later, this is how Raskolnikov recalled it.

The noise grew louder, and the door cracked open.

"What is it?" Porfiry barked, irritated. "I gave orders..."

No answer at first, but clearly a crowd was pushing someone back.

"What is it?" Porfiry repeated, uneasy.

"The prisoner Nikolay's here," someone replied.

"We don't need him! Take him away! What's he doing here? This isn't right!" Porfiry yelled, rushing to the door.

"But he..." the voice started then stopped.

About two seconds of struggle, then a hard shove, and a pale man stormed in.

At first glance, he was odd. Eyes fixed ahead, seeing nothing. Determined look but ghostly pale, like he was walking to his death. Lips twitching.

Dressed like a worker, medium height, young, thin, cropped hair. The guy he shoved followed him in, tried grabbing his shoulder; a guard, but Nikolay shrugged him off.

People crowded the doorway, trying to peek in. All this happened fast.

"Go back, too soon! Wait till we call you!... Why bring him early?" Porfiry snapped, thrown off balance.

But Nikolay dropped to his knees.

"What's this?" Porfiry asked, surprised.

"I'm guilty! It's my sin! I'm the murderer," Nikolay blurted, breathless but clear.

Silence for ten seconds; everyone stunned, even the guard backed off, froze by the door.

"What is this?" Porfiry asked, shaking off his stupor.

"I... am the murderer," Nikolay said again, after a pause.

"What... you... who did you kill?" Porfiry was clearly confused.

Nikolay paused again.

"Alyona Ivanovna and her sister Lizaveta Ivanovna, I... killed... with an axe. Darkness overtook me," he added suddenly, then silent.

Still on his knees. Porfiry stood thinking for a moment, then waved the onlookers away. They disappeared, shutting the door. He turned to Raskolnikov, who stood frozen, staring at Nikolay, moved towards him, but stopped, looked between Nikolay and Raskolnikov, then couldn't hold back, lunged at Nikolay.

"You're in too much of a rush," he yelled, almost ticked off. "Didn't ask what got into you... Speak up, did you kill them?"

"I'm the murderer... I want to confess," Nikolay said.

"Ach! What'd you use?"

"An axe. Had it ready."

"Ach, he's in a hurry! Alone?"

Nikolay didn't get it.

"Did you do it alone?"

"Yeah, alone. Mitka's not guilty, had no part in it."

"Don't rush about Mitka! A-ach! Why'd you run downstairs like that? The porters saw you both!"

"Threw them off... I ran after Mitka," Nikolay replied quick, like he'd practiced.

"Knew it!" Porfiry snapped. "This isn't his story," he muttered, then focused on Raskolnikov again.

He'd been so wrapped up with Nikolay, he kinda forgot Raskolnikov. Seemed surprised.

"Dear Rodion Romanovitch, sorry!" he jumped up, "this won't do; you gotta go... no point in staying... what a surprise!... Bye!"

He took him by the arm and led him out.

"Guess you didn't see that coming?" said Raskolnikov, finding his courage.

"You didn't either, buddy. Look, your hand's shaking! He-he!"

"You're shaking too, Porfiry Petrovitch!"

"Yeah, I am; didn't see it coming."

They were at the door; Porfiry wanted Raskolnikov gone.

"And your little surprise, not gonna show me?" Raskolnikov said, sarcastic.

"Why, his teeth chatter asking, he-he! You're a funny guy! 'Til next time!"

"I think it's goodbye!"

"That's up to God," murmured Porfiry, with a weird smile.

Walking through the office, Raskolnikov noticed folks watching him. Saw the two porters from the house, waiting. But on the stairs, Porfiry's voice called after him. Turned around, saw him catching up, out of breath.

"One word, Rodion Romanovitch; the rest's in God's hands, but formally, I gotta ask some questions... we'll meet again, right?"

Porfiry stood there, smiling.

"Right?" he added.

Looked like he had more to say, but couldn't.

"You gotta excuse me, Porfiry Petrovitch, for what's just happened... I lost my cool," Raskolnikov started, feeling bold again.

"Don't worry about it, don't worry," Porfiry replied, almost happy. "I got a nasty temper too, I admit! But we'll meet again. If it's God's will, we might see a lot of each other."

"And get to know each other inside out?" added Raskolnikov.

"Yeah; inside out," Porfiry agreed, squinting at Raskolnikov. "Now you're off to a birthday party?"

"To a funeral."

"Right, the funeral! Take care, and get better."

"Don't know what to wish you," said Raskolnikov, starting down the stairs, but glancing back. "I'd wish you success, but your job's kinda funny."

"Why funny?" Porfiry turned to go, but seemed interested.

why'd you give nikolay so much grief, twisting him till he confessed? bet you worked him over day and night, convinced him he did it. now he's finally broken, you'll probably tell him he's lying. "not your story, nikolay!" admit it, it's kinda funny.

"ha! noticed i told him it wasn't his own story?"

"hard to miss."

"you're sharp. catch everything! always find the humor... like gogol."

"yep, like gogol."

"yeah, gogol... looking forward to seeing you again."

"me too."

raskolnikov went straight home, head spinning. once there, he just sat, trying to piece it all together. nikolay's confession? crazy, made no sense, but it happened. he knew it'd unravel and they'd be back on his trail. but for now, he was free. needed to act.

but how soon was trouble coming? the picture got clearer. thinking of porfiry, he shuddered. didn't know all of porfiry's plans, but he'd shown some cards. raskolnikov was in deep, but no hard evidence yet. was he seeing it right? what was porfiry really after? any surprises? what if nikolay hadn’t shown up?

porfiry had risked a lot showing his hand. if he had more, wouldn't he have played it? what was the "surprise"? a joke, or hiding something real? what about yesterday's guest? where'd he go? if porfiry had proof, it might link to him...

sitting with his head in his hands, still trembling. finally got up, grabbed his cap, thought, and headed for the door.

felt like for today, at least, he was safe. almost joyful. wanted to hurry to katerina ivanovna’s. might miss the funeral, but he'd catch the dinner, see sonia.

paused, a pained smile crept in.

"today! today," he told himself. "yeah, today! it's gotta be...."

just as he reached for the door, it started opening. he stepped back. the door creaked open, and there was the figure from yesterday—his visitor from underground.

guy showed up in the doorway, just staring at Raskolnikov, not saying a word. Stepped in. Looked the same as yesterday, clothes and all, but his face was different. Looked down, sighing like he was carrying something heavy. You'd think he was a tired peasant woman if he tilted his head.

“What do you want?” Raskolnikov asked, frozen in fear. Man didn't answer, just bowed low, touching the ground with a finger.

“What is it?” Raskolnikov shouted.

“I have sinned,” he said softly.

“How?”

“Evil thoughts.”

They locked eyes.

“I got upset. When you came, maybe tipsy, told the porters to hit up the police and ask about the blood, I got angry they let you go, thought you were just drunk. Kept me up at night. Remembered the address, came looking for you yesterday....”

“Who came?” Raskolnikov cut in, piecing it together.

“Me, I wronged you.”

“So you're from that place?”

“I was at the gate with them... remember? We've worked there for years, tanning hides, taking jobs home... I was mad....”

Raskolnikov remembered the scene — the gateway, the people, the voices. One had wanted him dragged to the station. Couldn't place the face, but recalled talking back.

So that's how yesterday's nightmare wrapped up. Almost ruined himself over something so small. This guy had nothing but his questions about the flat and the stains. Porfiry had nothing but that wild theory, no solid facts. If no more comes up (and it better not), what can they pin on him? Even if they nab him, what then? Porfiry only just heard about the flat then.

“Did you tell Porfiry... that I'd been there?” he asked, a thought striking him.

“Who's Porfiry?”

“Head detective?”

“Yeah. Porters didn't go, but I did.”

“Today?”

“Got there right before you. Heard everything, how he grilled you.”

“Where? What? When?”

“Next room. Sat there the whole time.”

“What? So you were the twist? How'd that happen? My word!”

saw the porters weren’t keen on my idea. too late, they said, he might get mad we didn’t come earlier. kept me up, so i started digging for answers. found out where to go yesterday, showed up today. first try, he’s not there. came back an hour later, still couldn’t see me. third time, finally got in. told him everything, just like it happened. he starts pacing, thumping his chest. ‘what's this about? if i’d known, i'd have arrested him!’ then he’s off, talking to someone in the corner. turns back, grilling me, scolding. i lay it all out, said you didn’t have the guts to speak up yesterday, didn’t recognize me. he’s off again, pacing and chest-thumping. when you got announced, he tells me, ‘sit tight, don’t move no matter what.’ locks me in a room. ‘maybe i’ll need you,’ he says. after Nikolay shows up, he lets me out once you’re gone. ‘i’ll call you back for more questions,’ he says.

“did he question Nikolay with you there?”

“nah, booted me like he did you, then talked to Nikolay.”

man stood there, bowed low, touching the ground.

“forgive my nasty thoughts and lies.”

“may God forgive you,” Raskolnikov says.

as he said it, the man bowed again, not as low, then slowly left the room.

“cuts both ways, it cuts both ways,” Raskolnikov muttered, leaving more sure than before.

“now we fight,” he said with a bitter smile, heading down the stairs. anger aimed at himself, recalling his “cowardice.”

next morning was rough for Pyotr Petrovitch. yesterday's interview with Dounia and her mom hit him harder than he thought. the ego got bruised bad. spent the night wrestling with it. first thing out of bed, he checked himself in the mirror, worried about jaundice. no sign of it, thankfully. he's still got that clear skin, maybe a bit fuller face now. for a moment, he thought, "no big deal, i'll find someone else." but reality hit, and he spat in frustration. Andrey Semyonovitch, his roommate, caught it and smirked. that smirk was just another thing on the list bugging him. Pyotr Petrovitch shouldn't have confided in him about yesterday's mess. second mistake, acting on impulse.

day just got worse. legal troubles in the senate, and the flat he rented for the marriage he’s not having anymore. the German landlord wouldn’t back down on the contract or the fees, even with the place all done up. same story with the furniture guys — no refunds.

"am i supposed to marry for the furniture now?" he muttered, teeth clenched. thought of Dounia stung. if he could've just wished Raskolnikov away, he'd have done it in a heartbeat.

"should’ve loosened the purse strings," he thought, heading back to Andrey’s room. "acting stingy was my downfall. should've spent on them — gifts, trousseau, the works. would've made things harder for them to call it off. they’d feel guilty, dismissing a generous guy. blew it."

gritting his teeth again, he knew he’d been a fool. but kept that part to himself.

came home twice as pissed off. funeral dinner prep at katerina ivanovna's caught my eye. heard about it yesterday, thought i was invited but had too much on my mind to bother. asked madame lippevechsel, busy setting the table, found out it was a big deal. everyone invited, even those who didn't know the deceased. andrey semyonovitch lebeziatnikov got an invite despite his beef with katerina. turns out, im not just invited, they're counting on me. amalia ivanovna's all dressed up like it's her party, buzzing around looking pleased. got me thinking as i headed back to my place, or andrey's, since raskolnikov's coming too.

andrey semyonovitch was home all morning. strange relationship with him. despise and fear him at once. he was my ward once, now he's a big noise in some circles that freak me out. heard these circles in petersburg love to expose people. seen it happen to others back home, big scandals, big trouble. don't want that happening to me. came here planning to get on their good side if i had to. thought andrey would help me understand them but turns out he's just a fool. doesn't make me feel any better though. need to know if these guys got teeth. can they ruin me? should i try to cozy up to them? lots of questions swirling around.

andrey semyonovitch was a skinny guy with these odd pale whiskers he thought were something special. real quiet type, always had some eye issue. soft heart but had a big mouth sometimes. amalia ivanovna liked him cause he paid up and didn’t get drunk. honestly, the guy wasn't too sharp. thought he was all about progress and the young crowd, but really he just tagged along with whatever trend was going.

lebeziatnikov, even with his good nature, started to see pyotr petrovitch wasn’t all that. took a while, but he figured out pyotr was playing him. tried talking about fourier and darwin, but pyotr just got sarcastic and rude. started noticing andrey was maybe just a bunch of talk, didn’t know much about his own ideas, really. pyotr liked the praise and didn’t mind when andrey went on about him being part of the new age commune or whatever, even if it was nonsense.

that morning, pyotr sold some bonds, counted his cash, while andrey circled the room trying to act like the money didn’t faze him. pyotr didn’t buy it for a second. andrey thought pyotr was just rubbing it in how much better off he was.

andrey tried talking about his favorite topic, some new commune idea, but pyotr was too busy with his counting to care, just threw in sarcastic bits here and there. andrey was itching to talk about pyotr’s breakup with dounia, thought he had some big ideas that’d help pyotr move on.

“there’s some party at the widow’s place, right?” pyotr cut him off.

“you didn’t hear? told you last night what i think about all that. she invited you too, didn’t she? saw you talking…”

shouldn't have thought that fool would blow all her cash from Raskolnikov on this feast. walked in and saw the setup — wine, guests, the works. makes no sense. and you say im invited? since when? don't remember that. not going anyway. why bother? just mentioned she could get a year's pay as a widow, so i guess that's why she asked. heh.

“not going either,” Lebeziatnikov chimed in.

“yeah, after you hit her, makes sense you'd skip.”

“who hit who?” Lebeziatnikov snapped, turning red.

“you hit Katerina Ivanovna last month. heard it yesterday... so much for your big ideas on women, heh!” Pyotr Petrovitch smirked, fiddling with his beads.

“all lies!” Lebeziatnikov shot back. “wasn't like that. she attacked me first, ripped my whiskers out... anyone can defend themselves, right? didn't use violence, just pushed her back.”

Pyotr Petrovitch kept laughing.

“you're just in a bad mood... nothing to do with the woman question! used to think if women were equal in everything, even strength, then in fighting too. but realized later, fighting shouldn't happen at all. society shouldn’t need it... but now, yeah, there's still fighting. anyway, not going on principle. memorial dinners are a joke. if there were priests, maybe i'd go just for a laugh.”

“so you'd show up, eat their food, and mock them?”

not insult, but protest is what i should aim for. doing it with purpose. could spark something, a seed of enlightenment. people might get upset at first, sure, but later they’d see it was a service. that terebyeva, remember her? she got flak for telling her parents she was ditching convention for a free marriage. folks said she was too harsh. nonsense. protest needs to be sharp. like varents, married seven years, left her kids, told her husband straight up she found another way to live. no sugarcoating. that’s how it should be done.

“that terebyeva, she's on her third free marriage?”

“nah, just the second! but even if it was the fifteenth, what's it matter? sometimes i wish my parents were around just so i could show them, amaze them with my protest. shame there's no one to shock."

“to surprise! ha! anyway, what about the dead man’s daughter, the frail one? the rumors true?”

“why not? i think it’s how things should be. in today’s world, it’s not normal because it’s forced. in the future, it’ll be normal because it's choice. she was right to use her struggle as her asset. in the future, there won’t be need for assets, but her role will fit the environment. as for sofya semyonovna, her action’s a strong protest against society. i respect her for it, she’s inspiring."

“heard you got her kicked out of these lodgings.”

lebeziatnikov was furious. “that’s a lie! katerina ivanovna cooked that up, she didn’t get it! never made a move on sofya semyonovna, just wanted to inspire her protest. she wouldn’t have stayed here anyway!”

“asked her to join your community?”

you keep laughing, and it's like you don't get it. no role like that in a community. we're trying to build something where roles transform. what's dumb here might make sense there. it's all about the environment. person ain't much without it. sofya and i, we're cool. she never thought i wronged her. i'm trying to bring her into the community, on a new level. why laugh? we're setting up a special kind of community, a broader one. rejecting more old ideas. and sofya, she's got a great character.

“oh, so you exploit that?”

“no, no, not at all.”

“really? sounds funny!”

“believe me, why hide it? it's strange how modern she is with me!”

“and you’re developing her, huh? trying to ditch modesty?”

“not at all! you misunderstand 'development'. we’re after women's freedom, and you’re stuck on one thing... sure, chastity and modesty might be old ideas, but her choice is hers. if she wanted me, i'd be lucky. i like her, but i treat her with respect and dignity. i wait, that's all.”

“maybe try giving her a gift. bet you never thought of that.”

“you don’t get it. it’s different. you look down on her, miss the human side. she's got character! i’m just bummed she stopped reading. i used to lend her books. she’s got energy but not much independence to shake off those old ideas. she gets some stuff though, like the hand-kissing thing. we talked about it. i explained the workers' associations in france to her too. now i'm on about entering rooms in future society.”

“and what’s that about?”

“we debated if a community member can enter another's room anytime... and we said yes!”

“could be awkward timing, haha!”

lebeziatnikov was really ticked off.

"man, you're always fixated on something negative," he said, annoyed. "ugh! i'm really frustrated that when i was explaining our system, i brought up personal privacy too soon! folks like you just mock it before getting it. and they're so smug about it! ugh! i've always said a newbie shouldn't touch that topic till they truly get the system. and tell me, what's so shameful about cesspools? i'd be the first to clean any cesspool. it's not about self-sacrifice, it's just work. honest, useful work, much better than what a Raphael or Pushkin did because it's more practical."

"and more respectable, more respectable, ha-ha-ha!"

"what's 'more respectable' even mean? i don't get using those words for human work. 'more respectable,' 'nobler'—those are old prejudices i don't buy. everything useful to people is respectable. i only get one word: useful! laugh all you want, but that's the truth!"

Pyotr Petrovitch laughed loud. he'd finished counting the money and was putting it away. but he left some notes on the table. they'd argued about the "cesspool question" before. lebeziatnikov got mad about it, while luzhin found it funny and wanted to tick him off.

"it's your bad luck yesterday making you so grumpy and annoying," lebeziatnikov said, even though he acted all "independent" he still showed pyotr petrovitch some respect from before.

"tell me this," pyotr petrovitch cut in, annoyed, "can you... or are you close enough to that young lady to have her come in here for a minute? i think they're back from the cemetery... heard footsteps... i want to see her, that young lady."

"why?" lebeziatnikov asked, surprised.

"oh, i want to. i'm leaving today or tomorrow, wanted to talk to her about... anyway, you might as well be here. better you are. no telling what you'd imagine."

"i won't imagine anything. just asked, and if you need to talk to her, i'll call her in. i'll go now and won't be in your way."

five minutes later lebeziatnikov came back with sonia. she walked in, surprised and shy like always. she was shy with new people, always had been since she was a kid, even more now.... pyotr petrovitch met her "politely and kindly," but with a hint of teasing that he thought suited a man of his standing with someone as young and "interesting" as her. he quickly "reassured" her and had her sit across from him at the table. sonia sat down, glanced around—at lebeziatnikov, the money on the table, then back at pyotr petrovitch, her eyes locked on him. lebeziatnikov started to leave. pyotr petrovitch signaled sonia to stay seated and stopped lebeziatnikov.

"is raskolnikov in there? has he come?" he whispered to him.

"raskolnikov? yeah. why? yeah, he's there. just saw him come in.... why?"

"well, i really need you to stay here with us, don't leave me alone with this... young lady. i just want a few words with her, but who knows what they'll say. i don't want raskolnikov to repeat anything.... you get what i mean?"

"got it!" Lebeziatnikov caught on. "yeah, you're right... i'm sure you've got no reason to worry, but... still, you're right. i'll stay. i'll stand right here by the window and keep out of your way... i think you're right..."

Pyotr Petrovitch went back to the sofa, sat across from Sonia, locked eyes with her, looking all serious, saying without words, "don't mess this up, ma'am." Sonia felt pretty embarrassed.

"first off, Sofya Semyonovna, pass my apologies to your dear mamma... that's how it is, right? Katerina Ivanovna's like a mom to you?" Pyotr Petrovitch started, all dignified but nice about it.

you could tell he meant well.

"yes, like a mom," Sonia replied, shy and quick.

"so will you apologize for me? i've got to miss the dinner 'cause of unavoidable stuff, even though your mom was kind to invite me."

"yeah... i'll let her know... right away."

Sonia jumped up from her chair.

"hang on, there's more," Pyotr Petrovitch stopped her, amused at her not knowing the ropes, "you don't know me well, dear Sofya Semyonovna, if you think i'd bother you for something so minor. there's another reason."

Sonia sat back down, glancing at the colorful notes on the table but quickly looked away, focusing on Pyotr Petrovitch. Felt wrong staring at someone else's money. She shifted to the gold eyeglass in his hand and the big ring on his finger. Then back to his face. After a pause, he went on.

"yesterday, while passing by, i had a word with Katerina Ivanovna, poor soul. just enough to see she's in quite a situation."

"yes... quite a situation..." Sonia agreed, quick.

"or, simply put, she's ill."

"yes, that's simpler... yes, ill."

"exactly. so, out of humanity and compassion, i'd like to help her, seeing her tough spot. i guess the whole family relies on you now?"

"may i ask," Sonia stood, "did you mention a pension to her yesterday? she said you promised to get her one. was that right?"

"not even close, it's nonsense! i only suggested temporary aid as the widow of a service official—if she had connections... but your late parent hadn't finished his term and hadn't been in service for a while. so any hope's really slim, no claim at all... and she's dreaming of a pension already, he-he-he!... quite the optimist!"

"yeah, she is. she's trusting and kind, believes everything from the goodness of her heart and... and... she's just like that... yes... please excuse her," Sonia said, standing up again to leave.

"but you haven't heard what i've got to say."

"no, i haven't," Sonia mumbled.

"then sit down." she was all turned around; she sat back down for the third time.

seeing her situation with the kids, i want to help, but only as much as i can. maybe start a fund or a raffle, something friends or even strangers do to help out. that’s what i wanted to discuss; we could kick it off today, talk more this evening, maybe at seven. i think Lebeziatnikov might chip in too. but heads up, i dont think it's wise to give Katerina Ivanovna the money directly. today’s dinner is why. she spent on rum, Madeira, coffee, while tomorrow there's no bread. it’s a mess. so, maybe you keep the fund quiet, manage it. am i off here?

“i don’t know... it’s just today, a one-time thing... she wanted to honor the memory... and she’s usually sensible... but whatever you think, i’ll be very... they’ll be... and God will... and the orphans...”

sonia started crying.

“ok, remember that. now, will you take this small sum for your family? i’d rather my name stays out of it. here... i’ve got my own worries, so this is all i can do...”

Pyotr Petrovitch handed her a carefully unfolded ten-rouble note. sonia took it, turned red, stood up, mumbled something, and went to leave. he walked her to the door with ceremony. she left, upset and embarrassed, back to Katerina Ivanovna.

Lebeziatnikov had been pacing or staring out the window, not wanting to interrupt. after sonia left, he approached Pyotr Petrovitch, hand extended.

“I saw and heard everything,” emphasizing “saw.” “That’s honorable, humane! You didn’t want gratitude, I noticed! and while i’m not a fan of private charity since it doesn’t solve the root issue, I enjoyed seeing what you did.”

“That’s nonsense,” muttered Pyotr Petrovitch, a bit thrown off, eyeing Lebeziatnikov closely.

no way that's nonsense! you went through hell yesterday, yet you still care about others. that deserves respect, even if you're making a social misstep. didn't expect this from you, Pyotr Petrovitch. your ideas? they're holding you back. you were so upset by yesterday’s bad luck. but marriage? legal marriage, Pyotr? why hold onto that? glad it didn't work out, glad you're still free, still part of humanity... had to say it!

“i don’t want to be fooled in a free marriage or raise someone else’s kids. that’s why i want legal marriage,” Luzhin finally said, distracted by something else.

“kids? you mentioned kids,” Lebeziatnikov jumped in. “kids are a huge social issue, sure, but there’s another way. some avoid kids to dodge the family trap. we’ll talk kids later, but honor? that’s my soft spot. that old idea of honor is ridiculous. no deception in a free marriage! it’s just a response to legal marriage. it’s not degrading... if i married legally, i'd respect my wife for protesting! you laugh because you're stuck in old ideas. sure, being deceived in legal marriage sucks, but it's a mess for both. when deception's out in the open in a free marriage, it’s not deception. your wife respects you by thinking you won’t block her happiness. damn it! if i married, legally or not, i’d gift my wife a lover if she didn’t have one. ‘i want you to respect me. see!’ am i wrong?”

Pyotr Petrovitch chuckled without much joy, barely listening. he was caught up in something else, and even Lebeziatnikov noticed. Pyotr was clearly stirred, rubbing his hands, which Lebeziatnikov remembered and thought about later.

trying to figure out why katerina ivanovna thought throwing that dinner made sense is a mess. nearly half of raskolnikov’s money for marmeladov’s funeral got blown on it. maybe she felt she had to show everyone, especially amalia ivanovna, that marmeladov wasn’t below them. maybe it was about that stubborn pride some folks have, spending their last bit just to keep up appearances. she wanted to prove to those lousy lodgers she knew how to host a proper event, that she came from a decent family, not just for scrubbing floors.

she wasn’t broken inside, though. pushed to her limits, sure, but not broken. sonia had a point about her mind being off balance. doctors say consumption can mess with your head.

the booze wasn’t fancy—vodka, rum, some cheap wine. but there was enough. food included rice and honey, pancakes, all cooked in amalia’s kitchen. two samovars for tea and punch after. katerina did the shopping with a little pole from the lodgings, who ran around like a madman helping her out. she praised him to the skies at first, but eventually got tired of him. that was her way—she’d build people up, then turn on them when reality hit. she wanted everyone to be happy and peaceful, but when things went wrong, she’d lose it fast, swinging from hope to despair like lightning.

Katerina Ivanovna gave Amalia Ivanovna more credit than usual, just 'cause she threw herself into getting everything ready. Amalia handled the table, the linens, the dishes, and cooked the food in her kitchen while Katerina headed off to the cemetery. It all turned out pretty decent. The tablecloth was almost clean, and the mix-matched crockery and cutlery from different lodgers were set up right on time. Amalia, feeling good about her work, wore a black silk dress with new mourning ribbons, meeting everyone back with a bit of pride. Katerina didn’t much like that pride. She thought, "Like the table couldn’t have been set without Amalia!" And that cap with ribbons? Really got to her. She wondered if Amalia thought she was all that just because she owned the place and helped out as a ‘favor’. Katerina’s dad, a colonel and almost a governor, used to set tables for forty, and folks like Amalia wouldn't have even sniffed the kitchen then.

Katerina held back her feelings for the moment, just treating Amalia coldly, but she decided she’d need to set her straight later. What really got under her skin was how few lodgers showed up for the funeral, aside from the Pole who barely made it, yet the dinner brought out the poorest, half-sober bunch. The more respectable ones stayed away, like Pyotr Petrovitch Luzhin, who was supposed to be the most distinguished of them all. Katerina had sung his praises the night before, saying he was generous and well-connected, had promised to help her get a pension. When she praised someone’s status, it was just for the pleasure of it, no hidden agenda. But Luzhin didn’t show, and neither did that Lebeziatnikov guy, who probably thought too much of himself. He was only invited 'cause he roomed with Luzhin, and it would’ve been awkward not to.

among those who didnt show up were the "genteel lady and her old-maidish daughter" who'd only been in the house a couple weeks. theyd complained about the noise in katerina ivanovna’s room, especially when marmeladov came back drunk. katerina heard this from amalia ivanovna, who was in a spat with katerina and threatened to kick them all out, shouting that they "werent worth the foot" of the proper lodgers. katerina decided to invite this lady and her daughter, "whose foot she wasnt worth," to show she held no grudges and was of finer upbringing. she wanted to drop hints at dinner about her late father’s governorship and point out how dumb it was for them to snub her like that. the fat colonel-major (not much of a rank really) was missing too, but hed been out of sorts for days. 

the party was a mess. there was the Pole, a miserable clerk with a spotty face and bad smell, a deaf old man once in the post office, and a drunk retired clerk laughing loudly without a waistcoat. one guest sat without a word to katerina ivanovna. another turned up in a dressing-gown, but amalia and the Pole got him out. the Pole brought two more Poles as random guests. katerina was furious. who were they setting all this up for? the kids didnt even get a seat, eating off a box in the corner with polenka looking after them.

katerina ivanovna held herself with extra dignity, almost haughty. stared daggers at some guests, invited them to sit with a sharp tone. blamed amalia for the absentees and treated her coldly, which amalia noticed and didnt like. not a great start. but everyone sat down at least.

raskolnikov showed up from the cemetery visit. katerina was thrilled to see him. partly cause he was the only "educated visitor" who was gonna be a professor soon, and partly cause he apologized for missing the funeral. she sat him on her left, amalia on her right. even with her cough getting worse, katerina whispered to raskolnikov her frustrations about the dinner, laughing about the guests and especially amalia.

"it's that cuckoo's fault! you know who i mean? her! her!" katerina ivanovna nods at the landlady. "look at her, making those wide eyes, knows we're talking about her, can't figure it out. pfoo, the owl! ha-ha! (cough-cough-cough.) and what's with that cap? (cough-cough-cough.) she wants everyone to think she's doing me a favor by being here. i asked her to invite folks who knew my late husband, and look at the bunch she brought! the sweeps! see that one with the spotty face? and those poor poles, ha-ha-ha! (cough-cough-cough.) never seen them before. why are they here? they just sit there. hey, _pan_!" she calls to one of them, "tried the pancakes? have more! some beer? vodka? look, he's up and bowing, poor guys must be starving. let them eat! they’re quiet at least, though i'm worried about the landlady's silver... amalia ivanovna!" she shouts abruptly, "if your spoons go missing, not my problem! ha-ha-ha!" laughing, she turns to raskolnikov, nodding at the landlady, enjoying herself. "she didn’t get it! look at her, mouth open! a real owl in new ribbons, ha-ha-ha!"

then her laughter turns into a coughing fit, lasts five minutes. sweat on her forehead, blood on her handkerchief. she shows raskolnikov the blood, silent, then starts whispering again, cheeks flushed.

"you know, i gave her really delicate instructions for inviting that lady and her daughter, you know who i mean? needed the utmost tact, but she bungled it. that conceited woman, the major's widow, here to get a pension and fray her skirts, fifty and painting her face... she didn't even think to come, no reply to the invite, which basic manners required! why hasn’t pyotr petrovitch come? where’s sonia? where's she gone? ah, there she is! what’s up, sonia, where were you? odd you’re late even at your father’s funeral. rodion romanovitch, make room for her. that’s your spot, sonia... take what you like. the cold entrée with jelly's best. pancakes soon. kids got their food? polenka, you all set? (cough-cough-cough.) good. be good, lida, and kolya, sit still, like a gentleman. what are you saying, sonia?"

sonia hurries to give pyotr petrovitch’s apologies, speaking loud enough for all to hear, choosing respectful phrases he supposedly said. adds he’ll come as soon as he can to discuss _business_ and see what can be done for her, etc., etc.

sonia figured being nice to katerina ivanovna would lift her spirits a bit, maybe stroke her ego. she sat next to raskolnikov, gave him a quick nod, curious but avoiding eye contact. mostly, she kept her eyes on katerina ivanovna, trying to keep her happy. neither had proper mourning clothes; sonia in dark brown, katerina in her usual striped dress.

pyotr petrovitch's message hit the mark. katerina ivanovna, all dignified, asked about him and then whispered to raskolnikov how odd it'd be for someone like pyotr petrovitch to be in such company, despite his loyalty.

"thats why im grateful, rodion romanovitch," she said, "for not turning away from us, even here. but i know its your care for my late husband that brought you."

she looked around the table, proud, then asked the deaf man if he wanted more meat or wine. he didnt get it, just stared, mouth open, while folks around had a good laugh.

"what a fool! look at him! why'd he come? but pyotr petrovitch, i trusted him," katerina ivanovna went on, "not like..." she snapped at amalia ivanovna, "not like your raggedy bunch my father wouldnt let cook in his kitchen."

"yeah, he liked his drink!" the commissariat clerk shouted, downing vodka.

"my husband had his flaws," katerina ivanovna fired back, "but he was honorable, loved us. too trusting, though, drank with lowlifes. imagine, rodion, a gingerbread cock in his pocket when he was plastered, still thinking of the kids!"

"a cock? really?" the clerk yelled.

she ignored him, deep in thought. "people think i was too harsh," she told raskolnikov, "but he respected me. i pitied him sometimes, but knew kindness meant more drinking. only sternness kept him in line."

"got his hair pulled plenty!" the clerk laughed, drinking more.

"some fools need a beating, not just a hair pull. not talking about my husband!" she snapped.

her cheeks were bright red now, and she was breathing heavy. another minute and she might’ve caused a scene. everyone around seemed to enjoy the show, nudging the clerk and whispering stuff to him, trying to stir him up.

“what are you implying?” the clerk stammered, “who are you talking about... but whatever! that’s nonsense! widow, i forgive you... move along!” then he knocked back more vodka.

raskolnikov sat there, disgusted, nibbling at the food katerina ivanovna kept placing on his plate, just being polite. he kept an eye on sonia, who looked more and more worried. she knew dinner wouldn’t end well and dreaded katerina ivanovna’s rising temper. sonia realized she was the reason for the ‘genteel’ ladies’ condescension toward katerina ivanovna’s invite. she’d heard how amalia ivanovna mentioned the mother was offended by the idea of her daughter sitting near sonia. it was clear katerina ivanovna knew too, and any insult to sonia cut deeper for her than if it was directed at her own family. katerina ivanovna wouldn’t rest until she’d put those snobby ladies in their place. then someone sent sonia a plate with two hearts and an arrow, made from black bread. katerina ivanovna turned crimson, calling out that whoever did it was “a drunken idiot!”

amalia ivanovna sensed trouble and, offended by katerina ivanovna’s arrogance, tried to lighten the mood with a story about “karl from the chemist’s,” who was in a cab one night when the driver threatened him, and karl, scared, begged not to be hurt, clasping his hands and crying. katerina ivanovna, holding back laughter, commented that amalia shouldn’t tell stories in russian. amalia, even more offended, shot back that her “father from berlin” was quite important and always had his hands in his pockets. katerina couldn’t help but laugh, nearly making amalia lose her cool.

“listen to her!” katerina whispered, almost amused again, “she meant he kept his hands in his pockets, but said he put them in other people’s. (cough-cough.) and rodion romanovitch, haven’t you noticed how all these foreigners in petersburg, especially the germans, are dumber than us! can you imagine one of us telling a story like ‘karl from the chemist’s’ piercing his heart from fear? and the fool didn’t even punish the cabman, just cried and begged. what an idiot! and she thinks it’s touching, clueless to how dumb she sounds! honestly, that drunk clerk’s smarter, even if he’s fried his brain with booze, but these foreigners, they’re always so proper and serious... look at her glaring! she’s furious, ha-ha! (cough-cough-cough.)”

katerina ivanovna finally lightened up and started talking to raskolnikov about her big plan. she'd get her pension and open a school for girls back in her hometown. this was news to him, and man, did she go into details. turns out, she had that certificate of honor marmeladov mentioned to raskolnikov at the tavern. it was proof she could start a boarding school, but mostly she wanted to show off to those “stuck-up draggletails” at the dinner. she was determined to prove she came from a noble, even aristocratic, background—her dad was a colonel after all.

as katerina got rolling, she painted this picture of life in T----, all calm and happy. she’d get teachers for her school, like this old french guy, mangot, who used to teach her. she also wanted sonia to come help her out. someone laughed at that, but katerina didn’t back down. she got louder, praising sonia’s qualities, and even kissed her twice, which made sonia blush. then katerina suddenly started crying, calling herself nervous and silly, and said it was time for tea.

amalia ivanovna, feeling left out, tried to weigh in. she made some big comment about how the school should really focus on the laundry and stop the girls from reading novels at night. katerina, tired and fed up with the dinner, shut her down quick. said that's the laundry maid’s job, not the head of a top-notch school, and called the novel comment rude. amalia got mad and said she was just trying to help, reminding everyone she’d paid good money for the lodgings.

katerina ivanovna snapped back, calling it a lie that she wished her well since just yesterday, with her husband lying there dead, she’d been nagged over the lodgings. amalia ivanovna shot back that she had invited some ladies, but they didn’t show because real ladies don’t visit someone who isn’t a lady. katerina fired back that being a slut doesn’t qualify her to judge a lady. amalia, not backing down, claimed her father from berlin was important, puffed up her cheeks, and strutted around while everyone laughed, hoping for a fight.

that was it for katerina, who loudly declared that amalia probably never had a father, just a drunk petersburg finn, maybe a cook or worse. amalia turned red as a lobster and squealed back that katerina might not have a father, but hers was from berlin, wore a long coat, and always said poof-poof-poof.

katerina, unimpressed, reminded everyone of her family’s honor, with her father a colonel, while amalia’s was likely a finnish milkman, if he existed at all. amalia, now furious, slammed the table and shrieked her father was johann, a burgomeister, unlike katerina’s father. katerina stood, calm but pale, and warned if amalia dared compare their fathers again, she’d rip her cap off and stomp it. amalia ran around yelling she owned the house and katerina should leave, grabbing the silver spoons for some reason. chaos erupted, kids crying, and sonia tried to calm katerina, but when amalia brought up “the yellow ticket,” katerina pushed sonia aside, ready to make good on her threat.

just then, the door opened, and pyotr petrovitch luzhin walked in, scoping the scene with sharp eyes. katerina rushed to him.

“pyotr petrovitch,” she pleaded, “help me... at least you! make this woman see she can’t treat a distressed lady like this... there are laws... i'll go to the governor-general myself... she’ll pay for it... remember my father’s kindness, protect these orphans.”

“hold on, madam... hold on,” pyotr petrovitch waved her off. “i didn’t know your papa” (someone chuckled) “and i’m not getting into your endless fights with amalia ivanovna... i’m here about my business... and need to speak with your stepdaughter, sofya... ivanovna, right? let me through.”

pyotr petrovitch edged past her, heading to where sonia was.

katerina just stood there like shed been hit by lightning. couldnt believe pyotr petrovitch denied her dad's hospitality. she made it up but bought into her own story. his cold, business tone shocked her. whole room quieted when he came in. he looked out of place, like something big brought him here. raskolnikov stepped aside for him, but pyotr didnt even notice. then lebeziatnikov showed up, hanging back, listening, looking confused.

"sorry if im interrupting, but this is important," pyotr petrovitch said to everyone. "glad more folks are here. amalia ivanovna, as the hostess, please listen close to what i gotta say to sofya ivanovna." he turned to sonia, who already seemed on edge. "right after you visited, a hundred-rouble note went missing from my table at mr. lebeziatnikov's place. if you know anything and tell us, it'll end here. otherwise, i'll have to take serious steps, and blame will be on you."

room went dead silent. even the kids quit crying. sonia just stood there, pale, staring at luzhin, speechless, like she couldnt process it. a few seconds ticked by.

"well, whats it gonna be?" luzhin pressed.

"i dont know... i dont know anything about it," sonia finally whispered.

ok lets cut the fancy talk. i know what i saw. earlier today, i swapped some securities, ended up with about 3k roubles. counted 2300, left 500 on the table. three hundred-rouble notes were right there. you came in — you were jumpy, like you wanted outta there. lebeziatnikov saw it. i brought up helping your relative, katerina ivanovna, and you got emotional. i gave you a ten-rouble note, first part of my help. then after you left, bam, a hundred-rouble note vanished. i cant point fingers at lebeziatnikov, and my math didnt lie. your nerves, wanting to leave, your hands on the table... all point to one thing. im not here to ruin you, but this cant slide. its about respect. i helped, and you repay like this? think it over, im not budging. what do you have to say?

“i didnt take anything,” sonia said, trembling. “you gave me ten roubles. here, take it.” she pulled out the note from her handkerchief, handed it back.

“and the hundred? not admitting to that?” he pressed, ignoring the note.

sonia's surrounded. eyes everywhere. raskolnikov's against the wall, arms crossed, eyes on fire.

“god!” sonia blurts.

luzhin, all soft-like, says, “amalia ivanovna, call the porter. police need to know.”

“_gott der barmherzige!_ knew she was the thief!” amalia ivanovna throws her hands up.

“you knew it?” luzhin jumps on that. “then you must've had a reason. remember your words; there are witnesses.”

room's buzzing. people moving.

“what!” katerina ivanovna snaps to, charging luzhin. “you call sonia a thief? wretches!”

she grabs sonia, hugging tight.

“sonia! why take ten roubles? foolish! give it here!”

she snatches the note, crumples it, and flings it at luzhin. hits him in the eye, drops. amalia ivanovna rushes to pick it up. luzhin's temper flares.

“hold her!” he shouts.

more people crowd in, among them two ladies.

“mad? me? idiot!” katerina ivanovna yells. “you’re the fool, pettifogging lawyer, base man! sonia, a thief? she’d give her last penny!” hysterical laughter hits. “ever see such an idiot?” she spins around, spots the landlady. “you too, sausage eater, calling her a thief? she hasn’t left this room! sat by rodion romanovitch. search her! if not on her, you'll answer! i’ll go to the tsar, today, now! i’ll get in! you banked on her meekness! not me! search her!”

katerina ivanovna, in a frenzy, shakes luzhin, pulling him at sonia.

“i’ll be responsible... calm down, madam, calm down. i see... but, well... police should, but we’ve witnesses.... i’m ready.... but it’s tricky... her being a woman.... with amalia ivanovna... not the way, but how?”

“do it!” katerina ivanovna cries. “sonia, empty pockets! look, monster, empty! handkerchief here! other pocket too, see?”

katerina snatches both pockets out. a paper flies, arcs, lands at luzhin’s feet. gasps. luzhin bends, picks it up, unfolds it—a hundred-rouble note. he holds it up.

amalia yelled, "Thief! Out of my place. Police, police! They gotta go to Siberia!"

Everyone started shouting. Raskolnikov stayed quiet, eyes on Sonia, just a quick look at Luzhin now and then. Sonia stood there, like she didn't even know what was happening. Then her face went red, she cried out, covered her face with her hands.

"No, it wasn't me! I didn't take it! I know nothing!" she wailed, running to Katerina Ivanovna, who hugged her tight, like she was shielding her from everything.

"Sonia! Sonia! I don't believe it! You hear? I don't believe it!" she cried, rocking her, kissing her face, kissing her hands. "You took it? How stupid can they be! Fools, all of them," she shouted, "they don't know her heart, don't know her! She'd sell everything to help you, even went barefoot for us! Husband, look at this! What a memorial for you! Why are you all just standing there? Rodion, why aren't you defending her? You think she did it too? You're not even worth her little finger! God, help her!"

The room felt her pain. Her worn-out face, bloody lips, hoarse voice, tears like a kid's—it hit everyone. Even Pyotr Petrovitch felt something.

"Madam, this doesn't reflect on you," he said, trying to sound grand. "Nobody thinks you had a hand in this. You showed her guilt with the empty pockets. I'm ready to be understanding if poverty pushed Sofya Semyonovna to this, but why not confess, mademoiselle? Afraid of shame? Maybe you panicked? It can happen... but why lower yourself? Gentlemen," he said to the room, "I'm willing to overlook this for now, despite the insult to me. Let this be a lesson," he told Sonia, "I won't push it further. Enough!"

Pyotr Petrovitch glanced at Raskolnikov. Their eyes met, Raskolnikov's stare could burn him up. Meanwhile, Katerina Ivanovna was lost to everything, just holding Sonia. The kids hugged Sonia too, and Polenka, not fully getting it, cried hard, her face buried in Sonia's shoulder.

"What vileness!" a loud voice suddenly said from the doorway.

Pyotr Petrovitch spun around.

"What vileness!" Lebeziatnikov repeated, staring right at him.

Luzhin jumped—everyone saw it and remembered. Lebeziatnikov walked in.

"And you called me as a witness?" he said to Pyotr Petrovitch.

"What do you mean?" Luzhin muttered.

"I mean you're a slanderer," Lebeziatnikov said, eyes fixed on him.

he was real mad. Raskolnikov stared hard at him, weighing every word. Silence again. Pyotr Petrovitch looked shocked, like he didn't know what hit him.

"If that's aimed at me..." he started, stumbling. "What's wrong with you? Lost your mind?"

"nah, i'm clear-headed. you're the crook! heard everything. waited to get it straight, but it still doesn't add up... why you did it, i can't figure."

"what did i do then? stop with the riddles! maybe you're drunk!"

"you might be the drunk, scoundrel, not me! vodka's not my thing. would you believe, he handed that hundred-rouble note to Sofya Semyonovna himself—I saw it, swear it! he did it!" Lebeziatnikov repeated to everyone.

"are you nuts, weakling?" Luzhin squealed. "she said herself i only gave ten roubles. how could i have slipped her the note?"

"saw it happen," Lebeziatnikov insisted, "and though i hate oaths, i'll swear it in court. you slipped it in her pocket while shaking her hand. caught it, plain as day!"

Luzhin went pale.

"lies!" he shouted. "how could you see that from the window? your eyes played tricks. you're delusional!"

"no delusion. even from a distance, i saw it. hard to spot a note, sure, but i knew it was a hundred-rouble because when you meant to give ten, you picked up a hundred. saw the switch, remembered it. when you got up, you swapped hands and nearly dropped it. watched you slip it in her pocket. saw it, swear it."

Lebeziatnikov was out of breath. murmurs all around, some threatened. crowd pressed in on Pyotr Petrovitch. Katerina Ivanovna rushed to Lebeziatnikov.
