"i misjudged you! protect her! you're her only ally! she's an orphan. heaven sent you!"

Katerina Ivanovna, half aware, knelt before him.

"nonsense!" Luzhin yelled, furious. "all nonsense! 'an idea struck you, you noticed'—what's that mean? so i gave it to her on the sly? why? for what? what's my deal with this...?"

what's the point of doing that? i don't get it, but i know what happened. no mistake. i remember thinking about it when i shook your hand. why slip it in her pocket secretly? trying to hide it from me because you know i don't agree with private charity? maybe you were embarrassed to give that much in front of me. or maybe a surprise for her, like finding a hundred-rouble note by chance. people love making charity look like magic. maybe you wanted to see if she'd thank you. or maybe you just didn't want anyone to know. thought about it so much, decided not to say anything, but then worried she'd lose it before noticing. that's why i came to tell her. but first, had to drop off a book and recommend some articles, then came here. and look at this mess now. could i have thought all that if i hadn't seen you do it?

lebeziatnikov finished, sweat pouring down. he was exhausted, couldn't even speak clearly. but his speech hit hard. everyone believed him. pyotr petrovitch was in trouble.

"what's it to me if you had silly ideas?" he yelled. "that's no proof. you might've dreamed it! you're lying, sir, out of spite because i don't agree with your ideas!"

but that didn't help him. people murmured disapproval.

"oh, that's your game now!" lebeziatnikov shouted. "nonsense! call the police, i'll swear to it! what i don't get is why he'd risk such a low move. pathetic man!"

"i can explain why," raskolnikov said, stepping forward. he was calm and sure. everyone could tell he knew what was up and the truth was coming.

“now i get it,” raskolnikov said to lebeziatnikov. “from the start, i thought there was something shady going on. had a hunch based on some things only i knew, and now i’ll break it down for everyone. your info cleared it up. listen up. this guy (pointing at luzhin) was engaged to my sister, avdotya romanovna raskolnikov. but he came to petersburg, we argued the day before yesterday, and i kicked him out. two witnesses can back me. he’s a spiteful guy. didn’t know he was staying here in your room, and saw me give katerina ivanovna money for the funeral, as a friend of marmeladov. he then wrote my mom a note saying i gave all my money, not to katerina ivanovna, but to sofya semyonovna, and hinted at my relationship with her in a nasty way. he wanted to drive a wedge between me and my family, making them think i wasted their money on unworthy things. yesterday, i told my mom and sister, in front of him, that i gave the money for the funeral and didn’t know sofya semyonovna. also said he wasn’t worth sofya’s little finger, despite how he talked about her. when he asked if i’d let sofya sit by my sister, i said i already did. he got rude when my family wouldn’t turn against me. we broke ties, and he got kicked out. now, pay attention: if he could prove sofya was a thief, he’d look right to my family, showing he had reasons to be mad about me treating sofya and my sister equally. he hoped to ruin my family ties and get back in their good books while getting back at me. that’s his game. that’s the whole point.”

raskolnikov wrapped up like this, and the crowd listened, though they interrupted with exclamations. he spoke clearly, calmly, and with conviction. his firm tone and serious face made an impact.

“yeah, yeah, that’s it,” lebeziatnikov agreed excitedly, “that must be it. he asked me if you were here when sofya walked in, if i saw you at katerina ivanovna’s. he pulled me aside, asked secretly. needed you here! that’s it!”

luzhin just stood there, pale and silent, like he's plotting his escape route. all this noise around him, and he knows running now means admitting guilt. the room's a mess, half-drunk folks shouting, especially that commissariat guy, who doesn’t even get it all. the poles are yelling insults in polish, and sonia's locked on raskolnikov, like he's her lifeline. meanwhile, katerina's exhausted, barely holding on, and amalia's just standing there, clueless, mouth open.

raskolnikov tries to talk, but nobody’s listening. they’re all on luzhin, throwing threats. but luzhin doesn’t care. his accusation against sonia failed, so he gets cocky: “let me through! no threats. you're not gaining anything here. i’ll see you in court. our judges aren’t blind or drunk like you say.”

he pushes through, says, “don’t let me see you in my room again! everything’s over. all my effort, wasted. you’re a fool. see a doctor.”

he forces his way out, but the commissariat clerk isn’t done. he throws a glass, misses luzhin, hits amalia. she screams, the clerk topples under the table. luzhin heads to his room, bails half an hour later. sonia, she’s always felt she could be wronged easily. thought being gentle would keep her safe. but now, she’s crushed. her heart aches, she cries, then bolts out after luzhin leaves. when the glass hits amalia, she snaps, blames katerina, and charges at her like a madwoman.

"Get out of my place! Now! Move it!"

Amalia Ivanovna started grabbing Katerina Ivanovna's stuff, chucking it on the floor. Katerina, looking like she might pass out, jumped from the bed and went for Amalia. But she didn’t stand a chance. Amalia waved her off like she was nothing.

"What! As if that slander wasn't enough—now this witch comes at me! What! On the day of my husband's funeral, I'm kicked out! After all I've given, she throws me and my kids out! Where do I go?" she cried, eyes blazing, "Is there no justice? Who protects us if not the law? I'll find it! Just wait, wicked woman! Polenka, stay with the kids. I'll be back. Even if it means waiting on the street. We'll see if there's justice!"

She threw on the green shawl Marmeladov talked about to Raskolnikov, squeezed through the chaotic, drunk crowd, and ran to the street—determined to find justice. Polenka sat scared with the little ones, waiting for her mom to return. Amalia Ivanovna stormed around, yelling and tossing things. The lodgers mumbled, argued, or broke into song...

"Time for me to go," thought Raskolnikov. "Alright, Sofya Semyonovna, let's see what you say now!"

And he headed toward Sonia’s place.

CHAPTER IV

Raskolnikov had been all in for Sonia against Luzhin, even with all his own mess. After dealing with the morning's chaos, he felt some relief in the shift, aside from his personal drive to stand up for Sonia. He was a bit on edge, especially thinking about telling Sonia who killed Lizaveta. He knew it’d be painful and tried to push it away. So when he left Katerina’s shouting, “Well, Sofya Semyonovna, let's see what you say now!” he was still riding high from beating Luzhin. But when he got to Sonia’s door, fear hit him. He paused, wondering if he really had to say it. Odd question, cause he knew he couldn't hold it in. Didn't know why, just felt it, and the weight of it almost got him. He opened the door fast and saw Sonia, sitting with her head in her hands. She got up as soon as she saw him, like she’d been waiting.

"What would've happened to me without you?" she said quickly, meeting him in the middle of the room.

She'd been waiting to say that.

Raskolnikov sat down in the chair Sonia had just left. She stood there, just like yesterday.

"Well, Sonia?" he asked, voice shaky. "This is all about 'your social position,' right? Did you catch that?"

Her face said it all.

"Just don't talk like yesterday," she cut in. "Please, not that. There's enough misery."

She forced a smile, worried he'd take her wrong.

"Leaving was dumb. What's happening there now? I thought... you’d show up."

He told her Amalia Ivanovna was kicking them out and Katerina Ivanovna was off "seeking justice."

"My God!" Sonia exclaimed. "Let's go now...."

She grabbed her cape.

"Same thing every time!" Raskolnikov snapped. "You only think of them! Stay with me a bit."

"But... Katerina Ivanovna?"

"You won't lose her," he said, annoyed. "She'll find you. And if you’re not here, you'll get blamed...."

Sonia sat, tense. Raskolnikov stared at the floor, thinking.

"Luzhin didn't want to press charges this time," he started, not looking at her. "But if he did, he could've put you in prison if not for Lebeziatnikov and me. Understand?"

"Yes," she whispered, worried.

"And what if I wasn't there? Lebeziatnikov showing up was luck."

Sonia stayed silent.

"And if you went to prison, then what? Remember what I said yesterday?"

Still no answer. He waited.

"I thought you'd say 'don't speak of it, stop.'" Raskolnikov laughed, but it was forced. "Silent again?" he asked after a minute. "We gotta talk about something. How would you handle a 'problem' like Lebeziatnikov might say?" (He was losing the thread.) "No, really. Imagine you knew all Luzhin's plans. Katerina Ivanovna and the kids and you—since you don’t count yourself. Polenka too... going the same way. If it was up to you whether he or they should live, what would you do?"

Sonia looked at him, uneasy. There was something off in his question, roundabout.

"I knew you'd ask something like that," she said, curious.

"Bet you did. But what's the answer?"

"Why ask about impossible things?" Sonia replied, hesitant.

"So it's better Luzhin lives and does bad things? You can't decide even that!"

"But I can’t know Providence’s plan... Why ask what can't be answered? How is it my choice who lives and dies?"

"Oh, if Providence is involved, can't do anything," Raskolnikov grumbled.

"Just say what you mean!" Sonia cried, distressed. "Are you just here to torment me?"

She lost control, crying hard. He watched her, gloomy. Time dragged on.

"yeah, Sonia, you're right," he finally said, voice dropping. Arrogance fell away, he seemed smaller. "Told you yesterday I wasn't here to ask forgiveness, but here I am doing just that... what I said about Luzhin and Providence was for me. I was asking forgiveness, Sonia..."

He tried to smile, but it was weak, incomplete. Head down, face buried in his hands.

Then, out of nowhere, a strange hate for Sonia flared up. But when he looked at her, saw those anxious eyes full of love, that hate just disappeared. It wasn't real; just a passing storm. The moment had come.

He hid his face again, then went pale, stood up, sat on her bed without a word.

It felt like that moment before he took the axe to the old woman. Felt like he couldn't waste another minute.

"What's wrong?" Sonia asked, scared.

He couldn't speak. This wasn't how he planned to say it. She sat beside him, waiting. Her heart raced while his lips struggled to form words.

"What's wrong?" she asked again, moving back a little.

"Nothing, Sonia, don't worry... it's nothing, really," he muttered, delirious. "Why am I here torturing you?" he suddenly added, looking at her. "Why indeed? I've been asking that."

He'd probably asked himself that not long ago, but now he was lost, trembling.

"You're suffering," she said, distressed.

"It's nonsense... listen, Sonia." He managed a pale smile for a moment. "Remember what I was gonna tell you yesterday?"

Sonia waited, tense.

"I said maybe I was saying goodbye forever, but if I came today, I'd tell you who killed Lizaveta."

She started trembling.

"So you meant it yesterday?" she whispered. "How do you know?" she asked, regaining her senses.

Sonia's face turned even paler, breath shallow.

"I know."

She paused.

"Have they found him?" she asked, timid.

"No."

"Then how do you know?" she pressed, almost silent.

He looked at her, intense.

"Guess," he said, with that helpless smile.

She shuddered.

"But why scare me like this?" she asked, smiling like a kid.

"I must be close to him... since I know," Raskolnikov continued, eyes locked on her. "He... didn't mean to kill Lizaveta... it was an accident. He went for the old woman when she was alone, but Lizaveta walked in... so he killed her too."

Another intense moment passed. They just stared.

"You can't guess?" he asked suddenly, like jumping off a cliff.

"n-no..." Sonia whispered.

"take a good look."

soon as he said it, that same feeling hit his heart cold. looking at her, he saw lizaveta's face in hers. remembered lizaveta's look when he came at her with the axe, how she backed to the wall, hand out like a scared kid, eyes wide. sonia looked the same now. helpless and terrified, she pressed her fingers to his chest, slowly got up from the bed, backing away but keeping her eyes locked on him. her fear crept into him too. he stared back, almost with a child's smile.

"have you guessed?" he whispered.

"good god!" she wailed, her voice breaking.

she fell to the bed, face in the pillows, but then sprang up, grabbed his hands tight, eyes searching his face for any flicker of hope. nothing there, only truth. later she'd wonder why she was so sure, why she felt she’d known all along.

"stop, sonia, enough! don't torture me," he pleaded, sounding broken.

not at all how he planned to tell her, but here they were.

she stood, lost, hands wringing, then sat back next to him, shoulder nearly touching. suddenly, she dropped to her knees, crying out, not sure why.

"what have you done to yourself?" she said, desperate, then threw her arms around his neck, clutching tightly.

raskolnikov pulled back, gave her a sad smile.

"you’re strange, sonia—you kiss and hug me after that... you don’t know what you’re doing."

"no one, no one’s as unhappy as you!" she cried, not hearing him, breaking into hysterical tears.

a feeling, long buried, filled his heart, softening it. he let it. tears formed, clung to his lashes.

"then you won’t leave me, sonia?" he asked, almost hopeful.

"no, no, never, nowhere!" sonia cried. "i’ll follow you everywhere. oh, my god! why didn’t i know you before! why didn’t you come sooner?"

"here i am now."

"yes, now! what do we do? together, together!" she repeated, hugging him again. "i’ll follow you to siberia!"

he pulled back, a cold smile on his lips.

"maybe i don’t want to go to siberia yet, sonia," he said.

sonia looked at him quickly.

she was hit hard by the thought of murder. his voice sounded different, like it wasn't him. she looked confused, questions pouring in. she couldn't believe it: "him? a murderer?"

"what's going on? where am i?" she asked, lost. "how could you, a man like you... do this? why?"

"oh, to steal. drop it, sonia," he said, tired and annoyed.

sonia was shocked but then cried out: "you were starving! was it for your mother?"

"no, sonia," he mumbled, turning away. "i wanted to help her, but that's not it... don't make it worse, sonia."

she clasped her hands. "is this real? how could you give your last penny and then... murder? the money you gave katerina... was it?"

"no," he cut in. "that was from my mother, came when i was sick. razumihin saw it. it was mine."

sonia struggled to understand.

"and that money... i don't even know if there was any," he added, thinking aloud. "i took a purse, didn't look inside. buried it all. it's still there."

"then why... you said you robbed, but took nothing?" she pressed.

"i don't know... haven't decided yet," he said, snapping back with a wry smile. "what nonsense, huh?"

sonia wondered if he was mad but dismissed it. "no, something else."

"sonia," he said, serious now, "if i'd killed just for hunger, i'd be happy now. you must see that! what do you gain if i admit wrong? was that why i came to you?"

sonia tried to speak, but couldn't.

"i asked you yesterday because you're all i have."

"where to?" sonia asked, hesitant.

"not to steal or kill," he smiled bitterly. "we're different... only now i see where i asked you to go. yesterday i didn't know. i just wanted you not to leave me. you won't, right, sonia?"

she squeezed his hand.

"why’d i tell her? why’d i let her know?" he said, looking at her with a mess of emotion. "you’re waiting for an explanation, Sonia, but if i tell you... you won’t get it, and it’ll just hurt you. but here you are, crying and hugging me. why? cause i can’t handle this alone, so i dump it on you, and you’re the one who suffers. how can you love such a mess?"

"but aren’t you suffering too?" Sonia replied.

that hit him. softened him up for a second.

"Sonia, i've got a bad heart. remember that. i came here cause i’m no good. some men wouldn’t have. but i’m a coward. a mess. but that’s not the point. i need to talk, but don’t know where to start."

he stopped, thinking hard.

"we’re so different," he said again. "and why did i come? i’ll never forgive myself for it."

"no, it’s good you came," Sonia said. "better i know. much better!"

he looked at her, pained.

"what if that’s true?" he said, realizing something. "yeah, that’s it! i wanted to be like Napoleon, that’s why i killed her. get it now?"

"n-no," Sonia whispered. "just talk, i’ll understand eventually!" she pleaded.

"you will? alright, let’s see." he paused, lost in thought.

"here’s the thing: i asked myself, what if Napoleon was in my shoes, no Toulon, no Egypt, just some old pawnbroker in the way? would he hesitate? feel bad it wasn’t grand? i tortured myself over it. then i realized, nah, he’d do it without a second thought, wouldn’t even see the problem. so i followed his lead. that’s how it was. think it’s funny? Sonia, the joke’s on me."

Sonia didn’t find it funny at all.

"just tell me straight," she asked quietly.

he turned to her, looked at her sadly, and took her hands.

you're right again, sonia. most of it’s just talk. look, my mom’s got almost nothing, and my sister, she got a good education but ended up stuck as a governess. all their hopes were pinned on me. i was in college, but couldn’t afford to stay, had to drop out. even if i’d stayed, ten or twelve years later, maybe i’d be a teacher or clerk making a thousand roubles, tops. by then, mom would be worn out, and my sister... well, who knows how she'd be doing. it’s tough to ignore everything, let your family suffer, and just accept it. why do that? then, if i got married, had kids, they’d be in the same mess. so i decided to take the old woman’s money, use it to stay in school, and build a new, independent life. yeah... i messed up killing her... that’s enough.

finished talking, he slumped over.

“that’s not right, that’s not it,” sonia cried. “how could you... no, it’s wrong.”

“you see it’s wrong. but it’s the truth.”

“that’s not truth! good god!”

“i killed a louse, sonia, a worthless creature.”

“a human being—a louse!”

“i know it wasn’t a louse,” he replied, eyes on her. “i’m talking nonsense, sonia. have been for a while. it’s not that, you’re right. there were other reasons! haven’t talked to anyone for so long, sonia... my head’s killing me.”

his eyes were feverish, almost delirious. the exhaustion was clear. sonia saw his pain. she felt dizzy too. he talked so oddly; it seemed to make sense, but... “but how, how! good god!” she wrung her hands, desperate.

no, sonia, that's not it, he started again out of nowhere, like a new thought hit him hard—better... imagine this: i'm vain, jealous, mean, low, vengeful, and maybe leaning a bit toward crazy. (let's lay it all out! they've talked about madness already, i noticed.) i told you i couldn't keep up at university. but maybe i could've. mom would've sent the money for fees, and i could've earned enough for clothes, boots, and food, no doubt. lessons showed up at half a rouble. razumihin works! but i got sulky and refused. (yeah, sulkiness, that's it!) i holed up in my room like a spider. you've seen my place.... and sonia, you know low ceilings and tiny rooms shrink the soul and the mind? hated that garret! but i wouldn't leave it! wouldn't on purpose! didn't go out for days, wouldn't work, wouldn't even eat, just lay there doing nothing. if nastasya brought food, i'd eat, if not, i went all day without; wouldn't ask, on purpose, out of sulkiness! no light at night, lay in the dark, wouldn't earn money for candles. should've studied, sold my books; dust thick on notebooks. preferred lying still, thinking. kept thinking.... had dreams, weird dreams, no need to describe! then i thought... no, that's wrong! i kept asking: why am i so stupid? others are stupid—I know they are—why can't i be wiser? then i saw, sonia, waiting for everyone to get wiser takes too long.... later i realized it'll never happen, men won't change, not worth the effort. that's the law of their nature, sonia.... and now i know, sonia, whoever's strong in mind and spirit will rule. anyone daring is right in their eyes. he who despises most things will make laws for them, and the one who dares most is most right! that's how it's been and will always be. blind not to see it!

while raskolnikov looked at sonia, he didn't care if she got it or not. fever had him; he was in a dark ecstasy (definitely too long without talking to anyone). sonia felt his grim belief had become his faith and code.

i realized then, sonia, power is given to the man who dares to grab it. only one thing needed: one must dare! then for the first time, an idea formed in my mind that no one thought of before me, no one! saw clear as day how odd it is no one in this crazy world dared to go straight for it and send it all flying to hell! i... i wanted _to have the daring_... and i killed her. i just wanted the daring, sonia! that was it!

oh hush, hush, cried sonia, clasping her hands. you turned away from god and god's let you fall, handed you to the devil!

then sonia, i'd lay there in the dark, everything just clicking, and i’d wonder, was it the devil tempting me?

“stop laughing, you blasphemer! you don’t get it, you just don’t! oh god, he just won’t understand!”

“sonia, i’m not laughing. deep down, i know it was the devil leading me. just stop, sonia, listen.” he pushed on, sounding all heavy. “i’ve mulled over it, whispered it to myself in the dark... argued every angle. sick of it, sick of thinking. wanted to forget, start fresh, stop the thinking. you think i just jumped in like a fool? no, i thought i was wise, and that was my downfall. i knew i didn’t have the right to seek power, i wasn’t napoleon. if i asked those questions, i knew i wasn’t meant for it. it was torture, sonia, and i just wanted to break free: murder for my own sake. no excuses. not for my mother, not to become some benefactor. nonsense. it was for me, only me. didn’t care if i helped or harmed others. wasn’t about the money either, sonia, it was something else.... i wanted to know if i was just like everyone else or something more. could i cross lines? am i bold or just scared?

“to kill? have that right?” sonia clasped her hands.

“sonia!” he snapped, ready to fire back but held silent, annoyed. “stop interrupting. i’m proving one thing: the devil led me, showed me i wasn’t meant for that path, just another louse. he mocked me, and now i’m here with you. if i wasn’t a louse, would i be here? listen: when i went to the old woman’s, i only went to try.... know that!

“and you killed her!”

“but how? is that how murder’s done? i’ll tell you someday. did i kill her? no, i killed myself. crushed myself forever. it was the devil, not me. enough, sonia, enough! just let me be!” he suddenly broke, agony ripping through, “just let me be!” 

he leaned forward, elbows on knees, head clamped tight in his hands.

"what suffering!" Sonia's voice cracked.

"well, what now?" he snapped, lifting his head, face twisted with despair.

"what to do?" she shouted, leaping up, eyes suddenly sharp. "stand up!" (she yanked him by the shoulder; he rose, dazed.) "go now, right now, to the cross-roads. bow. kiss the earth you dirtied and bow to the world. yell, 'im a murderer!' then God might give you life again. will you do it?" she trembled, clutching his hands, eyes blazing.

he stared at her sudden fire.

"Siberia, Sonia? i have to turn myself in?" he asked, voice heavy.

"suffer and make it right, that's what."

"no! im not going, Sonia!"

"how will you live? what for?" Sonia cried, "how can you face your mom? (oh, what'll they do now?) what am i saying? you left them already. oh, God! he knows it. how can he live alone? what will you become?"

"don't be childish, Sonia," he said soft. "what wrong did i do them? why go? what to say? it's just a shadow.... they kill millions and call it virtue. they're liars, Sonia! not going. and what to say? i killed her but didn't take the money, hid it under a stone?" he smirked bitterly. "they'd laugh, call me a fool for not taking it. coward and fool! they wouldn't get it and don't deserve to. why go? i won't. don't be a child, Sonia...."

"it'll crush you, too much!" she pleaded, hands out, desperate.

"maybe i've been too hard on myself," he mused, "maybe i'm a man, not a bug, too quick to judge myself. i'll fight again."

a proud smile crept on his lips.

"what a load to carry! all your life, all your life!"

"i'll get used to it," he said, grim and deep in thought. "listen," he started, "stop crying, it's time to deal with the facts: cops are after me...."

"oh!" Sonia gasped in fear.

why are you worried? you want me in siberia, now you're scared? listen, im not giving up. i'll fight it. they got no real evidence. yesterday felt like the end, today looks better. everything they got can be explained two ways. i'll twist their accusations to my favor, understand? i learned my lesson. they'll probably arrest me. but something happened, they didn't today. maybe they'll come, maybe not. doesn't matter, Sonia. they'll let me out. no real proof. can't convict on what they've got. just letting you know... i'll try to explain it to my mom and sister so they're not scared. my sister's future's secure, i think... my mom's too. well, that's it. be careful. will you visit me in prison?

"i will, i will."

they sat quiet, like they were tossed up by a storm on a deserted shore. he looked at Sonia, felt her love, and it was heavy, painful. strange feeling. on the way to her, he thought she'd ease the pain, but now felt worse.

"Sonia," he said, "better not visit me in prison."

Sonia cried, said nothing. moments passed.

"have a cross?" she asked, like it just hit her.

he didn't get it at first.

"no, of course not. take this cypress one. i got another from Lizaveta. swapped my ikon for her cross. i'll wear Lizaveta's, you take this. it's mine, you know," she pleaded. "we'll suffer together, bear our cross together."

"give it to me," said Raskolnikov.

he didn't want to upset her, but pulled back his hand.

"not now, Sonia. later," he said to comfort her.

"yes, later," she echoed, "when you meet your suffering, then wear it. come to me, i'll put it on you, we'll pray, go together."

then came three knocks at the door.

"may i come in, Sofya Semyonovna?" a familiar voice said.

Sonia jumped. Lebeziatnikov's head popped in.

CHAPTER V

Lebeziatnikov looked uneasy.

"i've come to see you, Sofya Semyonovna," he started. "excuse me... thought i'd find you," he said to Raskolnikov, "didn't mean anything... like that... just thought... Katerina Ivanovna's gone mad," he blurted, turning from Raskolnikov to Sonia.

Sonia screamed.

looks like we're in a mess. she got kicked out somewhere, maybe roughed up. tried to see your dad's old boss, but he was out, so she went to another general's place. she was so determined she got him pulled from dinner. you can guess what happened next—yep, thrown out. she claims she yelled at him and tossed something. sounds about right. how she didn't get arrested, who knows? now she's telling everyone, even Amalia Ivanovna, but she's a mess, shouting and thrashing around. says she'll take the kids, hit the streets with a barrel-organ, have them sing and dance, collect money. wants to make a scene under the general's window to show everyone these kids, once respectable, are begging. she's got them crying, beating them, teaching them songs and dances. tearing up clothes to make little caps like they're actors. she's out of control, won't listen to reason. it's chaos.

Sonia hears this, grabs her stuff, and bolts out. Raskolnikov and Lebeziatnikov tail her. 

“she's lost it,” Lebeziatnikov says to Raskolnikov on the street. “didn't want to scare Sofya, so i said ‘it seems like it,’ but no doubt she's mad. heard tubercles can hit the brain in consumption. shame i don't know medicine. tried talking sense to her, no dice.”

“did you mention the tubercles?”

“not exactly. wouldn’t have helped. but if you can logically prove someone shouldn't be crying, they’ll stop, right? you think they won't?”

“life’s not that simple,” Raskolnikov replies.

“sorry, sorry. might be hard for Katerina Ivanovna to get, but in Paris they’ve been trying to cure insanity with logic. a professor thought madness was just bad logic, an error in judgment. he’d correct them, and supposedly it worked. but he used douches too, so who knows what really did the trick.”

Raskolnikov tunes out, reaches his place, nods to Lebeziatnikov, and goes in. Lebeziatnikov snaps back to reality and hurries off.

raskolnikov stepped into his tiny room and just stood there. why come back? he looked at the worn-out wallpaper, dust, his sofa. outside, someone was hammering away. he tiptoed to the window, peered out, but the yard was empty. same old scene—sickly geraniums in the neighbor's window, laundry flapping. all too familiar. he turned away, sunk into the sofa.

never felt this alone before.

thought he'd end up hating sonia, now that he'd messed up her life too.

"why beg for her tears? why drag her down? it's low."

"i'll stay alone," he decided, "she shouldn't visit the prison."

few minutes later, a weird smile crossed his face. maybe siberia's not so bad after all.

he sat lost in thought until dounia walked in. she paused at the door, then sat across from him, like before. he just stared at her.

"don't be mad, brother. just a minute," she said.

her eyes were soft, filled with love.

"i know everything now. dmitri prokofitch told me. they're hounding you on some stupid suspicion. he says there's no real danger, but i get why you're upset. just don't let it change you. i'm not judging you for pulling away. i'd do the same with such a burden. i'll keep mom calm, tell her you'll visit soon. just come once, she's your mom. i came to say—" she got up "—if you need anything, i'll be there. goodbye!"

she moved to leave.

"dounia!" he called, stepping towards her. "that razumihin, dmitri prokofitch, he's a good guy."

she blushed.

"well?" she waited.

"he's solid, works hard, loves truly. goodbye, dounia."

she turned red, then looked worried.

"are we parting for good? is that why—"

"doesn't matter. goodbye."

he faced the window. she lingered, uneasy, then left, troubled.

wasn't cold to her. just a moment there he wanted to hug her goodbye, maybe even confess everything, but didn't dare touch her hand.

"later, she'll remember, shiver thinking i stole a kiss."

would she handle it? nah, girls like her don't. never do. thought of sonia.

air from the window felt fresh. daylight fading. grabbed my cap, headed out.

sick? sure, but don't dwell on it. mind's constantly racing, keeps me on my feet. won't last forever though.

wandered without a plan. sun setting. misery's been creeping in, not sharp, but heavy. feels like it'll last forever. gnaws at me every evening.

“this weakness, just physical, tied to the sunset or something, makes you do dumb things. you'll end up at dounia's, and sonia's too,” muttered to myself.

heard my name. turned around. leb ran up.

“you won't believe it. checked your room. she's gone through with it, took the kids. sonia and i tried finding them. she's banging a frying-pan, making them dance. kids are crying. stopping at corners, shops; crowd's following. come on!”

“sonia?” i asked, rushing with leb.

“she's a mess. well, not just sonia, katerina's lost it too. she's completely mad. cops'll be involved. imagine the chaos... they're by the canal, near the bridge, close to sonia's.”

just by the bridge on the canal, not far from sonia's place, there's this crowd gathered. mostly kids from the street. you could hear katerina ivanovna's voice from the bridge, loud and breaking. she's got on this old dress and a green shawl, straw hat that's seen better days, crushed and all. she's all over the place, tired and gasping for breath. her face, you can tell she's sick, worse off in the sun than she'd be inside.

she's not letting up though, keeps getting more worked up. she's trying to get the kids to dance and sing, yelling, coaxing, even hitting them when they dont get it. then she's at the crowd, appealing to anyone who looks like they've got money, talking about how these kids are from a "genteel" home, how they're not supposed to be like this. if someone laughs or jeers, she's right there, arguing.

some are laughing, some just watching, but everyone's curious. no frying-pan like lebeziatnikov mentioned, but she's clapping her hands, making the kids perform. she tries to sing too but that cough stops her, makes her curse and cry. it's kolya and lida's tears that's really setting her off. they've tried dressing the kids up, kolya in a makeshift turban, lida in a cap with an old feather. polenka's in her regular clothes, clinging to her mom, scared of the crowd.

sonia's trailing after katerina, crying, begging her to come back home. but katerina's not hearing it.

"Stop it, Sonia, just stop," she said, breathing heavy and coughing. "You don’t get it; you're like a kid! I told you before, im not going back to that drunk German. Let everyone in Petersburg see the kids begging, even if their dad was an honorable man who served true and died for it." (Katerina Ivanovna made this story up but believed it by now.) "Let that general see it too! And you're silly, Sonia: what do we have to eat? Tell me. We’ve burdened you enough, I won’t keep on like this! Oh, Rodion Romanovitch, you’re here?” she called, spotting Raskolnikov and rushing to him. “Tell this silly girl, nothing better could be done! Even organ-grinders make a living, and everyone will know we're not the same, that we’re an honorable, grieving family brought to beg. That general will lose his post, mark my words! We'll play under his windows daily, and if the Tsar passes by, I'll kneel, put the kids in front, show them and say ‘Defend us, father.’ He’s the father of the fatherless, he’s merciful, he’ll protect us, you’ll see, and that despicable general... Lida, stand up straight! Kolya, you'll dance again. Why the tears? Crying again! What’s wrong with you, foolish child? Man, what do I do with them, Rodion Romanovitch? If you only knew how clueless they are! What do you do with such kids?”

And she, almost on the brink of tears herself—still didn’t stop rambling on—gestured to the crying kids. Raskolnikov tried convincing her to head home, even hinting, hoping it’d appeal to her pride, that it wasn’t right for her to roam the streets like an organ-grinder when she was to become a school principal.

"boarding-school? ha! that's a pipe dream," katerina laughed, cough interrupting. "no, rodion, that dream's dead! everyone's left us!... and that general... i threw an inkpot at him, right there in the waiting room. signed my name, tossed it, ran. they're scoundrels! but nevermind them, i'll handle the kids myself. polenka, what you got? just two farthings? those stingy fools! they just mock us. look at that idiot over there laughing." she pointed at a man. "it's kolya's fault he's such a handful. what is it, polenka? speak in french, _parlez-moi français_. i taught you some words, remember? show them you're well-bred, not like street performers. no puppet shows here, we're singing a proper song. what should we sing? come on, rodion, we're here to pick a tune, get some coins, something kolya can dance to. it's all on the fly. got to plan it out and rehearse, then we'll go to nevsky where the folks are classier, they'll notice us. lida only knows 'my village' and it's overdone. we need something classier. thought of anything, polenka? help your mom out! my memory's shot. can't do 'an hussar.' let's sing in french, 'cinq sous,' i taught you that. it's in french, folks will see you're well-bred, it'll touch them. maybe 'marlborough s’en va-t-en guerre,' that's a lullaby in fancy homes.

“_Marlborough s’en va-t-en guerre Ne sait quand reviendra_...” she started. “no, ‘cinq sous’ is better. kolya, hands on hips. lida, turn around. polenka and i will sing and clap!

“_Cinq sous, cinq sous Pour monter notre menage_.”

(cough-cough-cough!) "fix your dress, polenka, it’s slipping," she panted. "act proper and genteel, show you're well-born. i said the dress should be longer, sonia, your fault it’s short. now see how it fits... why the tears, you all? come on, kolya, start. hurry up! oh, what a handful!

“Cinq sous, cinq sous.

"policeman again! what's he want?"

a cop pushed through, but then this official guy, maybe fifty, with a medal, stepped up and handed katerina a green three-rouble note. she gave him a polite bow, grateful.

"thanks, sir," she starts, all high and mighty. "we’re in a tough spot (grab the cash, Polenka: see, there's good folks ready to help a lady in trouble). these kids, they’re from a decent family, even got some fancy connections. and that general, eating his grouse... got mad at me for interrupting. ‘sir,’ i said, ‘help the orphans, you know my late husband, Semyon Zaharovitch, and on the day he died, some jerk slandered his only daughter.’... that cop again! help me," she yells at the official. "why’s that cop coming over? we just ran from one. what do you want, fool?"

"can’t make noise here. not allowed."

"you’re making noise. might as well be an organ grinder. what’s it to you?"

"need a license for that, and you don’t have one. draws a crowd. where you staying?"

"a license?" Katerina Ivanovna cries. "just buried my husband today. why would i need a license?"

"calm down, madam," says the official. "come on, i’ll take you out of here. not a place for you. you’re sick."

"sir, you don’t get it," Katerina Ivanovna screams. "we’re heading to Nevsky.... Sonia, Sonia! where's she? she’s crying too! what’s with all of you? Kolya, Lida, where are you going?" she yells, scared. "oh, silly kids! Kolya, Lida, where’d they run off to?..."

Kolya and Lida, freaked by the crowd and their mom's wild antics, grab hands and bolt when they see the cop. crying and wailing, poor Katerina Ivanovna chases them. she’s a sad mess, running and gasping for air. Sonia and Polenka race after them.

"bring them back, Sonia! oh dumb kids!... Polenka! grab them.... i'm doing this for you..."

she trips and falls.

"she’s hurt, she’s bleeding! oh no!" Sonia cries, bending over her.

everyone rushes over. Raskolnikov and Lebeziatnikov get there first, then the official, and the cop mutters, "great," knowing this’ll be a hassle.

"move along! move along!" he tells the crowd pressing in.

"she’s dying," someone shouts.

"she’s lost it," says another.

"lord have mercy," a woman says, crossing herself. "did they catch the kids? they’re coming back, the older one’s got them.... ah, naughty brats!"

on checking Katerina Ivanovna, they see she didn’t cut herself on a stone like Sonia thought. the blood’s from her chest.

"seen this before," the official mutters to Raskolnikov and Lebeziatnikov; "that’s consumption; blood chokes them. saw it with a relative recently... almost a pint of blood, all at once.... what now? she’s dying."

"this way, to my room!" Sonia begs. "i live here!... see, second house from here.... come on, hurry," she pleads. "get a doctor! oh, dear!"

official got the plan through. policeman even carried Katerina Ivanovna. they got her to Sonia's room, almost out cold, laid her down. still bleeding, but she's coming around. Raskolnikov, Lebeziatnikov, and the official went in with Sonia. crowd pushed back by the policeman. Polenka came with Kolya and Lida, all shaking and crying. some folks from Kapernaumovs’ room followed too. landlord's odd – one-eyed, hair like a brush. his wife looks scared, kids just staring. Svidrigaïlov showed up outta nowhere – Raskolnikov didn’t see him in the crowd. talk of a doctor, priest. official said doctor might be too late, but sent Kapernaumov to fetch one anyway.

Katerina Ivanovna caught her breath. bleeding stopped for now. her eyes, sick but sharp, on Sonia. Sonia's pale, sweating. Katerina asked to sit up. they propped her up.

“Where are the kids?” she murmured. “Polenka, you brought them? oh, silly kids, why’d you run...”

blood on her lips again. eyes moving around.

“so this is your room, Sonia. never been here.”

a pained look at Sonia.

“we’ve ruined you, Sonia. Polenka, Lida, Kolya, come! here they are, Sonia. they're yours now! im done! party's over.” (coughing) “lay me down, let me be.”

back on the pillow.

“priest? don’t want him. no spare rouble. no sins here. God knows my suffering... and if He won’t forgive, so be it.”

slipping in and out of delirium. sometimes she’d recognize folks, then back to mumbling. breathing rough, rattling.

“told him, your excellency,” she gasped. “that Amalia Ludwigovna, ah! Lida, Kolya, quick! _Glissez, glissez! pas de basque!_ Tap your heels, be graceful!

“_Du hast Diamanten und Perlen_

“what’s next? sing that.

“_Du hast die schönsten Augen Mädchen, was willst du mehr?_

“what a thought! _Was willst du mehr?_ what nonsense! ah, yes!

“midday heat in Dagestan.

“loved it! loved that song, Polenka! your dad sang it when we were engaged... oh, those days! let’s sing it! how’s it go? I forget. remind me!”

she got worked up, tried to sit. finally, in a hoarse, broken voice, she shrieked, every word a struggle, fear growing in her eyes.

“midday heat!... in Dagestan!... lead in my chest!...”

“your excellency!” she cried, tears flooding, “protect the orphans! you were their father’s guest... a real aristocrat...” she jolted, awareness returning, looked around terrified, then saw Sonia.

“Sonia, Sonia!” she whispered, surprised to see her. “Sonia dear, you’re here?”

they lifted her up again.

"enough! it's done. farewell, poor thing. i'm broken!" she said, her voice full of despair, and her head dropped back on the pillow.

she slipped into unconsciousness again, but it didn't last long. her face turned pale and still, mouth open, leg twitching. a deep sigh, and she was gone.

sonia clung to her, arms wrapped tight, head pressed against her chest without moving. polenka fell at her mom's feet, kissing them and crying hard. kolya and lida, not really getting it, just felt it was bad. they stood there in their costumes, turban and feathered cap, staring at each other, mouths open, both screaming.

and there was the "certificate of merit" by katerina ivanovna's pillow. raskolnikov noticed it when he walked to the window. lebeziatnikov came over.

"she's dead," he said.

"svidrigaïlov wants a word," he said, stepping back.

svidrigaïlov pulled raskolnikov aside. "i'll handle the funeral and stuff. got money to spare. i'll make sure the kids are set, good orphanage, money when they grow up. sofya won't have to worry. she's good, right? tell avdotya that's where the ten thousand's going."

"why are you doing this?" raskolnikov asked.

"oh, come on," svidrigaïlov laughed. "just being human. she wasn't just some pawnshop hag. luzhin, he keeps doing bad, but should she die? without help, polenka would end up the same."

he said it with a sly grin, eyes on raskolnikov, who went pale, hearing his own words from before. he stepped back, shocked.

"how do you know?" he whispered, barely breathing.

"i lodge next door at madame resslich's. kapernaumov's here, resslich's there. i'm a neighbor."

"you?"

"yeah," svidrigaïlov said, laughing hard. "you interest me, rodion. told you we'd be friends. now you see. you'll get along with me!"

strange times for Raskolnikov. felt like he was stuck in a fog, totally alone. looking back, he figured his mind was messed up, drifting in and out until everything blew up. got the dates and details all wrong. others had to fill in the blanks for him, and he realized he'd mixed up real events with stuff he just imagined. sometimes he'd panic out of nowhere, but there were also stretches where he just didn’t care, almost like he was shutting down. he avoided dealing with the important stuff that could ruin him if he ignored it.

Svidrigailov was on his mind a lot. ever since that tense moment with him in Sonia's room, Raskolnikov's thinking just fell apart. he knew he needed to sort things out with Svidrigailov, but kept putting it off. he'd find himself wandering alone, deep in thought, suddenly realizing he needed to face him. sometimes he even imagined they'd planned to meet. once he woke up outside under some bushes, not sure how he got there.

in the days after Katerina Ivanovna died, he bumped into Svidrigailov at Sonia’s place a few times. they talked but avoided the real issues. Svidrigailov was handling funeral plans and told Raskolnikov he’d sorted something out for Katerina’s kids, getting them into good places with the help of some money he’d arranged. mentioned he’d come by soon to discuss some things.

they had a quick chat on the stairs. Svidrigailov looked at him and said, “what’s up, Rodion? you're not all there. let’s talk it out, though I’m swamped with stuff. but really, what everyone needs is fresh air... more than anything.”

he shuffled aside for the priest and server coming up the stairs for the requiem. svidrigailov ordered it twice a day, no fail. svidrigailov moved on. raskolnikov paused, thought, and went after the priest into sonia’s room. stood at the door, listening to that slow, mournful singing. death always pressed on him, felt heavy and strange. hadn’t heard a requiem in ages. something else too, unsettling. looked at the kids kneeling by the coffin; polenka cried. sonia behind them, praying, weeping softly.

“she hasn’t spoken to me these last two days, not even a glance,” raskolnikov thought. sunlight filled the room, incense thick in the air; the priest read, “give rest, oh lord....” raskolnikov stayed the whole time. the priest gave his blessing and left, looking oddly around. after, raskolnikov approached sonia. she took his hands, rested her head on his shoulder. baffled him — no disgust, no hesitation, nothing. like she’d forgotten herself, at least that’s how he saw it.

sonia said nothing. raskolnikov squeezed her hand and left, feeling miserable. wanted to disappear somewhere, be alone forever. hadn’t managed to feel alone, though. even walking out of town, even in a quiet wood, felt something there, uneasy. didn’t scare him, just irritated him, so he’d hurry back to town, into the crowds, restaurants, busy streets. there felt easier, even more alone. one time, sat in a tavern listening to songs for an hour, actually liked it. then that unease hit again, like a conscience jab. “sitting here listening to songs, is this right?” but knew that wasn’t the only reason. something needed deciding, but couldn’t figure it out. a tangled mess. “no, better to struggle again! porfiry... or svidrigailov... need a challenge, an attack. yeah!” he thought. ran out of the tavern. thinking of dounia and his mother nearly panicked him. woke up that night on krestovsky island, feverish. walked home, got there at dawn. slept a few hours, fever gone, woke at two in the afternoon.

remembered katerina ivanovna’s funeral was today, glad he missed it. nastasya brought food; he ate like he hadn’t in days. clearer head now, calmer than past three days. wondered at his panic attacks earlier.

door opened, razumihin walked in.

“ah, eating, so not sick,” said razumihin. pulled up a chair across from raskolnikov.

he was pissed, didn’t hide it. spoke annoyed but calm, like he knew what he wanted.

“listen,” he started, dead serious. “honestly, y’all can go to hell, but I’m lost here. not asking questions, don’t want answers. if you start spilling secrets, I’d probably curse you out and leave. I’m here to know if you’re actually mad. everyone thinks you are. I kinda do too, seeing your crazy, nasty actions, especially how you’ve treated your mom and sister. only a monster or a madman would do that. so, are you mad?”

“when did you see them last?”

“just now. haven’t you? what’s up with you? I’ve come by three times. your mom’s really sick since yesterday. she wanted to see you. Avdotya tried stopping her; she wouldn’t listen. ‘if he’s losing it, who better than his mom to help?’ she said. we all came together, couldn’t let her come alone. begged her to stay calm. came in, you weren’t here; she waited ten minutes, then said, ‘if he’s out and forgot his mom, it’s humiliating for me to stand here.’ she went home sick, now she’s feverish. ‘he’s got time for his girl,’ she said. meant Sofya, your girl or whatever. I went to Sofya’s, wanted to see what’s up. saw the coffin, kids crying, Sofya fitting them in mourning clothes. no sign of you. I apologized, left, told Avdotya. so, no girl, likely you’re mad. but here you are, eating beef like you’ve been starving. madmen eat too, but you’re not mad. I’d swear it! not mad! so to hell with it. there’s a secret here, and I won’t bust my brain over it. just came to vent,” he said, standing up. “know what I’ll do now.”

“what’s that?”

“not your business.”

“you’re gonna go drink.”

“how... how’d you guess?”

“it’s obvious.”

Razumihin stood there.

“you’ve always been reasonable, never mad,” he said, a bit warmer. “you’re right: I’m gonna drink. bye!”

he started to leave.

“I talked to my sister—day before yesterday—about you, Razumihin.”

“about me! but... when’d you see her?”

Razumihin stopped, went pale.

you could see his heart thumping.

“she came here alone, sat, talked to me.”

“she did!”

“yeah.”

“what’d you say to her... about me?”

told her you were a good guy. honest and hardworking. didnt mention you love her. she knows already.

she knows?

yeah, clear as day. no matter what happens to me, youd be there for them. i trust you with that, Razumihin. i see how you love her, and i bet she feels the same. decide what you need to do about it.

Rodya! look... damn it! where you headed? if its a secret, fine. but ill figure it out... sure its something silly you cooked up. youre a solid guy, really.

thats what i was trying to say before you cut in. good call not digging into these secrets. let time work it out. a guy told me we need fresh air, and im gonna see what he meant.

Razumihin stood there, thinking hard.

hes into politics, for sure. about to do something big. and Dounia knows, he realized.

so Avdotya Romanovna sees you, and youre off to hear about fresh air, so that letter... must tie in, he thought.

what letter?

she got one today. really shook her. big time. talked about maybe parting soon... thanked me... locked herself in.

she got a letter? Raskolnikov mused.

yeah, and you didnt know? hmm...

both fell silent.

goodbye, Rodion. there was a time... never mind, goodbye. im not drinking. dont need to... all nonsense!

he left, but came back quick, looking away:

oh, remember that murder, Porfirys case, the old woman? murderer confessed. one of those painters. defended them before, remember? all that laughing and fighting was cover. cunning guy. confessed it all. fooled me. hes sly, but confession makes it believable. what a fool i was! i was on their side!

why does this matter to you? Raskolnikov asked, clearly shaken.

why? heard from Porfiry... mostly him.

Porfiry?

Porfiry.

"what... what did he say?" raskolnikov asked, a bit thrown.

"gave me a solid explanation. his own take on it, psychologically."

"he explained it? himself?"

"yeah, yeah; bye. i'll fill you in later, but i'm busy now. once i thought... but nevermind, another time!... why do i need a drink now? you've got me spinning without it. i'm dizzy, rodya! bye, i'm off. i'll be back soon."

he left.

"he's a political agitator, no doubt," razumihin decided, heading down the stairs. "dragged his sister into it; fits avdotya romanovna's style. they've got meetings!... she hinted at it too... her words, her hints... it all lines up! and how else to untangle this mess? hm! and i almost thought... good grief, what was i thinking! yeah, i lost it and wronged him! it was under that lamp in the hall. pfoo! what a crude, nasty thought! nikolay's a champ for confessing... it's all clear now! his sickness, his odd behavior... before this, at the university, how dark he was, how grim... but what's up with that letter? there's something there too, maybe. who sent it? i've got my suspicions...! no, i gotta find out!"

thinking of dounia, he pieced it all together, heart racing, and suddenly took off running.

as soon as razumihin left, raskolnikov got up, turned to the window, paced to a corner then another, like he forgot how small the room was, and sat back on the sofa. he felt, in a way, renewed; the struggle was back, and so was a way out.

"yeah, a way out had arrived! too stifling, too tight, the weight was too much. sometimes it was like a fog. since the scene with nikolay at porfiry’s, he felt trapped, no way out. nikolay’s confession, that day, then the scene with sonia; he acted and spoke in ways he never imagined before; he got weaker, instantly and deeply! and he agreed with sonia then, agreed in his heart he couldn’t keep living with this on his mind!

"and svidrigaïlov was a puzzle... he bothered him, sure, but not quite the same way. there might still be a fight with svidrigaïlov. svidrigaïlov could be an escape; porfiry was different.

"and so porfiry laid it out to razumihin, explained it _psychologically_. brought in his cursed psychology again! porfiry? but to think porfiry would even consider nikolay guilty, after what went down before nikolay showed up, after that one-on-one, which had only _one_ explanation? (raskolnikov couldn't stop replaying things from that scene with porfiry; he hated dwelling on it.) such words, such gestures between them, such looks, things said in such a tone and reached such a point, that nikolay, seen through by porfiry from the first word, first gesture, couldn't shake his belief.

"and to think even razumihin started to doubt! the scene under the lamp did its thing then. he rushed to porfiry... but why did porfiry handle him like that? what was the point of sidestepping with nikolay? he's got a plan; there's a scheme, but what's it? true, ages passed since that morning—way too long—and no sign or word from porfiry. well, that's not a good sign..."

raskolnikov grabbed his cap and headed out, still deep in thought. clarity, finally. "gotta deal with svidrigailov," he figured, "and quick. feels like he's waiting for me to make the first move." hate surged in him, enough to make him think he could take out porfiry or svidrigailov if pushed. maybe not now, but later.

"we'll see," he muttered.

then he opened the door and bam, there's porfiry. caught him off guard for a sec, but not shocked, not scared. just startled, then back on his game. "could this be it? how'd porfiry sneak up so quiet, like some cat?"

"didn't expect me, did you, rodion romanovitch?" porfiry chuckled. "been meaning to drop by. saw the chance and took it. you heading out? won't take long. just need a smoke."

"sit down, porfiry petrovitch." raskolnikov offered a seat, surprisingly warm. he'd be amazed at himself if he could see it.

this was it, the last stretch. like facing a brigand, but when the knife's finally at your throat, fear's gone.

he sat right across porfiry, eyes steady. porfiry lit a cigarette, squinting.

"come on, say it," raskolnikov's heart almost shouted.

porfiry finally spoke, "ah, these cigarettes! terrible habit, but i can't quit. doc says they're wrecking my lungs, but what's the alternative? i don't drink. it's all relative, rodion romanovitch!"

"playing games again," raskolnikov thought, disgusted. memories of their last chat hit him hard.

"came by the other night, you weren't here," porfiry continued, scanning the room. "walked in, saw the door open, looked around, then left. don't you lock up?"

raskolnikov's mood darkened. porfiry seemed to read him.

"came to talk things out, rodion romanovitch," porfiry said, patting his knee. then his face shifted, serious and worn. a glimpse of sadness, unexpected. never seen that look on him.

last time was strange, rodion. first time too, but this was something else. maybe i wasn't fair to you. remember how we left things? both shaky. felt ungentlemanly. but we're supposed to be gentlemen, right? remember where we ended up? really wasn't appropriate.

what's he playing at? raskolnikov thought, watching porfiry.

“better to be open," porfiry said, looking away. "these suspicions and scenes can't last. nikolay stopped it, or who knows what would've happened. that workman was next door then—can you imagine? you know he spoke to you after. but what you thought wasn't true: i didn't arrange anything. why not? it all hit me fast. barely had time to call the porters, you saw them. an idea hit me; i was sure then. even if i missed something, i'd find something else. wouldn't lose what i needed. you're irritable, rodion, more than your other traits. i saw that even back then. not everyone just blurts out their story. sometimes, if you push hard enough, but it's rare. i knew that. i needed a fact, anything solid, not just psychology. need something real if a man's guilty. surprising results come from that. i bet on your temperament, rodion, above all. had high hopes for you."

“what's your point now?” raskolnikov asked, not really thinking.

what's he going on about? does he think i'm innocent?

“my point? explaining myself. feel like i owe you that. want to clear up the whole mess, the misunderstanding. i caused you a lot of trouble, rodion. not a monster, understand what it means for a proud, impatient man to deal with that. i see you as noble, even if i don't agree with you. wanted to tell you straight, no tricks. liked you from the start, maybe you’ll laugh. you have the right. know you didn't like me, no reason to. but i want to fix that, show i'm a man with heart and conscience. speaking sincerely.”

Porfiry Petrovitch paused, all dignified. Raskolnikov felt that alarm kick back in. The idea that Porfiry thought he was innocent? That started to make him uneasy.

i won't bore you with the details, but here's the gist. rumors started floating around, no clue how they got to me, and then a random event set off my suspicions. could’ve missed it entirely. anyway, i was the first to zero in on you. the old lady’s notes didn’t help much. you were just one in a pile. then i heard about that scene at the office, vivid as anything. everything pointed your way, man. hundred suspicions don't make a proof, but you can't help where your mind goes. remember that article you wrote? we talked about it. i teased you, but honestly, it struck a chord. full of youthful fire and a bit gloomy, but raw and real. i thought, “this guy's not gonna take the usual path.” so, when things started happening, i was already watching. don’t think i was being malicious. i even had your room searched when you were down sick—unofficially, of course. nothing turned up, though. figured if you were guilty, you’d eventually show up yourself. and here you are. psychology’s tricky; it can twist both ways. but my gut kept telling me you’d come around. and you did.

why'd you have to come? your laugh, too, when you came in. remember that? i saw it clear as day, but if i wasn't expecting you, i wouldn't have caught it. moods twist perception, huh? then there's Razumihin... that stone where stuff was hidden. kitchen garden, right? told Zametov, then repeated it to me. when we tore into your article, how you explained things... every word had a double edge, like you hid a deeper meaning.

i hit a wall, started questioning. maybe it all means something else. i couldn't deny it seemed more natural. i was stuck. thought i'd find a small fact to ground me. when i heard about the bell, my heart jumped. that was my fact, didn't overthink it. would've paid to see you when that guy called you a murderer and you kept quiet. and then your trembling, the bell-ringing when you were out of it?

so can you blame me for messing with you? why'd you come right then? it's like someone sent you. if Nikolay hadn’t stepped between us... remember Nikolay? it was like lightning, caught me off guard. even when he gave good answers later, i didn't buy it. being stubborn has its perks. Morgenfrüh, i thought. what's Nikolay got to do with it?

Razumihin said you think Nikolay's guilty and told him so yourself...

his voice faltered, and he stopped. he'd been listening, all wound up, as this guy who saw right through him, started backpedalling. afraid to believe, yet searching those vague words for something solid.

"hey Razumihin," Porfiry Petrovitch said, almost relieved Raskolnikov finally spoke. "He-he-he! Had to sideline him; you know, two's company. Razumihin's not fit for this, he's just an outsider. He rushed over, looking pale... but let's not get into him. Back to Nikolay, want to know what kind of guy he is? First off, he's like a kid, not really a coward, more like an artist. Don't laugh. He's got heart, easily swayed. Can sing, dance, tell stories, folks come from miles to hear him. He's in school, laughs till he cries over nothing, drinks heavy sometimes, but like a child, not a habit. Stole once, didn't realize it, thought 'how's it stealing if you just pick it up?' Also, he's an Old Believer, a dissenter. His family's got Wanderers, and for two years, he was under some elder's wing. Learned all this from Nikolay and his village folks. And get this, he wanted to retreat into the wild, was full of zeal, prayed all night, read the old 'true' books till he went nuts.

Petersburg hit him hard, mainly women and booze. He forgot the elder and all that. An artist here liked him, visited him, then this mess hit him.

Got scared, tried to hang himself! Ran off! Russian trials freak people out. Who's to blame? We'll see how new juries do. Hope they do good! In prison, he remembered the elder; Bible came back. You know, Rodion Romanovitch, the power of 'suffering' with these folks. Not about suffering for anyone, just 'must suffer.' If it's by authorities, even better. I knew a prisoner, quiet guy, Bible every night, went mad, threw a brick at the governor, never did him wrong. Threw it wide, scared to hurt him. Well, we know what happens when a prisoner hits an officer. So 'he took his suffering.'"

so, pretty sure nikolay's caught up in this mess himself. got facts to back it. he doesn't know i know. you'd be surprised how many peasants are like this. the elder's got a hold on him, especially after the hanging attempt. he'll come back to me, you'll see. won't keep up his story for long. im watching him closely. he's clever on some things, but on others, he's lost. doesn't even know he's lost.

no, rodion, nikolay's not the guy. this is a twisted deal, a modern mess. all talk about blood renewing, life's comforts. it's all theories gone wrong. he went for it like jumping off a cliff, legs shaking. forgot to close the door, killed two for a theory, couldn't even take the money right. left it under a stone. suffered behind the door, relived the whole thing half-crazed. calls himself honest, thinks he's the victim. not nikolay's doing, rodion.

everything before sounded like backtracking, but this hit hard. raskolnikov jolted like he'd been struck.

"then... who...?" he gasped.

porfiry leaned back, shocked.

"who's the killer?" he echoed. "it's you, rodion. you're the one," he whispered, certain.

raskolnikov jumped up, stood, then sank back without a word. his face twitched.

"your lip's twitching again," porfiry noted. "think you're misunderstanding me, rodion. i'm here to lay it all out."

"i didn't kill her," raskolnikov whispered, scared.

"no, it was you, rodion," porfiry replied, firm.

ten minutes of silence. raskolnikov ran fingers through his hair. porfiry waited. suddenly, raskolnikov glared at him.

"same old game, porfiry. don't you get tired?"

"oh, cut it out. doesn't matter now. no witnesses here. i'm not here to hunt you down. whether you confess or not, i'm already convinced."

"Why’re you here?" Raskolnikov shot back, annoyed. "If you think I'm guilty, why not arrest me?"

"Oh, that’s your question? Let's break it down. First, arresting you right away doesn't help me."

"Why? If you’re sure, you should..."

"Ah, even if I'm convinced, that’s just in my head for now. Why lock you up? You seem to want it, since you’re asking. If I put you and that workman together, and you say, 'Were you drunk? Who saw me with you? I just thought you were drunk, and you were.' What can I say? Your story sounds better than his. The guy's a known drunk. Psychology’s all I’ve got, and it can flip. Besides that, nothing on you yet. I’m here to say I’ll arrest you, but it's not in my interest. Second reason I’m here..."

"Yeah, what’s the second reason?" Raskolnikov leaned in.

"I owe you an explanation. Don’t want you thinking I’m a monster. I genuinely like you, believe it or not. And third, I’m offering you a deal—confess. It’ll be better for you and me. Honest offer, right?"

Raskolnikov thought for a moment.

"Listen, Porfiry. You said there's only psychology, now you’re talking math. What if you're wrong?"

"No, Rodion Romanovitch, I’m not wrong. I’ve got a small fact, a gift from Providence."

"What fact?"

"Not telling. I can’t delay. I must arrest you. Think it over: it’s all the same to me now. I’m speaking for your sake. It’s better, Rodion."

Raskolnikov smirked darkly.

"That’s not just ridiculous, it’s shameless. Even guilty, why confess, if prison’s safer?"

"Rodion, don’t trust words too much. Prison might not be restful. That’s just theory, my theory. Maybe I’ve got secrets? Can’t show all my cards, heh! How can you ask why? A confession cuts your sentence. Another’s taken the blame, muddling the case. Think! I swear, your confession will shock everyone. We’ll wipe out the suspicions, make it seem like a moment of madness. I’m honest, Rodion, and I’ll stick to my word."

Raskolnikov stayed quiet, head down, thinking. Eventually, he smiled again, a sad, gentle smile.

"no!" he shot back, dropping the act with porfiry. "doesnt matter, im not interested in reducing the sentence."

"thats what i was afraid of!" porfiry blurted out, like it just slipped. "i feared you wouldnt care about that."

raskolnikov stared at him, eyes heavy.

"come on, dont throw life away," porfiry pressed. "youve got a lot ahead of you. why act like you dont want a lighter sentence? youre rushing."

"a lot of what ahead?" raskolnikov asked.

"life. you think you know it all? maybe this is gods way to pull you in. the chains wont last forever..."

"the time will be cut short," raskolnikov chuckled, bitter.

"is it the shame holding you back? maybe youre scared without even knowing it, cause youre young. but you cant fear confessing."

"ugh, cut it out," raskolnikov muttered, disgust thick in his voice.

he stood up like he was leaving, but sank down again, looking defeated.

"cut it out if you want, but youve lost hope, think im just buttering you up. how much life have you really seen? you cooked up a plan, then got mad when it failed and wasnt even unique. yeah, it was bad, but youre not all bad. you didnt lie to yourself for long, you jumped right to the end. how do i see you? like someone who'd grin at the tormentor, if they found faith or god. find it and youll live. you need a new scene. suffering can be good. suffer! maybe nikolay's right wanting to suffer. i know you doubt it, but dont overthink it; dive into life, dont hesitate. the floodll carry you to shore, set you right. what shore? cant say. but i believe you got a long road. you think my talk is just a script, but maybe later itll stick. might help you. thats why i talk. lucky you only killed the old lady. another idea mightve led to worse. maybe gods got a plan for you. take heart, fear less! scared of whats coming? dont be. you made your choice, brace for it. theres justice in it. you gotta meet it. i know you doubt, but life will bring you through. youll outlive this. what you need now is fresh air, fresh air, fresh air!"

raskolnikov jolted.

"but who are you? what prophet are you? from what high place you spouting this wisdom?"

who am i? just a guy with no more dreams left, that's all. maybe i got some feelings, some smarts, but my time's done. you're different though, life's still ahead. but who knows? maybe it'll just go up in smoke too. so what if you join another crowd? it's not about comfort, with your heart. no big deal if no one sees you for a while. it's not time deciding that, it's you. be the sun, everyone sees you. but first, you gotta be the sun. you smiling 'cause im sounding like a poet? think im buttering you up? maybe i am, heh. but decide for yourself how much im bluffing or being straight.

when you gonna arrest me?

i can give you a day or two more. think it over, pray if you want. it's for your good, trust me.

and if i run?

nah, you won't. a peasant would, someone following another's thoughts would. but you've stopped believing in your theory, what would you run with? hiding would be tough, you need a place that fits you. if you ran, you'd be back. you can't do without us. if i lock you up for a bit, you'll confess. might surprise yourself. suffering's something, Rodion. there's meaning there. don't laugh, there's truth in it. you won't run, Rodion.

Raskolnikov stood up, grabbed his cap. Porfiry did the same.

going for a walk? weather's nice unless a storm hits. could use fresh air.

Porfiry, don't think i confessed today, Raskolnikov said. you're odd, listened out of curiosity. admitted nothing, remember.

i know, i'll remember. look at him, he's shaking! don't worry, walk around. you won't go far. if something happens, got one request. awkward but key. if you think of ending things in some wild way, leave a note. mention the stone. more generous. till next time! good thoughts and choices to you!

porfiry left, duckin his head like he didnt want to catch raskolnikov's eye. raskolnikov stared out the window, waiting impatiently till he figured porfiry was out on the street. then he bolted too.

chapter 3

he was in a rush to get to svidrigaïlov's. what he expected from the guy, he didnt know. but svidrigaïlov had some strange hold on him. once he realized that, he couldnt let it go, and now was the time.

on the way, one thing bugged him: had svidrigaïlov gone to see porfiry?

he'd bet not. replayed porfiry’s visit in his head; nah, he wasnt there. but if he hasnt gone yet, will he? right now, he didnt think so. why? couldnt explain it, and didnt want to waste brain space on it then. it all nagged at him, but he couldnt focus. weird, but his worry about the future was just a dull buzz. something else gnawed at him—about himself, deeper. felt drained, even if his mind was sharper today.

was it all worth it? dealing with these little hassles after everything? trying to keep svidrigaïlov from going to porfiry’s—was that worth it? checking facts, spending time on a guy like svidrigaïlov?

sick of it all, really.

yet he was rushing to svidrigaïlov; hoping for something new? info, an out? clutching at straws, maybe. fate or instinct pulling them together? maybe just tired, hopeless; maybe svidrigaïlov was just there. sonia? why go to her now? for more tears? scared of sonia too. she was like a final judgment. his path or hers. right then, he couldnt face her. better to try svidrigaïlov? deep down, he knew he had to see him.

but what did they share? even their wrongs were different. the guy was shady, no doubt sly and maybe even nasty. stories about him were wild. sure, he was helping katerina ivanovna’s kids, but why? always had an angle, a plan.

another thought had been haunting raskolnikov. painful enough he tried hard to shake it. sometimes seemed like svidrigaïlov was trailing him. had he found out the secret? had plans for dounia? still did, right? likely. and if he knew the secret and used it to get to dounia...

this idea haunted him even in his sleep, but it had never been so clear till he headed to Svidrigaïlov. just thinking about it made him angry. if this changed everything, he'd have to spill his secret to Dounia. would he need to turn himself in to stop her from doing something crazy? that letter—she got one this morning. who in Petersburg would write to her? Luzhin? sure, Razumihin's around to watch over her, but he doesn't know the whole story. maybe he should clue Razumihin in. not a pleasant thought.

he had to see Svidrigaïlov fast, he decided. the specifics of their meeting didn’t matter much, as long as he got to the bottom of it. but if Svidrigaïlov was plotting against Dounia, then...

Raskolnikov was so beat by the past month that he saw only one answer: “then I’ll kill him,” he thought, cold and desperate.

suddenly he felt a crushing weight on his heart, stopped in the street, trying to figure out where he was. he'd ended up on X. Prospect, not far from the Hay Market. second floor on the left, a tavern, windows wide open, packed with people. music, shouting, chaos. he nearly turned back, confused why he was there, when he spotted Svidrigaïlov at a window, pipe in mouth, watching him. the shock was real. Svidrigaïlov looked like he was ready to slip away unnoticed. Raskolnikov pretended not to see, but his heart pounded. Svidrigaïlov didn’t want to be seen. but then he realized Raskolnikov had spotted him. the same sly game they played before. Svidrigaïlov grinned wider and wider. both knew they were watching each other. finally, Svidrigaïlov laughed.

“come on in if you're looking for me!” he yelled.

Raskolnikov went inside. found him in a small back room, away from the noisy crowd. merchants, clerks, everyone crammed in, drinking tea, singing loudly. billiard balls clicked nearby. on Svidrigaïlov’s table, a champagne bottle and half-full glass. a boy with a hand organ and a red-cheeked girl in a striped skirt, singing, stopped when Raskolnikov entered.

“enough,” Svidrigaïlov told her. she waited, serious.

“hey, Philip, a glass!” Svidrigaïlov called.

“i won’t drink,” Raskolnikov said.

"Drink up, Katia. I didn't mean this for you, but take it anyway. I'm done for today, you're free to go." He fills her glass and drops a yellow bill.

Katia downs the wine in one go, grabs the note, and gives Svidrigaïlov's hand a quick kiss, which he accepts like it's the norm. She leaves, the kid follows with his organ. Both pulled off the street. Svidrigaïlov's barely been in Petersburg a week, but he's settled in like an old-timer. Philip the waiter acts like they're best buddies now.

The door to the main room's got a lock. Svidrigaïlov owns this space, probably spends all day here. The place is a dump, nowhere near second-rate.

"Was looking for you," Raskolnikov starts, "but took a wrong turn from Hay Market to X. Prospect. Never go this way, usually right from Hay Market. But here you are. Weird."

"Why not just call it a miracle?"

"Could just be chance."

"That's the thing with you guys," Svidrigaïlov laughs. "Won't admit a miracle, even if you do think it. Always hedging. But not you, Rodion Romanovitch. You've got your own mind. That's why I got curious."

"Only that?"

"That's plenty," Svidrigaïlov's a bit buzzed, but just a bit—half a glass in.

"You came before you knew I had my own opinion," Raskolnikov notes.

"Different reasons then. Everyone's got their plans. About the 'miracle'—you've been out of it a few days. I told you about this place, no miracle. Told you where, when, how to find me. Remember?"

"I don't remember," Raskolnikov replies, surprised.

i’ve told you twice. address is stuck in your head now. you turned like a machine, didn't even know it. when i told you, didn't think you got it. you’re too obvious, Rodion. and plenty in Petersburg talk to themselves. city full of crazies. if we had scientists, they’d find a goldmine here. gloomy, strong, weird vibes all over. climate messes with you. it's the admin center too, so it spreads. but that's not the point. i’ve watched you. you leave your house all proud, then slump after a few steps. hands behind your back. you see nothing, start talking to yourself, waving your hands. you stop in the street. not good. someone else might be watching. it's not up to me to fix you, but you know what i mean.

“do you know im being followed?” raskolnikov asked.

“no idea,” svidrigaïlov replied, acting surprised.

“then leave me alone,” raskolnikov muttered.

“alright, we'll leave you alone.”

“tell me, if you come here to drink, and called me here, why'd you hide when i saw you from the street?”

“he-he! why'd you lie on your sofa pretending to sleep when i was there? i saw you were awake.”

“had my reasons. you know that.”

“and i might have mine, you just don't know them.”

raskolnikov leaned on the table, studying svidrigaïlov. strange face, like a mask. pale and red, lips too bright, beard and hair flaxen. eyes too blue, too heavy. something off about that face, looked young for his age. dressed sharp, big ring with a stone.

“do i have to deal with you too?” raskolnikov snapped. “you might be dangerous if you want to hurt me, but i won't bother anymore. if you mess with my sister or use what’s surfaced lately, i’ll deal with you before you get me locked up. you know i mean it. and if you’ve got something to say, say it quick. time’s short, might be too late soon.”

“why rush?” svidrigaïlov asked, curious.

"everyone's got plans," Raskolnikov replied, sounding all gloomy and impatient.

"you just told me to be open, and now you won't answer," Svidrigaïlov grinned. "you think I'm up to something and you're suspicious. that's fair. i want to get along, but i won't bother convincing you otherwise. it's not worth it, and i didn't plan to chat about anything serious."

"then why'd you want me? you came looking for me."

"just curious about you. your situation caught my interest. plus, you're her brother, and she talked a lot about you. seemed like you had some influence. isn't that enough? ha! your question's tricky. hard for me to answer. you came to hear something new, right? can't you imagine i wanted the same? maybe get something out of it. we're both rich in that way!"

"what could you get?"

"how should i know? look where i hang out—a crappy tavern. not much fun, but i gotta be somewhere. poor Katia, right? if i was a foodie, i'd be at a club. but look at this."

he pointed to a sad beef-steak and potatoes on a tin dish.

"you eaten? i'm good. don't drink much. just champagne sometimes, and even that gives me a headache. ordered it to perk up 'cause i'm heading out. hid like a kid before, thought you'd stop me. it's half-past four. got an hour. if i were something—a landowner, a dad, cavalry officer, whatever—might not be bored. thought you'd have something new."

"so, what are you? why'd you come here?"

"i'm a gentleman. did two years in the cavalry, then wandered Petersburg. married Marfa Petrovna, lived in the country. that's my story!"

"you're a gambler, right?"

"not a good one. more like a card-sharper than a gambler."

"you did that?"

"yeah, i did."

"ever get beat up?"

"it happened. why ask?"

"could've challenged them. must've been lively."

"not disagreeing. philosophy's not my thing. came here for the women."

"right after Marfa Petrovna?"
