"yep," Svidrigaïlov smiled, honest-like. "what's wrong with that? you think there's something off about how i talk about women?"

"you asking if i see a problem with vice?"

"Vice, huh? That's what you're on about? Alright, let's break it down. Women first. You know I like to chat. Why hold back? Why ditch women if I'm into 'em? It's something to do, ain't it?"

"So you're just chasing vice?"

"Sure, call it vice if you want. I like straight questions. This vice, at least, is solid, ties to nature, not just fantasy. It's in the blood, like a fire that doesn't quit, even with age. Yeah, it's a kind of job, you know?"

"That's nothing to celebrate. It's a disease and a risky one."

"Oh, that's your view, huh? I get it, it's a disease if you go overboard. And yeah, this one needs you to go overboard. But everyone's got their excesses. Sure, be moderate and wise, sounds dull, but what else? Without this, I might as well off myself. A decent guy should deal with boredom, but still..."

"And could you really do that?"

"Come on," Svidrigaïlov cut in, annoyed. "Don't bring that up," he added quickly, dropping the bravado. His face changed. "I'll admit, it's a weak spot, but I can't shake it. I fear death, hate talking about it. You know, I'm kind of a mystic?"

"Ah, Marfa Petrovna's ghosts! Do they still haunt you?"

"Oh, drop it; they've stopped in Petersburg, damn them!" he snapped, irritated. "Let's talk about... though... I don't have much time, can't stick around, shame! I'd have plenty to say."

"Got an appointment, a woman?"

"Yeah, a woman, a random thing... But that's not it."

"And all the grime and mess around you, doesn't it get to you? Lost the will to quit?"

"And you act all strong, too? Ha! You got me there, Rodion Romanovitch, though I figured you would. Preaching to me about vice and aesthetics! You—a Schiller, an idealist! That's how it should be, strange as it is... Shame I gotta go, you're a fascinating character! By the way, like Schiller? I'm a big fan."

"But you're such a braggart," Raskolnikov said, a bit disgusted.

"Honestly, I'm not," Svidrigaïlov answered, laughing. "But fine, call me a braggart, why not brag if it harms no one? Spent seven years in the country with Marfa Petrovna, so now when I meet someone smart like you—smart and interesting—I'm glad to chat, plus, I've had some champagne and it's hit me a bit. Plus, there's a thing that's really got me wound up, but I'll keep that to myself. Where you going?" he asked, alarmed.

Raskolnikov started to get up. Felt suffocated, uneasy, regretted coming here. He was sure Svidrigaïlov was the worst scoundrel alive.

“Sit down, hang out a bit,” Svidrigaïlov insisted. “Let’s get you some tea. Stay for a while, I’ll keep it sensible. Want to hear how a woman tried to 'save' me? It'll answer your first question — turns out, that woman was your sister. Can I tell you? It'll pass the time.”

“Go ahead, but I trust you...”

“Don’t worry. Even a guy like me has nothing but respect for Avdotya Romanovna.”

CHAPTER IV

you know, i did tell you before, i was stuck in the debtors’ prison here, owing a huge amount, with no real hope of paying it off. marfa petrovna bailed me out; no need to get into how. you ever seen how far a woman’s love can go? she was honest and practical, even if she wasn’t educated. believe it or not, this honest, jealous woman, after a lot of hysterics and blame, made a deal with me. she was older, always had something like a clove in her mouth. i was honest enough to tell her straight up i couldn’t be completely faithful. that drove her nuts, but she seemed to like the honesty. she thought it meant i wouldn’t deceive her. for a jealous woman, that’s key. after a lot of tears, we had an unwritten deal: i’d always stay her husband, wouldn’t leave without permission, wouldn’t set up a permanent mistress. in return, i had some freedom with the maids, but only if she knew. never fall for a woman of our class, though. if i ever did, i had to tell her. marfa was calm about that; she thought i was too much of a rogue to really love. but a sensible woman and a jealous one are worlds apart. that’s where the problems started. to see people clearly, we have to drop our biases and usual views. i trust your judgment. you might’ve heard silly things about marfa. she had her quirks, but i’m honestly sorry for the trouble i caused her. that’s enough of an obituary for a tender wife. when we fought, i stayed quiet; it calmed her down. she even got proud of me sometimes. but she couldn’t stand your sister. strange she’d bring such a beautiful person as a governess. i think marfa fell for her—literally. no surprise, look at avdotya romanovna! i saw the risk and decided not to even look at her. but avdotya made the first move. marfa was mad at first because i didn’t talk about your sister, despite marfa’s praises of her. i don’t know what marfa wanted! obviously, she told avdotya everything about me, loved to share secrets and complain. so, naturally, avdotya knew all the rumors about me. i bet you’ve heard some too, right?

“yeah, Luzhin said you caused a kid's death. True?”

“dont bring up that nonsense,” said Svidrigaïlov with disgust. “if you really want to hear, maybe someday, but not now...”

“heard about some footman you mistreated.”

“drop it,” Svidrigaïlov cut in, clearly annoyed.

“was it the footman who showed up after death to pack your pipe? you told me about that.” Raskolnikov was getting more irritated.

Svidrigaïlov looked at him, and for a moment, Raskolnikov thought he saw something nasty in his eyes. But Svidrigaïlov kept it cool and replied politely.

yeah, it was. i see you're curious too. ill fill you in when i can. honestly, some folks think im a romantic figure. Marfa Petrovna spread some tales to Avdotya Romanovna, and even if she found me gloomy, she felt sorry for me. pity's powerful, makes a girl want to 'save' you. and Avdotya, she's got that in her. she'd fit right into those old martyr stories. she’s got this craving to suffer for a cause. i heard about this Razumihin guy. seems smart. he should look after her.

i got to know her, and she’s stunning, which didn’t help. it started with a strong physical attraction. she's incredibly pure. i remember this other girl, Parasha. Avdotya told me to leave her alone. and i played along, acted embarrassed. that led to talks, pleas, even tears. flattery was my trick. it’s easy and often works, even on someone principled. Marfa Petrovna liked flattery too. i could have got her to give me everything.

i was getting the same effect on Avdotya, but i messed up. she hated the look in my eyes. we parted ways. i acted stupid, mocked her efforts, and Parasha got involved again. Avdotya’s eyes, man, they haunted me. i thought id go mad. i wanted to make up, but it was too late. in my frenzy, i even thought of offering her all my money to run away with me. i was crazy enough to do anything for her back then.

but you know how it ended. Marfa Petrovna almost set her up with that jerk attorney, Luzhin. wouldve been the same as my offer. you’re paying attention now, huh? interesting young man, indeed.

svidrigaïlov slammed the table, his face red. Raskolnikov noticed he was feeling the champagne, even if he hadn't had much. Raskolnikov thought he might be able to use that. He didn't trust svidrigaïlov at all.

"So, you came to Petersburg with plans for my sister," Raskolnikov said, trying to get under his skin.

"That's nonsense," svidrigaïlov replied, shaking himself awake. "I've told you before... and your sister doesn't even like me."

"Yeah, she doesn't, but that's not the issue."

"Sure about that?" svidrigaïlov squinted and smirked. "You're right, she doesn't love me. But between husband and wife, or lover and mistress, there's always something nobody else knows. Can you guarantee Avdotya Romanovna hates me?"

"You've slipped up. I know you're planning something bad for Dounia."

"Did I say that?" svidrigaïlov looked surprised but didn't care about the insult.

"You're saying it now. What are you scared of?"

"Me, scared? Of you? You should be scared of me, my friend. But this is silly... I've had too much to drink, I get it. Almost said too much again. Damn wine! Water!"

He tossed the champagne bottle out the window. Philip brought him water.

"That's all nonsense," svidrigaïlov said, wetting a towel for his head. "I can wipe out your suspicions in one word. I'm getting married."

"You mentioned that already."

"Did I? Forgot. But I couldn't have been sure because I hadn't met her. Now it's settled. If I didn't have business, I'd introduce you. I'd like your advice. Damn, only ten minutes left! Look at the watch. But my marriage story's interesting. Where you going?"

"I'm not leaving now."

not at all? we’ll see. i’ll take you there, show you my betrothed, but not now. you got to head right and i got to go left. you know madame reslich, where im staying? yeah, same woman whose girl drowned in winter. are you listening? she set it up for me. said i needed something to fill time. i’m a gloomy guy, you know? not light-hearted. i can sit silent in a corner for days. reslich is crafty, thinks i’ll get sick of it, leave my wife, and she can profit. says the father’s a broken-down retired guy, stuck in a chair, legs paralyzed. mom’s sensible. they got a son in the provinces, doesn’t help. a daughter, married, doesn’t visit. two nephews to care for, and pulled their youngest from school. she’s almost sixteen, ready to marry. she’s for me. we went there. it was funny. i come in—a landowner, widower, well-known, with money. i’m fifty, she’s not sixteen. who cares? fascinating, right? you should’ve seen me talk to the parents. she comes in, curtseys, in a short frock—an unopened bud, blushing. sixteen, shy eyes, better than beauty. perfect little picture. fair hair, little rosy lips, tiny feet. we made friends. i said i was in a hurry, next day we’re betrothed. now, she’s on my knee, blushing, i kiss her every minute. mom tells her i’m the husband, that’s how it is. delicious! betrothed state’s maybe better than marriage. talked twice, she’s no fool. sometimes her look scorches me. face like raphael’s madonna, something fantastic. bought her presents—diamonds, pearls, big silver dressing-case. madonna’s face glowed. sat her on my knee, maybe too rough—she flushed, cried, but hid it. when alone, she hugged me, kissed me, promised to be good, make me happy, sacrifice everything, just wants respect, no presents. hearing that from an angel of sixteen, in a muslin frock, with curls, shy blush, and tears—fascinating, right? worth it? listen, we’ll visit her, just not now.

"You're getting all worked up about the age gap and all that. You really gonna go through with it?"

"Sure thing. Everyone's looking out for themselves. The happiest ones are the best at pretending. Ha-ha! But what's got you so fired up about virtue? Give me a break, pal. I'm no saint. Ha-ha-ha!"

"But you did take care of Katerina Ivanovna's kids. Even if you had your reasons... I get it now."

"I've always liked kids, really liked them," Svidrigaïlov chuckled. "Let me tell you something funny. First day here, I checked out all the old spots, seven years away and I just had to dive in. You see I’m not rushing to catch up with old pals. I’ll manage without 'em for as long as I can. When I was out in the country with Marfa Petrovna, I kept thinking about these places. Man, anyone who knows their way around here can find a lot. Seriously! Peasants have their vodka, young educated types are wasting away in big dreams, folks are tangled in theories; Jews popping up, making money, and others just losing themselves in debauchery. The town hit me with its old smells right away. Ended up in a rough joint—love my places gritty—it was a dance, they called it. Never seen a cancan like that in my day. That’s progress for you. Suddenly, I spot this kid, thirteen, dressed nice, dancing with some pro, another across from them. Her mom's watching from a chair. You wouldn't believe that cancan! The kid got embarrassed, blushed, then felt insulted, started crying. Her partner grabbed her, spinning her around, showing off; crowd laughed—gotta love your crowd—even the cancan crowd—they laughed and yelled, ‘Serves her right—shouldn’t bring kids!’ Not my place to say if that thought was logical. I made my move, sat by the mom, said I was a stranger too, complained about the rude folks, hinted I had cash, offered them a lift home. Took them home, got acquainted. They were staying in a dump, just arrived from the country. She said knowing me was an honor. Learned they had nothing, came to town on some legal stuff. Offered help and cash. They hit that dance spot by mistake, thought it was a real class. Offered to help the girl with French and dance lessons. They jumped at it, honored—and we’re still friends... We can visit if you want, just not now."

"Stop! Enough with your filthy stories, you depraved man!"

"Schiller, you're a real Schiller! _O la vertu va-t-elle se nicher?_ But you know I’ll keep telling you these for the fun of hearing you yell!"

"Yeah, I get it. I look stupid too," Raskolnikov muttered, mad.

Svidrigaïlov laughed hard; called Philip, paid up, started getting up.

"Man, I'm drunk, _assez causé_," he said. "It's been fun."

"I bet it is!" Raskolnikov shot back, getting up. "A washed-up scoundrel reliving tales with a nasty plan brewing—especially with me... it's thrilling!"

"sure, you're a cynic," Svidrigaïlov said, eyeing Raskolnikov. "you get it, you can do a lot too. but alright, i'm sorry we didn't talk more. i won't lose track of you... just wait."

Svidrigaïlov left the place, Raskolnikov on his heels. Svidrigaïlov wasn't that drunk, the wine hit him for a bit but wore off. he was focused, looking worried like something big was coming. he'd turned ruder to Raskolnikov, more sneering. Raskolnikov picked up on it, got suspicious, decided to tail him.

they hit the street.

"you go right, i'll go left, or flip it. goodbye, maybe we'll cross paths again."

he headed right to the Hay Market.

Raskolnikov followed.

"what's this?" Svidrigaïlov snapped around, "i thought i said..."

"means i'm not letting you out of sight."

"what?"

they squared off, sizing each other up.

"from your half-drunk tales," Raskolnikov said sharply, "i know you're still after my sister, pushing harder now. she got a letter today. you've been restless. maybe you found a wife, but that’s nothing. i need to see for myself."

Raskolnikov wasn’t sure what he wanted, but he had to be sure.

"oh really! i'll call the cops!"

"go ahead!"

they stood, staring. then Svidrigaïlov's face shifted. he saw Raskolnikov wasn't scared, so he went friendly.

"what a guy! i held back on your business, though i'm burning with curiosity. it's wild. i put it off, but you stir things up... alright, let's go. but i'm just grabbing cash at home, then locking up, taking a cab to the Islands. you following?"

"i'm heading to your place, not for you, but to see Sofya Semyonovna, missed the funeral."

"your call, but she's not home. she's with the kids at some high-rank lady's place, patroness type. i knew her back when, won her over with money for the kids and a donation. told her Sofya's story, laid it all out. she was blown away. Sofya's meeting her today at the X. Hotel."

"doesn't matter, i'm still coming."

"up to you, no concern of mine, but i'm not coming with you; we're home. by the way, you suspect me 'cause i showed restraint, didn't pry... you get it? it seemed weird to you; i'd bet on it. teaches you about showing restraint!"

"and listening at doors!"

"ah, that’s it, huh?" laughed Svidrigaïlov. "figured you wouldn’t let it slide with all that’s gone down. haha! i kinda knew the trouble you’ve been stirring, telling Sofya Semyonovna about it, but what’s that all about? maybe im out of the loop. explain it to me, will ya? spill the latest theories."

"you couldn’t have heard anything. you’re just making it all up!"

"but im not talking about that (though i did hear a bit). no, its about how youre sighing and groaning now. your inner Schiller is kicking around, and now you tell me not to eavesdrop. if that’s your stance, go tell the cops you messed up: made a little error in your theory. but if you think it’s fine to eavesdrop, but not kill old women, head to America now. hurry up, young man! might still be time. im serious. no cash? i’ll cover the fare."

"im not thinking of that at all," Raskolnikov cut in, disgusted.

"i get it (but don’t stress, don’t discuss if you don’t want). i see the questions bugging you—moral ones, right? duties as a citizen or man? forget them. they don’t matter now, haha! you’ll say you’re still a man and a citizen. then you shouldn’t have gotten into this mess. don’t take on a job you can’t handle. well, shoot yourself, or don’t you want to?"

"you’re trying to provoke me, make me leave."

"what a strange guy! but here we are. welcome to the staircase. see, that’s Sofya Semyonovna’s way. look, no one’s home. don’t believe me? ask Kapernaumov. here’s Madame de Kapernaumov. hey, what? she’s kinda deaf. she gone? where to? did you hear? she’s out and won’t be back till late. well, come to my room; you wanna see me, right? here we go. Madame Resslich isn’t home. she’s a busy woman, a great lady, really... she’d be useful to you if you were more sensible. now, watch! i pull this five-percent bond out—see how many i got? this one’s gettin’ cashed today. no more time to waste. bureau’s locked, flat’s locked, and here we are on the stairs. shall we grab a cab? im heading to the Islands. need a lift? i’ll take this carriage. ah, you say no? you’re tired! come for a drive! i bet it’ll rain. no worries, we’ll put the hood down..."

Svidrigaïlov hopped in the carriage. Raskolnikov thought his doubts were, at least for now, wrong. Without saying a word, he turned back to the Hay Market. If he’d looked back, he’d have seen Svidrigaïlov hop out a hundred paces away, ditch the cab, and walk on. But he’d turned the corner and saw nothing. intense disgust pulled him away from Svidrigaïlov.

"to think i’d look for help from that brute, that depraved scoundrel!" he cried.

Raskolnikov judged too quick: Svidrigaïlov had something original, even mysterious. About his sister, Raskolnikov knew Svidrigaïlov wouldn’t leave her alone. But going on thinking about it was too painful and unbearable.

not twenty steps alone and he's already deep in thought. he's leaning on the bridge railing, staring at the water. his sister's there too.

she caught up to him at the bridge entrance but he didn't notice her. first time she's seen him like this outside. she's worried. she's frozen, unsure if she should call out. then she spots Svidrigaïlov heading over from the Hay Market.

he's sneaky, hanging back on the pavement, avoiding Raskolnikov's eyes. he's been watching Dounia, signaling her. she thinks he's telling her not to speak to her brother, but to come to him.

that's what she does. she slips past her brother and heads to Svidrigaïlov.

“let's move quickly,” he whispers, “i don't want Rodion to know we met. i've been with him at the restaurant nearby. he found me and it was tough shaking him off. he knows about my letter to you. wasn't you who told him, right? then who?”

“we're out of sight now,” Dounia cuts in, “not going further. talk here. say it all in the street.”

“can't talk here; you need to hear from Sofya too. got papers to show you. if you don't come, i won't explain and will leave. but remember, i hold a secret about your brother.”

Dounia hesitates, eyes searching Svidrigaïlov's face.

“what's the fear?” he asks quietly. “this isn't the countryside. even there, you did me more harm.”

“is Sofya ready?”

“no, haven't told her. not even sure she's home; she buried her stepmother today. can't talk to anyone yet. regret telling you. any slip-up is like betrayal here. i live in that house there. see, that's the porter. he knows me; he's bowing. if you're wary, now you know. sorry for being blunt. i don't have a flat alone; Sofya's in the next room. whole floor's rented out. why so scared? am i that bad?”

his lips twist in a mock smile, but he's not amused. heart's pounding, can't breathe right. he's loud to mask his nerves. Dounia's too annoyed by his words to notice his tension.

“i know you're not honorable, but i'm not scared. lead on,” she says, trying to stay calm, but her face is pale.

Svidrigaïlov stops at Sonia's room.

"is she home? nope, she's out. rough time, but she'll be back soon. probably seeing a lady about the orphans. their mom passed... ive been trying to sort things for them. if Sofya Semyonovna doesn't show up in ten, i'll send her your way today if you want. this is my place. two rooms here. Madame Resslich, my landlady, she's next door. look here. this door in my bedroom? leads into two empty rooms, up for rent. take a good look."

Svidrigaïlov's got these big rooms. Dounia's checking them out, skeptical, but nothing stands out with the furniture or layout. something though — his place is smack between almost empty apartments. you get in through the landlady's rooms, not directly from the hall. he unlocks a door from his bedroom, shows Dounia the empty rooms. she's in the doorway, confused about what she's supposed to see, but he explains fast.

"check this second big room. see that door? it's locked. there's a chair by it, only one here. i brought it from my room to listen better. Sofya Semyonovna's table is just on the other side; she was talking to Rodion Romanovitch. i sat listening two nights, two hours each. caught quite a bit, wouldnt you say?"

"you listened?"

"yep. now, let's head back to my room; we cant sit here."

he leads Avdotya Romanovna back, offers her a chair. he sits across the table, maybe seven feet away, but his eyes probably still have that look that once scared Dounia. she shudders, looks around again, trying not to show she's uneasy. the remote spot of his place just hit her. she wants to ask if his landlady's home, pride stops her. she's got bigger worries than her own fears. she's distressed.

"here's your letter," she says, putting it on the table. "can what you wrote be true? you hint at a crime by my brother, too clearly to deny now. i heard this nonsense before you wrote and don't buy a word. it's a gross, silly suspicion. i know why and how it started. you got no proof. you promised proof. speak! but i warn you, i don't believe you! i don't believe you!"

Dounia says this, quick, face flushed for a moment.

"if you didn't believe it, why risk coming here alone? just curious?"

"don't torture me. speak, speak!"

"you're brave, i'll give you that. i thought you'd have Mr. Razumihin with you. but he's not here. i watched. it's bold, shows you wanted to protect Rodion Romanovitch. everything about you is impressive... your brother, what should i say? you just saw him. what do you think?"

"that's not all you're relying on, is it?"

no, not just on that, but on his words. he came here two evenings in a row to see Sofya Semyonovna. i showed you where they sat. he confessed to her. he's a murderer. killed an old pawnbroker woman, and her sister, Lizaveta, who walked in. used an axe. took some money and stuff. told all this to Sofya Semyonovna, she's the only one who knows. she's not involved, horrified by it. she won't betray him.

"can't be," Dounia muttered, lips white. gasping. "no cause, no ground... it's a lie!"

"he robbed her, that's the cause. took money and things. admitted he hid them under a stone, unused. he didn't dare use them."

"but how could he steal? dream of it?" Dounia cried, jumping up. "you know him, seen him, can he be a thief?"

"thousands of possibilities, Avdotya Romanovna. a thief knows he's a scoundrel, but there's stories, gentlemen breaking the law thinking they're right. i wouldn't have believed it if told, but i heard it. he explained to Sofya Semyonovna, and she didn't believe at first, but her eyes convinced her."

"what were the causes?"

"long story, Avdotya Romanovna. a theory, like one where a single wrong is okay if the main goal is right. a young man with pride might think, if he had three thousand, his future would change. hunger, bad lodging, ragged clothes, pride, vanity, these hit hard. a theory dividing people into regular and superior, where laws don't apply to the superior. Napoleon was a big influence, thinking geniuses don't hesitate at wrongs. he thought himself a genius, but couldn't break the law boldly. humiliating for a proud young man today."

"but remorse? no moral feeling? is he like that?"

everything's tangled up now, Avdotya Romanovna. not that it was ever neat. russians, broad in ideas, broad like the land. love the chaos. but broad without genius is a mess. remember our talks on the terrace after supper? you said i was too broad. who knows, maybe while we chatted, he was here plotting. no sacred traditions here, especially among the educated. they make them up from books or old stories. mostly the scholars and the old-timers. society frowns on that. you know my stance. i blame no one. i just sit tight. we've been over this. i was glad you cared about my views... you're looking pale, Avdotya Romanovna.

i know his theory. i read his article on men who think they’re above all. Razumihin brought it.

Razumihin? your brother's article in a mag? didn’t know. sounds interesting. where you headed, Avdotya Romanovna?

i want to see Sofya Semyonovna. how do i get to her? she might be here. must see her now. maybe she...

she couldn't finish. breath just left her.

Sofya Semyonovna won’t be back till night, i think. she should’ve been back, but if not, it’ll be late.

then you're lying! i see... lying... lying all along... i don’t believe you! i don’t believe you! Dounia shouted, losing it.

nearly fainting, she dropped into a chair Svidrigaïlov hurried to offer.

Avdotya Romanovna, what's wrong? get hold of yourself! here’s water. drink...

he sprinkled water over her. Dounia shuddered, then came around.

“acted violently,” Svidrigaïlov muttered, frowning. Avdotya Romanovna, calm down! he's got friends. we’ll save him. want me to take him abroad? i’ve got money, can get a ticket in three days. as for the murder, he’ll make up for it with good deeds. calm down. he might still do great things. how are you feeling?

cruel man! jeer like that! let me go...

where you going?

to him. where is he? do you know? why's the door locked? we came in that way, now it's locked. when did you lock it?

couldn’t shout about this all over. not jeering; just sick of this talk. can’t go like this. want to betray him? you'll drive him mad, and he’ll give up. they’re watching him. you’ll just hand him over. wait a bit: i just talked to him. he can still be saved. sit down; let’s think it through. asked you here for this. to plan it out. sit down!

how can you save him? can he really be saved?

Dounia sat. Svidrigaïlov joined her.

it’s all on you, you alone, he started, eyes bright, almost whispering, words choked with emotion.

Dounia pulled away, eyes wide. He was shaking too.

"Just say the word, and he's safe. I'll save him. Got money, got friends. I'll get him out, passport ready, two passports. One for him, one for me. Friends with pull... serious people. Want one for you? For your mother? Why Razumihin? I love you... more than anything. Let me kiss your dress, just let me. Just the sound of it... it's too much. Tell me what to do, I'll do it. Anything. Anything! Don’t... don’t look at me like that. You’re killing me..."

He was losing it... something snapped. Dounia jumped, ran for the door.

"Open it! Open it!" She rattled the door. "Is anyone there?"

Svidrigaïlov snapped out of it, lips curling into a mocking smile.

"Nobody home," he said, calm and clear. "Landlady's out. No point yelling. You're just getting worked up."

"Where's the key? Open the door, right now!"

"Lost it. Can't find it."

"This is an outrage," Dounia was pale as a ghost. She backed into a corner, pulled a table in front of her.

She didn't scream, just stared him down, watching every move.

Svidrigaïlov stood there, calm but pale. That mocking smile stayed put.

"You mentioned outrage, Avdotya Romanovna. I've planned for this. Sofya's not here. Kapernaumovs are far—five rooms in between. I'm way stronger. Nothing to fear. You wouldn’t betray your brother, right? No one would believe you. How does a girl end up alone in a man's room? Even if you sacrifice your brother, can't prove anything. Hard to prove an assault, Avdotya."

"Scoundrel!" Dounia whispered, her voice full of anger.

"Call it what you want, but just a general point. You're right—violence is awful. Just saying you wouldn’t feel guilty if... you saved your brother like I'm suggesting. You'd just be a victim of circumstance. Think about it. Your brother's and mother's fate are in your hands. I'll be your slave... forever... I'll wait."

Svidrigaïlov sat, eight steps from her. She knew his resolve. Suddenly, she pulled a revolver from her pocket, cocked it, laid it on the table. He jumped.

"Aha! So that's how it is?" he said, surprised but still smiling. "Changes everything, Avdotya Romanovna. Makes it easier for me. But where'd you get the revolver? Razumihin? It's mine! An old friend! I taught you to shoot with this."

that's not yours, it's Marfa Petrovna's. you killed her, didn't you? nothing of yours was in her house. i took it when i started to see what you might do. one step closer, and i swear, i'll kill you.

"but your brother? just curious," said Svidrigaïlov, standing still.

"go ahead, tell him! don't move! i'll shoot! you poisoned your wife, i know it; you're a murderer!" she was ready with the revolver.

"are you sure i poisoned Marfa Petrovna?"

"you did! you hinted at it; you talked about poison... i know you got it ready. it was you... had to be you... scoundrel!"

"even if true, it’d have been for you... cause of it."

"you're lying! i hated you, always."

"oh, Avdotya Romanovna! you forgot how you softened to me in the propaganda heat. i saw it. remember that moonlit night, with the nightingale?"

"lie!" Dounia's eyes flashed, "that's a lie and a libel!"

"a lie? if you say so. i made it up. women shouldn't be reminded of such things," he smiled. "i know you'll shoot, you wild thing. go on!"

Dounia raised the revolver, pale, watching him, waiting for him to move. her lip quivered, eyes like fire. he'd never seen her look so fierce. that fire seemed to hit him hard. he stepped forward, a shot rang out. bullet grazed his hair, hit the wall. he stood and laughed softly.

"the wasp stung me. aimed for my head. what's this? blood?" he pulled a handkerchief, wiped the thin stream from his temple. just grazed him.

Dounia lowered the revolver, more amazed than terrified. seemed like she didn't understand what she'd done.

"missed! shoot again, i'll wait," Svidrigaïlov said softly, still smiling but darkly. "if you keep this up, i'll grab you before you cock it again."

Dounia started, quickly cocked the pistol, raised it again.

"let me go," she cried, desperate. "i'll shoot again. i'll kill you."

"at three paces, you can't miss. but if you don't..." his eyes lit up, took two steps forward. Dounia shot again: misfire.

"didn't load it right. never mind, got another shot. get it ready, i'll wait."

he faced her, two paces away, eyes locked, determined. she saw he wouldn't back down. she'd kill him at this range! suddenly, she flung the revolver away.

"she dropped it!" Svidrigaïlov said, surprised, breathing deep. weight lifted—maybe not just fear of death. something darker, bitterer, gone.

he went to Dounia, gently put an arm around her waist. she didn't resist, just trembled, looking at him with pleading eyes. he tried to speak, but no sound came.

"let me go," Dounia begged. Svidrigaïlov shuddered. her voice was different now.

"so you don't love me?" he asked softly. Dounia shook her head.

"and... and you wont? never?" he whispered, all despair.

"never!"

svidrigaïlov just stood there, caught in some silent struggle. he stared at her, eyes saying what words couldn't. then he pulled back, turned to the window, just stood. 

"here's the key."

he dug it out of his coat pocket and set it on the table behind him, not even glancing back.

"take it! hurry up!"

kept staring out the window. dounia moved to grab the key.

"make haste! make haste!" he said again, not budging. but man, that "make haste" carried a weight.

dounia got it, grabbed the key, flew to the door, unlocked it, and bolted out. soon she was out by the canal, heading for X. bridge.

three minutes at the window, then svidrigaïlov turned around, rubbed his forehead. a twisted smile, sad and weak, crossed his face. blood on his hand, drying up. he scowled at it, wet a towel, and cleaned his temple. saw the revolver dounia tossed, near the door. picked it up, checked it. an old-school three-barrel revolver. two shots left. thought for a sec, pocketed it, grabbed his hat, and left.

CHAPTER VI

spent that night till ten, bouncing between dives. katia showed up, sang about some "villain and tyrant" kissing her.

svidrigaïlov bought drinks for katia, the organ-grinder, some singers, waiters, and two clerks. drawn in by their crooked noses, one left, one right. they ended up at a pleasure garden, and he paid their way. the place had a scraggly pine, three bushes, a "Vauxhall" bar. few tables, chairs. sad singers, a drunk german clown with a red nose put on the show. clerks got into it with another group, almost brawled. svidrigaïlov got dragged in to settle it. listened for fifteen minutes, but it was all noise. one clerk had swiped something and sold it to a jew, but didn't split the take. turns out it was a teaspoon from the Vauxhall. things got sticky. svidrigaïlov paid for the spoon, stood, walked out. about six o'clock. hadn't touched a drop of wine, ordered tea to keep up appearances.

dark, heavy air. storm clouds rolled in by ten. thunder cracked, rain poured like a river. not drops, but streams pounding the ground. lightning flashed every minute, lasting as long as you could count to five.

he's soaked, heads home, locks himself in. opens the bureau, grabs all his cash, rips up some papers. pockets the money, thought about changing clothes, but nah. looks out the window, hears the storm, grabs his hat, leaves without locking up. straight to Sonia's place. she's home.

she's got the Kapernaumov kids with her, having tea. Sonia's quiet when he shows up, curious about his drenched clothes. the kids? they bolt in fear.

he sits at the table, asks Sonia to join. she's cautious, ready to hear him out.

"might head to America, Sofya Semyonovna," he says. "probably last time i see you, need to sort some things. did you meet the lady today? i know what she said, no need to tell me." (Sonia shifts, blushes.) "they've got their ways. your siblings, they're taken care of. money's in safe hands, got the receipts. take them, just in case. here, take 'em! that's done. here, three 5-per-cent bonds, three thousand roubles. all yours, just for you, keep it quiet, no matter what. you'll need it, living like before's no good now."

“im so grateful, and so are the kids and my stepmom,” Sonia says quickly. “if i haven’t said enough... don’t think...”

“that’s enough! enough!”

“but the money, Arkady Ivanovitch, grateful, but i don’t need it. i can earn my living. don’t think im ungrateful. if you’re so kind, that money...”

“it’s for you, Sofya Semyonovna, don’t waste words. no time. you’ll need it. Rodion Romanovitch has two choices: bullet or Siberia.” (Sonia looks shocked.) “don’t worry, i know from him, not gossiping; wont tell. you gave good advice to confess. better for him. if it’s Siberia, you’ll go too. right? you’ll need money. for him. giving you is like giving him. and you promised Amalia Ivanovna to pay her. i heard. how can you take on those debts so carelessly? was Katerina Ivanovna’s debt, not yours. should’ve ignored the German. can’t go through life like that. if asked about me—tomorrow, day after—don’t mention i came or show the money. goodbye.” (he stands.) “say hi to Rodion Romanovitch. better put the money with Mr. Razumihin. know him? not bad. take it to him when needed. hide it till then.”

Sonia jumps up, looks at him in panic. wants to speak, ask, but unsure how to start.

“how can you... leave now, in this rain?”

why start for america and stop ‘cause of rain, huh? goodbye, sofya semyonovna. live long and be useful. oh, and tell mr. razumihin i said hi. arkady ivanovitch svidrigaïlov sends his greetings. don’t forget.

he left, and sonia was left wondering and a bit anxious.

later that night, around 11:20, he made another odd visit. still raining. soaked, he walked into his fiancée's parents’ place on third street, vassilyevsky island. knocked for a bit before they let him in. they were a bit startled, but svidrigaïlov could charm when he wanted. their first thought that he was drunk vanished quick. the frail father was brought in by the caring mother, who started with unrelated questions. she never asked straight but danced around topics. wanted to know about paris and court life before circling back to third street. normally impressive, but arkady was impatient this time. insisted on seeing his fiancée, even though she was already in bed. she showed up, of course.

svidrigaïlov showed up late with 15,000 roubles, saying he had to leave petersburg for a bit. gave it as a gift before their wedding. odd timing, pouring rain, middle of the night, but no one asked too much. everyone was surprised but grateful, especially the mother, who cried. svidrigaïlov laughed it off, kissed his fiancée, promised to return soon. noticed her curiosity, kissed her again, but was annoyed knowing mom would stash the cash. left them buzzing with excitement. mom whispered he's a big deal, unpredictable, like an englishman, and advised to keep quiet about it. they whispered till late, but the girl went to bed confused.

svidrigaïlov left at midnight, crossed the bridge. rain stopped, wind picked up, he shivered. looked at the river for a moment, then moved on. wandered down the long street, stumbling on the wooden pavement, looking for a hotel he'd seen before. finally found the adrianople, big wooden building. went inside, asked a scruffy guy for a room. got a small one under the stairs, last one available. ordered tea and veal, ignoring the guy's surprise. assistant left, unimpressed.

"Must be a decent spot," Svidrigaïlov thought. "Why didn't I know about it? Probably look like I wandered out of a café after some adventure. Wonder who else has holed up here?"

He lit the candle and gave the room a better look. Ceiling so low he could just stand. One grimy window. Bed's filthy. Chair and table, nothing fancy, packed the place tight. Walls seemed like plank under ragged, dusty paper. Faded yellow, if you squint. Ceiling's a slope, room's not an attic, just tucked under the stairs.

He set the candle down, sat on the bed, got lost in thought. But some weird murmur next door wouldn't quit, sometimes a shout. Caught his ear. Listened: someone's ranting, almost crying, just one voice.

He got up, hand over the light, noticed a crack in the wall. Peeked through. Room next door's bigger. Two guys. One's got wild hair, face all red, playing the orator. No coat, legs spread, pounding his chest. Yelling about the other's a nobody, pulled him from the gutter. Says he could toss him back, only Providence knows. Other guy's slumped in a chair, looks like he needs a sneeze that won't come. Eyes glazed, like he barely hears. Table's got a dying candle, wine glasses, almost empty vodka, bread, cucumber, stale tea dregs. Svidrigaïlov watched a bit, then turned away, sat back on the bed.

the attendant came back with the tea, couldn't help but ask again if he wanted anything else. svidrigaïlov said no, again, and the guy finally left. he drank a glass of tea to warm up but couldn't eat. started feeling feverish. took off his coat, wrapped in a blanket, and lay on the bed. annoyed. "should've been well for this," he thought, smiling. room was stuffy, candle dim, wind howling outside. heard a mouse scratching and the place smelled like mice and leather. his mind wandered. "must be a garden under the window," he thought. "sound of trees. hate the sound of trees on a stormy night, in the dark. gives you that creepy feeling." remembered disliking it near petrovsky park. thought of the bridge over the little neva, felt cold again. "never liked water," he thought, "even in a landscape," smiled at the idea: "taste and comfort shouldn't matter now, but i'm getting picky, like an animal choosing a spot... for this. should've gone to petrovsky park! seemed dark, cold, haha! as if i were looking for comfort! why haven't i put out the candle?" he blew it out. "they're asleep next door," he thought, not seeing light at the crack. "well, marfa petrovna, now's your time; it's dark, and the place is right for you. but you won't show."

thought about how, an hour before his plan with dounia, he told raskolnikov to trust her with razumihin. "guess i did it to mess with myself, like raskolnikov thought. but that raskolnikov's a rogue! been through a lot. could be a successful rogue once he's over his nonsense. but he's too eager for life now. young guys are pathetic that way. but, whatever! let him do what he wants, it's not my problem."

couldn't sleep. dounia's image came to mind, and he shuddered. "no, gotta give it up," he thought, shaking it off. "weird and funny. never hated anyone much, never wanted revenge, and that's not good. never liked fighting or losing my temper—that's not good either. and those promises i made her—damn! but—who knows?—maybe she'd have changed me somehow…"

he clenched his teeth, went silent again. dounia's image came back, like when she lowered the revolver in fear after the first shot, looking at him blankly, like she wouldn't defend herself unless he reminded her. remembered feeling almost sorry for her, a pang in his heart…

"ugh! damn, these thoughts again! gotta push them away!"

he was slipping into sleep, fever chills gone, when he felt something skitter across his arm and leg. jerked awake. "ugh, damn, probably a mouse," he thought, "left that veal out." didn't want to get up, cold outside the blanket, but then it ran over his leg again. threw the blanket off, lit a candle. still shaking from fever, checked the bed. nothing. shook out the blanket, and there it was, mouse on the sheet. tried to catch it, but it zigzagged, slipped right through his fingers, across his hand, then under the pillow. tossed the pillow but felt it leap on his chest, skitter down his back under his shirt. shivered, woke up.

room dark. back under the blanket. wind howling. "gross," he thought, annoyed.

sat on the bed, back to the window. "better not sleep," he decided. cold draft from the window, wrapped the blanket tighter. didn't want to think, but images floated up. random scraps, no start or end. slipped into drowsiness. maybe it was the cold, or the damp, or the wind. mind drifted to flowers, imagined a garden, trinity day, warm and bright. country cottage, english style, covered in flowers. roses around the porch. stairs with plants, noticed white narcissus in the windows. reluctant to leave them, but went up to a drawing-room, flowers everywhere, open windows, fresh air. birds chirping. in the middle, a table with a coffin, satin cover, flowers all around. a girl in a white dress inside, arms crossed, hair wet, rose wreath. face like marble, sad smile. he knew her. no candles or prayers. she drowned herself, just fourteen. insult crushed her, unheeded scream in the night, cold and wet, wind howling.

svidrigaïlov woke up, got off the bed, and headed to the window. felt around, opened it up, and the wind hit him hard, like frost on his face and chest. outside, seemed like there was a garden, maybe a pleasure garden. probably had tables and singing during the day. now, rain drops came flying in from the trees; dark as a cellar — just vague shapes out there. he leaned on the sill, staring into the night for a bit. heard a cannon, then another. “ah, that's the signal! river's overflowing,” he thought. “by morning, it'll flood the lower streets, cellars swamped. rats swimming out, folks cussing, hauling their junk upstairs. what time is it?” just then, a clock ticked fast, struck three.

“aha! light in an hour. why wait? heading to the park now. find a big, rain-soaked bush, get drenched.” moved from the window, shut it, lit a candle, got dressed, and went out, candle in hand, down the passage to find the sleepy attendant amid candle-ends and trash. pay for the room, leave the hotel. “this is the moment; couldn't pick better.”

walked a while down a narrow corridor, no one around. just about to shout when he spotted something alive in a dark corner. bent down with the candle — found a little girl, maybe five, shivering and crying, clothes soaked like a wet rag. she wasn't scared, just stared at him, big black eyes. sobbed like kids do when they're calming down. pale face, tired, cold. “how'd she get here? hid here all night.” started asking her. suddenly, she perked up, babbling in baby talk about “mammy,” getting beaten, and a cup she “bwoke.” kept on, seemed like she was a neglected kid, mom probably a drunk cook at the hotel, who whipped her. girl broke a cup, got scared, ran away, hid outside in the rain, then snuck in here, spent the night behind the cupboard, crying, shaking from the cold, dark, and fear of a beating. he picked her up, took her to his room, sat her on the bed, started undressing her. shoes soaked through, like they'd been in a puddle all night. once undressed, he tucked her in, wrapped her in the blanket. she fell asleep right away. then he drifted back into his somber thoughts.

"what a waste of time," he thought, pissed off. "idiot!" grabbed the candle to find that ragged attendant and get out. "damn kid," he muttered, but turned back to check if she was sleeping. lifted the blanket gently. she was asleep, warm now, cheeks flushed. but something was off. the flush was too bright, not the usual rosy cheeks of a kid. "fever," he figured. looked like she'd had a drink—lips red and hot. then, weirdly, her eyelashes started twitching like she might be awake, sneaky, pretending. yep, there it was, a grin creeping on her face, not a kid's grin. provocative, like she'd seen more than she should. her eyes flicked open, too knowing for a child, almost inviting. it was sickening, that look on a child. "what, at five?" he muttered, genuinely horrified. she reached out, face glowing. "damn kid!" he almost hit her, but then woke up.

still in bed, wrapped in the blanket. hadn't lit the candle, daylight flooding in. "nightmares all night." got up, sore all over. mist outside, couldn't see a thing. nearly five. overslept. put on his damp jacket and coat. felt the revolver, pulled it out. sat down, grabbed a notebook, and scrawled some big letters on the first page. read it, thinking hard, elbows on the table. revolver and notebook beside him. flies buzzing, landing on the untouched veal. tried to catch one, got nowhere, just tired himself out. realized what he was doing, snapped out of it, got up, and walked out. hit the street a minute later.

mist was thick, like milk, hanging over town. Svidrigaïlov walked on the slippery, dirty wooden pavement towards the Little Neva. Thoughts of swollen waters, Petrovsky Island, wet paths, grass, trees, bushes filled his mind. Tried to shake it off, stared at the houses instead. Streets empty. Yellow wooden houses looked dirty, sad, shutters closed tight. Cold seeped through him, made him shiver. Read shop signs as he passed. At the end of the wooden pavement, a big stone house. A dirty dog, tail down, crossed his path. Drunk man lay face down on the pavement. He looked, moved on. High tower to the left. “Bah!” he said, “this will do. Don’t need Petrovsky. Official witness will see it anyway....”

Almost smiled, turned to the street with the big house and tower. At the gates, a little man leaned, wrapped in a grey coat, copper helmet on. Gave Svidrigaïlov a sleepy, indifferent look. Face had that sour dejection, like all Jewish faces he thought. They stared at each other a bit.

“What do you want here?” said the man, not moving.

“Nothing, brother, good morning,” Svidrigaïlov replied.

“Not the place.”

“Going to foreign parts, brother.”

“To foreign parts?”

“To America.”

“America.”

Svidrigaïlov pulled out a revolver, cocked it. Man raised his eyebrows.

“This isn’t the place for jokes!” 

“Why not?”

“Because it isn’t.”

“Well, brother, it’s a good place. When asked, say he was going to America.”

Revolver to the temple.

“You can’t do it here,” shouted the man, eyes wide.

Svidrigaïlov pulled the trigger.

---

Same day, seven in the evening, Raskolnikov headed to his mother's and sister's place. Bakaleyev’s house, Razumihin found it for them. Up the stairs from the street, walked slow, hesitating. But he wouldn't turn back. Decision made.

“Doesn't matter, they don't know yet,” he thought, “they think I'm just eccentric.”

Clothes a mess: torn, dirty, soaked from last night's rain. Face looked worn, from fatigue and the fight inside him. Spent last night alone, no clue where. But he'd made up his mind.

Knocked on the door, his mom opened. Dounia wasn't home. Even the servant was out. Pulcheria Alexandrovna, speechless with joy at first, then pulled him in.

“Here you are!” she started, joy and tears mixed. “Don’t be mad, Rodya, for my silly tears: I’m laughing, not crying. Did it seem like crying? No, I’m happy, just stuck with this crying habit since your dad passed. Cry over anything. Sit down, boy, you look tired; I see it. Ah, how muddy you are.”

"was out in the rain yesterday..." raskolnikov starts.

“no, no,” pulcheria jumps in, “you thought i’d start questioning you like before; don’t worry, i get it now. i’ve seen how things work here and honestly, they’re better. i’ve decided: how can i expect to understand your plans or ask you to explain them? who knows what you’re up to or what ideas you’ve got; it’s not my place to keep bugging you. but why am i all over the place like i’m losing it...? i’m reading your article again, rodya. dmitri prokofitch brought it. as soon as i saw it, i thought: ‘there, that’s what he’s been working on; that’s the answer!’ smart folks are like that. maybe you’ve got some new ideas brewing and i’m just in the way.’ i read it, dear, and sure, there’s a lot i didn’t get; but that’s normal—how would i?”

“let me see, mother.”

raskolnikov grabs the magazine and skims his article. doesn’t fit his mood or his situation, but he gets that weird mix of pride and bitterness seeing his name in print for the first time; he’s only twenty-three after all. lasts a second. reads a bit, frowns, heart aches. remembers the struggle of the past months. throws the article on the table, disgusted and angry.

“but, no matter how clueless i am, rodya, i can tell you’ll soon be one of the top—if not the top—in russian thought. and they dared call you mad! they really thought so. ah, idiots, they can’t grasp genius! and dounia almost believed it—what do you say to that? your father sent stuff to magazines twice—first poems (i’ve got the manuscript to show you) then a whole novel (i begged him to let me copy it) and we prayed they’d be accepted—they weren’t! i was worried sick over your food and clothes just a week ago. now i see how silly that was, you’ve got the talent to go wherever you want. though, i bet you’re on to bigger things right now....”

“dounia’s not here, right, mother?”

no, Rodya. i don't see her much; she's off doing her own thing. Dmitri Prokofitch drops by, talks about you a lot. he respects you, you know. Dounia's got her own ways, and i've got mine. she's keeping some secrets now, while i've never had any from you two. she's smart, and she loves us, but who knows where all this is heading. it made me happy you came by, but she missed you. when she gets back, i'll let her know you stopped in. don't worry about spoiling me, Rodya; come when you can, and when you can't, that's okay too. knowing you care is enough. i'll read your letters, hear things about you, and sometimes you'll visit. that's good enough. you've come to comfort your mother, and i see that.

Pulcheria Alexandrovna started crying.

“oh, look at me! don’t mind this. why am i just sitting here?” she said, jumping up. “there's coffee, and i didn’t offer you any. how selfish of me. let me get it!”

“mother, don’t bother, i'm leaving soon. i didn’t come for that. please just listen.”

Pulcheria Alexandrovna approached timidly.

“mother, no matter what happens, what you hear, what people say, will you always love me like now?” he asked, not thinking, just speaking from his heart.

“Rodya, what’s wrong? why ask that? who would tell me anything bad about you? i wouldn’t believe them.”

“i came to tell you i love you, that’s why i'm glad we’re alone, and Dounia’s not here,” he continued. “i want you to know, even if things get rough, your son loves you more than anything, and whatever you thought about me being cruel, it was wrong. i'll never stop loving you... that's all i wanted to start with.”

Pulcheria Alexandrovna hugged him silently, tears in her eyes.

“i don’t know what’s wrong with you, Rodya. i thought we were just boring you, but now i see something heavy’s coming your way, and that’s why you’re upset. i've felt it for a while. forgive me for saying this. it keeps me up at night. Dounia talked about you in her sleep last night, but i couldn’t make sense of it. i've felt all day like something's coming, and now it has! Rodya, where are you going?”

“yes.”

“i thought so! i can come with you if you need me. Dounia loves you too, and Sofya Semyonovna can join us if you want. Dmitri Prokofitch can help us all go together. but... where are you going?”

“goodbye, mother.”

“what, today?” she cried, like she was losing him forever.

“i can’t stay, i have to leave now....”

“can’t i come with you?”

“no, but pray for me. maybe your prayer will reach Him.”

"let me bless you and sign you with the cross. yeah, that's right, that's right. God, what are we doing?"

he was relieved no one else was around, just him and his mom. first time in months he felt his heart soften. he dropped down, kissed her feet, and they both cried, hugging. she wasn’t surprised, didn’t ask questions. she’d sensed something was up for a while and now it was hitting him hard.

"rodya, my darling, my firstborn," she sobbed, "you’re just like you were when you were little. you'd run to me, hug me, kiss me. when your father was alive and we had nothing, you made it better just by being there. after he passed, we cried together at his grave like we are now. if i’ve been crying lately, it’s because a mother knows when trouble’s coming. when i saw you that night, i knew from your eyes. my heart dropped, and today when you walked in, i thought the worst had come. rodya, you’re not leaving today?"

"no."

"you'll come again?"

"yeah... i’ll come."

"rodya, please, i'm not asking questions. i know i can't. just tell me... is it far where you’re going?"

"very far."

"what's there for you? a job?"

"whatever God sends... just pray for me." raskolnikov headed for the door, but she grabbed him, looking desperate. her face was all fear.

"enough, mother," raskolnikov said, regretting the visit.

"not forever, right? you’ll come back tomorrow?"

"i will, i will, goodbye." he finally pulled away.

evening was warm, clear. raskolnikov rushed to his place. wanted to get things done before sunset. didn’t want to run into anyone till then. going up, he saw nastasya watching him. "someone come for me?" he thought. dreaded seeing porfiry. but opening the door, there was dounia, deep in thought, like she'd been waiting a while. he stopped at the door. she rose, shocked, eyes full of horror and grief. he saw she knew.

"should i come in or leave?" he asked, unsure.

"i’ve been with sofya semyonovna all day. waiting for you. thought you’d come."

raskolnikov sank into a chair, exhausted.

"i’m weak, dounia, so tired. wish i could control myself right now."

he looked at her, wary.

"where were you all night?"

"can't remember clearly. sister, i wanted to decide once and for all. walked by the neva, thought about ending it there, but... couldn't," he whispered, looking at her again.

"thank God! we were so worried, sofya semyonovna and i. so you still believe in life? thank God, thank God!"

raskolnikov gave a bitter smile.

"no faith, but i was just crying in mom’s arms; no faith, but asked her to pray. don’t know, dounia, don’t understand."

"you went to mom’s? told her?" dounia cried, horrified. "you didn’t do that?"

no, i didn’t tell her with words, but she got it. heard you talking in your sleep. she might already half know. maybe it was wrong to visit her. dont even know why i went. im just contemptible, dounia.

"contemptible, but ready to face the pain. you are, right?"

"yeah, im going. now. thought about drowning myself to escape the shame, dounia, but staring at the water, figured if i thought i was strong, better not be scared of disgrace," he said, rushing. "its pride, dounia."

"pride, rodya."

his eyes had a spark; seemed glad to still feel proud.

"you dont think, sister, i was just scared of the water?" he asked, with a dark smile.

"oh, rodya, stop!" cried dounia bitterly. silence. he stared at the floor; dounia watched him with anguish from across the table. suddenly, he stood up.

"its late, time to go! im going to turn myself in. but dont know why."

tears rolled down her cheeks.

"youre crying, sister, but can you shake my hand?"

"you doubted it?"

she embraced him.

"arent you half paying for your crime by facing this?" she cried, holding him tight and kissing him.

"crime? what crime?" he shouted, angry. "i killed a nasty old pawnbroker, useful to no one!... killing her was payback for forty sins. she was draining the poor. was that crime? im not thinking about it or paying for it, and why do you all harp on 'crime, crime!' just now i see the stupidity of my cowardice, now that ive decided to face this useless disgrace. just because im contemptible and empty, ive decided to, or maybe for my gain, as that... porfiry... hinted!"

"brother, what are you saying? you shed blood!" cried dounia in despair.

"which all men do," he added almost frantically, "flows like rivers, spilled like champagne, crowning men in the capitol as heroes. look closer and see it! i too wanted to help people, would have done hundreds, thousands of good deeds to balance that one dumb move, not dumb, just clumsy, the idea wasnt so dumb—it only looks that way since it failed... (everything looks dumb when it fails.) by that clumsiness, i only wanted independence, to start, get means, then smooth it all with benefits beyond measure... but i... couldnt even start, because im contemptible, thats it! and still, i dont see it your way. if id succeeded, id be a hero, but now im caught."

"but thats not true, not true! brother, what are you saying?"

"ah, its not pretty, not aesthetic! i dont get why bombarding people by siege is more noble. fear of appearances is impotence's first sign. ive never seen this clearer than now, and im further than ever from seeing what i did as crime. never been stronger or more sure than now."

his face was flushed even though he was worn out, and when he looked at Dounia, he saw all that pain in her eyes and it stopped him. he knew he'd made things miserable for these two women, and he was the reason why.

“Dounia, if im guilty, forgive me. goodbye. we’re not gonna argue. it’s time to go. don’t follow me, please. i’ve got somewhere else to be... but you go sit with mom. it’s my last request. she’s in a state she can’t handle; she’ll lose it. stay with her! Razumihin’s there too. i’ve talked to him... don’t cry for me: i’ll try to stay honest, even if i am a murderer. maybe i’ll make a name for myself someday. i won’t disgrace you, you’ll see. now goodbye for now,” he said quickly, seeing that look in Dounia’s eyes again. “why are you crying? don’t cry: we’re not saying goodbye forever! ah, wait, i forgot something!”

he went to the table, grabbed a dusty book, opened it up, and took out a small watercolor portrait. it was the landlady’s daughter, the strange girl who wanted to be a nun and died of fever. he looked at her face for a minute, kissed it, and gave it to Dounia.

“i talked a lot with her, only her,” he said. “shared much of what’s now happened. don’t worry,” he said to Dounia, “she didn’t agree with it either, and i’m glad she’s gone. but everything’s changing now, everything’s breaking,” he said, slipping back into his gloom. “everything, and am i ready? do i even want it? they say i need to suffer! what’s the point of this suffering? will i understand why when i’m crushed by hardship? what will i live for then? why am i agreeing to this life now? i knew i was worthless when i saw the Neva at dawn today!”

they both left. it was tough for Dounia, but she loved him. she walked away, then after a bit, turned to look back. he was still there. at the corner, he turned too, their eyes met one last time; seeing her look, he waved her off impatiently, annoyed, and turned the corner.

“im wicked,” he thought, ashamed of his angry wave at Dounia. “but why do they love me if i don’t deserve it? oh, if i were alone and no one loved me and i never loved anyone! _none of this would’ve happened._ will i, in those years, grow so meek i’ll whimper at every word that i’m a criminal? yes, that’s why they’re sending me, what they want. look at them all rushing around, each one a scoundrel and worse, an idiot. but try to save me and they’d be furious with righteousness. oh, how i hate them!”

he sat there thinking, how did i end up so broken in front of everyone? twenty years of this will grind you down, no doubt. like water on rock. and then what? why bother sticking around when you already know where this leads? he'd asked himself this a hundred times since last night, but he still went on.

CHAPTER VIII

he stepped into sonia's room and it was almost dark. she'd been waiting all day, worried sick. dounia was with her, remembering what svidrigailov said about sonia knowing. no need to go into their talk and tears, but they got close. dounia took comfort knowing her brother had someone. he went to sonia first with his confession; she was his human connection. dounia didn't need to ask—she knew sonia would follow him anywhere fate took him. she looked at sonia with a kind of reverence that almost embarrassed her. sonia felt unworthy to even look at dounia. that first respectful meeting in raskolnikov’s room stayed with sonia as something almost sacred.

dounia got restless, left sonia to wait in her brother's room, thinking he’d show up there. alone, sonia was tormented by the thought he might end his life. dounia feared it too, but together they convinced each other it wouldn't happen. apart, it was all they could think about. sonia recalled svidrigailov saying raskolnikov had two choices—siberia or... she knew his pride, his vanity, his lack of belief.

“is it just cowardice and fear of death keeping him alive?” she despaired.

sunset came. sonia stood, staring out the window, seeing nothing but a blank wall. just when she was sure he was gone for good—he walked in.

she cried out in joy, then turned pale seeing his face.

“yes,” raskolnikov said, smiling. “i'm here for your cross, sonia. you told me to go to the crossroads; why’re you scared now?”

sonia looked at him, stunned. his voice was off; a chill ran through her. then she realized he was hiding behind his words, avoiding her eyes.

sonia, i've made up my mind, it's just gotta be this way. there's a lot to it, but not worth going into. what's bugging me is all those idiots staring at me, asking their dumb questions. i mean, i can't stand porfiry right now. i'm sick of him. i'd rather hit up my buddy, the explosive lieutenant. surprise him, you know? gotta keep it cool though, been losing my temper too easy. almost lost it on my sister just for looking back at me. what am i turning into? anyway, where are the crosses?

he was all over the place, couldn't focus, mind racing, hands a bit shaky.

sonia didn't say a word, just took two crosses out—a cypress wood one and a copper one. she crossed herself and him, then put the wooden one around his neck.

"it's like taking up my own cross," he chuckled. "as if i haven't had enough already. the wood one's like the peasants; copper's lizaveta's—keep that one yourself. she had it on then, right? i remember a silver one and a little ikon. tossed them back on the old woman's neck. those fit better now. but i'm just rambling. came to warn you, sonia, just so you know... that's it. thought i had more. you wanted me to go, now i'm heading to prison. why the tears, sonia? cut it out! hate all this."

his heart hurt seeing her. "why's she so upset?" he wondered. "what am i to her? why's she looking after me like my mom or dounia? she'll end up being my nurse."

"cross yourself, say a prayer," sonia pleaded softly.

"sure, whatever you want, sonia, sincerely..."

but he meant to say something else.

he crossed himself a few times. sonia grabbed her shawl, the green one marmeladov talked about, "the family shawl." raskolnikov noticed but didn't ask. he felt like he was missing stuff and it made him uneasy. suddenly realized sonia was planning to come with him.

"what're you doing? stay here! i'll go alone," he snapped, feeling a mix of fear and irritation, heading for the door. "no need for a parade," he muttered on his way out.

sonia stayed in the room. he'd forgotten to say goodbye. doubt hit him hard.

"was this the right call?" he questioned himself on the stairs. "could he just stop it all and not go?"

but still he went on. couldn't ask any more questions. remembered leaving sonia in that green shawl, right where i shouted at her. stopped short. thought hit me like it was waiting.

what was i doing there? said it was business. what business? none. told her i was leaving, but why? do i love her? no. pushed her away. wanted her tears, her fear. needed something to hold me back, a friendly face to see. and i had the nerve to think i was something. pathetic.

walked along the canal. not far now. reached the bridge, took a detour to the hay market. 

looked around, nothing stuck. everything slipped away. thought about riding a prison van over this bridge someday. how will i see the canal then? remembered a sign, "campany," that missing "a" might matter later. what'll i feel then? such nonsense im worried about now. god, im acting like a child.

crowd was a mess, but i stayed. alone was worse. some drunk guy causing a scene. watched him, laughed a little. forgot him soon after. kept moving, not sure where. hit the square and got hit with a wave of something. body and mind. overwhelming.

man, sonia’s words hit him hard. “go to the cross-roads, bow down, kiss the earth, tell everyone ‘im a murderer.’” it just overwhelmed him. he felt all that misery and anxiety bear down, but there was something freeing about it, like a single spark lighting a fire inside. he knelt right there in the square, kissed the dirty earth, and did it again. folks around thought he was drunk. they laughed, called him a gentleman. he almost said the words out loud, but they died in his throat. kept his head down, headed for the police office. on the way, he spotted sonia hiding, knew she’d follow him anywhere. that hurt, but he kept moving.

he headed up to the third floor, legs shaky. place was a mess, eggshells and fumes everywhere. hadn’t been there since that day. paused to breathe, thought about going to someone else like nikodim fomitch, but nah, straight to the “explosive lieutenant.” might as well face it head-on.

opened the office door, not many folks around. walked to the next room, wondering if he’d have to say it. just a clerk at a desk. looked around, asked, “no one in?”

“Ah, not a sound or sight, but I smell Russian... how’s it go again? I forgot! ‘At your service!’” a familiar voice yelled. 

Raskolnikov flinched. The Explosive Lieutenant was there. He’d just come from the next room. “Fate’s hand,” Raskolnikov thought. “Why him?”

“You’re here to see us? What’s up?” Ilya Petrovitch shouted, all cheerful, maybe a bit tipsy. “If it’s business, you’re early. I’m just here by chance... but I’ll see what I can do. I must say, I... what is it? Excuse me....”

“Raskolnikov.”

“Of course, Raskolnikov. Didn’t think I’d forget, did you? Not me... Rodion Ro--Ro--Rodionovitch, right?”

“Rodion Romanovitch.”

“Yeah, yeah, Rodion Romanovitch! Was just about to say that. I’ve asked around about you. I’ve been real sorry since... since how I acted... heard later you were a literary man... educated too... starting out... Mercy! What writer or scholar doesn’t start with some quirks! My wife and I respect literature, she’s passionate about it! If a man’s a gentleman, the rest comes with talent, smarts, genius. Hats, they’re easy to buy; but what’s under it, can’t buy that! I even thought to apologize, but thought you’d... But I forgot, is there something you need? Family’s here?”

“Yeah, my mom and sister.”

“I met your sister--a smart and lovely lady. Sorry I got heated with you. But about your fainting fit--all cleared up! Bigotry! I get why you were upset. Changing lodgings cause your family’s here?”

“No, just stopped by... I wanted to find Zametov.”

“Oh, right! You’re friends, I heard. Well, no, Zametov’s not here. We’ve lost him. Not since yesterday... left all mad. Just a flighty kid, expected more, but you know our bright young men. He wanted exams, but it’s all talk. Different for you or Razumihin, your friend. Your path’s an intellectual one, can’t be stopped by failure. For you, life’s attractions _nihil est_--an ascetic, a monk, a hermit!... A book, pen behind your ear, research--that’s your thing! Same here.... Read Livingstone’s Travels?”

“No.”

“Oh, I have. Lots of Nihilists around nowadays, not surprising. What times are they? I ask. But we thought... you’re not a Nihilist, right? Tell me, straight up!”

“N-no...”

talk to me straight, like you would to yourself. work is work, but this ain't about friendship. it's about being human and having some love for the bigger picture. sure, i've got my duties and a family, but so do you, right? you mentioned Zametov. he's all about making a scene, nothing but noise over a drink. while i'm over here trying to keep things together, with a rank and a job to do.

Raskolnikov raised an eyebrow, not quite getting Ilya Petrovitch's drunken ramblings, though bits made sense. he watched, unsure where this was going.

"those midwives," Ilya Petrovitch went on, calling them "crop-headed wenches." they study anatomy at the Academy. i mean, if i fall sick, am i gonna call one of them to help? what do you think? ha, it's like they're too eager for education. once you got it, enough's enough. why insult folks like Zametov does? take these suicides, happening all the time. spent their last penny just to end it. like this morning, we heard about some guy.

“Svidrigaïlov,” someone called.

Raskolnikov jumped. “Svidrigaïlov shot himself?”

“you knew him?”

“yeah, he hadn't been here long.”

“right. lost his wife, lived wild, then bam, gone. left a note saying it was his choice, no blame. had money too. how'd you know him?”

“my sister was his family’s governess.”

“really? you must know something. any suspicions?”

“saw him yesterday, drinking. knew nothing.”

Raskolnikov felt crushed, like the air was gone.

“you look pale. it’s stuffy in here.”

“i should go,” Raskolnikov muttered. “sorry for the bother.”

“no bother, come by anytime. good to see you.”

Raskolnikov nodded. “just came for Zametov.”

“i get it, good to see you.”

“yeah, thanks... goodbye,” Raskolnikov managed a smile.

he stumbled outside, dizzy and lost, leaning against the wall while he headed down the stairs. thought someone brushed by him, maybe a dog barking below, maybe a woman yelling. out in the yard, there's Sonia, looking pale and scared. she stared at him, and he just stood there, that weird smile on his face. then he turned and headed back inside to the police office.

Ilya Petrovitch was busy with some papers, that same peasant from the stairs was there too.

"back again? forgot something? what's up?"

Raskolnikov, pale and wide-eyed, shuffled closer. leaned on the table, tried to speak, only managed some sounds.

"you look sick, sit down. water?"

he sat, eyes on Ilya Petrovitch, who looked confused. they just stared at each other. water arrived.

"it was me..." Raskolnikov started.

"drink some water."

he waved away the water, speaking softly but clear:

"i killed the old pawnbroker woman and her sister Lizaveta with an axe and robbed them."

Ilya Petrovitch's jaw dropped. people gathered around.

Raskolnikov said it again.

EPILOGUE

Siberia. on a broad river's banks, a town stands, an administrative hub in Russia. inside the town, a fortress, and in that fortress, a prison. nine months in there for second-class convict Rodion Raskolnikov. a year and a half since he did it.

Trial was straightforward. He stuck to his story, didn't twist facts or leave anything out. He laid out every detail of the murder, explained the pledge piece found in her hand, and how he took her keys, described them, even the chest and its contents. He unraveled the mystery around Lizaveta’s death, recounted Koch and the student knocking, all their words. Described fleeing, hiding, and finally pointed to the stone under Voznesensky Prospect where the purse and trinkets were. 

What got the lawyers was him stashing the purse and trinkets, untouched. He couldn’t even recall the trinkets or how much was in the purse. Turns out it held 317 roubles and 60 copecks. Damp got to the notes, too. Lawyers puzzled over why he’d lie about this when he was honest elsewhere. Some finally figured he might not have checked the purse at all. Led them to think maybe he did the crime in a fit of madness, no real motive or gain, just mania. His hypochondria backed this, confirmed by witnesses like Dr. Zossimov and others. This made them see him as more than just a common criminal.

But those pushing this angle were frustrated when he didn’t really defend himself. When asked why he did it, he bluntly said it was poverty and desperation, hoping to start life anew with the money he thought he'd find. Said his confession came from genuine regret. All this was pretty raw.

sentence came down lighter than expected. guy didn't try to defend himself, more like he piled on his own guilt. weird details of the crime got considered. no doubt he was broke and not right in the head back then. he didn't use what he stole, probably felt remorse or just wasn't thinking straight. the whole thing with Lizaveta backed that up: two murders and leaves the door wide open? plus, he confessed when the case was a mess thanks to Nikolay's false testimony, and no one even suspected him, no evidence. Porfiry Petrovitch kept his word on that. Razumihin dug up some good stuff too, like how Raskolnikov helped a sick student at university, spent his last money on him, cared for the student's old man, even paid for his funeral. landlady vouched for a time he saved kids from a fire, got burned doing it. witnesses confirmed. these things helped him.

in the end, they gave him eight years of penal servitude, second class, considering all that.

at the trial's start, Raskolnikov's mom got sick. Dounia and Razumihin moved her out of Petersburg during it. Razumihin picked a nearby town, kept tabs on the trial and saw Avdotya Romanovna. Pulcheria Alexandrovna's illness was some strange nervous thing, messed with her mind a bit.

when Dounia got back from seeing her brother, her mom was already out of it, feverish. Razumihin and Dounia cooked up a story for her about Raskolnikov going on a business trip, something that'd earn him money and respect.

but Pulcheria Alexandrovna never asked about Raskolnikov, not then, not later. she spun her own tale about him saying goodbye, hinted she knew secrets, powerful enemies after him, so he had to hide. she believed he'd be a top politician someday, thought his article showed his talent. she read it over and over, even out loud, almost took it to bed. but didn't really ask where Rodya was, even when others dodged the topic, which should've made her curious.

they started to get worried when pulcheria alexandrovna went quiet about some stuff. usually, she'd be upset not getting letters from rodya — she lived for those. dounia got uneasy thinking maybe her mom knew something bad happened to rodya and was too scared to ask.

sometimes, pulcheria would steer the talk where you couldn't avoid mentioning rodya's whereabouts. when she got vague or suspicious answers, she'd go silent and moody for ages. dounia figured it was tough to fool her and decided to just keep quiet on certain things. but it was clear pulcheria suspected something awful. dounia remembered rodya saying their mom heard her talking in her sleep after meeting svidrigailov, probably pieced something together from that. she'd swing between long spells of silence and bursts of chatty hope about rodya's future. they played along, even if she knew they were faking it.

five months after rodya's confession, he got sentenced. razumihin and sonia visited him in prison whenever they could. when it was time to part, dounia and razumihin promised rodya it wouldn't be forever. razumihin was determined to set up a stable life and save enough to move to siberia, where they could start anew with rodya. they all cried when saying goodbye.

rodya had been spaced out for days, always worrying about his mom. hearing about her illness made him more somber. he kept distant from sonia, though she was ready to follow him to siberia with the money from svidrigailov. they didn't talk about it, but they both knew it was happening. at their final goodbye, he gave a strange smile at his sister's and razumihin's dreams of a good future after prison, predicting their mom wouldn't last much longer. sonia and rodya left together.

two months later, dounia married razumihin. it was a quiet, sad affair, but porfiry petrovitch and zossimov were there. razumihin was determined, and dounia trusted him completely. he even started back at university to finish his degree. they kept planning for the future, aiming to move to siberia in five years, pinning hopes on sonia for now.

Pulcheria Alexandrovna was thrilled about Dounia marrying Razumihin, but after the wedding, she got more anxious. To cheer her up, Razumihin shared stories about Raskolnikov: how he helped a poor student and his father, and how he got burned saving two kids from a fire. Pulcheria was over the moon about these stories. She wouldn’t stop talking about them, even to strangers, with Dounia tagging along. On buses, in shops, she’d start up about her son, the article he wrote, his good deeds, and so on. Dounia struggled to keep her in check, worried someone might connect Raskolnikov to his trial. Pulcheria even tracked down the mother of the kids he saved and insisted on visiting her.

Her anxiety hit a peak. She’d cry out of nowhere and got feverish and delirious. One morning, she suddenly decided Rodya should be home soon, remembering he said he’d be back in nine months.
