She started preparing his room, cleaning, putting up new curtains. Dounia was worried but helped her. After a tiring day of dreams and tears, Pulcheria got really sick at night, and by morning she was feverish and out of her mind. It was brain fever. She passed away in two weeks. In her delirium, she let slip she knew more about Raskolnikov’s grim fate than they thought.

Raskolnikov didn’t hear about his mom's death for a while, even though Sonia was writing regularly to the Razumihins. At first, her letters seemed dull, but eventually, they realized she was painting a full picture of Raskolnikov’s life in Siberia. Her letters were full of straightforward details about his life as a convict—no personal feelings or guesses about the future. She just laid out the facts: his health, what he said, what he asked her to do. Her precision gave a clear image of their troubled brother, with no room for error because everything was just facts.

Dounia and her husband didn't find much comfort at first. Sonia wrote that he was always sullen, not ready to talk, barely cared about the news in their letters. Sometimes he'd ask about his mom, and when Sonia finally told him she'd passed, he didn't show much reaction, at least not on the outside. He seemed closed off, but he had a clear view of his new life. He knew where he stood, didn't expect much, and wasn't surprised by his surroundings, even if everything was different. His health was fine, did his work without fuss, didn't care about food much. The food was so bad, he ended up taking money from Sonia for tea. He didn't want her worrying about him, saying it just bugged him. Sonia mentioned the prison was crowded and rough, he slept on a plank bed with a rug, and didn't seem to care about changing that. His rough living wasn't planned, just came from not caring.

At first, he wasn't interested in Sonia's visits, almost bothered by them, didn't want to talk, was rude. But over time, he got used to them, needed them. He was upset when she couldn't visit because she was sick. She'd see him on holidays at the gates or in the guard-room for a few minutes. On workdays, she'd visit him at the workshops, kilns, or sheds by the Irtish. 

Sonia wrote she made some acquaintances in town, did sewing since there weren't many dressmakers around. She didn't mention the authorities were interested in Raskolnikov through her, or that his tasks were made easier. Then came news (Dounia had noticed signs of this in earlier letters) that he kept to himself, prisoners didn't like him, he'd go silent for days, and was getting pale. Sonia's last letter said he was seriously ill in the prison hospital.

he'd been sick a lot. prison life didn't break him though. not the hard labor, the bad food, the shaved head, or the patched clothes. he didn't care about those. he even welcomed the hard work. being worn out meant a few hours of solid sleep. thin cabbage soup with beetles? wasn't much, but he'd had less as a student. clothes were warm, fit the life. didn't even feel the chains. was he embarrassed by the shaved head and mismatched coat? not really. especially not in front of Sonia. she was scared of him, so why be ashamed? but he was, and he took it out on her with a rough attitude. it wasn't the look that shamed him; it was his pride. hit him hard. he wished he could blame himself, that would be easier. he judged himself harshly but found only a stupid mistake, nothing horrible. shame was because he, Raskolnikov, messed up so badly by blind chance and now had to bow down to a dumb sentence to find peace.

he felt anxious without reason, and the future looked like endless sacrifice for nothing. and what was eight years? he'd be thirty-two, starting new. what was the point? why push forward? living just to exist? that wasn't enough. he'd been ready to give up life for an idea or hope before. mere existence was never enough; he always wanted more. maybe because he thought he deserved more than others.

if only fate had given him real regret, the kind that tears at you, keeps you up at night. he would've welcomed it. pain and tears would mean feeling alive. but he didn't regret his crime.

he couldn't even find relief by blaming his stupidity. in prison, with time to think, his actions didn't seem as foolish as they had. 

"how," he wondered, "is my theory more foolish than others that've been around forever? look at it independently, and it doesn’t seem so strange. oh, doubters and cheap philosophers, why stop halfway?"

why do they see what i did as so horrible? is it just cause it was a crime? what even is a crime? my conscience is clear. sure, legally, it was a crime. law was broken, blood was spilled. fine, punish me for breaking the law... that's it. think about it, a lot of folks who took power without inheriting it should've been stopped right at the start. but they made it, and so they were right. i didn’t, so i had no right to try.

that's where i see my fault, in failing and admitting it.

why didn’t i just end it? why stand by the river and choose to confess? is living really that strong of a pull? svidrigaïlov managed it, even scared of death.

i was a mess with this question, not seeing that when i stood by the river, maybe i sensed a lie in myself and my beliefs. didn’t get that this might mean a future change, a new look at life.

blamed it on instinct, not stepping over it cause of weakness. looked at the other prisoners, amazed how much they loved life, maybe even more in here. saw their pain, but they still cherished the little things, like sunlight or a hidden spring, like a lover waiting for them.

in prison, i missed a lot, didn’t want to see. kept my eyes down. nasty to face it. but then things surprised me, noticed stuff i hadn’t before. what hit me hardest was the gap between me and them. like different species, distrust on both sides. knew why i was isolated, but didn’t realize how deep it was. some polish political prisoners were there too, looking down on everyone. but i saw the so-called ignorant ones were wiser in many ways. some russians felt the same superiority. saw their mistake clear as day. everyone disliked me, even started to hate me. no idea why. worse criminals mocked my crime.

"you’re a gentleman," they'd say. "axes aren’t for gentlemen."

come lent, my turn came for the sacrament. went to church, prayed like the rest. one day, a fight broke out, not sure how. they all turned on me.

"you’re an infidel! don’t believe in god," they yelled. "you ought to be killed."

he never talked to them about God or what he believed, but they wanted to kill him for being an infidel. he kept quiet. one prisoner lost it, charging at him. Raskolnikov stayed calm, didn't move a muscle. a guard got between them just in time, or it would've been bloody.

another thing he couldn't figure out: why did they all like Sonia so much? she didn’t try to charm them, barely saw them. sometimes she'd stop by while he was working. everyone knew she was there for him, knew her life. she didn’t hand out money or do favors. just once, at Christmas, sent pies and rolls. slowly, they got closer. she'd help mail letters for them. relatives visiting brought gifts for the prisoners, left them with Sonia. their families knew her, visited her. when she visited Raskolnikov or passed by prisoners, they tipped their hats. “little mother Sofya Semyonovna, you're our good little mother,” they’d say. she'd smile and bow, and it made their day. they even watched how she walked, couldn’t decide what to admire most. they’d even ask her for help when sick.

he was laid up in the hospital from Lent till after Easter. once he got better, he started remembering the wild dreams he had during the fever. dreamt of a new plague out of Asia wiping out nearly everyone. only a few chosen spared. some new microbes with a mind of their own messing with people. made people crazy and violent. but they thought they were the sharpest they'd ever been. everyone sure they were right, couldn't understand each other, tearing at each other. whole places fell apart. armies turned on themselves. chaos everywhere. just a few could make it through. a pure group to start fresh, but nobody saw them or heard them.

raskolnikov couldn't shake this dream. it stuck with him long after the fever. second week after Easter now. spring days, warm and bright. prison windows cracked open, guard pacing outside. sonia managed to visit twice, hard to get permission. often she just stood in the yard, looking up.

one evening, when he was on the mend, raskolnikov saw sonia at the gate. something hit him hard. he pulled back. she didn't come the next couple of days. he found himself waiting for her. finally, he got discharged. back at the prison, heard she was sick at home. worried, he sent to check on her. turns out, just a cold. sonia sent him a note saying she'd be better soon and would visit him at work. his heart hurt reading it.

another warm bright day. 6 am, off to the river bank for work. they used to pound alabaster there, kiln in a shed. three were sent. one convict with the guard to fetch a tool; the other sorting wood, laying it in the kiln. raskolnikov sat on logs, staring at the wide deserted river. from the high bank, broad view opened up. faint singing from the other bank. the steppe in sunshine, nomads' tents like black specks. freedom out there, men living different lives, time like it stood still, age of Abraham still hanging on. raskolnikov lost in day-dreams, restless without knowing why. then sonia appeared beside him, quiet as ever. early morning, chill still hanging in the air. she wore her old burnous, green shawl. face thinner, paler, still showing illness. she smiled, timidly offered her hand. she always did, sometimes not at all, afraid he'd reject it. he took it, though, always looking vexed. now, their hands stayed together. he glanced at her, eyes back to the ground. no one was watching. the guard had turned away.

suddenly something hit him. he was at her feet, crying, arms around her knees. she was scared at first, went pale. jumped up, trembling. but then she got it, happiness in her eyes. she knew he loved her, that moment finally came.

they wanted to talk, but couldn’t. tears there instead. both pale, thin, but their faces bright with a new beginning, a full resurrection, a new life. love renewed them; their hearts held endless life for each other.

they decided to wait. patient. seven more years, with suffering and happiness ahead. but he was reborn and he felt it deeply. she lived through him now.

that evening, barracks locked, raskolnikov on his plank bed thinking of her. felt even the convicts, once his enemies, looked at him differently. even talked with them, and they responded friendly. he remembered and thought, wasn't everything bound to change now?

he thought about her. remembered how he kept hurting her over and over. her face, so pale and thin, stuck with him, but it didn't gnaw at him like before. he knew he'd make it right with all the love he had now. the past? all those pains and even his crime and time inside felt like something outside of him, like it happened to someone else. he wasn't thinking much that night, just feeling. life was taking over the theories in his head.

under his pillow was the New Testament. sonia's book. she used it to read him the story of Lazarus. he thought she'd push religion on him, but she never did. not a word. he asked for it before he got sick, and she brought it to him quietly. he hadn't opened it yet.

didn't open it now either, but wondered if her beliefs could be his. her feelings, maybe...

she was upset that day too, and got sick again later. but she was also happy, almost scared of how happy. seven years, just seven. at first, they thought of it like a week. he didn't realize a new life wouldn't come free, that he'd pay with effort and struggle.

but this is where a new story starts. a man's slow renewal, his crossing into a new life. that could be a new tale, but for now, this one's done.
